# 天原发微

**Heaven’s Origin Unfolded**

*Scripture Title:* *Heaven’s Origin Unfolded*
*Author:* Bao Yunlong of the Song Dynasty
*Eighteen fascicles*
*Source of the base text:* *Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era*, *Taiqing* Section
*Collated edition:* Photographic reproduction from the *Wenyuan Pavilion* edition of the *Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*

---

**Heaven’s Origin Unfolded, Fascicle One**
*Compiled by* Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Lu-Qi
*Edited by* Xu Gu
*Collated by* Fang Hui Wanli

### The Supreme Polarity’s Primordial Name: *Zhongmo*

The Supreme Polarity (*Taiji*) resides within the soundless and scentless, yet it is the ultimate principle. It is the pivot of creation and transformation, the root and foundation of all things.

In Heaven and Earth, the Supreme Polarity exists within them, like a hinge in a door or a knot in a garment—it grasps the essential framework. In the myriad things, the Supreme Polarity exists within them, like a root in a tree or a trunk in a plant—it establishes their fundamental basis.

To call it "non-being" (*wu*) is to say that within the vast and formless, the ten thousand images are already fully present. To call it "being" (*you*) is to say that in the primordial state, before even a single thing existed, it could not be exhaustively explained.

As principle (*li*), it is supremely lofty yet does not abandon the lowly; supremely subtle yet does not depart from the manifest; supremely refined yet does not mingle with *qi*; supremely numinous yet does not stagnate in things. Between Heaven and Earth, what has not yet been born is born, what has not yet emerged emerges—none escapes birth and death within its domain.

Like the Northern Asterism, fixed in its place, while the circumpolar stars and the Seven Governors revolve around it day and night, what moves is all suspended from this, yet what makes it so has never itself moved.

The *Great Commentary on the Changes* states: "The *Changes* possesses the Supreme Polarity." Master Zhu [Xi] replied to Cai Xishan: "That which opens and closes, comes and goes without end, does so because it possesses this principle." With this principle, Heaven and Earth take their positions, and the *Changes* operates within them.

Two produces four, four produces eight. By the time of eight, three transformations occur in succession, and the Three Powers (*sancai*) become visible. The sages followed this, drawing the Eight Trigrams to embody the marvelous transformations of the *Changes* and determine fortune and misfortune.

Only afterward can this be expressed in writing; the word *Changes* (*Yi*) itself cannot be so expressed. *Yi* means "transformation." The intermingling of *yin* and *yang*, the thousand changes and ten thousand transformations, all emerge from this. This is what the Master [Confucius] meant by the "formless *Changes*"—speaking of transformation as formless yet possessing an ultimate principle. It encompasses both movement and stillness, the already-manifest and the not-yet-manifest.

The Supreme Polarity is the marvel of *xing* (nature) and *qing* (emotions), the principle of movement and stillness, the already-manifest and the not-yet-manifest. Within the Supreme Polarity, there is nothing but goodness. As for the three hundred eighty-four lines [of the *Yijing*], where good and evil appear, these arise only after the transformations of *yin* and *yang*.

The *Comprehensive Record* states: "Sincerity (*cheng*) is the non-doing of the Supreme Polarity; the incipient (*ji*) is the good and evil of *yin* and *yang*." The virtues—benevolence, righteousness, propriety, wisdom, and trust—are the Five Phases (*wuxing*), all explained in terms of the Diagram.

Zhu Hanshang said: "The *Changes* possesses the Supreme Polarity, which is the Great Void (*Taixu*)." *Yin* and *yang* arise when the Great Void gathers into *qi*. Softness and hardness arise when *qi* gathers into form. Benevolence and righteousness are born from the Great Void, manifest in *qi* and form, and activated in consciousness.

Divided into two, they become *yin* and *yang*. Divided into five, they become the Five Phases. Divided into the ten thousand affairs and things, all share this single origin, which is called *xing* (nature).

Nanxuan said: "The Supreme Polarity is the name for the convergence of the ten thousand principles, the marvel of *xing* (nature)." If one speaks only of *xing* and not of the Supreme Polarity, focusing solely on the not-yet-manifest, its functioning remains unseen. By calling it the Supreme Polarity, the marvel of *xing* becomes apparent.

Viewed from the perspective of individual endowment, it is called *ming* (destiny). Viewed from the perspective of Heaven, Earth, and humanity, it is called *li* (principle). Each of the ten thousand things possesses its own principle—this is "one Supreme Polarity in each thing." The ten thousand principles all emerge from a single source—this is "one Supreme Polarity encompassing all things."

This fool says: The "Polarity" (*ji*) is omnipresent. The "Polarity of the People" (*minji*) has the meaning of convergence; the "Northern Polarity" (*beiji*) has the meaning of a pivot; the "August Polarity" (*huangji*) has the meaning of a standard. To call it the "Supreme Polarity" (*Taiji*) is to speak of the source. This principle alone stands prior to the Two Modes (*yin-yang*) and the Five Phases, and operates within them.

Though it has no form of convergence, it is in truth the convergence of the ten thousand things. Though it has no form of a pivot, it is in truth the pivot of the ten thousand things. Though it has no form of a standard, it is in truth the standard of the ten thousand things.

Master Zhou said: "Non-polar (*wuji*) yet Supreme Polarity." This fool notes: Master Cheng said this sentence expresses the principle of "the vast and formless, where the ten thousand images are already fully present." Cai Jieqi said: "This clarifies the statement in the *Changes* that 'the *Changes* possesses the Supreme Polarity,' revealing the true reality of the Non-polar and the insight gained from the Master’s single word on the *Changes*." Those who consider it an arbitrary addition are mistaken. Here, I cite six statements from Master Zhu to explicate this sentence.

Those that take the Supreme Polarity as their subject are three:
1. Before images and numbers take form, the principle is already present.
2. Before Heaven and Earth existed, this principle was ultimately present.
3. The Non-polar (*wuji*) merely states that in the beginning, there was originally not a single thing—only this principle, which then gives rise to movement and stillness, producing *yin* and *yang*.

Those that take *yin* and *yang* as their subject are also three:
1. Viewed from the perspective of *yin* and *yang*, the Supreme Polarity resides within them. Today, some say that above *yin* and *yang* there exists another formless and shadowless entity called the Supreme Polarity—this is incorrect.
2. The Supreme Polarity is simply the principle of Heaven, Earth, and the ten thousand things, each of which contains a Supreme Polarity.
3. It is not that there is something separate from *yin* and *yang*; rather, within *yin* and *yang*, one points to their fundamental substance, speaking of it without mingling it with *yin* and *yang*.

When speaking of the Supreme Polarity in terms of the Supreme Polarity, it exists prior to *yin* and *yang*, before *yin* and *yang* have arisen. When speaking of the Supreme Polarity in terms of the generation of *yin* and *yang*, it exists within *yin* and *yang*, after *yin* and *yang* have arisen. To say that beyond *yin* and *yang* there exists a separate Supreme Polarity is mistaken.

Those who insistently regard it as their master fall inevitably into the error of Liezi’s "neither arising nor transforming." Those who cling solely to the claim that the Supreme Polarity exists within *yin* and *yang* remain blind to where the Great Root truly lies.

Some say: before *yang* there is *yin*, and before *yin* there is *yang*. To speak of it in terms of *yin* and *yang* not yet arisen may raise doubts. Yet if one takes the moment just before the first stirring of *yang* and calls it the state before *yin* and *yang* have arisen, this may also be acceptable. Though *yin* and *yang* have not yet arisen, the principle that generates *yin* and *yang* is already fully present in the human heart as the "center before arousal"—this is collectively named the Supreme Polarity.

Master Zhu said: The Supreme Polarity is what points to the root of Heaven, Earth, and the ten thousand things; the Non-Polar is what reveals the wondrousness of the soundless and scentless. Because it has no vessel or form, yet the principles of Heaven, Earth, and the ten thousand things are all contained within it, it is called the Non-Polar. And because the Supreme Polarity encompasses the principles of Heaven, Earth, and the ten thousand things yet has no vessel or form, it is called the Supreme Polarity, which is fundamentally non-polar. It exists before there are things, yet it is never absent after things come to be. It is said to be beyond *yin* and *yang*, yet it never fails to operate within *yin* and *yang*. If one speaks of it as pervading the entire body, present everywhere without exception, then there is nothing to be said of sound, scent, influence, or trace. Its loftiness reaches the Non-Polar, and the subtlety of the Supreme Polarity truly does not depart from the inquiries of daily life.

He also said: "The bearing of high Heaven" speaks of the Non-Polar within the realm of existence. "Non-Polar yet Supreme Polarity" speaks of existence within the realm of the Non-Polar. The Non-Polar describes the wondrousness of the soundless and scentless; the Supreme Polarity names the ultimate perfection of this principle. Tracing downward from the Non-Polar and Supreme Polarity, the ten thousand things each attain their proper place, and the great and small are fixed in measure—each thing possesses a Supreme Polarity. Tracing upward from the Supreme Polarity, which is fundamentally non-polar, the ten thousand things are one, and the one is truly the ten thousand parts—all things share a single Supreme Polarity in their totality. Though the two *qi* and five phases give rise to humans and things, tangled and intertwined in endless transformations, the Supreme Polarity moves within them, inherently ordered and structured, not a chaotic void. Thus, when something is sensed externally, the center responds internally; whatever one encounters before the eyes is this principle. Great and small, joy and sorrow...

I heard from my teacher: Zhou Dunyi’s phrase "Non-Polar yet Supreme Polarity" is a forward expression; "the Supreme Polarity is fundamentally non-polar" is a reverse expression. "The bearing of high Heaven, soundless and scentless" is like Zhou’s "Non-Polar yet Supreme Polarity." If one were to reverse the lines of the *Odes* and say, "Soundless and scentless, the bearing of high Heaven," how would this not also be acceptable? The phrase "Non-Polar yet Supreme Polarity" does not mean that the Non-Polar is placed above the Supreme Polarity. Xiangshan refused to acknowledge the phrase "the Supreme Polarity is fundamentally non-polar" and attacked only the first line, pointlessly engaging in contentious debate. If I had met Xiangshan, I would have dared to ask the late learner, still unenlightened: "Please show me the Supreme Polarity." Xiangshan would surely have become furious: "The Supreme Polarity is a formless thing, the fundamental principle upon which the great earth is rooted. How could you bring it forth to look at? I have nothing to present, and you have nothing to see—this is the Non-Polar." Thus, Zhou Dunyi’s "Non-Polar yet Supreme Polarity" and "the Supreme Polarity is fundamentally non-polar" are truly unassailable discussions for all time.

He also said: From the Supreme Polarity to the transformation and generation of the ten thousand things, it is all but a single cycle. In the second cycle, Zhou Dunyi separated out a circle above to show the supremely undifferentiated principle of the Supreme Polarity in its formless state. This cycle alone has existed since the beginning of Heaven and Earth, endlessly revolving within it. Bestowed within each of the ten thousand things, the principle of Heaven is complete, neither borrowing from nor encroaching upon others. The entire body of the Supreme Polarity is wholly present within this Non-Polar, and the two and five phases wondrously unite and congeal. Thus it is said: Every person possesses the Supreme Polarity, and every thing possesses the Supreme Polarity—all is naturally so. The difference between humans and things lies solely in the endowment of *qi*. Master Zhu’s explanation of the *Diagram of the Supreme Polarity Unfolding* first shows people their origin, for only humans possess it. The subsequent stages show the foundation of the human Dao, enabling one to understand what makes a human human, a sage a sage, and why the noble and the petty each have their differing endowments of *qi*.

Some say: The first part of the *Diagram of the Supreme Polarity* resembles a chessboard, while the latter part resembles a person playing chess. I say: The upper circle represents the nature of Heaven and Earth; the lower circle, divided into *yin* and *yang*, contains the nature of *qi*-endowment. Master Shao said: The Dao is the Supreme Polarity. Master Zhu said: This refers to the natural principle of Heaven, Earth, and the ten thousand things. I say: The Dao is the Supreme Polarity—there are not two principles. Some claim that the Dao is a separate, suspended entity existing prior to the Supreme Polarity, but then the Dao and the Supreme Polarity would be two. How could the Supreme Polarity exist outside the Dao? In terms of its all-pervading movement, it is called the Dao; in terms of its ultimate perfection, it is called the Polarity. The Supreme Polarity is the undifferentiated Dao; it is the Polarity from which all principles branch. Wherever these branching principles extend, there is the Dao. Like piercing the nose of an ox or bridling the head of a horse, it follows wherever it flows—how could the Polarity and the Dao be two principles?

He also said: The heart-mind is the Supreme Polarity. Master Zhu said: This refers to the principle that humans receive as the master of their bodies. I say: The heart-mind is a spiritually luminous entity, the dwelling of human nature. The Polarity is the principle of utmost goodness, the form of human nature. When the heart-mind governs principle, the undifferentiated Supreme Polarity emerges. In responding to the ten thousand affairs, each thing possesses its own Polarity.

Thus Master You said: In stillness without thought, before the myriad virtues arise, this is the Ultimateless. When this heart-mind is luminously clear, its spiritual source undimmed, this is the Supreme Polarity. If within stillness a single thought stirs, then movement and quiescence become yin and yang.

Master Zhu said: Fu Xi composed the *Yi* beginning with the single line; King Wen expanded the *Yi* beginning with "Qian's origination." They never spoke of the Supreme Polarity, yet Confucius did. Confucius commented on the *Yi* beginning with the Supreme Polarity, yet never spoke of the Ultimateless—until Master Zhou did. Fu Xi and King Wen explained from yin-yang downward; Confucius, in turn, revealed the Supreme Polarity from above yin-yang. Cai Jitong said: "Had Heaven not given birth to Confucius, all ages would have been as a long night." Heaven first gave birth to Fu Xi and King Wen; had Confucius not come after, three thousand years would have been lost. Had the Cheng brothers and Master Zhou not come after, it would have been the same.

In my humble opinion: Without Fu Xi and King Wen’s *Yi*, there would be no *Yi*; without the Master’s discourse on the Supreme Polarity, people would not know the *Yi*’s root. Knowing the root, without Master Zhou’s discourse on the Ultimateless, people would not know the root of the Supreme Polarity. The *Yi* is one Supreme Polarity; the Supreme Polarity’s root is the Ultimateless. Once the subtlety of tracing things to their origin is made clear, the hearts of people across myriad ages will all know where to find their ultimate refuge.

Master Zhu once said: Master Zhou’s explanation of the Ultimateless and the Supreme Polarity is most excellent. He also cited Master Cheng’s discussion of Zhang Zai’s "pure emptiness, one greatness," which leads people astray if parsed too finely—his meaning was profound. I have studied the earlier masters, consulted teachers and friends, and arrived at this understanding:

The *Yi* contains the phrase "there is the Supreme Polarity." After Fu Xi and King Wen, none but Confucius could have articulated it. After Confucius, for fifteen hundred years, no one fully grasped it—until Shao Kangjie first recognized it as the root of the *Yi*, and Master Lianxi (Zhou Dunyi) first elucidated its meaning through the Ultimateless. The sages and worthies transmitted the Dao, gradually clarifying it to its most intimate essence.

From antiquity past to eternity future, without this single phrase in the *Yi*, one might add the word "change" (*yi*) to the Supreme Polarity, thereby revealing the Supreme Polarity’s rootedness—not like the heterodox, who fall into emptiness and nothingness. This word *yi* is most subtle and profound; it is the wondrous locus of yin-yang transformation, what is called the *Yi* without form. It exists before the Two Modes and the myriad transformations, yet embodies their essence. Though not yet manifest as yin and yang, it is the source from which the Supreme Polarity emerges. To clarify its non-being is not to speak of emptiness, but to point to the most real principle within. Thus it is called *yi*, called "being," called the Supreme Polarity—each word has its proper place. The Master feared people would not understand this principle, so he stated it clearly. To view the *Yi* merely as a book is to miss the mark entirely.

Master Zhou feared people would not grasp the principle of the Supreme Polarity, so he said: "Ultimateless and yet Supreme Polarity." By using the two words "Ultimateless," he explained the single word *yi* in the *Yi*, thereby clarifying the original meaning of the Supreme Polarity. Master Zhu, in turn, feared people would separate the phrase "Ultimateless and yet Supreme Polarity" into two, so he said: "Each thing possesses its own Supreme Polarity." Knowing this, one understands that the two qi, the Five Phases, and the thousand transformations and myriad changes constitute the *Yi*. Yet within each thing resides a naturally complete and undifferentiated Supreme Polarity that cannot be altered. This principle fills the cosmos, present everywhere—people simply fail to recognize it.

"Production and reproduction is called *yi*": this is the *yi* of "there is the Supreme Polarity" in the *Yi*. From this arise the Two Modes, the Four Images, and the Eight Trigrams. Not only within the *Yi* as a whole, but in every instance—whether in yin-yang, in male and female, in the human heart, or among the myriad kinds—each unfolds according to this principle, which cannot be altered. This is why the *Yi* generates yin and yang, yet transcends yin and yang. The *Yi* cannot be confined to yin-yang as its essence, nor can it be equated with the changes of yin-yang. Yin-yang is not the *Yi*; yin-yang’s transformations arise from the formless *Yi*. Thus I say: "There is the Supreme Polarity in the *Yi*," for the *Yi* is without form.

"Without thought, without action" is the root of the *Yi*. "Establishing positions, arranging ranks, the *Yi* is established and enacted"—this is the function of the *Yi*. In humans, it is called *yi*; in humans, it is called nature—none is apart from the one Supreme Polarity. The *Yi* of the Master (Confucius) speaks of "there is"; the *Yi* of Master Zhou speaks of "without." Shaozi’s (Shao Yong) earlier teachings may follow a different path. Later generations, confused by theories of the *Yi*’s "Great Beginning" and "Great Simplicity," lost the meaning of the single word *yi*. Those who take "there is a thing formed in solitude" as the sole transmission of the Supreme Polarity come close to the Master’s words, yet still fall short.

*Tianyuan Fawei*, Scroll One, concludes.

This chapter speaks of the Supreme Polarity (*Taiji*), wherein movement and stillness give rise to the division of yin and yang. The Supreme Polarity is without form, while yin and yang possess *qi*. Movement and stillness are *qi*; principle (*li*) rides upon *qi* and acts through it. The transformations of odd and even, the mutual engagement of female and male—those who obtain the yang *qi* follow the *qian* Dao and become male; those who obtain the yin *qi* follow the *kun* Dao and become female. *Qi* gathers to form shape, shapes interact and *qi* responds; yin and yang interweave and intermingle—this is the meaning of the *Yi*.

Between heaven and earth, there is only the ceaseless cycling of movement and stillness; beyond this, there is nothing more. Someone asked: "Yang moves and serves as function; yin is still and serves as substance. Now Master Zhou speaks of the Supreme Polarity, saying that movement generates yang—yet he begins from the state of movement, so function precedes substance. Moreover, what was it like before movement began?" I reply: Before movement began, it was principle—what is called 'the Non-Ultimate and yet the Supreme Polarity.' Principle should not be spoken of in terms of movement and stillness. Before movement, it is stillness; before stillness, it is movement; before movement, it is stillness again. Thus, principle has no opposite; it cannot be described in terms of movement and stillness. Rather, principle is revealed through movement and stillness.

As for Master Shao’s saying, "Function arises before heaven and earth," and Master Zhu’s saying, "The function of the Supreme Polarity is what enables its activity"—both refer to the aspect of function. The deeper meaning of "movement generates yang" is that nothing can be said of what lies beyond it. Master Zhou said: "The Supreme Polarity moves and generates yang. When movement reaches its limit, it returns to stillness, and stillness generates yin. When stillness reaches its limit, it returns to movement. Movement and stillness alternate, each serving as the root of the other. Yin and yang are divided, and the Two Modes are established." Master Zhu’s commentary on this chapter is most subtle and wondrous. He said: "The Supreme Polarity’s possession of movement and stillness is the flowing forth of Heaven’s Mandate—this is what is meant by 'the Dao of one yin and one yang.' Sincerity (*cheng*) is the root of the sage, the end and beginning of things, and the Dao of the Mandate. Its movement is the unfolding of sincerity; what follows it is the goodness by which the myriad things are endowed with their beginning. Its stillness is the return of sincerity; what completes it is nature, by which the myriad things each rectify their natures and lives. Movement reaches its limit and becomes stillness; stillness reaches its limit and returns to movement. Movement and stillness alternate, each serving as the root of the other—this is the flowing forth of the Mandate without cease. Movement generates yang; stillness generates yin. Yin and yang are divided, and the Two Modes are established. Their division has a definite principle that does not shift."

The Supreme Polarity is the wondrousness of what is so of itself. Movement and stillness are the mechanism it rides upon. The Supreme Polarity is above form—it is the Dao. Yin and yang are below form—they are the vessels. I say: The Supreme Polarity is principle; movement and stillness are *qi*. The mechanism it rides upon is the bearing and carrying of movement and stillness. This principle rests upon *qi*, unaware that it moves and then is still, is still and then moves again. Thus it is said: The Supreme Polarity is like a person; movement and stillness are like a horse. The horse carries the person; the person rides the horse. The horse goes out and comes in; the person goes out and comes in with it. Therefore, the Supreme Polarity rides upon this *qi* of movement and stillness to generate yin and yang. When it moves, it is the Supreme Polarity of yang; when it is still, it is the Supreme Polarity of yin. Viewed from the perspective of yin and yang, yin and yang exist only within the Supreme Polarity. To say that the Supreme Polarity exists within yin and yang is also acceptable—yet if one clings to fixed forms, one may flow into movement without stillness or sink into stillness without movement, and things will not flow smoothly.

The spirit (*shen*) does not depart from form yet is not confined by form. Within movement there is stillness; within stillness there is movement—this is what is meant by the spirit’s wondrousness in the myriad things. Proceeding upward from the Five Phases and the two *qi*, one finds the root in the oneness of the Supreme Polarity. Movement and stillness, yang and yin—this is the origin of the myriad things’ single nature. Proceeding downward from the two *qi* and the Five Phases, one finds them dispersed into the ten thousand things. Stillness and movement, yin and yang—one reality divides into ten thousand parts, and the Mandate flows forth. In stillness, the Supreme Polarity merges and returns to oneness; in movement, the Supreme Polarity opens and scatters into the ten thousand things.

Someone asked: "Does this mean the Supreme Polarity is also subject to division?" I reply: It is fundamentally one Supreme Polarity. The myriad things each receive their allotment, and each in turn possesses a complete Supreme Polarity. It is like the moon in the sky—there is only one. Yet when it scatters upon rivers and lakes, its reflection appears everywhere. Can one say the moon is divided? One root, ten thousand branches; ten thousand branches, one root—there is no doubt about this.

Master Cheng said: "Movement and stillness have no beginning; yin and yang have no origin. Movement generates yang, yet originally it was stillness. Above stillness, there must be movement. Before movement, there is stillness. Pushing upward, its beginning is without end—this is the Dao of Heaven. Beginning with yin, completing with yang, rooted in stillness, flowing into movement—this is the Dao of Humanity. Yet yang returns to its root in stillness, and stillness in turn is rooted in movement—its movement and stillness also have no beginning." He also said: "Movement and stillness serve as each other’s root—the word 'root' most deserves careful consideration. There is movement and stillness that are already fixed and visible, and there is movement and stillness that are not yet fixed and not yet visible—these are the root of movement and stillness. This word 'root' is difficult to explain. To say that movement is rooted in stillness and stillness is rooted in movement is merely to speak of their mutual exchange and alternation, without distinguishing host and guest. The meaning is not fully conveyed; Master Zhu’s explanation is more rounded and lively."

He said: "*Yuan* and *heng* are the unfolding of sincerity in movement. *Li* and *zhen* are the return of sincerity in stillness. *Yuan* is the constancy of movement, rooted in stillness. *Zhen* is the substance of stillness, manifest in movement—this is what is meant by *zhen*. It is how the myriad things complete their ends and begin anew. Thus, one cannot stand as a person without movement. The ultimate (*ji*) is fundamentally rooted in stillness; when what is manifest in movement is always properly regulated, it does not lose its original stillness. Stillness is how nature is established; movement is how the Mandate flows forth."

Indeed, in every movement and stillness, the Mandate flows forth. Yet what courses through movement and stillness is the constancy of nature. Thus it is said: "The constancy of the Limitless." This encompasses movement and stillness, and speaks of the equilibrium before activation—when expressed in terms of stillness, it seems to point to *zhen* as the root of movement and stillness.

In my humble view: movement generates yang, yet it does not move of itself. Rooted in the Supreme Ultimate, it moves forth to bestow upon the myriad things. Stillness generates yin, yet it does not still itself. Rooted in the Supreme Ultimate, it enters to rectify the nature and Mandate of each. Thus, the Supreme Ultimate is the root of Heaven and Earth, governing the qi of yin and yang, movement and stillness, to enact the power of generating and completing the myriad things. As its root, how could this be lightly spoken of?

It is further said: Like a hundred-foot tree, from root to branch and leaf, all is one continuous whole, indivisible. To treat movement and stillness as two separate matters is to gradually impose an artificial arrangement. The Supreme Ultimate is like a tree growing upward: first dividing into branches and trunk, then further producing flowers and leaves, endlessly generating. When it comes to bearing fruit, within it lies the principle of ceaseless generation. As it generates outward, there emerge limitless Supreme Ultimates. Without pause, it continues until the fruit is formed, at which point it naturally halts for a time. The *Book of Changes* states: "In the beginning and ending of the myriad things, nothing surpasses *Gen*." *Gen* signifies the meaning of growth and rest. Creation naturally operates thus—how could it depend on human arrangement? All follows the principle of root and branch as one continuous whole.

Master Zhu said: Movement and stillness also constitute the Two Modes. One is *bianyi* (transformation), which is the flowing aspect; the other is *jiaoyi* (interchange), which is the correlative aspect. Movement and stillness generate yin and yang; continuing goodness and completing nature are the flowing aspect. Yin and yang divide, and the Two Modes are established—this is the correlative aspect. The movement of the Supreme Ultimate is the flowing aspect. When movement stills, it becomes the correlative of yin and yang. In correlation, it moves again to become flowing. Like the alternation of cold and heat, one is horizontal, the other vertical. Flowing returns to stillness and becomes correlation, as in Heaven and Earth, the four directions: east yang, west yin, south yang, north yin. Correlation is the substance; flowing is the function. The substance is still, and the function is dynamic—these two aspects of movement and stillness are why transformation is inexhaustible.

What is called *bianyi* refers to yang transforming into yin, yin transforming into yang, old yang transforming into young yin, old yin transforming into young yang. This pertains to the methods of divination, such as the alternation of contraction and extension. What is called *jiaoyi* refers to yang interchanging with yin, yin interchanging with yang, as in the hexagram images where Heaven and Earth take their positions.

It is further said: After Mencius, no one understood nature. Beginning with Master Lianxi, who spoke of the uneven transformations of movement and stillness, yin and yang, and the Five Phases, the Cheng brothers thereby deduced the nature of qi and disposition. Before a person is born, only this principle exists. Upon receiving this principle and coming to life, it is then infused as nature. The nature of Heaven and Earth is purely this principle without admixture; when it reaches the realm of yin and yang and the Five Phases, it becomes qi and disposition. Speaking of principle and qi together, there arise differences in clarity and turbidity, thickness and thinness. Thus it is said: "The Five Natures are stirred and good and evil diverge." Nature belongs to the formless realm and is entirely the heavenly principle. Qi belongs to the formed realm and is the sediment. As for physical form, it is the most turbid sediment of all.

It is further said: All people possess the principle of movement and stillness, yet they commonly lose it in movement. The sage fully embodies the virtue of movement and stillness, yet always roots it in stillness. The two words "movement" and "stillness" are correlative, a natural principle of Heaven. Without movement as its counterpart, stillness cannot be called stillness. Without stillness as its counterpart, movement cannot be called movement. Yet the movement of ordinary people flows into movement without stillness; their stillness sinks into stillness without movement. Thus, desires stir, emotions prevail, profit and harm contend, and they follow their inclinations like a rushing stream—scarcely different from birds and beasts. Only the sage is not thus. In movement, the principle of stillness is never lost; in stillness, the principle of movement never ceases. Therefore, their conduct is centered, their dwelling is upright, their actions are benevolent, and their judgments are righteous. In every movement and stillness, nothing fails to be complete. In the Dao of the Supreme Ultimate, nothing is lacking. Thus, what was previously called the stirring of desires, the prevailing of emotions, and the contention of profit and harm is settled here.

Before reaching this state, one cultivates it. Vigilance, apprehension, and reverence in stillness are constantly maintained—this is why the noble person is auspicious. Without understanding this and acting contrary to it, indulging in depravity and excess without restraint—this is why the petty person is inauspicious.

Master Shao said: Heaven is born of movement; Earth is born of stillness. At the beginning of movement, yang is born. At the extreme of movement, yin is born. At the beginning of stillness, softness is born. At the extreme of stillness, hardness is born. Within yin and yang, there are further yin and yang, hence the Greater Yin, Greater Yang, Lesser Yin, and Lesser Yang. Within hardness and softness, there are further hardness and softness, hence the Greater Hard, Greater Soft, Lesser Hard, and Lesser Soft.

In my humble observation: Master Shao’s explanation proceeds from the Supreme Ultimate to movement and stillness, thus "One generates Two." From movement and stillness, yin and yang, hardness and softness divide, and "Two generates Four." Further dividing into greater and lesser, "Four generates the Eight Trigrams." From this, the numbers are drawn, and from this, the principles are extended—Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things cannot escape. Within the undifferentiated Supreme Ultimate, all is so clearly ordered. Former scholars said of Kangjie that he saw clearly at the point where yin and yang intersect—how true are these words.

**Movement and Stillness**
This chapter speaks of movement being rooted in stillness. Heaven and Earth, the four seasons—all flow without ceasing.

Yet it constantly takes stillness as its root. Without night, day would be indistinct. Without winter, spring, summer, and autumn could not bring forth growth and fruition. Just as one who engages in affairs all day must rest briefly to restore vitality, so too if the four seasons had no winter and only endless growth, the primal *qi* would eventually be exhausted. The *Book of Changes* says: "Closing the door is called *Kun*; preceding the opening of the door is called *Qian*—this is movement arising from stillness." From the Summer Solstice, *Kun* proceeds with one yin moving rightward, and the myriad things follow it inward, thus it is called "closing." From the Winter Solstice, *Qian* proceeds with one yang moving leftward, and the myriad things follow it outward, thus it is called "opening." *Kun* closes, and yang transforms into yin; *Qian* opens, and yin transforms into yang. Thus, one closing and one opening are called "transformation." Closing recedes, opening advances—endless transformations. In a year, this is cold and heat; in a day, this is night and day; in a person, this is the cycle of life and death. Without stillness, there is no movement. Is this not why the world takes *Kun* as its beginning?

Though yin begins to arise at the Summer Solstice, it is not until the sixth yin line of *Wei* stands alone that one can speak of "stillness reaching its extreme and giving rise to movement." The *Appended Remarks* of the *Book of Changes* says: "Without thought, without action, utterly still and unmoving—yet when stimulated, it penetrates all things under Heaven." Master Cheng said: "The *zhong* ('center') refers to utter stillness; the *he* ('harmony') refers to its responsiveness that penetrates all things. In its stillness, there is no moment when it does not respond; in its responsiveness, there is no moment when it is not still. This is the full embodiment of Heaven’s mandate and the utmost correctness of the human heart." Before it is aroused, one sees the substance of its responsiveness; after it is aroused, one sees the function of its stillness. The human heart and the principle of Heaven are one.

Master Shao said: "Between one movement and one stillness lies the utmost subtlety of Heaven, Earth, and humanity!" Yang opens and moves; yin closes and is still—this is one movement and one stillness. Not enslaved by movement, not bound by stillness, neither movement nor stillness yet governing both—this is the space between one movement and one stillness.

Some say: The *Canon of Supreme Mystery* speaks of yang *qi* concealed within the chamber of *Huangzhong*—truly, it is present in all things. *Xin* ("trust") is nothing other than sincerity. Master Zhou’s *Comprehensive Discourse* says: "*Yuan heng* ('origination and flourishing') is the unfolding of sincerity; *li zhen* ('benefit and perseverance') is the return of sincerity." Between one unfolding and one return lies a principle of true reality. This is likely the *zhen* ("perseverance") of *yuan heng li zhen*. What follows *zhen* is *li*; what precedes *zhen* is *yuan*. From *li* to *zhen*, the subtlety of Heaven, Earth, and humanity is stored and concealed here. From *zhen* to *yuan*, the subtlety of Heaven, Earth, and humanity is released and expressed here. To be upright and firmly sincere, unwavering and unerring—this is what is meant by "endless transformations, yet the true reality of *zhen* lies here." The subtlety of *Wuji* ("Without Ultimate") and *Taiji* ("Supreme Ultimate") thus pervades the boundary between Heaven and humanity. The sage employs this principle, taking stillness as the foundation for establishing humanity. The ultimate truth lies precisely here. To call it "the utmost subtlety" is to emphasize that it cannot be captured in words or form. This subtlety is the great unfolding of creation.

When movement reaches its extreme and returns to stillness, this is death; when stillness reaches its extreme and returns to movement, this is life. When yang reaches its extreme and generates yin, this is transformation; when yin reaches its extreme and generates yang, this is change. One who can recognize the space between one movement and one stillness can thereby understand transformation, change, life, and death.

It is also said: Before *Wuji*, yin contains yang; after the appearance of images, yang divides yin. Master Zhu said: From *Gou* to *Kun* is yin containing yang; from *Fu* to *Qian* is yang dividing yin. Between *Fu* and *Gou* lies *Wuji*. From *Kun* returning to *Gou* is the time before *Wuji*. Master Zhu said: "This merely describes the cyclical meaning of the diagram. When *Kun*’s yin has gathered to its utmost, and *Fu*’s yang is contained but not yet manifest, this is the stillness of its original state. It does not oppose movement but is the root of movement and stillness." Former scholars said: "The stillness that does not oppose movement is *Wuji* and *Taiji*." At the time of utmost stillness, there is only the capacity for knowing and perceiving, without any object of knowledge or perception. In the *Book of Changes*, this corresponds to pure *Kun*, the image of no yang, yet possessing the utmost *zhen*. The indestructible principle resides within it. If we discuss the *Fu* hexagram, however, we must consider it as having objects of knowledge and perception—it cannot be conflated with the former.

Master Shao’s words—"Where the first yang stirs, before the myriad things are born"—likely refer to this idea. Viewed in terms of the human heart, this is the moment between desire stirring and not yet stirring. It is like the alarm and compassion felt when a child falls into a well—at this moment, this feeling can only be perceived with a mind empty of distractions and thoughts settled in stillness.

Now, let us examine the diagram by extending it left and right. The right side of the diagram pertains to yin, which governs closing. Within yin lies yang—this refers to the movement from *Xun* waning to *Kun* gathering, the subtlety of stillness. This is the time before *Wuji*, when yin contains yang. The left side of the diagram pertains to yang, which governs opening. Yang departs and divides yin—this refers to the movement from *Zhen* growing to *Qian* dividing. This is the time after images appear, when yang divides yin. *Qian* generates one yin, thus it is said: "The father gives birth to the eldest daughter, becoming *Gou*." *Kun* generates one yang, thus it is said: "The mother conceives the eldest son, becoming *Fu*." This unites *Qian*’s yang and *Kun*’s yin, discussing the entire diagram as one. If we speak solely of *Wuji*, it resides only between *Fu* and *Gou*—we cannot loosely refer to it as existing after the appearance of images. Thus it is said: "From *Kun* returning to *Gou* is the time before *Wuji*." It is also said: "Between *Fu* and *Gou* lies *Wuji*." What does this mean? It is without movement or stillness yet contains movement and stillness; without yin or yang yet contains yin and yang. There is such a principle, yet no sign of its advance. Without sound or scent, utterly subtle and wondrous—this is what Master Zhou meant by "*Wuji* and yet *Taiji*." It cannot be fully described in words.

Suddenly, at midnight, a single clap of thunder—
ten thousand doors and a thousand gates open in succession.
If you recognize that within this lies the primordial image,
you may behold Fuxi himself before you.
Master Zhu truly grasped this.

It is also said: "Primordiality has two aspects.
That which gives birth to the beginning of Heaven and Earth is *Taiji*.
That which gives birth to the beginning of the myriad things is the root of life."
Returning to the root in the precelestial, reverting to stillness, is called *Kun*.
In a single day, this is the hour of *hai*.
In a single year, this is the tenth month.
In a single epoch, this is *Taiji*—
the moment before movement, when there exists an unformed state.

In the postcelestial, yang stirs within to become *Fu*.
In a single day, this is the midst of *zi*.
In a single year, this is the winter solstice.
In a single epoch, this is the beginning of *Taiji*.
Discussing *Fu* in terms of hexagram qi, it is also the precelestial—
this is to observe the one epoch spanning ten thousand ages.

The winter solstice is the first of the primordial unity of Heaven.
The spring equinox is the second of the primordial duality of things.
The first is the essence of primordiality.
The second is the function of primordiality.

That which first closes and then opens is spring.
That which is purely open is summer.
That which first opens and then closes is autumn.
Winter is closure without sound—
though it is the end of storage, it is truly the beginning of generation.
"Primordial and then smooth, flourishing, and firm"—
storage is completed in constancy.
If closure is secure and dense, then spring’s yang will unfold and spread,
setting the norm for the entire year, for the workings of creation truly begin here.
Thus, if winter is warm and without frost or snow,
the following year’s five grains will not ripen,
precisely because the yang qi has been prematurely dispersed.

Master Zhu said: "Within stillness there is movement—
this reveals the heart of Heaven and Earth."
It is like a single point of light in profound darkness.
When the three yang are abundant, the myriad things flourish.
At this time, one sees only the flourishing of things,
not their heart.
Only when the myriad things have not yet arisen,
all is clear and still—
the one yang, the heart of the generation of things,
stirs beneath accumulated yin,
concealed and unable to remain hidden.
Thus, the heart of Heaven and Earth is visible here.

Master Shao said: "The heart of Heaven does not change or shift."
This is precisely how it is, year after year, month after month, day after day,
without the slightest deviation.

I have said before: In *Fu*, the one yang stirs beneath *Kun*—
the first emergence of the good, revealing the heart of Heaven and Earth.
In *Dazhuang*, the four yang, with *Zhen* atop *Qian*—
manifesting in countenance, revealing the emotions of Heaven and Earth.
In *Kun*, the six yin govern stillness—
profoundly unmoving, revealing the nature of Heaven and Earth.

It is also said: "Stillness can save movement—
this takes stillness as primary."
Stillness resides here, empty and luminous, thoroughly penetrating.
From here, the response naturally flows with clarity.
If the heart harbors selfishness, all is amiss.
When the heart is serene and purely present here,
without a trace of selfish intent,
then things come and are named,
and movement and stillness are both in accord with principle.

I once heard that Master Li of Yanping taught others
to generally engage in recognition within stillness.
When the great root has not yet arisen,
the qi’s appearance distinguishes stillness from movement.
In handling affairs and responding to things,
one naturally accords with the mean.
This is the transmitted oral instruction of the Guishan lineage.

The human heart should be like a bright mirror or still water,
not like withered wood or dead ashes.
Though the mirror is bright and the water still, their essence is stillness,
yet they can reflect things.
Within stillness lies movement; within essence lies function—
such is the wonder of the human heart.
If it were withered wood, it could not grow;
if dead ashes, it could not ignite—
these are things without use.

The heart possesses all principles and responds to all affairs.
If abandoned, can it be without use?

It is also said: Stillness does not mean shutting the door and sitting alone,
rigidly guarding oneself,
responding to nothing, like *Gen*’s cessation.
Cessation is stillness;
that by which it ceases is movement.
Here again, stillness contains movement,
avoiding the bias of one-sided speech.

The learning of Laozi lies in movement within stillness.
The learning of Su and Zhang flows into movement without stillness.
Thus, there are those who focus solely on comprehending the principle of profound stillness.
Yet when it comes to handling affairs, they are utterly confused,
even disturbing their own profound stillness.
There are others who focus solely on comprehending affairs,
yet neglect the root entirely.
One must penetrate above and below, inner and outer as one,
like dwelling in *ren* and thus being able to act through *yi*,
acting through *yi* and thus being able to dwell in *ren*,
being upright within and thus able to square without,
squaring without and thus able to be upright within—
only then is it achieved.

It is also said: Reverence pervades movement and stillness,
so seeking, releasing, grasping, and preserving are all within—
this is not what is meant by rigidly maintaining silence.
The learning of the noble person unifies inner and outer—
it cannot be divided in two.
If one speaks only of nurturing the unarisen,
yet cannot control the errors of the arisen,
this is having gained in stillness but not in movement.
If one only knows how to control the arisen,
yet does not nurture the unarisen,
this is having gained in movement but not in stillness.

Preservation is the work of stillness—
stillness is the mean, without bias.
Introspection is the work of movement—
movement is harmony, without discord.
In stillness, awareness is not obscured—
*Fu* reveals the heart of Heaven and Earth, movement within stillness.
In movement, all issuances accord with the norm—
*Gen*’s "not obtaining its body, not seeing its person,"
stillness within movement.

The previous chapter used the terms "movement and stillness"
to show that *Taiji* moves and generates yang—
this is the arising of function before Heaven and Earth.
This chapter uses the terms "stillness and movement"
to show that stillness contracts and movement expands—
the essence precedes the precelestial, and the function follows the postcelestial.

Thus it is said: If the dragon and snake do not coil,
they have nothing with which to soar to the heavens;
if the hawk does not bend,
it has nothing with which to extend.
This, too, is the natural principle of Heaven.

The noble person stores their instrument within,
waiting for the right time to act—
this, too, must be carefully considered.

Discerning directions:
The north—*ren*, *gui*, *hai*, *zi*—
is the land where water flourishes,
and its hexagram is *Kan*.

One yang lies trapped within two yins—
this is peril, toil, concealment, and latency.
Its direction is called the Dark Capital,
also named the Northern Beginning.
"Beginning" (*shuo*) means "revival" (*su*):
all things come here to die and revive,
as the moon wanes into darkness and then returns with the new moon.
When the sun reaches this point, it sinks beneath the earth,
and all phenomena fall into obscurity.

In the *Precelestial Diagram*, *Qian* lies to the south and *Kun* to the north.
*Qian* descends to join *Kun*, forming *Kan*;
*Kun* ascends to join *Qian*, forming *Li*.
Yang is born within *zi*; yin is born within *wu*.
*Kan* and *Li* unite in embrace,
with *zi* and *wu* facing one another.
Yang serves as sovereign; yin, as minister.
Heaven and humankind alike turn their backs to the north and face the south—
thus the south is manifest, while the north lies hidden.
The Celestial Pole resides in the north,
and the surrounding stars, numbering the four directions,
all regard the north as supreme, the south as its counterpart,
then east and west in proper sequence.
Thus *Tai Xuan* takes "boundless darkness" as the north,
while Shao Kangjie places the *Taiji* between *Kun* and *Fu*.

Master Cheng Shuzhi said:
"Beginning" (*shuo*) means "first"—
it refers to the direction where yang first stirs.
Like the north, it marks the end of yin and the birth of yang.
Master Shao said:
The *Book of Changes* speaks of yang *qi*
arising from the north and returning to the north—
this is the cyclical transformation of change.

In the *Canon of Yao*, the three directions—east, west, and south—
are all described as "properly ordered,"
while only the north is called "properly positioned."
The *Monthly Ordinances* states:
"As the year scatters toward its end,
the new year is about to begin."
Master Yichuan further said:
Having completed the end of the present year,
one must also consider the beginning of the coming year.
"End" and "beginning," "present year" and "coming year"—
all form the Two Modes.
Again, when *Kun* transforms into *Kan*,
one hexagram becomes two.
Last year’s yang *qi* ends here,
and this year’s yang *qi* arises from this same point.

Master Zhu said:
Between Heaven and Earth, there are fixed boundaries—
such as the four directions—
and there are cyclical movements—
such as the four seasons.
The north holds two meanings:
for instance, the forty-five days before the Winter Solstice belong to the present year,
while the forty-five days after belong to the coming year.
Similarly, the four *ke* before midnight belong to today,
and the four *ke* after belong to tomorrow.
*Gen* governs the beginning and end of all things.
The Azure Dragon, White Tiger, and Vermilion Bird are each a single entity,
while the Black Tortoise and Serpent are two.
Heaven has spring, summer, autumn, and winter—
its climatic patterns are uneven.
Earth has east, west, south, and north—
its boundaries are fixed and unchanging.
Thus the northwest region is both the Northern Beginning
and simply the North.

In Heaven’s southeast, there is both sovereign fire and ministerial fire.
The sun and moon are the true essences of yin and yang,
giving rise to water and fire.
Thus among the seven luminaries, there is the sun and also fire,
the moon and also water.
In the human body’s five viscera,
the heart has the pericardium,
and the kidneys have the Gate of Life.
Water and fire each have two aspects,
with sovereign fire and true essence truly interconnected.
True fire has *qi* but no form—
this is what is meant by "the heart being singular while the kidneys are paired."
The Vermilion Bird is one, while the Black Tortoise and Serpent are two.
Thus the heart is singular, while the kidneys are paired.
In the trigrams of *Qian* and *Kun*,
the north is represented by both tortoise and serpent.

In Kan’s *Book of Changes*, *Li* contains the image of the tortoise,
as well as armor and helmets.
The tortoise has a shell—hence its martial aspect.
*Sun*, *Yi*, and *Yi* all mention the tortoise,
and their interlocking trigrams contain *Li*.
Drawing *qi* inward resembles the image of yang lying hidden beneath the earth.
On its back, at the center, are five lines,
which extend outward to form the Eight Trigrams,
and further outward to form the twenty-four solar terms.
This imagery is apt,
yet it remains unclear why it corresponds to the twelfth earthly branch,
as *si* does not occupy the northern position.

Kan’s *Records* and *Book of Documents Commentary*
make no mention of this—why?
Perhaps the character *xuan* (玄) curves and winds like a serpent,
and when transformed, becomes a dragon.
*Zhen* represents the dragon lying beneath *Kun*—
this may relate to the meaning of *Fu*, but requires separate investigation.

Some say:
Yang *xuan* belongs to the serpent,
while martial yin belongs to the tortoise.
Master Wang stated:
The kidneys are two, and so are the tortoise and serpent—
thus the Northern Beginning pertains to *zhen* (貞),
which has two meanings: uprightness and constancy.
The character *zhi* (智) also has two meanings:
its first half, representing gathering and storage, belongs to yin;
its latter half, representing opening and release, belongs to yang.

The *Rites of Zhou* states:
The tortoise and serpent roam in four directions,
symbolizing the celestial lodges.
The tortoise has a shell, enabling defense—hence its martial aspect.
Its color is black—hence *xuan* (玄).
Master Wei said:
The male does not dwell alone,
nor does the female live in solitude.
The Black Warrior—tortoise and serpent—
coil and intertwine in mutual support.
If a hen crows at dawn,
her chicks will not thrive.
Observe the moment of mating between male and female:
hard and soft intertwine, inseparable.
They follow their natural rhythms,
without need for artifice or control.
When a boy is born, he lies prone;
when a girl is born, she lies supine—
this is how they received *qi* in the womb,
and in death, they return to this state.
This is not taught by parents—
it is the marvel of creation’s spontaneity.

Master Cai of Xishan said:
One singular, one paired—
these are the complementary forms of yin and yang.
Three yangs and one yin—
one abundant, one deficient—
these are the functions of yin and yang.
Thus in the four seasons, spring, summer, and autumn generate life,
while winter does not.
Heaven and Earth’s east, west, and south are visible,
while the north is not.
In human vision, what lies ahead and to the sides is visible,
while the back is not—
all these are the marvels of creation’s spontaneity.

Yang Xiong took the north as *xuan*,
encompassing the three directions.
"Boundless" and "dark"—
his intent lies precisely here.
Now, examining the *Tai Xuan*,
its text reads: "Boundless, straight, obscure, chief, dark."
"Boundless" is the north—
where the ten thousand things lie hidden in the Yellow Springs, beyond naming.
Thus the ten thousand things are "boundless" in the north,
"straight" in the east,
"obscure" in the south,
and "chief" in the north.
"Boundless" is where being emerges from non-being.
"Dark" is where brightness conceals itself in obscurity.
"Boundless" releases its *qi*—
yang *qi* is contained within.
"Dark" returns to its depths—
things lie in the Yellow Springs.

From obscurity they emerge, into obscurity they return—
the new and the old alternate, replacing one another in the depths of darkness.
What is to come advances; what has fulfilled its purpose retreats.
In this moment of *wang*, how profound is the Mystery!

The formed enters the formless; the formless issues forth as form—
thus is the Mystery.
The moment of *wang* can be regulated:
the eighty-one houses all derive from *wang*,
for the formless can be governed.

It is further said:
Heaven’s Mystery lies in its invisibility,
Earth’s Mystery in its formlessness,
Man’s Mystery in the heart and belly.
Heaven’s depths lie in the northwest, where essence transforms in seclusion.
In the tenth month, pure *kun* governs,
and yang *qi* accumulates beneath the earth.
Earth’s depths lie in the Yellow Springs, where souls and spirits flourish.
Man’s depths lie in thought and contemplation, containing the purest essence.

Pondering this passage,
the meaning of the north’s *wang* and *ming* becomes unmistakably clear.
For when the ten thousand things enter the north,
being transforms into non-being—thus it is called *ming*.
When the ten thousand things emerge from the north,
non-being transforms into being—thus it is called *wang*.

Master Zhang states:
*Ming* corresponds to the unused One,
the place where the Three rest, hidden in secrecy.
I say:
This is the first half before the winter solstice, belonging to the present year’s affairs.
It is further said:
*Wang* corresponds to the formless One,
the source from which the Four arise, issuing from the void.
This is the latter half after the winter solstice, belonging to the coming year’s affairs.
First, one enters *ming*; afterward, one emerges into *wang*.
*Ming* should be the north; *wang* should be the center.
Thus the center of Heaven lies in the north—this is the celestial pole.
*Ming* and *wang* first rest, then generate—
such is the way of those with a root.

Therefore it is said:
Heaven has four seasons, yet winter is unused;
Earth has four directions, yet the north is unused;
Man has four limbs, yet the back is unused.
One is fixed and unmoving; the Three are endlessly employed.
Heaven and Earth’s Mystery lies in the north—
how deep it is!

Some say:
*Ming* is the profound virtue, abiding at the center, unmoving.
*Wang*, *zhi*, *meng*, and *qiu* correspond to Qian’s *yuan*, *heng*, *li*, and *zhen*.
This interpretation is most fitting.

Zhu Hanshang states:
Kan is originally Kun’s body, residing in the north,
presiding over the affairs of Heaven, Earth, ghosts, and spirits.
The north’s entry into *ming* is Heaven’s form.
Kan is the abode of ghosts and spirits;
Dou is Heaven’s temple, also governing ancestral sacrifices.
This is one explanation.

Moreover, in the primordial arrangement, Kun is placed in the north,
opposite Qian in the south—encompassing both celestial and terrestrial affairs.
King Wen later transformed it into Kan,
for no other reason than that Qian’s single yang comes to interact with Kun’s body,
thus forming Kan.
Kan opposes Li, creating the Water-Fire *jiji* hexagram,
extending to the daily needs of the people.
Within it lies the mutual body of Gen,
where the northwest’s yin beauty finds its limit.
The southeast’s yang harmony is complete,
yet hidden within is Zhen.
From this, generation begins.
Kun contains hidden Kan.

Kun’s *sixth line* in the *Wenyan* states:
"Heaven is dark; Earth is yellow."
"Heaven’s darkness" refers to the color of Kan’s single yang;
"Earth’s yellow" refers to its outer four yin lines.

Thus at the winter solstice,
the sun, moon, and five planets all rise at the first degree of Qianniu.
Qianniu is the stellar chronicle, the Water position, the Kan hexagram.
Therefore the *gua qi* begins with Zhongfu,
and the *Tai Xuan* commences with *Zhongshou*—all for this reason.

The *Xuanhun* (Mystery-Encompassing)

Zhu Xi’s *Original Meaning* states: The yang qi is vigorous, and its greatest formed manifestation is Heaven. The nature of Kun is compliant, and its most expansive formed manifestation is Earth. Inspired by poetry, he further says:
*"Gazing upward at the encompassing Mystery, in a single breath it races ten thousand miles.
Looking downward at the square norm’s stillness, it abides in collapse through a thousand ages."*
Thus its vigor and compliance are evident.

Moreover, within the cosmos, all things undergo decline and rest. Only Heaven moves without ceasing—each day passing one degree, never pausing. Heaven is not like Earth, which possesses form; above Earth, there is nothing that is not Heaven. For every foot of Earth, there is a foot of Heaven—it is merely that people do not perceive it. That which is light and pure, ascending and floating, is Heaven. Heaven is round and in motion, enveloping Earth within its midst. Earth is square and still, and thus heavy and turbid, sinking downward—all being the dregs and congealed sediment of Heaven’s qi settling below. In its origin, it was but a single qi. Divided into two, yang encompasses yin, while yin does not encompass yang. Thus Qian Heaven’s One embraces Kun Earth’s Two to become Three.

Earth resides within Heaven, and Earth’s qi is entirely Heaven’s qi. Master Zhang [Zai] says: *"The Void is the ancestor of Heaven and Earth; Heaven and Earth emerge from the Void. Its Dao takes utmost emptiness as true reality. Gold and iron may corrode, mountains and peaks may crumble. All things possessing form are easily destroyed, yet the Great Void knows no disturbance—thus it is the most real. ‘What Heaven above bears has neither sound nor scent’—this is perfection."*

He further states: *"The empty and void, containing nothing, is that from which the myriad things arise."*

Master Shao [Yong] says: *"Heaven faces one center to divide creation."* Heaven is originally One; with Earth, it divides into Two. Yang is originally one line; with yin, it divides into two lines. Thus, in every affair and thing, all are pressing toward Earth. Where there is growth, there is decline; where there is a front, there is a back—all is natural, not something human effort can contrive. The so-called *"prior to Heaven, one thing is absent"* requires no arrangement to be so.

He further says: *"If we speak of the prior-Heaven single line, as soon as there is One, there is Two, Four, Eight, and Three Hundred Eighty-Four—though from this point onward it unfolds, how could it require arrangement?"*

Yaofu asked Master Cheng: *"Do you know how thunder arises?"*
Yichuan replied: *"I know it."*
Yaofu said: *"I do not."*

Having known it, why resort to tedious calculation?

Yaofu asked: *"From where does it arise?"*
Yichuan replied: *"It arises from where it arises."*
Yaofu, struck with sudden insight, praised this answer.

I say: Thunder arises from the One at Heaven’s root—this is the division of creation from within the One.

He further says: *"Heaven relies on form; Earth clings to qi."*
Someone asked Yaofu: *"To what does Heaven cling?"*
He replied: *"Heaven clings to Earth through qi."*
*"To what does Earth attach?"*
*"Earth attaches to Heaven through form."*
Heaven and Earth naturally depend on each other.

Speaking of their essence: Heaven divides to become Earth, and Earth divides to become the myriad things. Yet the Dao cannot be divided. In the end, the myriad things return to Earth, Earth returns to Heaven, and Heaven returns to the Dao. Heaven’s ceaseless motion alone holds Earth suspended at its center—were Heaven’s singular movement ever to pause, Earth would plunge downward, and all people would perish.

Master Cheng Bozi said: *"Heaven’s qi descends to Earth, and all living things within Earth are Heaven’s qi."*
Master Cheng Shuzi said: *"Wherever there is qi, it is Heaven; wherever there is form, it is Earth."*
Master Zhang said: *"The void is qi. Wherever there is a heaven and earth, there is a span of qi."*
Master Zhu said: *"Heaven’s form is round like a pellet, revolving day and night. Its north and south poles, higher behind and lower in front, are the unmoving pivot. What revolves has no fixed substance—it is but a fierce wind spiraling upward and downward without cease, the body of Heaven though not truly a body. Earth, however, is the dregs of qi condensed into form and substance. Yet bound within the fierce wind’s rotation, it remains steadfastly afloat in space, never falling."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qibo: *"Does Earth have support?"*
Qibo replied: *"The great qi lifts it."* This is what he meant.

Upon careful reflection: Qian is originally a single line, its substance entirely solid, like a single thing. From within, it expands to fill all of Heaven. The qi of Heaven’s inner covering all penetrates Earth’s center. Kun, however, divides into two lines—its capacity is thus hollow, allowing for opening and closing. Earth’s form is like the lungs: though its substance is solid, its center is empty. When Qian’s qi rises, Kun opens to either side, admitting Heaven’s qi to surge upward. Even metal and stone are permeated.

Heaven’s form is like a drum’s skin, its outer layer enclosing abundant qi. As the *Monthly Ordinances* states in *First Month of Spring*: *"Heaven’s qi descends; Earth’s qi ascends."* From the eleventh month, it accumulates until the fourth month, forming Qian. Qian’s qi lies beneath Kun, steaming upward like rice in a steamer. Yet in the fifth month, a single yin arises; by the seventh month, three yins below form Kun, and three yangs above form Qian. Heaven’s qi ascends, Earth’s qi descends—Heaven and Earth are cut off, sealed to become winter.

In the beginning, there were only the two qi of yin and yang. As they ground against each other, dregs rapidly accumulated within, with no exit. The sediment of water congealed into Earth, fixed at the center. Today, climbing high to view the mountains is like watching waves—akin to tide-borne sand rising. The extreme heat of fire’s qi generates wind, thunder, lightning, the sun, stars, and the like.

Heaven has nine layers, each ascending purer than the last. The clear and firm qi becomes Heaven; without firmness, it could not envelop and nurture. Heaven is merely a whirlwind, spinning outward and inward, spinning out dregs to form Earth at its center. Thus Master Shao said: *"Its form has limits; its qi is boundless."* And again: *"The Dao is the root of Heaven and Earth; Heaven and Earth are the root of the myriad things. Viewing the myriad things through Heaven and Earth, they are indeed myriad things. Viewing Heaven and Earth through the Dao, they too are but things."*

*"One yin, one yang—this is called the Dao."* The Dao belongs to what is above form; yin and yang belong to what is below form. The great yin and yang of Heaven and Earth disperse into the myriad things, which are born bearing Heaven and Earth’s yin and yang qi. All confined within form and vessels are called things. Each thing possesses a single Supreme Polarity—this is called the Dao.

Thus, observing Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things by form, Heaven is great and the myriad things small. Observing them by the Dao, Heaven and Earth are but one thing within the Supreme Polarity.

Dong Guangwei said: *"Gazing at the moon through drifting clouds, the moon moves while the clouds do not. Boarding a boat to cross water, the water flows while the boat does not."* These words carry meaning.

Master Zhou said: *"Observe the vital force of Heaven and Earth in generating things. Within each thing lies the heart of Heaven and Earth, naturally reaching everywhere."* This is the transformation of Qian’s Dao, each fulfilling its nature and destiny.

If one reflects inwardly at the arising of good impulses, one will see it clearly. Animals possess blood and qi, thus they know. Though plants cannot know, their vital force is equally visible. If one harms them, they wither and droop, as if aware. Grass and fish are seen by all, yet only Mingdao and Lianxi saw them alike. When grass thrives in the courtyard, they said: *"We wish to always witness the creative vitality of things."* When fish are kept in a basin, they said: *"We wish to observe the self-contentment of the myriad things."* Such is the bearing of those who embody the Dao.

Thus it is said: *"In tranquil observation, all things attain their nature."*
Master Cheng said: *"Heaven and Earth take the myriad things as their heart."*
Master Zhu said: *"Heaven and Earth have no other task but to take generating things as their heart."*

Beneath the pure Kun of the tenth month, a single yang revives. The heart of Heaven and Earth nearly extinguished, only then can it be seen again. Yichuan regarded the inception of movement as the heart of Heaven and Earth.

It is said: *"Movement is not the heart of Heaven and Earth, but only reveals the heart of Heaven and Earth."*

Regarding the *Fu* hexagram, if viewed from the *xiantian* (pre-celestial) perspective, the single yang line emerges only after the *Bo* hexagram’s top yang (ninth line) transforms into the *Kun* hexagram’s top yin (sixth line), with thirty parts already accumulated by the tenth month. Like a child within the mother, the heart of Heaven and Earth in generating things remains concealed. Yet if viewed from the *houtian* (post-celestial) perspective, the single line beneath *Kun* suddenly transforms into *Zhen*, originally a *Gen* hexagram. When *Gen* is inverted, within the stillness of yin’s cessation, the mechanism of yang’s revival and flow becomes manifest. Like a child outside the mother, the heart of Heaven and Earth in generating things is then clearly visible.

The qi of the primordial unity never pauses for even a moment, ceaselessly giving rise to the myriad things. Heaven and Earth merely contain this vast qi within, with no outlet. Each time it gathers, it generates another round of beings. The workings of creation resemble a mill grinding out life—what emerges is both refined and coarse, never uniform.

Master Zhu said: *"The four seasons proceed, the hundred things are born."* This is grand and evident, revealing the disposition of Heaven and Earth, yet it describes their effortless aspect. If they were truly without heart, then cows would give birth to horses, and peach trees would bear plums, for their heart would have no constancy.

Master Cheng said: *"To call it ‘Emperor’ is to speak of its governance; the heart is precisely where it governs."* In the *Book of Poetry* and *Book of Documents*, wherever the idea of governance appears, it is called *Di* (Emperor). Wherever the idea of encompassing and universal coverage appears, it is called *Tian* (Heaven).

Nanxuan remarked: *"One cannot take the azure vault alone as Heaven. One must seek it in the realm of seeing, hearing, speaking, and acting—wherever a single thought arises, there is the presence of the Supreme Emperor."* *"The Lord on high watches over you; His scrutiny is ever in His heart."* The moment a thought strays, it is as if divine wrath descends.

Master Zhu said: *"One must understand that it is without heart, yet also recognize that it has a heart."* Heaven envelops Earth, and what Heaven enacts is merely the knowing of generating things—this is a heart without intention. Yet when it rewards good and punishes evil, it seems as though there is a governor within. Similarly, the rising of winds and falling of rains, the crashing of thunder and flashing of lightning, the blooming and fruiting of flowers—how could these occur without a spirit?

It is further said: *"All men under Heaven are the qi of Qian; all women under Heaven are the qi of Kun."* From this, extending upward and downward, all is one qi. Though it permeates all, even the most minute and hidden things, down to the finest hair, none lack the image of Qian and Kun.

All the myriad sounds under Heaven arise from opening and closing; all the myriad principles arise from movement and stillness; all the myriad numbers arise from odd and even; all the myriad forms arise from square and circle—all originate from the two lines of Qian and Kun.

In terms of physical form, it is called Heaven; in terms of governance, it is called Emperor; in terms of function, it is called ghosts and spirits; in terms of its wondrous workings, it is called spirit.

Heaven and Earth are the outer shell of Qian and Kun; Qian and Kun are the nature and disposition of Heaven and Earth. It is simply that this qi, when it wills to generate, generates—nothing less than utmost vigor can achieve this.

That which is rooted in Heaven turns upward: the heads of all moving creatures face the same direction, as do humans. That which is rooted in Earth turns downward: the heads of all plants point downward, as do grasses and trees. The heads of birds and beasts are often horizontal, which is why they lack intelligence.

The Dao of Heaven is above; thus, the brain below gives rise to many things. Regarding the single origin of the myriad things, principle is the same while qi differs. Observing the distinct forms of the myriad things, qi remains somewhat similar, yet principle diverges entirely.

Qi is similar in that all beings know cold and warmth, recognize hunger and satiety, love life and hate death, pursue benefit and avoid harm—humans and creatures are alike in this. Principle differs in that, for instance, ants have a semblance of ruler and subject, revealing a trace of righteousness; tigers and wolves have a semblance of parent and child, revealing a trace of benevolence. Beyond this, further distinctions cannot be drawn.

This is why Heaven and Earth serve as the outer shell, encompassing all things within. Qian and Kun are their nature and disposition, which, as bestowed, vary in each being.

*The Western Inscription* states: *"Qian is called Father, Kun is called Mother; humans dwell within, embodying the way of the child."* Thus, the yang of Qian and the yin of Kun fill the space between Heaven and Earth, and all beings rely on them for their bodies. The vigor of Qian and the compliance of Kun are the aspirations of Heaven and Earth, leading the qi, and all beings derive their nature from them. This is why humans receive the uprightness of nature and destiny, becoming the most spiritually endowed of all beings, while other creatures receive only a partial endowment, sufficing for their kind alone.

Master Zhang said: *"From the Great Void comes the name of Heaven; from the transformations of qi comes the name of the Dao. The union of void and qi gives the name of nature; the union of nature and consciousness gives the name of heart."*

Master Zhu said: *"Originally, it was one Great Void, gradually refined into greater density."* The Great Void is the totality; speaking of the transformations of qi—those of yin and yang, the workings of creation, cold and heat, day and night, rain and dew, frost and snow, mountains and rivers, grasses and trees, metal and water, fire and earth—does not depart from it. Though discussed in terms of the manifold transformations of qi, in truth, it does not leave the Great Void.

The Great Void is the circle above the Supreme Ultimate, within which yin is still and yang is dynamic. With this qi, principle resides within; without this qi, principle would have no place to abide.

Like the moon reflected in water—only with this water can the moon above be mirrored. Qi has form, while the Great Void has no path. Through the forms of the tangible, the principle of the intangible is possessed, hence the consciousness of nature and heart. This is the luminous clarity of qi’s emptiness. Sight, hearing, action, and function—all depend on this consciousness to activate this principle.

Master Shao said: *"Only with yin and yang can heaven be generated; only with hardness and softness can earth be generated."* The *Record of Rites* states: *"Ritual fundamentally arises from the Supreme One, which divides to become heaven and earth."* The Supreme Ultimate is where qi has not yet appeared. The Great Beginning is the inception of qi. The Great Commencement is the inception of form. The Great Simplicity is the inception of substance. These four phrases together discuss the state before heaven and earth and the beginning of heaven and earth. Here, the origins of principle, qi, form, and substance are explained. Of all things, none are greater than heaven and earth.

Heaven is born of movement. The Supreme Ultimate moves and generates yang; yang, being light and pure, floats upward to become heaven. It is also said: *"Yin and yang generate heaven, for yang is rooted in yin."* Thus, in heaven as yin and yang, there are the sun, moon, stars, and constellations. Earth is born of stillness. The Supreme Ultimate is still and generates yin; yin, being heavy and turbid, sinks downward to become earth. And it is said: *"Hardness and softness generate earth, for yin is rooted in yang."* Thus, on earth as hardness and softness, there are water, fire, soil, and stone.

Speaking from their root, the Supreme Ultimate moves to generate yin and yang, thereby generating heaven and earth. Speaking separately: at the beginning of movement, yang is born; at the extreme of movement, yin is born. The alternation of one yin and one yang exhausts heaven’s function, hence it is said that yin and yang can generate heaven. At the beginning of stillness, softness is born; at the extreme of stillness, hardness is born. The alternation of one hardness and one softness exhausts earth’s function, hence it is said that hardness and softness can generate earth.

Regarding heaven and earth as one qi: yang ascends from below. Ascension is generation, generating yang to dissolve yin. Yin descends from above. Descent is dissolution, generating yin to dissolve yang. Speaking of the two qi separately: that which ascends from below is yang born within earth. From *zi* to *si*, it completes *qian*, generating heaven. At *wu*, heaven reaches its center, then generates yin to establish yang. Once established, yang proceeds again, rotating left as the Way of Heaven. That which descends from above is yin born within heaven. From *wu* to *hai*, *kun* is fully generated as earth. At *zi*, earth reaches its center, then generates yang to establish yin. Once established, yin proceeds again, rotating right as the Way of Earth.

Generating heaven gives birth to the myriad things; generating earth brings them to completion. *Qian* and *kun* use only six lines, with six as the central number. Advancing six is an end; retreating six is a beginning. The end of day is the beginning of night; the end of night is the beginning of day. Comprehending day and night, one knows that the numbers of the *Yi* can exhaust the beginnings and ends of heaven and earth, clarifying the principle of waxing and waning.

It is also said: *"Qian and kun determine the positions of above and below; kan and li arrange the gates of left and right."* Heaven has four images, earth has four images. Heaven has eight images, earth has eight images. Heaven and earth interlock, yin and yang intersect.
