Heaven governs the operation of the four seasons; earth governs the embodiment of the hundred things.

In the *Xiantian* arrangement, *qian*, *kun*, *kan*, and *li* correspond to *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*—the four cardinal directions, encompassing both above and below. Thus, the opening and closing of heaven and earth, the rising and setting of the sun and moon, the four seasons, the dark and new moons, the crescents and full moons, the lengthening and shortening of days and nights, the degrees of celestial motion, and the waxing and waning of all things arise from these four images.

In heaven, yin and yang are old and young; including the sun, moon, stars, and constellations, they become eight. On earth, hardness and softness are old and young; including water, fire, soil, and stone, they also become eight. As for their interlocking: in the *Xiantian* diagram, the left side has one hundred twelve yang and eighty yin, and the top is the same; the right side has one hundred twelve yin and eighty yang, and the bottom is the same. As for their intersection: like male and female, husband and wife, yin and yang, *qian* going to intersect *kun* forms *fu*, and *kun* coming to intersect *qian* forms *gou*.

*Qian* and *kun* stand divided in the center of yin and yang, establishing the bodies of heaven and earth. *Kan* and *li* intersect in the center of yin and yang, bringing forth the functions of heaven and earth. Yin and yang originally use *kan* and *li* to transform heaven and earth; heaven and earth in turn use *kan* and *li* to generate the myriad things.

In the *Houtian* arrangement of King Wen’s eight trigrams, the positions differ, with *kan*, *li*, *zhen*, and *dui* occupying the four cardinal directions, while the remaining four trigrams occupy the four corners. Why is this? The North Star resides in the north, slightly to the west, where the celestial spirits dwell, hence the northwest is *qian*. Kunlun resides in the west, slightly to the south, where the earthly spirits abide, hence the southwest is *kun*. Nothing stirs the myriad things like wind; as qi moves from east to south, the southeast is *xun*. Of the Five Peaks, none is more revered than the Eastern Peak; as earth’s form extends from north to east, the northeast is *gen*.

It is also said: *"Heaven is round and earth is square."* Heaven is high in the south and low in the north, so when observed, it appears like an overturned bowl. Tang monk Yixing said: *"The *gaitian* model is like a painted sphere, showing only half. The *huntian* model is like a viewed sphere, showing its entirety."* Shun’s *jixuan* follows the *huntian* method.

Cai Yong’s *Treatise on Astronomy* states: *"There are three schools of celestial bodies. The first is the *Zhoubi*. It describes heaven as an overturned basin, with the Dipper’s handle at the center, high in the middle and low at the edges, while the sun and moon move around it. When they are near, they are seen as day; when far, they are unseen as night. Though its calculations are detailed, they contain many errors, so the Bureau of Astronomy does not use it."*

It is also said: *"Heaven is round like an open umbrella; earth is square like a chessboard. Heaven moves like a turning millstone to the left; the sun and moon move to the right, following heaven’s leftward rotation."*

Thus, the sun and moon truly move eastward, yet are pulled to set in the west. When the sun rises high, it is seen; when it sets, it is unseen. At dawn, it emerges into the yang realm; at dusk, it enters the yin realm—yin qi is dark, hence it is unseen.

The second school is *Xuanye*. Yu Xi said: *"Xuan* means brightness; *ye*, darkness. Its calculations encompass both the bright and the dark."* You Meng’s *Records* state: "The sun, moon, and stars float in the void of empty space. Their movement and stillness all depend on qi." It is also said: "Heaven stands firm above, possessing a constant and stable form." This incorporates elements of the *Gaitian* theory.

Li Chunfeng’s *Gaitian* theory states: "Heaven and earth are high at the center and low at the four edges. The sun and moon conceal one another, creating day and night as they revolve. The North Pole is always visible as the *upper circle*; the South Pole is always hidden as the *middle circle*; the celestial equator stretches horizontally as the *lower circle*."

The third school is *Huntian*. Ge Hong said: "Heaven’s shape is like an egg; earth is like its yolk. Earth resides within heaven, heaven large and earth small. Beyond heaven’s surface lies water; heaven and earth each ride upon qi and float, carried by water as they move. Dividing the celestial sphere in half, the lower half covers the earth, while the upper half circles beneath it." Song scholar He Chengtian said: "Heaven’s form is perfectly round, with water occupying half. The east is where the sun rises, called *Yanggu*; the west is where it sets, called *Chengchi*. Zhuangzi spoke of the *Northern and Southern Limits*—this is evidence that water surrounds all four directions, hence the term *Four Seas*. Within the Five Phases, water is born of metal, and all rivers originate from mountains before flowing downward into the sea. The sun is the essence of yang; when it enters water at night, it would scorch and dry up, but the rivers’ return replenishes it. Thus, drought does not cause desiccation, nor does flooding cause overflow."

Wang Fan also said: "The sun and moon first ascend into heaven, then descend into earth. The sun is above the earth by day and below it by night." Today’s astronomical observatories largely follow this method. Yang Xiong said: "Luo Xiaheng designed it, Xianyu Wangren measured it, and Geng Zhongcheng modeled it." Later Han scholars Cai Yong, Zhang Heng, and Zheng Xuan all regarded *Huntian* as superior.

In my humble opinion, beyond these there are also the *Xuantian* and *Xintian* theories, but none are worth adopting. Wu scholar Yao Xin said: "Humans are like spiritual creatures, their forms most resembling heaven. The face tilts forward toward the chest, while the crown does not cover the back. Thus, heaven’s body slopes downward in the south, entering the earth, while the north is higher—this also reveals why humans are noble." Ouyang Xiu said: "The ancients measured the gnomon’s shadow, intending to regulate and harmonize qi to assist the proper timing of things, not to debate the merits of *Huntian* or *Gaitian* models. Whether one holds *Gaitian* or *Huntian*, the south’s degrees gradually narrow, while the north’s pole rises higher. Endless disputes over differences—what benefit do they bring to human ethics and transformation?"

Yet in investigating things, the noble person prioritizes exhausting principles and extending knowledge. It is also said: "The northwest is high, supporting the heavens’ summit." Master Zhang said: "Yu Xi’s words—'Heaven stands firm above, possessing a constant and stable form'—are all profoundly meaningful. It is only regrettable that he did not explore their beginning and end."

The *Luoshu*’s numbers begin with nine atop and one below. One arises from below, forming *Kan* Water—the inception of heaven’s image. Nine culminates above, forming *Qian* Metal—the completion of heaven’s image. Thus, *Kan* Water is yielding and moves, while *Qian* Metal is firm and congeals. Movement generates pulse and life, while the brain’s essence remains tranquil—this is the North Pole as heaven’s summit, steadfast and unmoving, possessing a constant and stable form, which is also the image of *Gaitian*.

It is also said: "Heaven’s yang lies in the south, its yin in the north. Earth’s yin lies in the south, its yang in the north. Heaven’s yang is in the south, hence the sun resides there. Earth’s firmness is in the north, hence mountains reside there. Thus, earth is high in the northwest, heaven high in the southeast. The far south is intensely hot; the far north, intensely cold. Therefore, the south melts while the north congeals—these are the deathly realms of the myriad things. Heaven slopes downward from south to north, yang in the south, yin in the north. Thus, in the *Xiantian* circular diagram, *Qian*’s position is south, *Kun*’s north. Earth slopes downward from north to south, yang in the north, yin in the south. Thus, in the *Xiantian* square diagram, *Qian* is positioned in the northwest, *Kun* in the southeast. When the sun is in the south, it is at meridian; when in the north, it is hidden. Heaven’s south is yang above, hence the far south is intensely hot. What appears on earth melts into water. Though earth has yin, it cannot suppress yang. Heaven’s north is yin above, hence the far north is intensely cold. What appears on earth congeals into mountains. Though earth has yang, it is suppressed by yin. Yang’s nature, when extreme, melts; yin’s nature, when extreme, congeals. Earth’s south should be cold but is low and hot; its north should be hot but is high and cold—this follows heaven. Water is yielding and belongs to yin. As yin cannot overcome yang, it serves yang’s purpose. Mountains are firm and belong to yang. As yang cannot overcome yin, they serve yin’s purpose. Thus, in form, they follow earth’s yielding and firmness; in qi, they follow heaven’s cold and heat. At the extremes of yang and yin, qi is not harmonious. The myriad things do not grow—hence, these are deathly realms. Only where heaven and earth’s cold and heat intermingle and harmonize do the myriad things flourish."

Master Zhu said: "Scholar Hu of Anding said: 'Heaven is the form of *Qian*; *Qian* is heaven’s function. Heaven’s form is azure, with the southern pivot sinking thirty-six degrees below earth and the northern pivot rising thirty-six degrees above it, resembling a leaning pestle. Its function is such that in one day and night, it travels over nine hundred thousand *li*. With each human breath, heaven moves eighty *li*. In one day and night, a person takes over thirteen thousand six hundred breaths.'"

Thus, heaven travels over nine hundred thousand *li* in a day and night. I humbly note: The *Book of Elixirs* states that in one day and night, a person takes thirteen thousand five hundred breaths. One thousand one hundred twenty-five breaths correspond to one *shi* (two-hour period), which would total thirteen thousand five hundred sixty breaths. Master Wending [Hu Hong] cited a round number.

Zheng’s commentary on the *Kao Ling Yao* says: The Great Void originally has no form or body; it is only by observing the movement of the stars that we conceive of heaven’s body. Yet the stars’ revolution is from east to west, and after three hundred sixty-five and a quarter days, they return to their original positions. While the stars revolve leftward, the sun moves rightward, also taking three hundred sixty-five and a quarter days to return to its original stellar position. Taking one day’s movement as one *du* (degree), and calculating across the twenty-eight lunar lodges, a full celestial circuit measures nearly three hundred sixty-five and a quarter *du*—this is the measure of heaven’s single revolution. Heaven is like a pellet from a sling, its circumference three hundred sixty-five and a quarter *du*. Each *du* spans two thousand nine hundred thirty-two *li* and three hundred forty-eight parts of one thousand four hundred sixty-one *li*, making the total circumference of heaven one million seventy-one thousand *li*. Speaking of circumference as three times the diameter, the straight diameter of the twenty-eight lodges’ circuit is three hundred fifty-seven thousand *li*. Beyond the twenty-eight lodges, above, below, east, and west each extend fifteen thousand *li*, forming the four extremes of celestial motion, called the Four Limits. Within the Four Limits and the lodges’ interior, the total distance is three hundred eighty-seven thousand *li*. At heaven’s central point, directly above and below, the distance is one hundred ninety-three thousand five hundred *li*. Earth lies at its center—this is the distance from earth to heaven.

It further states: Earth’s thickness increases by thirty thousand *li*. Wang Fan’s commentary on the *Shangshu* says: Heaven above earth spans one hundred eighty-two and a half *du* and more; the same is true below earth. The North Pole rises thirty-six *du* above earth, with Mount Song directly aligned with heaven’s center. Fifty-five *du* south of the pole lies the summit of Mount Song. Twelve *du* further south is the sun’s path at the Summer Solstice. Another twenty-four *du* south is the sun’s path at the Spring and Autumn Equinoxes. Yet another twenty-four *du* south is the Winter Solstice path. Southward, the distance to earth is only thirty-one *du*. Thus, at the Summer Solstice, the sun is sixty-seven *du* from the North Pole; at the Spring and Autumn Equinoxes, ninety-one *du*; and at the Winter Solstice, one hundred fifteen *du*. The North and South Poles hold heaven’s two ends, while heaven, the sun, moon, and stars revolve obliquely—this is the general principle. Ancient methods once existed but were lost during the Qin.

Master Zhang said: Though the celestial sphere encircles earth, earth remains centrally level, while heaven slopes downward to the south. The celestial circuit is divided equally between north and south. The North Pole rises thirty-six *du* above earth, with the rest submerged; the South Pole sinks thirty-six *du* below earth, with the rest visible. Heaven and humanity both face south with their backs to the north, hence the southern sky is seen while the northern is hidden.

He further said: The celestial circuit encircling the North Pole spans seventy-two *du*, always visible and never concealed—this is called the Upper Rule. The circuit encircling the South Pole spans seventy-two *du*, always hidden and never seen—this is called the Lower Rule. Though the yin realm divides into yin and yang, the constant concealment and visibility belong to the static reckoning, corresponding to the *Kun* hexagram’s one hundred forty-four counting rods. The east-west circuit in motion spans two hundred sixteen *du*. Though the yang realm divides into yin and yang, the alternating motion belongs to the dynamic reckoning, corresponding to the *Qian* hexagram’s counting rods. The hexagram represents earth; the lines represent heaven. The *Qian* hexagram’s counting rods total two hundred sixty.

Master Zhang said: Earth rises and falls; the sun’s path lengthens and shortens. Though earth is a congealed, indissoluble substance, the two qi rise and descend, endlessly following one another. When yang ascends daily, earth descends daily, and what descends becomes empty. When yang descends daily, earth ascends daily, and what ascends becomes full—this is the pattern of the year’s cold and heat.

The *Kao Ling Yao* states: Earth’s thickness increases by thirty thousand *li*. At the Spring Equinox, earth lies at heaven’s center; from then on, it gradually descends. By the Summer Solstice, earth has sunk fifteen thousand *li*, its upper edge level with heaven’s center. After the Summer Solstice, earth gradually rises. By the Autumn Equinox, it returns to heaven’s center; from then on, it gradually ascends. By the Winter Solstice, it has risen fifteen thousand *li*, its lower edge level with heaven’s center. After the Winter Solstice, it gradually descends—this is earth’s rise and fall within thirty thousand *li*. Heaven moves laterally within the Four Limits: south in winter, north in summer, west in spring, and east in autumn, each time brushing the Four Limits before stopping. Earth likewise rises and falls within heaven: descending at the Winter Solstice and ascending at the Summer Solstice, with the two solstices marking the extremes of earth’s thickness.

The *Kao Ling Yao* further states: In the first month, the sun is eight thousand *li* above and one hundred four thousand *li* below—hence the terms "above" and "below." Zheng’s commentary says: Heaven is one hundred ninety-three thousand five hundred *li* from earth. At the Rain Water solar term in the first month, the sun is above, eight thousand *li* from heaven, and one hundred thirteen thousand five hundred *li* from earth. At the Summer Solstice, the sun reaches its highest point, level with heaven’s edge, then gradually descends. By the Winter Solstice, the sun is eight thousand *li* from earth and one hundred thirteen thousand five hundred *li* from heaven.

As detailed in the *Kao Ling Yao* commentary: The twenty-eight lodges and all stars revolve leftward with heaven, completing one celestial circuit each day and night.

Beyond one celestial circuit, the heavens advance by one degree, totaling three hundred sixty-five and one-quarter circuits in a year. The sun, moon, and five planets, however, move rightward. The sun travels one degree per day, while the moon advances thirteen and seven-nineteenths degrees daily. These are the harmonized reckonings.

Master Zhu said: Heaven and earth drift in four directions, ascending and descending no more than thirty thousand *li*. The *Rites of Zhou* states that a one-*cun* shadow on the earthen gnomon corresponds to one thousand *li*. A shadow of one *chi* and five *cun* thus corresponds to fifteen thousand *li*. Since this measurement is taken at the center of the earth, the distances from north to south and east to west are each thirty thousand *li*—not that the space between heaven and earth is limited to this span. In spring, the earth drifts three thousand *li* past the east; in summer, three thousand *li* past the south; and likewise in autumn to the west and in winter to the north. Imagine a great basin filled with water, with an empty vessel floating at its center, marking the four directions. If the vessel drifts three *cun* eastward—where one *cun* equals ten thousand *li*—then it recedes three *cun* from the west. Just as the earth is deeper than the water, when it tilts three thousand *li* past the east, it withdraws three thousand *li* from the west. The same applies to north and south. Thus, the varying lengths of day and night in winter and summer are not caused by the gnomon’s shadow or the sun’s rising and setting, but rather by the earth’s cyclical drift through the four directions.

Someone asks: There is no such principle. Though it cannot be known for certain, the calculations of astronomers all align with this reckoning. Perhaps such a principle exists. Both the earth and the stars undergo fourfold drifting and ascending-descending. The fourfold drift begins at the *Lichun* solar term, when the earth and stars drift westward. At the *Chunfen* solar term, the westward drift reaches its extreme, though the earth’s ascent and descent remain centered. From there, it gradually drifts eastward until the end of spring, when it returns to its original position. After *Lixia*, it drifts northward; at the *Xiazhi* solar term, the northward drift reaches its extreme, and the earth’s ascent and descent reach their lowest point. By the end of summer, it returns to center. After *Liqiu*, it drifts eastward; at the *Qiufen* solar term, the eastward drift reaches its extreme, and the earth’s ascent and descent return to center. By the end of autumn, it is restored. After *Lidong*, it drifts southward; at the *Dongzhi* solar term, the southward drift reaches its extreme, and the earth’s ascent and descent reach their highest point. By the end of winter, it returns to center. This is the meaning of the earth’s and stars’ fourfold drift. The stars also rise and fall with the earth, never exceeding the thirty thousand *li* range. Halving this gives fifteen thousand *li*, matching the one *chi* and five *cun* of the earthen gnomon. A one-*chi* discrepancy corresponds to ten thousand *li*, while five *cun* correspond to five thousand *li*. A one-*fen* difference equals one hundred *li*. Thus, the lengthening and shortening of shadows all differ by one *cun*. Therefore, the royal domain of a thousand *li* takes its model from the sun. One *cun* is set as the standard, and when the sun is observed at its fullest, its breadth of one *chi* corresponds to a thousand *li*.

Someone asks: The theory of fourfold drift is baseless and unorthodox. Thus, the *Erya* commentary does not follow Zheng’s annotation. I say: Amidst the confusion of many voices, one need only consult the sacred classics and worthy traditions to resolve it. Now, both Master Zhang and Master Zhu have affirmed this theory—there is no cause for doubt.

Someone asks: Master Zhu said: *The Rites of Zhou* employs the method of the earthen gnomon to measure the depth of the soil, align the sun’s shadow, and determine the center of the land. When the sun is in the south, shadows lengthen and cold increases; when in the east, evening shadows bring frequent winds; when in the west, morning shadows bring much overcast.

Another asks: Zheng’s commentary states: “Evening shadows” refer to the moment when the sun’s reflection aligns the shadow. The gnomon is placed too far east, near the sun. “Morning shadows” refer to the moment before noon when the shadow aligns. The gnomon is placed too far west, distant from the sun.

Master Zhu replied: “Evening shadows bring frequent winds; morning shadows bring much overcast.” These two lines in Zheng’s commentary cannot be dismissed, though his explanation is inverted. It appears that “evening shadows” occur when the shadow aligns late. Before noon, the shadow has already aligned. If the gnomon is placed too far south, the sun is nearer, making shadows shorter before noon and longer after. “Morning shadows” occur when the sun has already passed noon, yet the shadow has not yet aligned. If the gnomon is placed too far north, the sun is more distant, making shadows longer before noon and shorter after.

Asked about the prevalence of wind and overcast: He said: The east is naturally windy, as seen in coastal prefectures where winds are extreme. They arrive as expected—spring brings east winds, summer brings south winds. Open land without mountains to obstruct them allows winds to arrive from their respective directions. Wind arises from the waxing and waning of yang qi: it stirs at dawn as yang ascends, peaks at noon, weakens in the afternoon, and ceases by evening. As for the west’s frequent overcast, it is not merely due to high mountains blocking the sky—yang qi naturally declines there. When the sun reaches its zenith there, it is already late in that region, and soon it sets, so the western lands seldom see the sun. The ancients said: “In Shu, the sun melts snow”; they also called it “the leaking sky,” for the sun is rarely seen. Observing heaven and earth thus, one sees they are not so vast. This can be verified through the sun, moon, cold, heat, wind, and overcast.

Tang scholar Jia Gongyan said: When the Duke of Zhou established Luoyang, he sought the land’s center and erected five gnomons. The central gnomon was placed in Yangcheng, Yingchuan. The site where shadows were measured still bears ancient traces. A thousand *li* south of the central gnomon, another was placed; a thousand *li* north, another; and a thousand *li* east and west of the center, two more were placed—this was the method for aligning the sun’s shadow to determine the center.

Furthermore, Master Zhu noted: People today no longer use a single earthen gnomon. Zheng Kangcheng also erred in assuming the gnomon’s length was used to measure qi. It was made of jade, one *chi* long and one *cun* wide. A gnomon was sought where the shadow’s length, measured by the jade gnomon, matched one *chi* and five *cun*—this was the land’s center. If the shadow lengthened, the gnomon’s shadow shortened; if the shadow shortened, the gnomon’s shadow lengthened. At the winter solstice, the gnomon’s shadow extended over thirteen *chi*. Now, the land’s center differs from ancient times.

The city of Hanyang was deemed the center of the earth; the Yue Terrace of the Song dynasty was likewise considered the center; today, it lies in Xunyi, the old Kaifeng County.

In the twelfth year of the Kaiyuan era of the Tang, envoys were dispatched throughout the realm. They observed the sun’s shadow from Linyi in the south to Hengye in the north, and determined that the Yue Terrace in Xunyi stood at the midpoint between the northern and southern arcs, thus marking the earth’s center. When the Great Zhou established its capital at Bian, it erected a jade gnomon and placed an arrow to measure the Yue Terrace. When the shadow’s length aligned with the center of the graduated scale, the solstice arrived, and the qi responded accordingly.

Lu Xiangshan said: A vessel of bronze served as the clepsydra, and a floating arrow marked the divisions. The day comprises twelve earthly branches, eight celestial stems, and the four cardinal directions; the year, twelve months and twenty-four solar terms. By measuring the sun’s shadow with a jade gnomon and discerning directions with a lodestone, the twenty-four positions were thus rectified. As the sun’s path shifts north and south, day and night vary in length, and so twenty-one arrows were established. South of the Yue Terrace, three relocations were made, rendering six arrows unusable; north of the Yue Terrace, three relocations were likewise made, and six arrows were also discarded due to the stems and branches. Occasionally, the shadow fell ahead or behind, or straddled two positions where the lodestone pointed—some took it as the third division of *chén* or the seventh of *bǐng*, others as the midpoint between *bǐng* and *wǔ*—yet there remained a fixed principle. Since the time of the Duke of Zhou, this method of correlating yin and yang has persisted, and scholars ought to heed it.

He further stated: Heaven envelops earth, and the heaven of earth moves within the earth’s center. Zhang Hengqu said: Earth, in relation to heaven, is but a single object within it—nothing more. Thus, the One vast and great is called heaven; the Two, small, is called earth.

He also said: The body of heaven is high in the north and low in the south, while the body of earth lies level within it. Shaozi remarked: Heaven covers earth, and earth bears heaven. Above heaven lies earth, and above earth lies heaven—yet men dwelling on earth perceive it not.

He further said: Heaven is round like a ball of qi, with earth slanting across its midst. Heaven’s expanse is boundless, yet the void within is finite. Like a hollow sphere in space, viewed from within, one faces east while the sphere turns west, and heaven rotates leftward. Yet the stars and the Pole share a single aspect, while the Four Movements press against the four limits and halt. Observing the *Xiantian Diagram*, from *Lin* upward, each set of sixteen hexagrams represents the heaven of heaven and the heaven of earth, encompassing the reckonings of *yuan*, *hui*, *yun*, and *shi* within heaven. From *Tongren* and *Dun* downward, each set of sixteen hexagrams represents the earth of heaven and the earth of earth, encompassing the reckonings of years, months, days, and hours within earth.

He also said: The positions on earth remain fixed and unchanging, while the celestial images revolve without cease. Only when the *Chunhuo* of heaven aligns with the *wǔ* position of earth do they converge, attaining the proper motion of heaven.

Zhu Xi said: In heaven, the twelve conjunctions are called *chén*. In the eleventh month, the *chén* resides in *Xingji*; in the twelfth month, in *Xuangan*; in the first month, in *Zouzi*; in the second month, in *Jianglou*—and so forth. If viewed from earth, standing facing south, the four directions and twelve *chén* positions likewise correspond, fixed and unchanging. Thus, the celestial sphere spans 365¼ degrees, with the twenty-eight lunar lodges arrayed to manifest the body of heaven and define the four directions. As heaven encircles earth, in one day and night it completes a full revolution, advancing by one degree. The sun, moon, and five planets likewise revolve around earth with heaven. Yet only the sun’s motion completes one circuit each day, neither exceeding nor falling short; the others vary in speed. Though suspended, they are neither affixed nor propelled, but rather, where their qi is abundant, their spirit naturally flourishes, each following its own order.

He further said: Heaven is deficient in the northwest, and earth incomplete in the southeast. Thus, the southeast abounds in water, while the northwest teems with mountains. The *Suwen* commentary states: The terrain of the Central Plains is high in the northwest and low in the southeast. The *Hetu Kuodi Xiang* says: The northwest is the Gate of Heaven, the southeast the Door of Earth. The Gate of Heaven has no above, the Door of Earth no below. Heaven descends from above to enter ten thousand *li*.

He also said: South of the sun’s zenith lies fifteen thousand *li*. Measured from the earth’s center, north and south, east and west each span thirty thousand *li*. Earlier scholars observed: The mountains and rivers of the Central Plains all originate in the northwest. Thus, those who describe mountains and rivers begin in the northwest and proceed southeast. Mountains and rivers on earth are like blood vessels in man—some lie close yet unconnected, while others distant are linked. Hence it is said: How could the Yangtze and seas sever the earth’s veins?

Cai Shi said: The great Yellow River and the northern mountains—all their roots and ridges stem from Dai in the north. The mountains of Wutai and Xian rise from lofty heights. The waters west of the ridge flow westward into the upper reaches of the Yellow River beyond Longmen. Those east of the ridge flow eastward as the Sanggan, forming the rivers of You and Ji, and empty into the sea. One western branch becomes the Kongkou Great Range; the next branch encircles the northern banks of Fen and Jin; another becomes the Taihang; yet another, the Hengshan—these form the northern range. The southern range comprises the mountains and rivers south of the Yangtze and Han. The veins of Mount Min divide: one northern branch becomes Mount Heng, ending west of Dongting; the southern branch crosses the Gui Range, passes north through the lands of Yuan and Jun, and reaches De’an, where it is called *Fuqian Plain*—some say it is today’s Lushan in Jiangzhou. Between these two branches, the Xiang River interrupts the connection. Mount Heng lies southeast of the Xiang, while *Fuqian Plain* lies northeast.

The origins and flows of these mountains and rivers may be traced. Master Zheng takes Mount Yan and Mount Qi as the true northern range, listing the regions through which the Yellow and Ji Rivers pass. Mount Xiqing forms the northernmost range, listing the regions through which the Yi, Luo, Huai, and Wei Rivers flow. Mount Fan Zhong serves as the secondary southern range, listing the regions through which the Han River flows. The Min River marks the true southern range, listing the regions through which the Yangtze flows.

The *Records of the Western Regions* states: The Yellow River has two sources. One emerges from the Congling Range; the other from Khotan, where two rivers converge and flow eastward into Lop Nur—also called the Salt Marsh—over three hundred *li* from the Yumen and Yang Passes. Its waters remain constant through winter and summer, flowing hidden beneath the earth before reemerging south at Jishi. Master Cheng says: Before the Nine Rivers were submerged by the sea, the land extended five hundred *li* northeast from the present coastline. The Yellow River branched into nine channels, entering the sea near Jieshi, which lay on the northwestern shore. Today, in the lands of Yan and Ji, no such stone remains, yet due south of Pingzhou, a mountain called Mashi stands in the sea, over five hundred *li* from the shore—this is precisely where the ancient Yellow River vanished into the sea. Wang Heng of the Han said: Once, the heavens sent continuous rain and a northeast wind, causing the sea to overflow and flood hundreds of *li* southwest. Thus, the Nine Rivers and Jieshi were engulfed by the sea. Later scholars sought the Nine Rivers on flat land but failed to verify the existence of Jieshi, leading to conflicting accounts and no resolution.

The *Tang Fa Xiang Zhi* states that the celestial patterns of the world’s mountains and rivers reside in the Two Boundaries. The Northern Boundary begins at Sanwei and Jishi, supporting the northern network of the Zhongnan Range, extending to Huimo and Chaoxian—this is called the Northern Ridge, by which Heaven restrains the Rong and Di. The Southern Boundary begins at Mount Min and Fan Zhong, supporting the southern network, extending to Dong’ou and Minzhong—this is called the Southern Ridge, by which Heaven restrains the Man and Yi.

The *River Sources* says: The Northern River flows from the head of the Northern Ridge to Huayin, where it meets the earth’s network. It then divides and flows eastward, with the Jing, Wei, Ji, and Zi Rivers forming its outer and inner courses. The *Yangtze Sources* says: The Southern River flows from the head of the Southern Ridge to Huayang, where it meets the earth’s network. At Mount Jing, it divides and flows eastward, with the Han River, Huai River, and Zi River forming its outer and inner courses. Observing the patterns of the Two Rivers and the beginning and end of the Milky Way, the celestial divisions become clear.

The Southern and Northern Boundaries are how Heaven demarcates the lands of the Central Hua. The Southern and Northern Rivers serve as the warp and weft to the mountains of the Two Boundaries. Within them, the states where civil and martial arts flourish, where commerce and resources abound, all nurture the people, complete the work of creation, and establish the realm of Chinese sovereigns, ministers, fathers, sons, benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music. In the universe, nothing is greater than Heaven, nothing deeper than Earth, and nothing more abundant than water. The essence of water rises as the Milky Way and flows downward as the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers. The moon’s reflection mirrors the forms of mountains and rivers, and water contains the patterns of the stars—all are the creations of a single *qi*. Recognizing the veins of mountains and rivers within the Two Boundaries, discerning the rise and fall of the Dipper and Net in the four directions, and correlating them with the ancient Han commanderies and kingdoms, the divisions of celestial regions become as clear as the lines on one’s palm—such is the grand pattern of mountains and rivers.

In terms of celestial correspondences, the Northern Boundary aligns with the Ximu constellation, while the Southern Boundary aligns with Xingji. As for the Quail’s Head, which governs the regions of Qin and Long, the heads of the Two Boundary mountains and rivers truly reside there. The ruler who harmoniously receives the pure and refined *qi* of the Central States, who grasps the celestial and terrestrial symbols of the Five Thearchs, and who governs the constant principles of the cosmic order may do so.

The Milky Way, extending from *kun* to *gen*, serves as the Earth’s network. The Northern Dipper, spanning from *qian* to *xun*, serves as the Heaven’s guiding principle. Their celestial divisions correspond to the regions of the Five Thearchs. *Kun* represents Earth, and the Milky Way is Earth’s *qi*. *Qian* represents Heaven, and the Northern Dipper is Heaven’s pole.

The Five Thearchs are: Shaohao of the Metal Virtue, Zhuanxu of the Water Virtue, Fuxi the Great Taihao of the Wood Virtue, Shennong the Lie Shan Clan of the Fire Virtue, and the Yellow Emperor of the Youxiong Clan of the Earth Virtue. Each of the Five Thearchs resides in one of the five directions, and their governance is said to revolve around the Xianchi.

When governance resides in *qian* within the inner quadrant, it is Jiang Lou, corresponding to Shaohao, with its domain at the Northern Palace. When governance resides in *qian* within the outer quadrant, it is Zou Zi, corresponding to Zhuanxu, with its domain at Sheti. When governance resides in *xun* within the inner quadrant, it is Shouxing, corresponding to Taihao, with its domain at Taiwei. When governance resides in *xun* within the outer quadrant, it is Chun Wei, corresponding to the Lie Shan Clan, with its domain encompassing the Four Seas. The central pillar of governance is Xuanyuan, corresponding to the Yellow Emperor, with its domain at the handle of the Dipper governing externally. Chun Wei governs the southern maritime states, while the Dipper’s bowl governs internally. Zou Zi governs the central states surrounded by conflict. The remaining constellations: eight lie on the yin side of the Milky Way, governing maritime states; four lie on the yang side, governing states surrounded by conflict.

The world’s mountains divide into four ranges, corresponding to the twenty-eight constellations. The first range includes Mount Yan, Mount Qi, Hukou, Mount Leishou, Mount Taiyue, and Dizhu—these are the eastern constellations. Next are Jiang Lou and Xugan, supporting the Eastern Sea. The spirit of Mount Tai presides, called Jupiter.

The second range includes Mount Xicheng, Mount Wangwu, Mount Taihang, Mount Heng, Mashi, Mount Xiqing, and Zhu Yu—these are the northern constellations. Next are Daliang and Ximu, supporting the Northern Sea. The spirit of Mount Heng presides, called Mercury.

The third range includes Mount Wushu, Mount Taihua, Mount Xiong’er, Mount Waifang, Mount Tongbai, Mount Fan Zhong, and Pei Wei—these are the western constellations. Next are Chun Shou and Shi Shen, supporting the Western Sea. The spirit of Mount Hua presides, called Venus.

The fourth range includes Mount Jing, Mount Neifang, Mount Dabie, Mount Min, Mount Heng, the Nine Rivers, and Fuqian Plain—these are the southern constellations.

Next are Xingji and Chunwei, supporting the Southern Sea. The spirit of Mount Heng presides, called Mars.

The Central States reside at the center of the world. Next are Chunhuo, Dahuo, Shouxing, and Shivei. The spirit of Mount Song presides, called Saturn. These are lands that rely on strategic terrain and martial prowess.

In celestial terms, this is Hongnong, where the division of Shan forms the convergence of the Two Rivers. West of Shan lies Qin and Liang. The northern bend of mountains and rivers marks the land of Jin, while the southern bend marks Ba and Shu—these are lands where culture and strategy contend.

East of Shan, the Three Rivers converge, with the Central Peak forming Chengzhou. To the west, it extends to Waifang and Mount Da Ren; to the north, it crosses the Ji and reaches south of the Huai; to the east, it stretches to Ju Ye—these are the lands of Song, Zheng, Chen, and Cai. North of the Yellow River and south of the Ji lie Yu, Wei, and Di. East along the Huaiyin lies Shen and Sui—these are lands where commerce and wealth flourish.

The northern bend extends east to the Northern River, where north lies Xing and Zhao. The southern bend extends east to the Southern River, where south lies Jing and Chu. Downstream from the Northern River, south to Mount Tai, lies the land of Qi; flanking Ma Shi to the right lies northern Yan. Downstream from the Southern River, north to Mount Tai, lies Zou and Lu; south across the Jiang and Huai lies Wu and Yue—these are the lands bordering the sea.

Other features, such as the nine strategic passes, the nine bends of the Yellow River, the nurturing waters of Sanwei, the nine twists of the Min River, all correspond to the constellations above.

The *He Luo* chapter states: "The central star of the celestial pole is the dwelling of Kunlun. The Heavenly Gate and Bright Hall are the essence of Mount Tai."

Zhang Heng said: "In heaven, images take form; on earth, shapes manifest. Heaven has nine positions; earth has nine domains. Heaven has three luminaries; earth has three terrains—all can be pointed to and spoken of."

If one pursues this to its utmost, the eight extremities span a diameter of 220,2300 li. The distance from north to south is shorter by a thousand li, while east to west is broader by a thousand li. From earth to heaven is half the span of the eight extremities, and the depth of the earth is the same. Measuring this comprehensively, the calculations employ the double-gougu method: the shadow of the suspended heaven and the principle of the earth’s surface both shift by one cun for every thousand li. Beyond this, what lies further remains unknown. What remains unknown is called the cosmos. The bounds of space are infinite; the origins of time are without end—inexhaustible and unknowable.

I have examined the realms above and below, consulting ancient records, and provisionally speak of the lands under the rule of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors, as measured by the steps of Da Zhang and Kui Hai. Confucius described these lands as: "North to Youling, south to Jiaozhi, west treading the Flowing Sands, east reaching the Coiling Tree." Heaven and earth converge, and the finest qi gathers. The Yellow River reveals its numinous power, and the *River Chart* and *Luo Writ* emerge. Thus, the most refined of humanity become sages, and the Zhou and Confucius arose as the masters of China’s rites, music, and civilization, inheriting the legacy of Fuxi and the host of sages.

Zou and Lu abound in refined scholars; Yan and Zhao teem with heroes. East and west of the mountains, generals and ministers arise—all working together to weave the fabric of the Central States.

Yu inherited the prosperity of Yao and Shun, pacifying the Nine Provinces, and the jade and silk of ten thousand states were settled. The arable land totaled 9,108,024 qing. The lands rising above water spanned 8,000 li; the lands receiving water spanned 8,000 li. Famous mountains numbered 5,350, extending 64,050 li. Copper-producing mountains numbered 467; iron-producing mountains numbered 3,609. Commerce thrived through men plowing and women weaving. By not encroaching on their time, wealth and resources were ensured. Frugality left surplus; extravagance brought insufficiency. The public treasury held thirty years’ reserves; private households stored nine years’ provisions.

At the height of Chengzhou’s prosperity, the population numbered 22,704,923—161,000 more than in Yu’s time. Thereafter, through the Qin, Han, Sui, and Tang dynasties, abundance and scarcity fluctuated. To the present day, the realm is unified, surpassing the primordial chaos to merge the subtle and indistinct, reaching the limits of heaven and earth. None fail to revere and cherish it, allowing one to trace the footsteps of the Three August Ones and Five Emperors.

Zhu Xi said: "The *River Chart* speaks of Kunlun as the center of the earth. Beneath the earth lie eight pillars, mutually restraining one another. Famous mountains and great rivers, with their caverns and channels, are interconnected."

The *Commentary on Sima Xiangru’s Biography* states: "Kunlun Mountain lies 50,000 li from China, spanning 10,000 li in breadth and height, rising 80,000 ren. Its layered cities have nine tiers, with nine wells on each face, jade railings, and five gates on the sides—it is the lower capital of the Celestial Emperor."

The *Water Classic* says: "Kunlun lies 50,000 li from China, and the mountain itself spans 11,000 li."

Hu Yongzhi asked: "Where does Tianzhu lie? Its lands produce many extraordinary people."

Zhu Xi replied: "China lies 20,000 li from Yutian, and Yutian is another 30,000 li from Kunlun without further barriers."

The *Wenchang Miscellany* records that envoys from Yutian came to pay tribute, stating that 1,300 li west of their country lay Kunlun Mountain. Thus, China lies to the southeast of Kunlun, while the lands of Tianzhu lie directly to its south. The Yellow River flows from the southeast of Kunlun into China. In this way, Kunlun lies to the southwest of Tianzhu.

The *Qian Book* states: "Mount Aru is Kunlun. At its summit lies the great Aru Pool, whose waters divide and flow in four directions. The stream entering China becomes the Yellow River, flowing into the Eastern Sea. The other three streams each enter the Southern, Western, and Northern Seas, like the Weak Waters and Black Waters."

Generally, the earth’s shape resembles a steamed bun, with its pointed tip being Kunlun. China’s terrain is perfectly round, and the people born there are likewise round and upright in form. Its geography benefits from the central qi of heaven and earth. Other worlds are shaped with slanted, pointed, or incomplete forms, encircling it externally, lacking the uprightness of heaven and earth. The people born there are often likewise unbalanced.

This is the theory of the beneficent heavens. Master Hengqu also subscribed to this view, stating:

Ancient records say: "The northwest abounds in mountains, with Kunlun as their ancestral root. Its ridge spans the cosmos, and the Five Peaks and Three Crossings stretch across it in interlocking grandeur." It is also said: "The Yellow River issues from Kunlun, the highest point on earth. Looking northward toward the sandy lands, no natural barriers obstruct the view. Should waters breach their bounds to the east and west, flooding would easily ensue. Yu dredged the Nine Rivers to temper their surging waves."

It further states: "Mount Song is not the center of heaven but the center of earth. The Yellow Path and Red Path both lie north of Mount Song. The Southern and Northern Poles are the pivots of heaven. Only this place remains unmoving, like the navel of a millstone. This is the central point of heaven."

According to Master Shao: "The origin of heaven and earth—does it not arise from the center? Where is the center of heaven? It is at the Celestial Pole. Where is the center of earth? It is at Mount Song. Only with the Celestial Pole as heaven’s center can calendrical calculations and seasonal divisions be derived. Only with Mount Song as earth’s center can directions be fixed and distances measured. Is not the *zi-wu* axis the center of heaven and earth?

At the winter solstice, yang is born at midnight, and half an hour past midnight marks the *zi* hour. Thus, *Qian* begins in *Kan* and ends in *Li*—this is why the Southern and Northern Poles alone serve as heaven’s pivot, unmoving. At the summer solstice, yin is born at noon, when the sun stands at the zenith. Thus, *Kun* begins in *Li* and ends in *Kan*—this is why the Yellow Path and Red Path also lie north of Mount Song, unwavering.

Only the central point of heaven and earth remains eternally fixed, and thus the sages emerge. They observe the great movements from the pivot of the celestial sphere, establish key principles to examine future trends, and derive all calculations from this foundation."

Someone once asked me: "Can your *Xuanhun* chapter fully encompass the structure of heaven and earth?" I replied: "No. For celestial patterns, there are astronomers and historians; for geography, there are maps like the *Fingertip Atlas*; for the correspondence of clouds and stars to regions, there is Tang Yixing; for the twists and turns of waterways, there is Li Daoyuan. Yet all these are but descriptions of what has been observed, not the ultimate truth."

The *Tai Xuan* states: "In accord with the *Xuanhun*, one moves without end, aligning with heaven’s pattern—its subtleties defy expression."

*Tianyuan Fawei*, Scroll Three, concludes.

---

*Tianyuan Fawei*, Scroll Four
Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai
Collated by Fang Hui Wuli of Xugu

**Section Two**

Master Kong of the Tang said: "Yin (陰) means 'shade'—yin qi resides within, concealed and sheltering. Yang (陽) means 'radiance'—yang qi extends outward, flourishing and manifest. Yang, as the odd, is qi; qi blends into oneness as chaos. It also becomes the sun, whose body is ever luminous, without waxing or waning. Yin, as the even, is form; form divides into self and other. It also becomes the moon, with its phases of darkness and renewal. Fuxi observed the numbers of yin and yang and drew one unbroken line to symbolize yang, one broken line to symbolize yin. Yang is singular and bestows; yin is dual and receives—yet both arise from the same qi. When active, it is yang; when receding, it is yin—these two are but one. When yang arrives, life begins; when yang departs, death follows. The life and death of the myriad things are governed by yang, thus all returns to the One.

Now, heaven and earth were formed from the primordial splitting of yin and yang qi, establishing the great foundation as their master—this is the uncarved block. Then they became father and mother, giving birth to humans and creatures, undergoing endless transformations, branching into countless threads—yet none escape this origin. Thus, filling the cosmos, what is there that is not the qi of yin and yang? All things are bound to these two principles.

At the dawn of creation, form was shaped from qi, so yin and yang gave rise to heaven and earth. With form as the vessel for qi, heaven and earth in turn generate yin and yang. Dong Zhongshu of the Han first expounded yin and yang, becoming the model for Confucians. Confucians cannot be ignorant of yin and yang.

Master Zhou said: 'Water, though yin, is rooted in yang, for it arises from the One. Fire, though yang, is rooted in yin, for it arises from the Two.' Thus, Master Zhang said: 'The essence of yin and yang dwells within each other’s abode.'

Master Zhu said: 'Master Zhou’s work revolves around the two poles of yin and yang. At the beginning lies the principle of ceaseless generation; at the end, the principle of settled order. Where generation occurs, life is spoken of; where order is fixed, death is spoken of. Before the Supreme Polarity (Taiji) divided, yin contained yang. After the Supreme Polarity divided, yang separated from yin. Yang submerged in yin becomes water; yin submerged in yang becomes fire.'

Master Zhang said: 'Qi, vast and boundless in the great void, rises and falls, soars and swirls, never ceasing. Here lies the mechanism of emptiness and fullness, movement and stillness—the origin of yin and yang, hardness and softness. That which floats upward is the essence of yang; that which sinks downward is the turbidity of yin. Their encounters and congealments become wind and rain, frost and snow. The fusion and solidification of mountains and rivers, the dregs and embers—all are teachings. Wandering qi, chaotic and intertwined, coalesces into substance, giving rise to the myriad distinct beings. The ceaseless cycling of yin and yang’s two poles establishes the great principle of heaven and earth.'

Master Zhu said: 'Observe wandering qi horizontally; observe yin and yang vertically. Yin and yang are like a fan; wandering qi is like the wind it produces. Yin and yang are like a millstone; wandering qi is like the flour it grinds. Yin and yang are like the root of a tree; wandering qi is like its blossoms and fruit. There is no wandering qi apart from yin and yang. The void is qi—able to rise and fall, soar and swirl to generate the myriad things, though people do not see it. Like the five-colored dragon qi seen from afar, qi scatters forth, containing both essence and excess, thus beings possess bias and uprightness. Their encounters and congealments—above, this speaks of the two qi; below, it speaks of wandering qi. The character *shi* (始, beginning) here refers to the mother of all things. The refined all emerge from the excess. In the affairs of excess, there is nothing that is not the Dao of heaven—thus it is the supreme teaching.

The two qi within heaven and earth ceaselessly revolve, unconsciously giving rise to countless beings. Each obtains a nature and life, each possesses a form and substance—all formed from the convergence of this qi. Though this is so, the two poles of yin and yang, emerging in great swaths, remain unchanged.

He also said: 'No two things created by the Maker are alike. Though the myriad things are many, there is not one without yin and yang.' From this, we know that the transformations of heaven and earth arise from these two poles alone. Observe the sun and moon, male and female, yin and yang—if you see that not a single thing lacks yin and yang, even the most minute being has a front and back. If one speaks of movement, it is all one qi.

Thus, Master Zhu said: 'Yang alone cannot generate; yin alone cannot complete. The ceaseless flow of creation requires their joint operation. Like east and west, south and north standing together, their positions oppose. It is simply movement opposing stillness, bending opposing extension, one opposing two, the Dao opposing the vessel. There is back and front, above and below, center and periphery—no thing exists without opposition. Form casts a shadow; sound produces an echo.'

Cai Yuanyuan said: 'When beings are born, yin is still and yang is dynamic. The still often resides in seclusion; the dynamic constantly comes and goes. That which comes is *xi* (息, breath); breath is yang. That which goes is *xiao* (消, dissipation); yang dissipates into yin. Between dissipation and breath, transformations occur according to the proportion of yin and yang—this is the flowing yin and yang. After forms take shape, there are heaven and earth, sun and moon, male and female, yin and yang—these are the yin and yang of correspondence. In flowing yin and yang, only yang is active. In corresponding yin and yang, their functions are equal. Though their functions are equal, what is called yin here is not the yin of flow. Thus, even the slightest movement or growth in plants and creatures, though yin, depends on yang to arise. Only withered wood and dead ashes are purely yin.'

He also said: 'If the Two do not stand, the One cannot be seen. If the One cannot be seen, the function of the Two ceases.' And: 'The One gives rise to spirit; the Two give rise to transformation.' The Two are the waxing and waning, advancing and retreating of yin and yang.

Master Zhu said: 'Master Zhang’s words here are exceedingly precise. Without the One, the waxing and waning of yin and yang would have no means of manifestation.'"

Without the waxing and waning of yin and yang, the One likewise cannot be seen. For yang does not stand alone—it necessarily attains its establishment through yin. Thus, yang takes yin as its foundation, while yin cannot manifest itself except by awaiting yang. Therefore, yin takes yang as its counterpart. Every movement and stillness, every advance and retreat, every cold and heat—all are the workings of yin and yang. Yet there is no agent acting upon them, nor is there a principle of two standing independently. Either yin prevails over yang, or yang prevails over yin. There is nothing that is not so, no time that is not so. Yin and yang are but a single ebb and flow: when advancing, it is called yang; when retreating, it is called yin. Yang’s retreat is yin’s arising—not that yang retreats and then some other yin arises. Viewing yin and yang as one is permissible, as is viewing them as two. In general, generation is always the work of a single yang. As yang grows by one part, yin arises by one part beneath it—not that when yang wanes, one seeks out yin to replace it. Thus, yang’s return is *fu* (復), the original state of things, while yin’s arising is *gou* (垢), a chance encounter. Yin and yang are the yin and yang within yang. When speaking of qi, yin and yang are primary; when speaking of hardness and softness, they are the yin and yang within form. Here, a thing is discernible as either hard or soft.

Master Cheng said: "At the juncture of yin and yang’s waxing and waning, there is no abrupt division, yet the principle of separation remains. Their ascending and descending, their life and death—these great distinctions cannot be absent." He also said: "The movement of yin and yang—its rising and falling, its fullness and contraction—never ceases for even a moment. Yang is ever full, yin ever deficient. The slightest imbalance gives rise to ten thousand changes. It is like a turning millstone: though its teeth are uneven, once set in motion, endless variations emerge."

Master Zhu said: "Yin and yang are the root of creation and cannot be without each other. Their waxing and waning follow a constant pattern, beyond human alteration. Yet yang governs life, yin governs death, and thus their categories admit distinctions of good and ill. In the *Yi*, the Sage, addressing their inseparability, clarifies them through attributes like strength and compliance, benevolence and righteousness, without partiality. But at the juncture of their waxing and waning, where good and ill diverge, he never fails to express his intent to uphold yang and suppress yin. Speaking of the natural qi of Heaven and Earth, they cannot be without each other. But speaking of the symbols of the noble and the petty, one would wish the world to be entirely noble, without a single petty man. When framed in terms of good and evil, as in *‘Treading on frost, solid ice arrives,’* the arising of a single yin is like the emergence of a thief. Yang is good, yin is evil; the noble and the petty, Heavenly principle and human desire—these contend in victory and defeat."

He also said: "Why compare yin to the petty man? Viewed in isolation, it seems fine. But by category, though its outward form may be flawed, its essence remains sound." Master Yi Chuan said: "The tenth month is called the yang month—one might doubt whether yang is present, yet yin is the same." The Sage did not speak of it thus. Cai Jitong disagreed, saying: "Yin cannot contend with yang, just as Earth cannot match Heaven." Master Zhu remarked that the reason the Sage did not speak of it was that this itself reveals the Way of assisting and completing. For to restrain yin and advance yang, to foster the good and diminish the evil, to employ the noble and dismiss the petty—this is clearly evident. When yang transforms into yin, it is like a gradual shrinking away without a trace, hence it is called *transformation*. When yin transforms into yang, its momentum gradually grows, becoming more distinct, hence it is called *change*. When yin is less than yang, in qi, principle, and number, whether used wholly or partially, the difference lies here.

Master Shao said: "Yang is the function of the Dao; yin is the substance of the Dao. Yang employs yin, yin employs yang. When yang is taken as function, yin is honored; when yin is taken as function, yang is honored." Master Zhang said: "Yang moves, yin is still; yang is noble, yin is base. Yin can never be spoken of on equal terms with yang. Yet when adapting to time and change, yang contains yin, yin contains yang, and they alternate in function. When yang is exhausted and yin is pure, Kun prevails; when yin is exhausted and yang is pure, Qian prevails."

I say: In the *zi* month, the *si* month honors yang; in the *wu* month, the *hai* month honors yin. In terms of division, Qian is noble and Kun is base—they cannot be equated. In terms of the family, though father and mother are both honored, the mother can never be equated with the father, for there is no second supremacy. Master Cai said: "Yang is not complete without yin, yin does not arise without yang, and yin and yang further rely on each other for function. Yet in qi, yang is the function, hence yang moves and yin is still. In form, yin is the function, hence yin is swift and yang is relaxed."

Master Zhu said: "When the Supreme Polarity divides, it is merely two yin and yang, which encompass all things under Heaven. The *Yi* does not depart from yin and yang; its thousand changes and ten thousand transformations are but these two. When yin qi flows, it becomes yang; when yang qi flows, it becomes yin. This single qi, in its waxing and waning, advancing and retreating, produces the boundless affairs of Heaven and Earth from ancient times to the present. Take day and night, cold and heat: one is horizontal, one is vertical. Viewed horizontally, the principle is uniform—left is yang, right is yin; front is yang, back is yin; facing is yang, turning away is yin; advancing is yang, retreating is yin; east and south are yang, west and north are yin; spring and summer are yang, autumn and winter are yin—all follow the same pattern. Viewed vertically, the principle is likewise uniform—above is yang, below is yin; facing upward is yang, covering is yin; ascending is yang, descending is yin; clear is yang, turbid is yin; great yang and small yin, high yang and low yin, beauty is yang, ugliness is yin. Extending this by category, there is nothing that is not so, no time that is not so. The more finely it is analyzed, the more subtle it becomes; the more deeply it is explored, the more boundless it grows."

The essence of yin and yang mutually conceals its nourishment.
The qi of yin and yang circulates in alternating arrival.
The substance of yin and yang—whether vertical or horizontal, curved or straight—is moved by none, restrained by none.
In heaven, yin and yang form images; on earth, hardness and softness form substance; in humans, benevolence and righteousness form virtue—all are none other than yin and yang.

Master Shao says: Yin takes yang as its body, and yang takes yin as its body.
Yang solidifies through yin, and the void relies on the solid to stand.
Yin awaits yang to manifest, and the solid emerges from the void.
When yin reaches *Kun* (☷), it forms a body, and the twelve yang of *Qian* (☰) are entrusted therein—thus yang takes yin as its body.
After thirty-one transformations emerge from *Kun* and culminate, spring and summer arise—then yin takes yang as its function.
When yang reaches *Qian*, it forms a body, and the twelve yin of *Kun* are entrusted therein—thus yin takes yang as its body.
After thirty-one transformations emerge from *Qian* and decline, autumn and winter arise—then yang takes yin as its function.

In heaven, yang moves and diminishes yin; on earth, yin moves and diminishes yang.
At the extreme of yin, the twelve residual yang of *Kun* ultimately complete *Qian*, yielding thirty-six yang.
At the extreme of yang, the twelve residual yin of *Qian* ultimately complete *Kun*, yielding thirty-six yin.
From the yang of *Kun* arise *Zhen* (☳), *Kan* (☵), and *Gen* (☶), totaling twenty yang; from the twelve yin of *Qian* arise *Xun* (☴), *Li* (☲), and *Dui* (☱), totaling twenty yin.
Dispersing one into three marks the beginning of generation.
Combined, they number thirty-two, with yin and yang each occupying half of the eight positions.
From the twenty-eight yang of *Xun*, *Li*, and *Dui* arises the thirty-six yang of *Qian*; from the twenty-eight yin of *Zhen*, *Kan*, and *Gen* arises the thirty-six yin of *Kun*.
Converging three into one marks the culmination of generation.
Combined, they number sixty-four, with yin and yang each occupying the full eight positions.
Thus, from the Beginning of Spring to the Beginning of Autumn, the sum of yang numbers is sixty-four, while yin numbers total thirty-two.
From the Beginning of Autumn to the Beginning of Spring, the sum of yin numbers is sixty-four, while yang numbers total thirty-two.
Four times eight is thirty-two, the completion of the four trigrams.
Eight times eight is sixty-four, the completion of the eight trigrams.
The left diagram shows thirty-two yang, the right thirty-two yin, dividing the bodies of heaven and earth—combined, they form the sixty-four hexagrams.
At the extreme of yang, no yin exists; at the extreme of yin, no yang exists.

Master Shao says: *Li* (☲) in the east and *Kan* (☵) in the west stand at the midpoint of yin and yang, serving as the gates of spring, autumn, day, and night.
He also says: Yang encroaches upon yin, and yin encroaches upon yang—thus *Kan* and *Li* occupy the midpoint.
The qi of the sixty-four precelestial hexagrams all arise from the center, with the eight trigrams corresponding to the twelve earthly branches.
*Zi* (子), *Wu* (午), *Mao* (卯), and *You* (酉) are the four centers.
*Qian* corresponds to *Wu*, *Kun* to *Zi*, *Li* to *Mao*, and *Kan* to *You*.
Thus it is said: *Qian* and *Kun* establish the positions of above and below; *Kan* and *Li* arrange the gates of left and right—this is their proper alignment.
The trigrams of the four corners are: *Dui* (☱) at *Chen* (辰) and *Si* (巳), *Zhen* (☳) at *Chou* (丑) and *Yin* (寅), *Xun* (☴) at *Wei* (未), *Gen* (☶) at *Shen* (申), and *Xu* (戌) and *Hai* (亥).
Master Shao further says: *Kan* and *Li* mark the boundary of yin and yang.
*Li* corresponds to *Yin* (寅), *Kan* to *Shen* (申)—those who examine the numbers often verify this as the overflow of yin and yang.
This means that the application of numbers does not exceed the center, with *Yin*, *Shen*, *Si*, and *Hai* occupying the heads of the four seasons.
*Qian* corresponds to *Si* and is born at *Si*; *Kun* corresponds to *Hai* and is born at *Hai*.
*Li* corresponds to *Yin* and is bounded by the midpoint of the three yang; *Kan* corresponds to *Shen* and is bounded by the midpoint of the three yin—when centered, they exceed their bounds and overflow.
Moreover, as qi waxes and wanes, the remainder is six.
The *Book of Changes* says: "At midday, it turns back; when the moon is full, it wanes."
This also reflects Master Shao’s caution regarding excess, retreating one position to reside in *Mao* (卯), *Chen* (辰), *Dui* (☱); *Zi* (子), *Chou* (丑), *Zhen* (☳); *Wu* (午), *Wei* (未), *Xun* (☴); and *You* (酉), *Xu* (戌), *Gen* (☶)—all positioned where numbers fall short, not exceeding the center.
Thus, the midpoint of *Kan* and *Li* corresponds to the midpoint of yin and yang.
If *Kun* is taken as the midpoint of *Zi*, then *Mao* and *You* are centered.
Therefore, *Mao* is the gate of the sun, residing on the left, where the great yang is born.
*You* is the gate of the moon, residing on the right, where the great yin is born.
As the gates of spring, autumn, day, and night: at the spring equinox, yang is centered; at the autumn equinox, yin is centered.
Thus it is said: "Midday and midnight are equal, as are day and night."
This is unlike the summer solstice, when daylight is longer, or the winter solstice, when night is longer.
Not only do the sun and moon rise and set here, but all things are born into life and enter death within it.

Master Weng says: Though heaven and earth begin at *Yin* (寅), the opening of things reaches *Mao* (卯), where the gate is fully opened.
Though they begin at *Xu* (戌), the closing of things reaches *You* (酉), where the gate is already shut.
In a year, there are spring, summer, autumn, and winter; in a month, there are crescent, full, waning, and new moons; in a day, there are dawn, day, dusk, and night.
Though all depend on the functions of *Kan*, *Li*, the sun, and the moon, none occur without the gates of left and right.

Master Zhang says: *Li* corresponds to *Mao* and ends at *Shen*—this is the division of day.
*Kan* corresponds to *You* and ends at *Yin*—this is the division of night.
He also says: *Li* corresponds to *Yin*, *Wei* (未), and *Mao*, where it is already bright; *Kan* corresponds to *Shen*, *Si* (巳), and *You*, where it is not yet dark.
Heaven overcomes earth to create the surplus, so daylight is always longer than night by five *ke*—*Li* yang encroaches upon *Kan* yin, and daytime yang encroaches upon nighttime yin.
In my humble opinion, speaking of the greater numbers: things open after the Waking of Insects (*Jingzhe*) and close before the Beginning of Winter (*Lidong*)—yin and yang mutually encroach upon one another.
Thus, *Qian* is fully used, *Kun* is fully unused, and *Kan* and *Li* are half-used.
The two hexagrams *Kan* and *Li* are the intermingling of the middle lines of *Qian* and *Kun*.
In the precelestial arrangement, *Kan* and *Li* occupy *Mao* and *You* as the sun and moon.
In the postcelestial arrangement, *Kan* and *Li* occupy *Zi* and *Wu* as water and fire.
Only these four positions are most excellent: within yang there is yin, and within yin there is yang—such are the subtle intentions of Fu Xi and King Wen.

The other hexagrams, if not biased, become mixed. Master Zhu said: The Five Phases mutually serve as yin and yang, and each also has its own yin and yang. Spring is *lesser yang*, summer is *greater yang*. Autumn is *lesser yin*, winter is *greater yin*. This is spoken in terms of their flow through the four seasons. From the perspective of a year, six months are yang and six months are yin. A day is likewise: daytime is yang, night is yin; before noon is yang, after noon is yin. In a month, the period before the full moon is yang, and after the full moon is yin. In the *six jia* cycles, *jiazi* is yang, *yichou* is yin. Speaking in divisions, *jia* is yang and *zi* is yin. From ancient times to the present, there has only been ceaseless gathering and dispersing, bending and extending, cycling without end. What causes this? Heaven and Earth are one great yin and yang, and a year, a month, a day, an hour—all flow forth from within them.

Moreover, spring is the beginning of yang, summer is the extreme of yang. Autumn is the beginning of yin, winter is the extreme of yin. When yang begins, there is warmth; when yang reaches its extreme, there is heat. When yin begins, there is coolness; when yin reaches its extreme, there is cold. Warmth gives rise to things, heat makes things grow. Coolness gathers things, cold destroys things. These are the transformations of the single *qi*.

Yang arises from below and ascends, thus advancing to generate things. Yin arises from above and descends, thus retreating to complete things. Yang resides in the southeast, governing spring and summer to give life. Yin resides in the northwest, governing autumn and winter to destroy. This is the general outline.

Master Shao said: *Greater yang* becomes the sun, *greater yin* becomes the moon. *Lesser yang* becomes the stars, *lesser yin* becomes the celestial bodies. Their transformations give rise to heat and cold, day and night, rain and wind, dew and thunder.

Someone asked about thunder, wind, rain, snow, frost, clouds, and dew. Master Zhang replied: When yin *qi* congeals and yang is trapped within unable to emerge, it struggles and strikes to become thunder. When yang is outside and unable to enter, it revolves ceaselessly to become wind. Their gatherings vary in distance and fullness, thus wind and thunder differ in magnitude and intensity. When dispersed gently and harmoniously, they become snow, frost, rain, and dew; when dispersed disharmoniously, they become perverse *qi* and yin haze. Yin constantly disperses and receives exchange from yang, thus wind and rain are regulated and cold and heat are balanced.

He also said: When yang is burdened by yin, they contend to form rain. When yin descends and yang obtains it, it scatters and rises as clouds. Thus, the patterned distribution of cloud things across the great void is yin driven by wind, gathered but not yet dispersed.

Someone asked Master Shao about rain, wind, clouds, and thunder. He answered: Yang obtaining yin becomes rain; yin obtaining yang becomes wind. Hard obtaining soft becomes clouds; soft obtaining hard becomes thunder. Without yin, there can be no rain; without yang, there can be no thunder. Rain, being soft, belongs to yin and awaits yang before arising. Thunder, being hard, belongs to yang and awaits yin before erupting.

Master Zhang explained: When yang leads and yin follows, flowing together, it becomes rain. When yin obstructs and yang presses, scattering, it becomes wind. When hard leads and soft follows, steaming, it forms clouds. When soft stores and hard moves, striking, it forms thunder. Whether as guest or host, preceding or following, yin and yang act in opposition or accord—these differ.

Wind and rain descend from heaven, thus they are spoken of in terms of yin and yang. Clouds and thunder rise from earth, thus they are spoken of in terms of soft and hard. Heaven’s yang, without yin, cannot form rain. Earth’s yin, without yang, cannot produce thunder. Rain, being yin in form and soft, originates from heaven’s yang *qi*. Thunder, being yang in sound and hard, emerges from earth’s yin body. Yin and yang mutually employ one another.

Someone asked Master Cai Jieqai about the line images of the eight hexagrams. He answered: When *Kan* yin is obtained by yang, it ascends as clouds; if yang is shallow, it becomes mist. When *Kan* yang is burdened by yin, it streams as rain; if yin is shallow, it becomes dew. When yin is outside and yang cannot emerge, it becomes thunder. If yin is solid, it becomes an earthquake—this is *Zhen*. When yin is inside and yang cannot enter, it becomes wind. If yin is solid, it becomes a great wind—this is *Xun*. When yang envelops yin, *Li* becomes sleet; when yang harmonizes with yin, it becomes snow—this is *Li* intersecting *Kan*. When yin envelops yang, *Kan* becomes thunder; when yin enters yang, it becomes frost—this is *Kan* intersecting *Li*. When the essence of yin and yang mutually reside in each other’s abodes, *Li* becomes the sun and *Kan* becomes the moon. When yin and yang clash, it becomes lightning; when yin and yang are out of place, it becomes a rainbow.

He also said: *Qi* ascends from *zi* to *wu* and descends from *wu* to *zi*. From *zi* to *yin*, it rises from within the earth to above the earth; from *mao* to *si*, it rises from within the earth to the midst of heaven. From *wu* to *shen*, it descends from the midst of heaven to above the earth; from *you* to *hai*, it descends from above the earth to within the earth.

In my humble observation: *Zi* and *wu* each have six, bounded by the earth. Yang arises from *zi* to *yin*, thus from the eleventh month to the first month, yang *qi* ascends above the earth. *Lesser yang* is seven, yang within yin. From *mao* to *si*, the second to the fourth month, it rises from within the earth to the midst of heaven. *Greater yang* is nine, yang within yang. Then, from the moment one yin arises at *wu* to *shen*, the fifth to the seventh month, yin *qi* descends from the midst of heaven to above the earth. *Lesser yin* is eight, yin within yang. From *you* to *hai*, the eighth to the tenth month, it descends from above the earth to within the earth. *Greater yin* is six, yin within yin. A day and a month are likewise.

Generally, *yin*, *shen*, *si*, and *hai* correspond to the transitions of *Qian*, *Kun*, *Tai*, and *Pi*. The three yangs and three yins are all employed by heaven. *Zhen* is lesser, reaching *Qian* it becomes greater. *Xun* is lesser, reaching *Kun* it becomes greater. *Kan* and *Li*, with their four yins and four yangs, generate things in their midst. The three yins and three yangs oppose *Tai* and *Pi*. The six yins and six yangs oppose *Qian* and *Kun*. Like the number of water being six, as in snowflakes—six is not fabricated by humans; all things in creation are thus. From this, the marvel of yin and yang can be seen.

He also said: *Greater softness* becomes water, *greater hardness* becomes fire. *Lesser softness* becomes earth, *lesser hardness* becomes stone.

Their transformations manifest as creatures that run and fly, grasses and trees, colors and sounds, *qi* and flavors. The yang gives rise to mobile beings; the yin gives rise to rooted plants.

Master Zhu said: "It is fundamentally one principle, yet through waxing and waning, expansion and contraction, yin and yang arise. Even in the most minute and subtle things—grasses, trees, birds, and beasts—this holds true. For example, the carp bears thirty-six scales, a yin number. The dragon’s back bears eighty-one scales, a yang number. The tortoise’s shell has five segments at its center, corresponding to the Five Phases. On either side are four segments each, corresponding to the Eight Trigrams. Around the perimeter are twenty-four segments, corresponding to the twenty-four solar terms. As for grasses and trees—whether male and female, ginkgo and paulownia, hemp and bamboo—all follow this pattern."

Master Zhang said: "Even dregs and ashes are nothing but teachings." I once applied the principles of the *Huangji Jingshi Shu* to deduce that there are creatures of flight—flying trees, flying grasses, flying creatures that run—and creatures of running—running fliers, running trees, running grasses, running runners. There are also tree-fliers, tree-runners, tree-grasses, tree-trees, as well as grass-fliers, grass-runners, grass-trees, and grass-grasses. Further, when divided by yin and yang, fliers belong to yang and runners to yin; mobile beings to yang and rooted plants to yin; horses to yang and oxen to yin; horns to yang and tails to yin. None escape the reckoning of yin and yang.

Generally, colors, sounds, *qi*, and flavors—each corresponds to the eyes, ears, nose, and mouth, and every thing possesses them. Yet some are partial and incomplete, while others are complete but ineffectual, as in the case of human intelligence.

Master Cheng said: "Look near at the body, and the hundred principles are all present in the *qi* of contraction and extension—this can be seen in the breath between the nostrils. The nostrils are the Gate of Heaven, through which *qi* enters and exits."

Master Zhu said: "In terms of *qi*, exhalation is yang and pertains to expansion, while inhalation is yin and pertains to contraction. As with the breath through the nose, there is no moment without inhalation, no moment without exhalation. When exhalation is exhausted, inhalation arises; when inhalation is exhausted, exhalation arises."

Minister Hu Wending said: "In one day and night, a person takes 13,560 breaths."

Master Zhang of Hengqu said: "The mingling of the *hun* gives rise to dreams, a tumult of a hundred sensations. Relative to waking, this is the day and night of the body. When *qi* presses forth, it is spring, and the myriad things intermingle. Relative to autumn, this is the day and night of heaven and earth."

Master Zhu said: "The Supreme Ultimate is nature; movement and stillness, yin and yang, are the heart-mind. Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, and Earth are benevolence, righteousness, propriety, wisdom, and trust. The body and its blood and flesh are the Five Phases. Thus, when the eyes open, it is either yin or yang, either benevolence or righteousness. Advancing is yang; retreating is yin. In self-cultivation, one line is yin; in responding to affairs and engaging with things, it is yang. Another line states: that which is not yet settled belongs to yang; that which is settled belongs to yin."

Master Shao said: "Heaven has the four seasons; earth has the four directions; humans have the four limbs. The joints of the fingers can reveal heaven; the lines of the palm can discern earth. The principles of heaven and earth are fully present in the palm."

The exegete said: "The hands face upward, aligned with heaven; the feet are square and face downward, aligned with earth. The hands can turn and flip, but the feet cannot—yang can encompass yin, but yin cannot encompass yang. The back of the palm is high, the front is low; the southeast has much water, the northwest has many mountains. Where they gather are rivers and marshes, and the lines in the palm resemble rivers and the shape of the hand. From the wrist to the elbow to the shoulder, and from the toes to the knee to the thigh and hip, there are three joints each, corresponding to the twelve stations. The four limbs correspond to heaven’s four seasons and earth’s four directions. The four fingers each have three joints, corresponding to the twelve earthly branches.
