Together, they correspond to the twenty-four solar terms. The thumb has three joints, two of which are yin and yang; the hidden one is the Supreme Ultimate, the great thing. Combined, they number thirty-two, corresponding to the heavenly hexagrams. Together with the hands and feet, they number sixty-four, encompassing the earthly hexagrams. The earth’s body culminates in sixteen; one hand has sixteen numbers, of which fifteen are manifest. The one is the Supreme Ultimate, hidden between the great things.

Each of the four limbs has one vessel, corresponding to the four seasons. Each vessel has three sections, corresponding to the three months of a season. Each section has three periods, corresponding to the three ten-day weeks of a month. This is the *Qian* strategy.

The *Suwen* speaks of the twelve seasonal nodes, all of which communicate with heavenly *qi*. The twelve seasonal nodes correspond to the twelve meridians of the human body—referring to the three yins and three yangs of the hands and feet—and the three periods refer to the deep, floating, and middle yin and yang, which may be excessive or deficient.

It also states: "Heaven and earth are but one *qi*. When divided into yin and yang, they become two things. Thus, yang is benevolence, and yin is righteousness. Yet yin and yang each further divide into two. Thus, the inception of yang is Wood, spring, and benevolence; the fullness of yang is Fire, summer, and propriety. The inception of yin is Metal, autumn, and righteousness; the extreme of yin is Water, winter, and wisdom. This encompasses the Three Powers and doubles them.

When viewed as one *qi*, yin and yang may also be seen as two *qi*. They may be observed as they change with the seasons, or in the great openings and closings, the great waxings and wanings of the cosmos, as well as in the small openings and closings, the small waxings and wanings of the minute. Whether in the vast span of 129,600 years or in a year, a month, a day, or an hour, it is all thus. Though there may be ten thousand changes within, their principle remains constant and unchanging. Thus it is said: 'One yin, one yang—this is the Dao.' That which continues it is goodness; that which completes it is nature."

Having thoroughly studied this, one may fathom its depths.

*Tian Yuan Fa Wei*, Scroll Four, concludes.

**Tian Yuan Fa Wei, Scroll Five**

*[Textual Notes:]*
#4 Originally "[又曰]"; emended to "Master Shao says" following the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#5 Originally "匹"; emended to "four" following the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#6 Originally "體"; emended to "one yang" following the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#7 Originally "用阳"; emended to "awaits yin" following the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#8 Originally "有"; emended to "with" following the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#9 This sentence appears as "处已是阴" in the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#10 "爻" is likely a superfluous character.
#11 Originally "支"; emended to "limbs" following the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#12 Originally "夫"; emended to "Heaven" following the *Siku Quanshu* edition.

*Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai; collated by Fang Hui Wanli of Xugu.*

**Expounding the Five [Phases]**

Master Zheng cites the *Baihutong*: "Movement is the qi of Heaven’s activity." Water serves as the standard, for water lies in the Yellow Springs, nurturing all things with impartial constancy. Fire is transformation. When yang qi prevails, the myriad things undergo change. Metal is restraint. In autumn, the myriad things are constrained by yin qi. Wood is arousal. Yang qi stirs and leaps, bursting forth from the earth. Earth is spouting. Earth, dwelling at the center, universally brings forth the myriad things.

That which engenders things follows the numbers seven and eight of wood and fire. That which completes things follows the numbers nine and six of metal and water. The *Book of Changes* states: "The essence of qi becomes things"—the commentary notes seven and eight. "The wandering soul undergoes change"—the commentary notes nine and six.

Master Zhu [Xi] says: The Supreme Polarity arrays the Five [Phases] beneath yin and yang. The Five Constants are principle, while yin and yang are qi. Without qi, principle would have no place to stand; thus the Five Phases follow yin and yang. He further states: Righteousness and wisdom pertain to yin; benevolence and ritual pertain to yang. Yin and yang are qi, while the Five Phases are substance. With this substance, and with the qi of the Five Phases, things are thus produced.

This fool says: The Five Phases belong to the postnatal. If one inquires into the prenatal, the One is the Supreme Polarity. From the One dividing into two, there arises yin and yang. Yin and yang are the qi of the Five Phases; the Five Phases are the substantial forms of yin and yang. One, three, five, seven, and nine are the Five Phases of Heaven. Two, four, six, eight, and ten are the Five Phases of Earth. Two and five make ten; when yin and yang unite as one, they can engender and complete the myriad things. Thus above, the *Daybook* says: "One is the Five Phases alone." Within the One there are two.

Jia (yang) and Yi (yin) form Wood; Bing (yang) and Ding (yin) form Fire; Wu (yang) and Ji (yin) form Earth; Geng (yang) and Xin (yin) form Metal; Ren (yang) and Gui (yin) form Water—these are of Heaven. Hai (yin) and Zi (yang), the north; Yin (yang) and Mao (yin), the east; Si (yang) and Wu (yin), the south; Shen (yang) and You (yin), the west; Chen (yang) and Xu (yang), Chou (yin) and Wei (yin)—these occupy the center and are of Earth. The spirit of Wood governs benevolence; the spirit of Metal governs righteousness; the spirit of Fire governs ritual; the spirit of Water governs wisdom; the spirit of Earth governs trust—these are of Man. Uniting the Three Powers as one, qi flows and principle likewise flows.

Master Zhou [Dunyi] says: Yang transforms and yin unites, giving rise to Water, Fire, Wood, Metal, and Earth. Master Zhu says: The Five Phases’ substance resides in Earth, while their qi flows in Heaven. Speaking of their generative sequence in terms of substance, it is Water, Fire, Wood, Metal, Earth—here, Water and Wood are yang, while Fire and Metal are yin. Speaking of their operational sequence in terms of qi, it is Wood, Fire, Earth, Metal, Water—here, Wood and Fire are yang, while Metal and Water are yin. For their transformations are boundless, and nowhere do they not follow the Dao of yin and yang.

He further states: Water’s yin is rooted in yang; Fire’s yang is rooted in yin. Interwoven, they give rise to life. In their cyclical movement, Water engenders Wood, Wood engenders Fire, Fire engenders Earth, Earth engenders Metal, Metal engenders Water, Water engenders Wood—thus they circulate in mutual flourishing. Again, Jia, Yi, Bing, Ding, Wu, Ji, Geng, Xin, Ren, and Gui are all of this same thing.

Huang Mianzhai says: The *Diagram Explanation* states that Water’s yin is abundant and Fire’s yang is abundant—this is their operational sequence, not their generative sequence. Viewed from the establishment of the *zi* [midnight] hour, Water and Wood are yang, while Fire and Metal are yin—this is their generative sequence. He further says: There is one point of doubt in the *Diagram of the Supreme Polarity*. The diagram places Water, with its abundant yin, on the right, and Fire, with its abundant yang, on the left. Metal, being yin, follows Water; Wood, being yang, follows Fire—this explains their generative sequence. Yet the text below states that Water and Wood are yang, while Fire and Metal are yin, thus making Water yang and Fire yin. In considering the initial arising of things, they are naturally tender and immature. When yang first arises, as Water, it is still soft and weak; by the time Wood is engendered, it has grown strong. When yin first arises, as Fire, it is still faint; by the time Metal is engendered, it has taken form. Thus Water is yang in its inception, Wood is yang in its fullness, Fire is yin in its inception, and Metal is yin in its fullness.

He further states: The generation of the Five Phases, each with its own nature. In terms of qi and substance, Water’s nature is cold, Fire’s is hot, Wood’s is warm, Metal’s is frozen, and Earth’s is harmonious. When humans are born, they likewise differ in intelligence, foolishness, strength, and weakness according to the qi and substance they receive. Yet when each returns to its root, it possesses its own nature. This is what is meant by the Supreme Polarity’s wholeness being fully present within each thing. Thus humans, alone among beings, receive the finest essence of the Five Phases, and their minds are most numinous.

Master Cheng says: Kan is Water. The One begins at the center, being the first of the engendered. Heaven engenders Water with the One, so Water stands first among the Five Phases. When things first arise, their forms are all water. Water is one source of the myriad things, all rooted in the creative transformation of Heaven’s One. Even the production of metal and stone begins as a fluid. The qi of the first yang, the first hour of the day, the eleventh month of the year, and the Winter Solstice all commence at *zi*. *Zi* is the position of Water. Now, Water is engendered by yang yet completed by yin. When qi first stirs, yang arises; when qi gathers and stills, it becomes water—observe the breath, and it is visible. The inception of Water belongs to the One, hence it is subtle. When Water is formed, it becomes six.

Someone asked: Is there evidence for Heaven engendering Water with the One? The answer: The human body provides evidence. When the heart of hunger stirs, saliva is produced; when the heart of sorrow stirs, tears are produced; when the heart of shame stirs, sweat is produced; when the heart of desire stirs, essence is produced. When the human heart is quiescent and unmoving, it is the Supreme Polarity. When this heart stirs, the Supreme Polarity moves and yang arises. Thus when the heart stirs and Water is engendered, this can serve as evidence for Heaven engendering Water with the One.

The spirit governs qi, and when the spirit moves, qi follows. Qi is the mother of Water, and when qi gathers, Water is born.

Master Zhu said: In the sequence of the Five Phases, Wood is the beginning, Water is the end, and Earth is the center. According to the numbers in the *Diagram* and the *Book*, Water is one, Wood is three, and Earth is five—all odd numbers of yang’s generative sequence and unalterable. Thus they take turns as the principal, forming the warp of the Five Phases.

The Five Phases: Spring begins with Wood in the East, Winter ends with Water in the North, and Earth presides in the center—this is the sequence of beginning, middle, and end. Yet the sequence of their generation all begins with Heaven.

Water can moisten and nourish the myriad things; Heaven’s One first engenders Water. Water has the nature of steadfast stillness, firm and potent, concealed in Winter. Wood can warm and nurture the myriad things; Heaven’s Three engenders Wood. Wood has the nature of sprouting forth, inexhaustible and unrestrained, flourishing in Spring. Earth can bear and sustain the myriad things; Heaven’s Five engenders Earth. Earth is the mother of nurturance, upright and vast, dwelling in the center and flourishing in the four directions. Once these three are born, they ceaselessly generate, each occupying its own direction, immutable.

Thus we see Heaven’s heart of loving life: the source is upright, and the offspring are good—all are Heaven-born. Fire and Metal, however, are those within the Five Phases that rely on one another for transformation and cannot be absent for a single day, yet they are Earth-born.

Fire is born from Earth’s Two; its nature is to flame upward, sometimes existing from non-existence, sometimes non-existing from existence. Its position may be in the South or the West. Metal is born from Earth’s Four; its nature is to follow and reform, sometimes square made round, round made square. Its position may be in the West or the South. Thus the *Diagram* and the *Book* speak of rearrangement, unlike Water, Wood, and Earth, which are Heaven-born and have a fixed sequence.

Yet within this fixed sequence, the Five Phases ceaselessly generate, mutually permeating one another. Though the sequence is fixed, their qi is never unconnected. Thus Master Zhu said: They take turns as the principal, forming the warp of the Five Phases. He also said: Wood encompasses the Five Phases, for it flows through and penetrates all without exception.

Rao Shuangfeng said: Qi moves through Heaven with generation as its principal. Thus Master Zhu took Spring as the first of the four seasons, running through Summer, Autumn, and Winter. In humans, compassion is the first of the four beginnings, running through shame, deference, and right and wrong. For Spring can generate the myriad things, and compassion can embrace all virtues.

Master An said: When One is beneath Wood, it is the root—returning to the root and recovering the mandate, uniting with Five. When One is above Wood, it is the branch—sending forth limbs and leaves, scattering into the myriad. From One, know the myriad. Heaven’s One engenders Water and thus Wood—all share this One.

He also said: Water encompasses the Five Phases, for it returns to the root, restores the mandate, and conceals itself therein. Wood without Water withers, Fire without Water dries, Metal without Water stagnates. With Water to encompass them, they flow through the four seasons, animating the myriad things. When its work is done, it retreats and hides within the two yin, lying hidden at the Yellow Springs’ depths—thus it can return to the root and recover the mandate. When it emerges, it is once more primal and penetrating, like thunder shaking the earth and rain soaking the world.

In humans, it is the essence stored in the kidneys, moistening all the body. He also said: Earth is the lodging of Water and Fire, the resource of Metal and Wood, dwelling in the center and responding to the four directions—one body bearing the myriad kinds.

Rao Shuangfeng said: Substance resides in Earth, with nurturance as its principal. Thus Earth dwells at the center of the Five Phases, governing Water, Fire, Wood, and Metal. In humans, the spleen dwells at the center of the five viscera, governing the heart, liver, lungs, and kidneys. For Earth can nurture the myriad things, and the spleen can nurture all forms.

In the last month of Summer, Earth is most flourishing, thus it can engender Autumn’s Metal. It also flourishes in the four seasons, each season for eighteen days, totaling seventy-two days. The other four Phases divide the four seasons, each also receiving seventy-two days, together making three hundred and sixty days.

Master Weng said: The Five Phases, separated from Earth, cannot each become a vessel. One united with Five becomes Water. Two united with Five becomes Fire. Three united with Five becomes Wood. Four united with Five becomes Metal. Five united with Five becomes Earth. Five is Earth, dwelling in the center. One, two, three, four, five—once five is reached, Water, Fire, Wood, Metal, and Earth are formed, called six, seven, eight, nine, and ten.

He also said: Heaven and Earth are the fixed forms of yin and yang’s opposition. One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, eight, nine, and ten are the sequence of yin and yang’s flow. Without opposition, there can be no flow, and no transformation. Without flow, there can be no self-movement. Thus the Five Phases flow through Heaven and Earth to fulfill their function.

In transformation: One transforms into six, becoming Water. Two transforms into seven, becoming Fire. Three transforms into eight, becoming Wood. Four transforms into nine, becoming Metal. Five transforms into ten, becoming Earth.

Heaven and Earth are the greatest of oppositions. Next, the Five Phases each form their own oppositions. This is the *Yi* of exchange: Fire opposes Water, Metal opposes Wood. In opposition, there are two—this is the fixed form. In flow, there is circulation, and in circulation, there is one—this is the *Yi* of change.

The Five Phases generate one another: Water, Wood, Fire, Earth, Metal, and Metal again generates Water. In their opposition, each is half a body.

In circulation, all are complete bodies. In opposition and flow, each has its own sequence.

Speaking of a single *qi*: when exhalation reaches its extreme, it turns to waning; when waning reaches its extreme, it turns to exhalation. Speaking of two *qi*: when yang reaches its extreme, yin arises; when yin reaches its extreme, yang arises.

Speaking of the four seasons: that which is overcome becomes husband and wife; that which is generated becomes mother and child. The husband takes a wife, and the husband’s blood and *qi* daily diminish—thus summer transforms into autumn. The mother bears a child, and the child’s blood and *qi* daily flourish—thus winter changes into spring.

Speaking of the Five Phases: *wu* and *ji* inquire among the ten Celestial Stems; *chen*, *xu*, *chou*, and *wei* inquire among the twelve Earthly Branches. The Center mediates between the four directions; *kun* inquires between *li* and *dui*. Fire generates Earth, and Earth generates Metal.

Master Cheng said: Yang transforms and yin unites, first giving rise to Water and Fire. Water and Fire are *qi*. Flowing and flickering, their forms remain insubstantial, their shapes not yet fixed. Next arise Metal and Wood, which are firmly established in fixed forms.

Heaven and Earth generate beings, first the light and pure, then the heavy and turbid. Among the Five Phases, Water and Fire are the lightest and purest; Metal and Wood follow, while Earth is the heaviest and most turbid.

Master Zhang said: Water and Fire are *qi*. Thus Fire flames upward and Water moistens downward, ascending and descending with yin and yang. Earth cannot restrain them. Wood as a thing: when Water is clear, Fire is born, yet they do not part.

Master Zhu said: *Zheng Meng* states only that the substantial forms of Metal and Wood belong to Earth, while Water and Fire do not belong to Earth. Someone asked: Since Fire arises attached to Water, does it not also belong to Earth? He replied: Fire is a thing of empty space. Asked: Is warmth and heat merely *qi* and thus Fire? He replied: Yes. The darkness within Fire is the yin within *li*. The clarity within Water is the yang within *kan*. Within Fire there is blackness—yin Water within yang. That which is black outside and bright inside is yang within yin. Thus Water may be called yang, and Fire may be called yin.

Master Zhang further said: When yang is submerged in yin, it becomes Water; when it clings to yin, it becomes Fire. Wood and Metal are the blossoms and fruits of Earth, their natures mingled with Water and Fire. Thus when Wood is steeped in Water, Fire is born, yet they do not part—further proof of Earth’s floating blossom at the intersection of Water and Fire. Metal obtains Fire’s essence from Earth’s dryness and Water’s essence from Water’s moisture. Thus Water and Fire sustain each other without harm; when smelted, they flow back without depletion—further proof of Earth’s essential solidity at the juncture of Water and Fire.

Huang of Mianzhai said: Examining the initial generation of humans and things, Heaven’s one generates Water, and Water at once takes form. When humans are born, essence and blood unite to form the body, just as in creation there is Water. Earth’s two generates Fire, and Fire at once has *qi*. When humans have this body, they can produce sound. Sound is the work of *qi*, just as in creation there is Fire. Water is yin and Fire is yang; appearance also belongs to yin, while speech also belongs to yang. Though Water and Fire have form and substance, they arise at creation’s beginning. Thus Water only moistens, and Fire only flames—yet their forms remain light and pure. When Heaven’s three generates Wood and Earth’s four generates Metal, their forms and substances are fully complete, just as when the human body’s ears and eyes are formed, the human shape is complete. Wood is yang and Metal is yin, just as seeing is yang and hearing is yin.

At creation’s beginning, Heaven’s one generates Water, and three generates Wood; Earth’s two generates Fire, and four generates Metal. Heaven obtains one odd number and generates Water; one’s extreme is three, thus three generates Wood. Earth obtains two even numbers and generates Fire; two’s extreme is four, thus four generates Metal. The completion of Water at six resembles *kan*, with one yang residing within—Heaven’s one generates Water. Earth’s six envelops externally, with yang scarce and yin abundant, and Water begins to flourish and form. The completion of Fire at seven resembles *li*, with one yin residing within—Earth’s two generates Fire. Heaven’s seven envelops externally, with yin scarce and yang abundant, and Fire begins to flourish and form.

Someone asked: If one generates Water but Water is not yet complete, and two generates Fire but Fire is not yet complete, must one wait until the Five Phases are all present before six and seven complete Water and Fire? If so, creation would never be whole. Rather, the *qi* of Water and Fire are one moist and one dry. When Water’s moisture reaches its extreme, Wood is born; when Fire’s dryness reaches its extreme, Metal is born. The beginning of humans and things is merely the gathering of essence and *qi*. Essence is moist and *qi* is dry; essence sinks while *qi* rises. Thus essence becomes appearance, and *qi* becomes speech. When essence flourishes, moisture reaches its extreme—thus it becomes the eyes, the liver, and sight. When *qi* flourishes, dryness reaches its extreme—thus it becomes Metal, the lungs, and hearing. Generally, appearance and sight belong to essence, so when essence wanes, the eyes dim. Speech and hearing belong to *qi*, so when *qi* is obstructed, the ears grow deaf. This is clearly evident.

Physicians assign the ears to the kidneys and the lungs to Metal. Compared with this arrangement, there are differences.

Liu of Yunzhuang said: Yin and yang are mutually rooted. Water resides in the *zi* position, the direction of extreme yin, yet yang is already born at *zi*. Fire resides in the *wu* position, the direction of extreme yang, yet yin is already born at *wu*. Wood, born from Heaven’s three, belongs entirely to yang, thus its movement in spring also belongs to yang. Metal, born from Earth’s four, belongs entirely to yin, thus its movement in autumn also belongs to yin. One cannot speak of them in terms of yin-yang mutuality, for Water and Fire have not yet parted from *qi*. At the beginning of yin-yang union, their *qi* naturally possesses the wonder of mutual rooting. Wood is yang’s expansion, and Metal is yin’s contraction—they have fixed substances. This is why they differ from Water and Fire.

Master Zhu said: The *Music* tones are Earth, Metal, Wood, Water, and Fire; the *Hong Fan* sequence is Water, Fire, Wood, Metal, and Earth.

The *Music* distinguishes the five tones as nineteen, eighteen, seventeen, and sixteen. The *Hong Fan* divides the four directions as one, two, three, four, and five.

It is further said: The *Hong Fan* speaks of one, two, three, four, and five, but does not mention six, seven, eight, nine, or ten. The *Monthly Ordinances* speaks of eight, seven, five, nine, and six, but does not mention one, two, three, four, or ten. The *Great Mystery* speaks of three, eight, four, nine, two, seven, one, six, and five, but does not mention ten.

The *Hong Fan* orders them as Water, Fire, Wood, Metal, and Earth; Yu orders them as Water, Fire, Metal, Wood, and Earth; Liu Xiang and Ban Gu order them as Wood, Fire, Earth, Metal, and Water; Fan Ye orders them as Wood, Metal, Fire, Earth, and Water.

It is also said: The five phases possess substance in Earth, while their *qi* moves in Heaven. Cai Jiufeng said: Flowing through the four seasons in Heaven, they are called the five phases; on Earth, where humans draw upon them, they are called the five materials. Tang scholar Kong said: The five phases are ordered from the subtle to the manifest. Water is the most subtle, thus ranked one; Fire gradually manifests, thus ranked two; Wood takes form and substance, thus ranked three; Metal is solid and firm, thus ranked four; Earth is vast and substantial, thus ranked five. This is verified by the *Hong Fan*.

Cai said: Moistening, flaming, bending-straightening, transforming, and sowing-reaping—these describe their natures by function and their virtues by capacity. Water moistens and flows downward; Fire flames and rises upward; Wood bends and straightens; Metal obeys and transforms; Earth encompasses the four directions and possesses the virtue of generation. Thus, its emphasis lies in sowing and reaping, for the people regard food as heaven.

Feng said: Sowing is *jia* (planting), and reaping is *se* (harvesting). Cai’s commentary speaks of *jia* and *se* together to express their ceaseless generation.

It is further said: Salty, bitter, sour, pungent, and sweet—these are the flavors of the five phases. The five phases possess sound, color, *qi*, and flavor, but only flavor is mentioned here because it is most essential to human use. The five flavors are based on the five grains. According to the *Monthly Ordinances*, the correspondence of the five grains to the five phases is: wheat to Wood, millet to Fire, broomcorn millet to Earth, hemp to Metal, and beans to Water. In terms of the five livestock: goose to Wood, sheep to Fire, ox to Earth, dog to Metal, and pig to Water. In terms of the five fruits: chestnut to Water, plum to Wood, apricot to Fire, peach to Metal, and jujube to Earth. All these possess substance in Earth and are indispensable to human sustenance, not to be lacking even for a day. When this *qi* circulates in Heaven, it becomes the five phases; when this substance congeals on Earth, it becomes the five materials.

Heaven has the sun, thus Earth has Fire. Heaven has the moon, thus Earth has Water. Heaven has rain, sunshine, cold, warmth, and wind, thus Earth has Water, Fire, Wood, Metal, and Earth. From Heaven to Earth, and from Earth to Heaven, filling the cosmos—what is not of the five phases? Moreover, humans are born with the finest *qi* of the five phases; who does not rely on the five phases for use?

Shaozi said: Fire is born from non-being, Water from being. Fire is dark within and bright without, thus *li* (Fire) has yang on the outside. Fire’s function lies in its exterior. Water is dark without and bright within, thus *kan* (Water) has yang on the inside. Water’s function lies in its interior. Fire functions externally, symbolized by the eye; Water functions internally, symbolized by the ear. The interior is the body, the exterior is function. Water’s exterior is its body, its interior is function. Yang is function.

Master Zhu said: Yin takes yang as its substance, and yang takes yin as its substance. Water is bright within and dark without; Fire is dark within and bright without. The essence of yin and yang mutually abides in their dwellings.

Xishan’s Master Zhen said: The sun and Fire are the outer illumination; Metal and Water are the inner illumination—this originates from the *Huainanzi*. The clear and bright belong to the inner illumination of Metal and Water. The turbid and bright belong to the outer illumination of the sun and Fire. Some say: The sun and Fire radiate light externally, thus the sun may be eclipsed and Fire may be extinguished. Metal and Water conceal their light internally, thus they are inexhaustible. By this, one may withdraw the gaze and reverse the hearing, concealing the spirit without display. This principle is wondrous and may be used for nurturing life. Yet in matters of governance, it is irrelevant. How can it compare to we Confucians, who, when it is time to be bright, act as *li* (Fire), and when it is time to be obscure, act as *mingyi* (Darkening)?

It is further said: Fire and the sun emit light externally, able to extend and bestow without limit. Metal and Water emit light internally, able to receive and adapt, each according to its capacity, to spirit and form. Is this not the Dao of Heaven and Earth?

Master Zhang said: Fire relies on Wood, and Wood generates Water; spirit rides *qi* and generates essence. In the prior Heaven, spirit generates essence—one gives birth to two. In the posterior Heaven, essence gathers spirit—two gives birth to one.

I have pondered this and say: Water, rooted in yang within yin, is thus lesser yang, aging from spring to summer. Fire, rooted in yin within yang, is thus lesser yin, aging from autumn to winter. *Qian* and *Kun* possess the bodies of *kan* and *li*. *Qian*’s nine-two exchanges with *Kun* to form *kan*. Within *kan*, one yang is born at *zi*, becoming true Water—this is the true yang within yin. *Kun*’s six-two exchanges with *Qian* to form *li*. Within *li*, one yin is born at *wu*, becoming true Fire—this is the true yin within yang. Two are many, one is few—*kan* is one, *li* is two. The few govern, thus the many serve. The few govern, thus *kan* belongs to winter. The years, months, days, and hours it generates, from *zi* to *si*, are all yang. The many serve, thus *li* belongs to summer. The years, months, days, and hours it generates, from *wu* to *hai*, are all yin. Between Heaven and Earth, nowhere is not the function of Water and Fire. Thus, *Qian* and *Kun* entrust their true natures to *kan* and *li*, and *kan* and *li* serve *Qian* and *Kun*.

It is further said: Clouds differ as Water, Fire, Earth, and Stone, and the types of Earth are likewise varied.

Master Zhang said: Water, Fire, Earth, and Stone are the bodies of Earth; all things possess Earth’s body.

The Master said: Water rains as drizzle, Fire rains as dew, Earth rains as mist, and Stone rains as hail.

Water’s wind is cool, Fire’s wind is hot, Earth’s wind is mild, and Stone’s wind is biting.
Water’s clouds are black, Fire’s clouds are red, Earth’s clouds are yellow, and Stone’s clouds are white.
Water’s thunder is dark, Fire’s thunder is rumbling, Earth’s thunder is rolling, and Stone’s thunder is crashing.
Thus, a single thing necessarily connects to the Four Images.

Heaven has the sun, moon, stars, and constellations; Earth has Water, Fire, Earth, and Stone.
The head has eyes, ears, mouth, and nose; the body has bones, flesh, blood, and marrow.
Marrow is Fire, blood is Water, flesh is Earth, and bones are Stone—these are the Four Images of living beings.
Sap is Water, blossoms are Fire, branches are Earth, and roots are Stone—these are the Four Images of plants.
Every person’s exhalations—blowing, sniffling, sighing, and breathing—respond to Heaven’s winds, rains, clouds, mists, and thunders.

The *Yi* has yang’s steadfastness and yin’s steadfastness.
Zhu Hanshang said: *Qian* is steadfast in the eleventh month, *zi*, and moves left, governing six yang periods.
Yin is steadfast in the sixth month, *wei*, and moves right, governing six yin periods.
Yang’s steadfastness takes the yang hexagrams: *Qian* gives birth to the three sons—*Zhen*, *Kan*, and *Gen*.
Yin’s steadfastness takes the yin hexagrams: *Kun* gives birth to the three daughters—*Xun*, *Li*, and *Dui*.
*Qian*’s first yang arises at *zi*, beginning at *Kan* and ending at *Li* as *Qian* concludes.
*Kun*’s first yin arises at *wu*, but yin does not dare occupy the *wu* position, so it retreats one period to be steadfast at *wei* and ends at *Kan* as *Kun* concludes.
Yang moves left, governing six periods in alternation.
Yin moves right, likewise governing six periods in alternation.
Following the completion of the year’s work, once the two steadfastnesses of yin and yang are established, one may then deduce the solar terms of the four seasons.

In my humble study of Kong’s commentary, numbers originate from yin and yang.
Yin and yang move to and fro along the sun’s path.
At the winter solstice, the sun reaches its southern extreme: yang advances as yin retreats.
Winter is Water’s position. The first yang arises as Water, its number one.
Thus, *Qian* is steadfast in the eleventh month, *zi*, and moves left.
At the summer solstice, the sun reaches its northern extreme: yin advances as yang retreats.
Summer is Fire’s position. It should take one yin as Fire’s number, but yin is not called an odd number, so it is paired.
Thus, in the sixth month, the second yin arises as Fire, its number two, and *Kun* is steadfast at *wei* and moves right.
From the winter solstice to the summer solstice, yang advances.
Thus, the first month is established at *yin*. The third yang arises as Wood, its number three.
From the summer solstice to the winter solstice, yin advances.
Thus, the eighth month is established at *you*. The fourth yin arises as Metal, its number four.
The third month is established at *chen*, occupying the first of the four seasons, as Earth’s position.
The fifth yang arises as Earth, its number five.

Water lies in the north, following the qi of flourishing yin, so that which moistens and descends follows yin.
Fire lies in the south, following the qi of flourishing yang, so that which flames upward follows yang.
Wood in the east and Metal in the west are balanced between yin and yang: the soft can bend and straighten, the hard can be shaped and transformed.
Earth contains and nourishes the myriad things, serving as the master of the Five Phases.

Master Zhu said: Jing Fang deduced many principles from divination.
His theory states: The *Great Expansion* is fifty, but one is not used, for Heaven’s generative qi, intending to empty and then fill, employs forty-nine.
Ten days, twelve branches, and twenty-eight constellations together make fifty.
One is not used; here, only the phrase “generative qi” is taken as the explanation.
The one is Heaven’s generative qi.
In my humble opinion: Heaven’s number does not use five, for one emerges from five.
The number of the *Great Expansion* does not use one, for one is Heaven’s spirit.
When one enters the north, it transforms *Kun* into *Kan* and generates Water, its number one.
When one enters the south, it transforms *Qian* into *Li* and generates Fire, its number two.
When one enters the east, Heaven first joins Earth and generates Wood, forming *Zhen*, its number three.
When one enters the west, yin delights in yang and generates Metal, forming *Dui*, its number four.
In the end, one returns and is stored in five, becoming the root of the myriad transformations.
The one that is preserved and not used is the generative qi.
This is the beginning of the *Tai Chu* qi.

Fifty-five retains five as the root of the Five Phases.
The *Great Expansion* of fifty retains one as the root of the Supreme Ultimate.
From the Supreme Ultimate to the Five Phases, one divides into five, and five divides into ten.
The forty-nine are the detailed numbers of the Five Phases.
Fifty is five times ten.
Ten is two times five.
Two is one.
One is Water.
The beginning of the Five Phases, the emergence of generative qi.
Ten is Earth.
The end of the Five Phases, the return of generative qi.

Thus, the ten days, twelve branches, and twenty-eight constellations are dispersed in Heaven.
Three hundred sixty are the degrees, dispersed on Earth.
Ten thousand one thousand five hundred twenty are the full numbers of the myriad things.
The one that is not used retreats into secrecy.
Thus, when Wood flourishes, Water retreats; when Fire flourishes, Wood retreats; when Earth flourishes, Fire retreats; when Metal flourishes, Earth retreats; when Water flourishes, Metal retreats.
These fifty-five, by retreating five and not using them, eliminate the surplus of the *Great Expansion*.
Fifty, by retreating one and not using it, empties the root number of the *Great Expansion*.
Thus it is said: Restrain its fullness and emptiness. The ten myriad things originate from five, and five originates from one. Heaven and Earth’s fullness and emptiness ebb and flow with time.
Heaven transforms one into four, and the four have form, but one has no form.
This one in humans is the heart; in virtue, it is benevolence; in the *Yi*, it is *Qian*; in time, it is spring.
Nowhere does it not serve as Heaven’s generative qi—how could the forty-nine stalks rely solely on this for their spirit?
Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things all depend on this as the ground of their existence.
Thus it is said: The one that is not used is the ancestral root of all that is used.

Master Zhu said: There are the Five Phases of the Eight Trigrams—Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, Earth—and the Five Phases of the Five Phases—Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, Earth.

The *Qian* Metal is the Metal of the *Yi* trigrams. The *Dui* Metal is the Metal of the Five Phases. *Xun* Wood is taken as a symbolic representation within the trigrams, while *Zhen* Wood is the Wood of the eastern direction. Earth is lodged in the four seasons to reign, hence only the four seasons are spoken of. Master Kong of the Tang said: The Five Phases, excluding Earth, are also called the Four Symbols—Metal, Wood, Water, Fire. Earth governs *Zhen* Wood, *Li* Fire, *Dui* Metal, and *Kan* Water, each presiding over one season. Furthermore, *Xun* shares the same Wood as *Zhen*, *Qian* shares the same Metal as *Dui*, and with the addition of *Kun* Earth, they are also called the seven, eight, nine, and six. In individual things, they are Wood, Fire, Metal, Water; in humans, they are benevolence, righteousness, propriety, wisdom; in directions, they are east, west, south, north; in the *Yi*, they are *yuan*, *heng*, *li*, *zhen*; in symbols, they are dragon, tiger, quail, tortoise; in the moon, they are first quarter, full moon, last quarter, new moon; in the day, they are dawn, day, dusk, night; in the head, they are eyes, ears, nose, mouth; in the body, they are bones, flesh, blood, marrow. Principle has these four aspects, and *qi* also has these four aspects. In every affair and every thing, they cannot be separated. Spring, with *zi*, *chou*, and *yin*, is Lesser Yang; summer, with *mao*, *chen*, and *si*, is Greater Yang; autumn, with *wu*, *wei*, and *shen*, is Lesser Yin; winter, with *you*, *xu*, and *hai*, is Greater Yin.

Shao Bowen said: Water, fire, earth, and stone are the fundamental substances, and the Five Phases reside within them. Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, and Earth are the manifestations of their function, from which the Five Materials emerge. Metal is extracted from stone, and Wood grows from earth. Only after stone exists does Metal come forth; only after earth exists does Wood arise. The Four Symbols and Four Bodies belong to the Prior Heaven. The Five Phases belong to the Posterior Heaven. He also said: The principles of the world are simply one giving rise to two. The *Yi* speaks in terms of eight and stops, while the *Hong Fan* speaks in terms of ten and stops. The trigrams are eight, but the numbers reach ten—eight is the number of yin and yang, while ten is the number of the Five Phases. One yin and one yang make two. Two multiplied by two makes four, and four multiplied by four makes eight. The Five Phases are fundamentally five, yet there are ten because one encompasses two. Wood encompasses *jia* and *yi*; Fire encompasses *bing* and *ding*; Earth encompasses *wu* and *ji*; Metal encompasses *geng* and *xin*; Water encompasses *ren* and *gui*. He also said: *Jia* and *yi* are two Woods, *bing* and *ding* are two Fires, *wu* and *ji* are two Earths, and Metal and Water are likewise. Combining the Three Powers and doubling them results in the mutual overcoming of yin-yang and the Five Phases, which is how they complete one another, and the virtues of the Five Constants are likewise. Nothing is greater than benevolence, the virtue of Wood. If benevolence errs by being too weak, it is cut by righteousness. Righteousness is the virtue of Metal. If righteousness errs by being too rigid, it is moderated by propriety. Propriety is the virtue of Fire. If propriety errs by being too restrictive, it is made flexible by wisdom. Wisdom is the virtue of Water. If wisdom errs by being too cunning, it is corrected by sincerity. The virtues of the Five Constants are the principle of the Five Phases overcoming one another. Old Su’s explanation is correct. Metal overcomes Wood, the hard triumphs over the soft. Water overcomes Fire, the soft triumphs over the hard.

He also said: There are those that mutually generate. Master Wang said: When Water is said to moisten, Fire is dry; when Earth is said to receive Wood, Metal’s restraint can be known. When Fire is said to flame, Water is cold, and Earth’s steaming, Wood’s warmth, and Metal’s clarity can be known. When Water is said to descend and Fire to ascend, Wood’s left, Metal’s right, and Earth’s center can be known. When Wood is said to be curved and straight, Earth’s roundness, Metal’s squareness, Fire’s sharpness, and Water’s levelness can be known. When Metal is said to be malleable and transformative, Wood’s change, Earth’s transformation, Water’s compliance, and Fire’s alteration can be known. When Earth is said to sow and reap, wells and ditches, cooking with Fire, implements of Wood and Metal can be known. What is meant by Wood’s change? It becomes bright as Fire and mottled as Earth. What is meant by Earth’s transformation? It can dry and moisten, spread and restrain. What is meant by Water’s compliance? It becomes sweet when sweet, bitter when bitter, green when green, white when white. What is meant by Fire’s alteration? It transforms the raw into the cooked, the soft into the hard, the hard into the soft. Metal can also transform—it can be round, level, sharp, curved, or straight. Yet without Fire’s alteration, it cannot transform itself, hence it is called malleable and transformative.

He also said: Those who obtain the essence of the Five Phases become humans—one speaks only of the Five Phases, not of yin and yang. To become human, one must have the Five Phases. The Five Phases are one yin and yang; apart from the Five Phases, there is no place to seek yin and yang. For example, *jia* Wood is yang, and *yi* Wood is yin. Master Zhu said: Numbers are merely the calculations of *qi*’s seasonal nodes; in general, they are simply one yin and yang. Dispersed, they become the Five Phases, and each of the Five Phases has its own yin and yang. *Jia* and *yi* are Wood, *bing* and *ding* are Fire; spring Wood and summer Fire, years, months, days, and hours—none are beyond the *qi* of the Five Phases. *Jia*, *yi*, *bing*, *ding* are also yin and yang, merely the *qi* of two and five. Humans are born when they encounter this *qi*, which varies unevenly, determining nobility or baseness, longevity or early death. When sages and worthies are above, their *qi* is harmonious. Otherwise, the *qi* is biased. Among the Five Phases, some obtain clear *qi*—they are intelligent but lack fortune and emolument. Some obtain turbid *qi*—they have fortune and emolument but lack wisdom. All is determined by their *qi* and fate. Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, Wu, the Zhou, and the Duke of Shao obtained the correct *qi*; Confucius, Mencius, and the Qi and Lu schools obtained the biased *qi*. During the Five Dynasties, when chaos was extreme, many sages and worthies were born, as in the time of our ancestors. It was like waking from a deep sleep, refreshed and full of spirit.

The Five Phases have principle, *qi*, and number, and they operate within the ten celestial stems and twelve earthly branches. Their numbers can be calculated: Heaven One and Earth Six combine to generate and complete Water, residing in the north. *Zi* is number one, *hai* is number six; *ren* is yang Water, one, and *gui* is yin Water, six. Earth Two and Heaven Seven combine to generate and complete Fire, residing in the south. *Si* is number two, *wu* is number seven; *bing* is yang Fire, seven, and *ding* is yin Fire, two. Heaven Three and Earth Eight generate and complete Wood, residing in the east.

Yin is number three, *mao* is number eight; *jia* is yang Wood, three, and *yi* is yin Wood, eight.

As for Earth, its position is at the center, where Five and Ten combine to generate and complete Earth. *Wu* and the branches *chen* and *xu* align with Ten and belong to yang, while *ji* and the branches *chou*, *wei*, and *si* also align with Ten but belong to yin.

In the west, the Tiger’s position completes Metal: the stem *geng* and the branch *shen* are number nine and belong to yang, while the stem *xin* and the branch *you* are number four and belong to yin.

It is also said: “One yin, one yang—this is called the Dao.” The Dao cannot operate without number, and number cannot be established without the Dao. Arranged among the ten celestial stems, it is thus; distributed among the twelve earthly branches, it is also thus. Within yin there is yang, and within yang there is yin; within the odd there is the even, and within the even there is the odd. Above, it enters the azure heavens; below, it enters the Yellow Springs. Vast, it contains the primordial *qi*; minute, it penetrates the boundless. None can escape this number.

The Six Classics speak of the Five Phases: the *Monthly Ordinances* records the numbers of mutual generation in the four seasons, while the *Yi and Shu* documents the numbers of mutual conquest in the Six Treasuries. As for the numbers of Heaven and Earth’s generation and completion, only the *Book of Changes* and the *Great Plan* discuss them. The legacy of Jichou and the lineage of Confucius both derived their teachings from the tradition of the River Chart and Luo Scripture.

*Tianyuan Fawei*, Scroll Five, concludes.
*Tianyuan Fawei*, Scroll Six.

Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai, collated by Fang Hui Wangu of Xugu, and proofread by Wanli

*On Observing Images*
The *Book of Changes* states: "The Two Modes generate the Four Images." It further says: "The Four Images are for demonstration." From Wang Fuxi to Cheng Yi, their explanations differ in many ways. Only Master Shao transmitted the learning of the Primordial Heaven, and at a single glance, its sequence could dispel a millennium of confusion. Master Zhu’s *Initiation into Learning* elucidates this most thoroughly, though some may not yet fully grasp it. I say: If you comprehend those eight characters within, then this entire scroll becomes but a fishing net and rabbit snare—what need is there for further words? Let those with insight inscribe this.

The *Book of Changes* speaks of the heavenly numbers as five and the earthly numbers as five. Retain one as the root and employ four for transformation. Applied to divination, these become the days of seven, eight, nine, and six. Applied to earth, they become water, fire, soil, and stone. Applied to observing celestial images, they become the sun, moon, stars, and constellations. Within the hexagrams, they divide into eight. The difference lies merely in the specific referents.

Master Shao said: "Of all things, none are greater than Heaven and Earth. The vastness of Heaven is exhausted by yin and yang; the vastness of Earth is exhausted by hardness and softness. Yin and yang exhaust themselves to form the four seasons; hardness and softness exhaust themselves to form the four directions. Qian is the yang thing; Kun is the yin thing. Heaven and Earth are but the greatest of things. Heaven is nothing more than the waxing and waning of one yin and one yang, the alternation of one cold and one heat to form the four seasons—this is the Dao of Heaven. Earth is nothing more than the interweaving of one hardness and one softness, the alternation of one level and one perilous terrain to form the four directions—this is the principle of Earth. Manifested in Heaven as the Four Images, arrayed in the year as the four seasons, shaped on Earth as the four bodies, and distributed in the directions as the four quarters, all are brought forth by the two things of Qian-yang and Kun-yin."

Master Cheng said: "The Four Images refer to yin, yang, hardness, and softness. Yin and yang generate Heaven; hardness and softness generate Earth."

Master Zhu said: "Master Shao’s explanation of numbers also begins with one yin and one yang. He divides yin and yang into greater and lesser, forming the Four Images of Qian. He divides hardness and softness into greater and lesser, forming the Four Images of Earth. Yin intermingles with yang, and yang intermingles with yin, generating the Four Images of Heaven. The so-called Four Images are: Heaven possesses yin and yang, and within these, it further divides into Greater Yang, Greater Yin, Lesser Yang, and Lesser Yin. The Four Images of Heaven are the sun, moon, stars, and constellations. Earth possesses hardness and softness, and within these, it further divides into Greater Hardness, Greater Softness, Lesser Hardness, and Lesser Softness. The Four Images of Earth are water, fire, soil, and stone. In truth, originally there was only one Supreme Ultimate. One divides into two, two into four, and four into eight. Heaven receives one set of four, and Earth receives one set of four. Each has one Supreme Ultimate operating within it, which is merely the doubling of the Five Phases."

The sun and moon constitute the *Changes*. The character *yi* (易) is composed of "sun" above as yang and "moon" below as yin. Thus, Zhuangzi said: "The *Changes* is for guiding yin and yang."

Master Shao said: "Greater Yang is the sun; Greater Yin is the moon; Lesser Yang is the stars; Lesser Yin is the constellations. The sun, moon, stars, and constellations intermingle to exhaust the body of Heaven. Greater Softness is water; Greater Hardness is fire; Lesser Softness is soil; Lesser Hardness is stone. Water, fire, soil, and stone intermingle to exhaust the body of Earth."

Shao Botian said: "*The Supreme Principle Governing the World* abandons metal, wood, water, fire, and soil, using instead water, fire, soil, and stone—why? The sun, moon, stars, and constellations are the Four Images of Heaven; water, fire, soil, and stone are the Four Bodies of Earth. Metal, wood, water, fire, and soil are called the Five Phases. The Four Images and Four Bodies belong to the Primordial Heaven; the Five Phases belong to the Manifested Heaven. The Primordial Heaven is that from which the Manifested Heaven emerges. Water, fire, soil, and stone are that from which the Five Phases emerge. Metal arises from stone; wood grows from soil. The Five Phases operate between Heaven and Earth, and within them lie water, fire, soil, and stone. *The Supreme Principle Governing the World* uses water, fire, soil, and stone to represent the fundamental body; the *Great Plan* uses water, fire, wood, metal, and soil to represent their application."

The sun is the essence of yang. In the *Primordial Heaven Diagram*, Qian represents the sun, and Qian’s position is due south. The moon is the essence of yin. In the *Primordial Heaven Diagram*, Dui represents the moon, and Dui’s position is southeast. Lesser Yang as stars belongs to Li, positioned due east. Lesser Yin as constellations belongs to Zhen, positioned northeast. Greater Softness as water resembles the moon. In the *Primordial Heaven Diagram*, Kun represents water, positioned due north. Greater Hardness as fire resembles the sun. In the *Primordial Heaven Diagram*, Gen represents fire, positioned northwest. Lesser Softness as soil resembles the constellations. In the *Primordial Heaven Diagram*, Kan represents soil, positioned due west. Lesser Hardness as stone resembles the stars. In the *Primordial Heaven Diagram*, Xun represents stone, positioned southwest.

The sun is yang; the moon is yin; the stars are hard; the constellations are soft—Heaven contains Earth. Water is yin; fire is yang; soil is soft; stone is hard—Earth contains Heaven. In Heaven, it forms images as the sun; on Earth, it forms shapes as fire. Fire and the sun are fundamentally one body, thus the yang-burning mirror obtains fire from the sun. In Heaven, it forms images as the moon; on Earth, it forms shapes as water. Water and the moon are fundamentally one body, thus the square vessel obtains water from the moon. In Heaven, it forms images as stars; on Earth, it forms shapes as stone. Stone and stars are fundamentally one body, thus tradition speaks of stars falling as stone. In Heaven, it forms images as constellations; on Earth, it forms shapes as soil. The constellations and soil are fundamentally one body, thus beyond the sun, moon, stars, and constellations, what is lofty and azure is all constellations. Beyond water, fire, soil, and stone, what is vast and boundless is all soil.

Now, the sun, moon, stars, and constellations are like a person’s ears, eyes, mouth, and nose. Water, fire, soil, and stone are like a person’s blood, qi, bones, and flesh. Thus, they are called the body of Heaven and Earth.

Yin and yang, hardness and softness—these are like a person’s spirit, the very source that gives rise to ears, eyes, mouth, nose, blood, qi, bones, and flesh.

It is further said: The sun brings heat; the moon brings cold. The stars bring day; the constellations bring night. When heat and cold, day and night intermingle, all the transformations of Heaven are complete. Water brings rain; fire brings wind; soil brings dew; stone brings thunder. When rain, wind, dew, and thunder intermingle, all the transformations of Earth are complete.

Heat alters the nature of things; cold alters their dispositions; day alters their forms; night alters their bodies. When nature, disposition, form, and body intermingle, all the responses of the moving and the growing are complete. Rain transforms the creatures that run; wind transforms those that fly; dew transforms the grasses; thunder transforms the trees. When the running, flying, grasses, and trees intermingle, all the correspondences of the moving and the growing are complete.

The sun, moon, stars, and constellations transform through heat, cold, day, and night. Water, fire, soil, and stone transform through rain, wind, dew, and thunder. Heat, cold, day, and night are Heaven’s transformations, which initiate the response of Earth. Rain, wind, dew, and thunder are Earth’s transformations, which harmonize with Heaven. Only after this initiation and response do things come into being.

Heat, cold, day, and night transform the nature, disposition, form, and body of things. Rain, wind, dew, and thunder transform the running, flying, grasses, and trees. Nature, disposition, form, and body originate from Heaven and are stimulated by Earth. The running, flying, grasses, and trees originate from Earth and respond to Heaven. Only after this stimulation and response are things brought to completion.

This initiation and response, this stimulation and response—such is the Dao of Heaven and Earth, the essence of the myriad things.

Heaven belongs to yang; Earth belongs to yin. Yang pertains to the moving; yin pertains to the growing. Within yang, the yang aspect soars upward in flight; within yin, the yang aspect runs downward. Heaven possesses the purest essence; Earth possesses the most refined essence. Among humans, the enlightened are the wise; among flying creatures, the phoenix and the luan; among running creatures, the qilin; among shelled creatures, the tortoise and the dragon; among grasses, the lingzhi and the orchid; among trees, the pine and the cypress; among stones, gold and jade.

Heaven also possesses the most extreme violence; Earth also possesses the deepest obscurity. Among humans, the malevolent become omens; among flying creatures, the owl and the shrike; among running creatures, the tiger and the wolf; among shelled creatures, the venomous snake; among grasses, the poisonous herbs; among trees, the parasitic mistletoe; among stones, the harsh and the brittle.

Where there is number, there are things; when number is exhausted, things come to an end. Where there are things, there is number; when things come to an end, number is exhausted. Heaven and Earth give birth to things, each distinct and different, and thus their mutual stimulation and intermingling give rise to transformation.

Master Shao said: The sun begins with one; the moon begins with two; the year begins with three; the constellations begin with four. One is the primordial unity; two is the twelve celestial meetings; three is the three hundred sixty days; four is the four thousand three hundred twenty hours—this refers to a single year.

It is further said: The sun follows Heaven in its turning; the moon follows the sun in its movement; the stars appear following the moon. Thus, the stars follow the law of the moon; the moon follows the law of the sun; the sun follows the law of Heaven. Heaven is half bright and half dark; the sun is half full and half waning; the moon is half full and half deficient; the stars are half moving and half still—this is the principle of yin and yang.

Though the sun moves to the right, it still turns left with Heaven. Though the moon moves swiftly, it meets the sun and conjoins with it, always remaining behind. The stars follow the moon because they appear at night.

What is called the Dao consists of the alternation of yin and yang. Heaven follows the Dao, thus it is half bright and half dark. The sun follows Heaven, thus it is half full and half waning. The moon follows the sun, thus it is half full and half deficient. The stars follow the moon, thus they are half moving and half still.

Where there is one, there is also two; solitary yin or solitary yang cannot stand alone. The sun’s half-full, half-waning state means it is full when in the yang degrees and waning when in the yin degrees. The stars’ half-moving, half-still state means they move when among the wandering stars and are still when among the fixed stars.

Master Cai said: The sun is the regular celestial cycles; the moon determines the new and full moons; the stars are the fixed and wandering stars. The constellations are the twelve celestial divisions where the sun and moon conjoin.

It is further said: Heaven is constantly visible day and night. The sun appears by day, the moon by night, but each is half invisible. The stars are half visible at night—this is the hierarchy of nobility and baseness. Though Heaven is half dark and half bright, it is constantly visible day and night. The sun is in the sky during the day, while the moon is beneath the earth at night, thus it is half invisible. The stars are even less visible than the moon—this is the distinction of nobility and baseness. The superior encompasses the inferior; the great contains the small. The stars are half visible because the five planets and the twenty-eight lunar lodges appear in alternation.

It is further said: The moon can be seen by day, thus it is yin within yang. The stars can be seen by night, thus they are yang within yin. In the *Xiantian* system, the sun, moon, stars, and constellations correspond to Qian, Dui, Li, and Zhen. The sun is yang within yang; the moon is yin within yang; the stars are yang within yin; the constellations are yin within yin. The moon can be seen by day, thus it is yin within yang. The stars can be seen by night, thus they are yang within yin. The stars also follow the moon, thus they are interwoven and mutually employed. The constellations are invisible, thus they are yin within yin. The constellations are Heaven, upon which the sun, moon, and stars are borne. Though the constellations are invisible, Heaven is visible day and night, thus the constellations are not used as one but are the foundation of what is used.

Master Zhu said: The Two Modes begin with one day to divide yin and yang and the Four Images. Next comes two days to divide the greater and lesser. The Two Modes are one line for yin and one line for yang. Adding one odd and one even line above each yields two lines. Four is called the Four Images, whose positions are: Greater Yang as one, Lesser Yin as two, Lesser Yang as three, Greater Yin as four. Their numbers are: Greater Yang as nine, Lesser Yin as eight, Lesser Yang as seven, Greater Yin as six. Thus, one, two, three, four contain nine, eight, seven, six.

According to the *River Chart*, six is one added to five; seven is two added to five; eight is three added to five; nine is four added to five. According to the *Luo Writing*, nine is the remainder of one part in ten; eight is the remainder of two parts in ten; six is the remainder of four parts in ten.

Master Zhou Dunyi referred to them as Water, Fire, Wood, and Metal. Master Shao Yong spoke of dividing two into four, where above the Four Images, each generates one odd and one even line. Thus, three lines yield eight, and with the Three Powers thus outlined, the eight trigrams emerge in name. Their positions are: Qian as one, Dui as two, Li as three, Zhen as four, Xun as five, Kan as six, Gen as seven, and Kun as eight.

Some have questioned the Four Images in the *Book of Changes*. Kong Yingda of the Tang identified them as Metal, Wood, Water, and Fire, while Zhuang of the Song saw them as the Image of Substance, the Image of Meaning, and the Image of Function. Those who speak of seven, eight, nine, and six do not directly equate them with yin and yang in their elder and younger forms. Master Zhang Zai said: the Dragon, Tiger, Quail, and Tortoise—Qian’s four virtues correspond to the four seasons. Zhu Zifa remarked: the four cardinal directions and four intercardinal points—none of the Confucians regarded these as the Four Images of Heaven. Yet the Master’s lines seem to differ from Master Zhu Xi’s sequence in drawing the trigrams.

This humble one says: Master Zhu Xi’s words are rooted in Master Shao Yong’s teachings on the Primordial Heaven, though earlier scholars did not elaborate on this. The Primordial Heaven encompasses all, for the Four Images are present everywhere. In the *Book of Changes*, they are seven, eight, nine, and six; in Heaven, they are the sun, moon, stars, and constellations. Elder yin transforms into younger yang, and elder yang transforms into younger yin. Younger yin and younger yang do not transform—they await the transformation of elder yin and elder yang. Tracing this back to antiquity, one may discern the omens of fortune and misfortune, regret and humiliation, advance and retreat, preservation and demise.

The sun, as Greater Yang, undergoes the changes of cold and heat, day and night. The stars, as Lesser Yang, share the sun’s light but show no change in form. The moon, as Greater Yin, undergoes the changes of new moon, crescent, half-moon, and full moon. The constellations, as Lesser Yin, form the body of Heaven and show no change in shape. The celestial images in Heaven are the manifest line-images. The hexagrams and lines in the *Book of Changes* are the concealed celestial images. Fu Xi gazed upon the celestial images to draw the eight trigrams. The Master gazed upon the celestial patterns to understand the hidden and the manifest—all may be unified as one. Who would say that those who divine with the *Book of Changes* cannot divine the heavens? #1 Greater Yang.

The *Discourse on the Trigrams* states: "Li represents the sun and fire."

The Tang scholar Kong [Yingda] said: The sun moves by taking the southern quarter. The accumulated heat of yang qi generates fire. The essence of fire qi becomes the sun. Fire has no form, yet when it attaches to things, it takes form. Born of earth, it rules as one of the two sovereigns in the south. Its spirit in Heaven is the sun, and on earth, it is fire.

Hu [Yuan] said: The sun’s body is Li, yin in structure yet yang in function. Being yin in structure, its movement is unhurried; being yang in function, it shines brightly by day. The *Canon of Yao* states: "At the solstice, the day is longest; at the equinox, the night is shortest." This refers to the four cardinal days. The *Monthly Ordinances* states: "Its days are jia and yi; its days are bing and ding; its days are wu and ji; its days are geng and xin; its days are ren and gui." This refers to the days of the four seasons.

This fool says: When the sun is at *zi*, it rises at midnight. As it ascends, it turns toward life, and the world grows bright. When the sun is at *wu*, it begins to descend after noon. As it descends, it turns toward death, and all things become ghosts. The mechanism of ghosts and spirits lies solely in ascent and descent.

The *Yuanming Bao* states: The sun’s form is round, and when viewed from afar, it spans the heavens, corresponding to a thousand *li*. Thus, the royal domain mirrors it.

Master Cheng said: Heaven, earth, sun, and moon are one. The moon receives the sun’s light, yet the sun is not diminished. Nevertheless, the moon’s light is the sun’s light.

Master Shao said: Yang embracing yin becomes (the sun, yang within yang). Master Zhu said: "Ceaseless activity" describes the sun and moon, while "the coming and going of cold and heat" describes their function. Substance and function are inseparable; one cannot speak of them as prior or posterior.

Heaven envelops earth externally. Earth’s form is small, while the sun’s light is vast, illuminating from all four sides. When the moon is in the center of the sky, it receives the sun’s light and becomes full. When the moon is far from the sun, its light waxes; when near, its light wanes.

Zhu Hanshang said: Heaven and earth interact through Kan and Li. Speaking of the sun, when the sun descends, the moon ascends; speaking of the moon, when the sun conjoins, the moon unites; speaking of the year, when cold comes, heat departs—all are instances of *jiji* (Completion). Kan above and Li below represent the extremes of water and fire. When they revert to their origins, within the extreme of *jiji* lies *weiji* (Incompletion).

Han scholar Zhang Heng said: The sun is the ancestor of yang essence, accumulating to form the crow. It has three talons, for yang numbers are odd. The rooster belongs to *you*, yin within yang, and the central void of Li harbors fire qi. He also said: The moon is the center of yin essence, accumulating to form the hare. Yin numbers are even, and it belongs to *mao*. It is yang within yin, the fullness of Kan, where the water qi of Heavenly One resides.

Jin scholar Du Yu said: The sun’s substance is red, the moon’s substance is white. The *Yuanming Bao* states: The word for sun (*ri*) means "fullness." It is the great brilliance, the fullness of light. The sun is the image of the ruler; when he travels through a land governed by the Dao, it shines brightly. When the sun displays five colors, his governance is at peace.

Master Shao said: In summer, the sun follows the Dipper northward; in winter, it follows the Dipper southward. At the summer solstice, heat reaches its extreme, and the sun thereafter follows the Dipper north. At the winter solstice, cold reaches its extreme, and the sun thereafter follows the Dipper south.

This fool once pondered Master Shao’s words, wondering if they accord with the teachings of the *Tai Xuan*. Let me set them forth in order. It says: When the sun moves slightly south, all things die; when the sun grows strong, all things live. At the summer solstice, the sun is in *Jing* (Well); the sun reaches the southern limit, and the host of yin gradually grows, so all things turn toward death. After the winter solstice, the sun is in *Qianniu* (Oxherd); it returns to the northern path, and the host of yang gradually grows, so all things turn toward life.

The *Xuan* also says: When the Dipper moves slightly north, all things are empty; when the Dipper moves slightly south, all things are full. After the Beginning of Winter, the Dipper’s handle points to *hai*, and yin qi conceals things, so the day is empty. At the Beginning of Summer, it points to *si*, and yang qi brings forth things, so the day is full.

It further says: When the sun moves south, it travels leftward yet rotates rightward. When the Dipper moves south, it travels rightward yet rotates leftward. The sun moves rightward against the heavens. In spring, it begins its journey, passing through the seven stars from the west to the south, hence it is said to travel rightward. In autumn, it returns, passing through the seven stars from the east to the north, hence it is said to rotate leftward. The Dipper follows the heavens leftward. In spring, it points east, passing through three celestial markers to the south, beginning from the east, hence it is said to travel leftward. In autumn, it points west, passing through two celestial markers to the north, returning from the west, hence it is said to rotate rightward.

It further says: The winter solstice and the time after midnight are images of approaching the Mystery. At the winter solstice, the Dipper points to *zi*; at midnight, it aligns with *zi*, signifying progression without reaching the extreme, advance without arrival, emptiness without fullness. At this time, the celestial Dao moves leftward, and the Dipper’s handle follows the heavens leftward. Though the sun moves rightward against the heavens, whenever it meets the moon, it aligns with the Dipper’s handle. The *Xuan* hexagrams, from *Zhong* (Center) to *Ying* (Response), number forty-one, with the odd as primary. In one hundred eighty-two and a half days, the sun moves through them—this is yang moving within yang. Moving through yang degrees, it waxes until *si*, completing *Qian* and giving birth to things.

The summer solstice and the time after noon are images of receding from the Mystery. At the summer solstice, the Dipper points to *wu*; at noon, it aligns with *wu*, signifying progression to the extreme and then retreat, advance to the limit and then return, fullness and then diminution. At this time, the celestial Dao turns rightward, and the Dipper’s handle also follows the heavens rightward. Though the sun moves leftward against the heavens, whenever it meets the moon, it aligns with the Dipper’s rightward pointing. The *Xuan* hexagrams, from *Ying* (Welcome) to *Yang* (Nourishment), number forty, with yin and even numbers in operation. In one hundred eighty-two days, this is yin moving within yin. The sun moves through yin degrees, waning until *hai*, completing *Kun* and storing away all things.

South and north are the fixed positions of yin and yang, hence the sun serves as the warp. East and west are the flowing qi of yin and yang, hence the sun serves as the weft. In Heaven, the sun divides day and night in a single day, and south and north in a single year; for the myriad things, it divides life and death. #2

Thus it is said: when yang arrives, life begins; when yang departs, death follows. The annotation states: the preceding text accounts for three hundred sixty-four and a half days, and adding the two *zàn* of *qíshū* completes one day, totaling three hundred sixty-five days and twenty-three parts. The twenty parts within a single day yield three seasons, forming three hundred sixty-five and one-quarter degrees.

As the *Han shang* records: Liu Zhao states that the sun’s passage through the northern *lu* is winter, the western *lu* is spring, the southern *lu* is summer, and the eastern *lu* is autumn. Common discourse rightly accepts the southern and northern *lu*, but regarding the eastern and western *lu*, it is either mistaken or doubtful. The *Sui Treatise* states that the eastern *lu* is spring and the western *lu* is autumn. This requires further examination and cannot be conflated.

For south and north serve as the warp, with *zǐ* and *wǔ* in opposition. At winter solstice, the first yang arises at *zǐ*, where the numinous and the reasoned unite heaven and earth to initiate the year’s transformations. The sun, moon, stars, and Dipper all begin here. Thus it is said: traversing the northern *lu* is winter; traversing the southern *lu* is summer. Direction and season cannot be altered.

From this, heaven turns from north to east, rotating leftward to generate all things. The sun, however, moves from north to west, turning rightward to serve as the weft. In early spring, the sun resides in *Yingshi*, a northern constellation—this is the first month, *jiàn yín*, meeting at *Kèzī*’s *hài*. In mid-spring, the sun is in *Kui*, a western constellation—second month, *jiàn mǎo*, meeting at *Jiànglóu*’s *xū*. In late spring, the sun is in *Wèi*—third month, *jiàn chén*, meeting at *Dàliáng*’s *yǒu*. Weaving west to east, it is thus said: the sun’s passage through the western *lu* is called spring.

By the fourth month, *sì* aligns with *shēn*, meeting at *Shíshěn*. The sun traverses the southern *lu*, and at *wǔ*, the first yin arises, as creation gathers its work and restrains the myriad things. Here, the Way of Heaven turns from south to north, rotating rightward from the west to complete all things. The sun then moves from south to east, traveling leftward to serve as the weft. In early autumn, the sun is in *Yì*, where *shēn* aligns with *sì*, called *Chúnwěi*. In mid-autumn, the sun is in *Jiǎo*, where *yǒu* aligns with *chén*, called *Shòuxīng*. In late autumn, the sun is in *Fáng*, where *xū* aligns with *mǎo*, called *Dàhuǒ*. Weaving east to west, it is thus said: the sun’s passage through the eastern *lu* is called autumn.

The reason spring is not called the eastern *lu* and autumn not the western *lu* is to show that the warp of south and north cannot change, while the weft of east and west mutually complement one another.

The sun is yang, and when yang governs, it rises from east to midnight, advancing southward. As the sun progresses northward, daylight lengthens, and yang’s advance brings warmth and heat. When yin governs, it rises from summer solstice to noon, advancing northward. As the sun retreats southward, daylight shortens, and yin’s dominance brings cold and coolness. Between the two solstices, the Way is balanced and shadows align—here lie the spring and autumn equinoxes.

The sun has its central path, while the moon has its nine courses. At new moon, the *lu*’s passage nears its node, and deficiency arises, giving rise to cold and warmth. At winter solstice, the sun reaches its southern extreme, and the shadow lengthens; if it does not reach its extreme, warmth becomes harmful. At summer solstice, the sun reaches its northern extreme, and the shadow shortens; if it does not reach its extreme, cold becomes harmful.

Shao Yong said: Heaven’s movement creates day and night; the sun’s movement creates cold and heat. Summer is shallow, winter is deep—this is the union of heaven and earth. Leftward rotation and rightward movement are the union of heaven and sun. Heaven completes one revolution in a day. The sun moves one degree, carried by heaven’s turning, following it. In summer, it rises at *yín* and sets at *xū*; in winter, it rises at *chén* and sets at *shēn*; in spring and autumn, it rises at *mǎo* and sets at *yǒu*. Rising marks day, setting marks night—though dependent on the sun’s rising and setting, its movement is governed by heaven.

When the sun is beneath the earth, it is cold; when it is above, it is hot. Traversing the northern *lu* in winter brings cold, as the sun travels deep beneath the earth. Traversing the southern *lu* in summer brings heat, as the sun travels shallow beneath the earth. Traversing the western *lu* in spring and the eastern *lu* in autumn brings the mean of cold and heat.

The sun’s movement creates cold and heat. As the Way of Heaven turns south, it moves from deep to shallow; as it turns north, it moves from shallow to deep—this is the union of heaven and earth. Some say: in summer, the southern pole is upright; in winter, the southern pole is inclined, likening it to a person’s head to explain the shallow and deep—this fails to account for the sun’s ecliptic.

At summer solstice, the sun is at *wǔ* and aligns with *wǔ*; at winter solstice, the sun is at *zǐ* and aligns with *zǐ*, following heaven’s movement. Thus, depth and shallowness mark the union of heaven and earth. At winter solstice, the sun rises, and *Xīngjì* moves rightward as the sun shifts one degree. Heaven rotates leftward one revolution and passes ten degrees, while the sun traverses the six *jiǎ* and meets the Dipper—this is the union of heaven and sun.

The night of winter solstice is like the day of summer solstice; the night of summer solstice is like the day of winter solstice. Winter’s night is only like the day of spring and autumn, as daylight encroaches upon night by five *kè*. The sun rises at its usual time, yet dawn appears two and a half *kè* before its rising, and dusk lingers two and a half *kè* after its setting.

It is also said: rising in the east and setting in the west follows heaven’s movement. Summer in the north and winter in the south follow heaven’s union. Heaven completes one revolution, surpassing one star, corresponding to the sun’s movement. Spring aligns with *yǒu*, summer with *wǔ*, autumn with *mǎo*, and winter with *zǐ*, corresponding to the sun’s union.

At winter solstice midnight, *zǐ*, the sun rises at *Xīngjì*. The sun moves rightward one degree, and heaven shifts leftward one degree, so the sun remains at midnight. Thus, it rises in the east and sets in the west—this is heaven’s movement, not the sun’s.

In summer, the sun’s path follows the northernmost point; in winter, it follows the southernmost. When the sun is farthest north, it is closest to the pole, so shadows are short and days are long. When the sun is farthest south, it is farthest from the pole, so shadows are long and days are short. This follows heaven’s union.

The sun moves one degree each day, and heaven completes one revolution, surpassing one degree.

Each "degree" refers to a degree of the stars, thus corresponding to the sun’s movement. At winter solstice, the sun is at *zǐ*; at summer solstice, at *wǔ*; at spring equinox, at *yǒu*; and at autumn equinox, at *mǎo*—this is heaven’s shifting. At winter solstice midnight, the sun aligns with *zǐ*; at summer solstice noon, it aligns with *wǔ*; at the spring and autumn equinoxes, it aligns either with *yǒu* or *mǎo*. East and west alternate in their weft, forming the intersection of yin and yang in spring and summer—this corresponds to the sun’s union.

I have examined ancient records: at winter solstice, the sun is at the first degree of *Qiānniú*; at spring equinox, at the fourth degree of *Lóu*; at summer solstice, at the twenty-first degree of *Dōngjǐng*; and at autumn equinox, at the tenth degree of *Jiǎo*. The *Suí Treatise* states: when the sun is closest to the pole, shadows are short and days are long—this describes the summer solstice. When the sun follows the inner path and is nearest the pole, daylight is at its longest. An eight-foot gnomon casts a shadow of one foot and five inches. At spring equinox in *Lóu* and autumn equinox in *Jiǎo*, day and night are equal. Thus, an eight-foot gnomon casts a shadow of seven feet and five inches. The *Suí Treatise* further states: when the sun is farthest from the pole, shadows are long and days are short—this describes the winter solstice. When the sun follows the outer path and is farthest from the pole, daylight is at its shortest. An eight-foot gnomon casts a shadow of thirteen feet. Subtracting one foot and five inches leaves a remaining shadow of eleven feet and five inches—this is the shadow’s variation between winter and summer. Hence it is said: the sun has a central path. North to *Qiānniú*, closest to the pole, marks the summer solstice.
