South to *Qiānniú*, farthest from the pole, marks the winter solstice. *Jiǎo* in the east and *Lóu* in the west lie at a median distance from the pole. Thus, south to *Jiǎo* and east to *Lóu* mark the spring and autumn equinoxes.

Master Zhèng says: at spring equinox, the sun is in *Lóu*, the moon’s first quarter appears at *Dōngjǐng*, its full phase at *Jiǎo*, and its last quarter at *Qiānniú*. At autumn equinox, the sun is in *Jiǎo*, the moon’s first quarter appears at *Qiānniú*, its full phase at *Lóu*, and its last quarter at *Dōngjǐng*. This also determines whether the qi has arrived by observing the shadows. When the qi of spring, autumn, winter, and summer all arrive, the four seasons proceed in proper order. The solstices mark the sun’s true position. Thus, at the extremes of length and shortness, governance reaches its peak and the qi arrives. Winter has no excess yang, summer no hidden yin. At the solstices, when the midday shadow matches the expected measure, the year will be prosperous and the people harmonious. If it does not match, the year will be harsh and the people deceitful. When the ruler’s governance and teachings are proper, the four seasons’ shadows follow the expected measure.

If the shadow advances, there will be floods—meaning the shadow is longer than expected. When the sun’s path lies outside the ecliptic, the shadow lengthens. A long shadow indicates yin’s dominance, hence floods. If the shadow retreats, there will be drought—meaning the shadow is shorter than expected. When the sun’s path enters within the ecliptic, the shadow shortens. A short shadow indicates yang’s dominance, hence drought. An advance of one foot and two inches in the shadow foretells a lunar eclipse, for the moon’s number is twelve. A retreat of one foot and two inches foretells a solar eclipse, for the sun’s number is ten. Shadow advance is surplus; shadow retreat is deficiency—this also determines whether the qi has arrived by observing the shadows.

It is further said: when the sage observes the movements of the sun and moon and pairs them with *kǎn* and *lí*, the meaning of the Eight Trigrams becomes manifest. The *Appended Judgments* states: "Displaying images and revealing brightness, nothing is greater than the sun and moon." Master Zhū says: as in the *nà jiǎ* method, *kǎn* corresponds to *wù*, *lí* to *jǐ*. One line of *qián* belongs to *wù*, one line of *kūn* to *jǐ*. Only when *wù* remains with *jǐ* do *kǎn* and *lí* form. For *qián* and *kūn* are the great parents, while *kǎn* and *lí* are the lesser parents.

Hànshàng says: Yú observed the sun and moon suspended in heaven to form the images of the Eight Trigrams. At dusk and dawn, the *kǎn* image appears, water flowing to *wù*. At noon, the *lí* image appears, fire conjoining *jǐ* to complete *wù*. The position of *jǐ* earth is the central image, manifest in the midst of sun and moon mutually hastening to generate brightness. When the second and fifth lines of *qián* and *kūn* intersect, *kǎn* and *lí* are born, and the sun and moon emerge. On the third day, the *zhèn* image appears, the moon emerging at *gēng*. On the eighth day, the *duì* image appears, the moon visible at *dīng*. On the fifteenth day, the *qián* image appears, the moon full at *jiǎ* and *rén*. On the sixteenth day, the *xùn* image appears, the moon waning at *xīn*. On the twenty-third day, the *gèn* image appears, the moon diminishing at *bǐng*. On the thirtieth day, the *kūn* image appears, the moon extinguished at *yǐ*. Taken together, *kǎn* and *lí* are the sun and moon, generating brightness at *zhèn*, generating darkness at *xùn*, waxing at *duì*, waning at *gèn*, full at *qián*, and extinguished at *kūn*. The ten days from *jiǎ* to *guǐ* are contained within the Eight Trigrams.

The *Records* state: "Great brightness is born in the east, the moon is born in the west." The *Book of Songs* says: "The sun of the east, the moon of the east." The moon’s beginning is born in the west, its fullness in the east. The sun’s rising place is in the east. Before dawn, it has already traveled beneath the earth, merely not yet emerged above it. The ancients explained the character for "sun" as *shí* (real), and for "moon" as *quē* (deficient). The sun is always thus real. Heaven’s movement has discrepancies, and the sun, moon, and stars are slower, unable to keep pace. Only the sun’s movement is fixed and unchanging, hence it is called *shí* (real).

It is further said: heaven and earth’s great awakening lies in summer, while human spirit resides in the union of the heart. At noon, the sun follows heaven to the south; at midnight, it follows heaven to the north—this is the awakening and sleeping of a single day. In summer, the sun aligns with noon; in winter, it aligns with midnight—this is the awakening and sleeping of a year. Thus, in summer, the vast sky is *lí*, the trigram of all things meeting. The sun is heaven’s spirit. Human spirit: by day, it resides in the heart—this is summer; by night, in the kidneys—this is winter. Day corresponds to night, the hidden storehouse. The *Mysterious* states: "Conceal the heart in the abyss, and the spirit will not stray." This means dwelling the heart in the qi’s viscera to preserve the spirit. Heaven and earth’s union is three-tenths. At summer solstice’s day, the sun is seven-tenths above heaven; at winter solstice’s night, it is seven-tenths below the earth.

From the perspective of heaven, seven-tenths of the sun lies above the earth, while three-tenths lies below—thus the yang number reaches its fullness at seven. The sun’s path differs from heaven’s because it follows northern and southern courses.

It is also said: when the sun enters the earth, it forms the image of conjoined essence. A single night is like a year’s winter. Heaven’s spirit and earth’s numen—yang *hun* and yin *po*—at midnight of the winter solstice, from the first half of *zi* to the latter half of *zi*, meet and unite in the Palace of the Yellow Springs. This is how the primordial origin is gestated and the myriad things sprout. Thus it is said: man and woman unite their essence and qi to give birth to humans; the sun, through its yang essence, joins with earth’s qi to give birth to creatures. Know this, and you know the study of nature and destiny.

It is further said: the sun is heaven’s yang *hun*. Yang embraces yin to form the sun, using *hun* to govern *po*. Yin embraces yang to form the moon, using *po* to restrain *hun*.

Master Zhu said: the sun and moon emerge from water before ascending to heaven; when they set in the west, they enter water again. Someone once asked Master Zhu about the Hun Tian theory, saying: "Beyond heaven is water, which floats heaven and bears the earth." He replied: "There is no water beyond heaven; below the earth is water that bears it." Mencius said: "Water flows through the earth." Su Shi said: "There is nowhere beneath the earth that is not water." Know this, and you will understand that beneath the earth lies water, while beyond heaven is qi—this is clear.

Yang metal is the mother of water, and water is the mother of the myriad things. Heaven is but a single, purely firm and luminous qi. The earth lies level within it.

Each night, the sun of heaven circles once beneath the earth, passing through one degree. The qi of Heavenly One generates water, which benefits the myriad things. The sun, as the essence of yang, can also dry the water beneath the earth, preventing it from harming the myriad things. How could mere "soaking of scorched tails" ever drain it? The spirit of Heavenly One is the mother of qi. When clouds arise, waters surge, and mists swirl, the ceaseless revolution of day and night causes water droplets to flow forth. If water were to swell unchecked by the sun’s drying power, mountains would burst, and timely rains would descend upon heaven and earth. Yin and yang naturally contain abundant qi. When excessive, it pours down in torrents, drowning the people below in darkness and deluge.

Historical records speak of ten suns appearing in the time of Yao, though this is hardly credible. Yet heaven’s compassion for the people below is a valid principle. The sun, though fiery, regularly brightens the world, advancing and retreating in due measure. As the *Yi* says: "Brightness enters the earth and turns." Heaven abides in water, as in the hexagram *Xu*. The sun is yang, and water is yin; yin qi disperses without fail, and water qi dissipates without exception. When the sun emerges, it becomes day within the cosmos—this is the true course of heaven’s movement. Such is the intent of creation to uphold yang.

Thus, the celestial body is perfectly round, encircling the heavens like an inner enclosure. The moon, being water, waxes and wanes, for it is said that Heavenly One generates water. Each day, the sun ascends in heaven while simultaneously circling beneath the earth, as evidenced by the tides. In the hexagram *Ming Yi*, the sun enters beneath the earth, becoming night. How could there be a single day without the image of yang brightness?

The *Sui Dynasty’s Treatise on Astronomy* states: "The sun dries water to its utmost." It also says: "Heaven has two paths: the yellow and the red." Shen Cunzhong remarked: "Astronomers speak of ten colors to record the movements of the sun and moon." The celestial body is perfectly round, like two lids joined together. The celestial equator lies horizontally at their juncture. North of the equator, it resembles an inverted lid; south of it, like an outer enclosure or an upturned lid. Both use the center of the diagram as their pole. North of the celestial equator lies the North Pole; south of it, the South Pole.

Master Zhu said: "The ecliptic is where the two halves of the celestial sphere join, at the center of heaven." He also said: "The celestial equator lies precisely at heaven’s center, like the seam of a joined box. The ecliptic crosses transversely through the celestial equator." Further, he stated: "Half of the celestial equator lies within the ecliptic, and half outside. At two points east and west, they intersect, dividing the heavens into degrees. When they meet, it is at the crossroads of the ecliptic and celestial equator." At the full moon, the moon and sun face each other directly, as if one were at *zi* and the other at *wu*, sharing the same degree. For instance, if the moon is at *Bi* 11°, the sun is also at that degree, though they face north and south. The reason the sun is eclipsed at the new moon is that the moon is always below, and the sun above. When they meet, the moon obscures the sun from below. Thus, it is said: "At the full moon, the moon is eclipsed, for yin dares to contend with yang."

The sun has a central path, called the ecliptic. Some say: "The central earth of *wu* and *ji* corresponds to the stars, and the four seasons have four movements: spring west, autumn east, summer north, winter south—different from the sun’s path of spring east, autumn west, summer south, and winter north. During the four seasonal months of *chen*, *xu*, *chou*, and *wei*, all belong to earth, which is the true path of the ecliptic. At these times, the stars also belong to the ecliptic. When they return to their proper positions, the sun always follows the ecliptic."

Zheng’s commentary on the *Hong Fan* asks: "What does it mean for the four seasons to align with the ecliptic?" I say: "Between winter and spring, spring and summer, autumn and winter—these are the four seasonal months belonging to earth. Each season lends its power for eighteen days, totaling seventy-two days, ending between fire and metal. Their position is *kun*, in terms of time. The ecliptic lies at the center, also called the central path. Divided into four—green, red, white, and black—it becomes eight, plus one, making nine months, assisting the sun in generating and completing the myriad things."

Thus, the *Treatise on Astronomy* says: "The path the sun follows is called the ecliptic." The moon has nine paths: two black paths emerge north of the ecliptic; two red paths emerge south of it; two white paths emerge west of it; and two green paths emerge east of it. Together with the ecliptic, they make nine. At the *Li Chun* and *Chun Fen*, the moon travels east along the green path; at *Li Qiu* and *Qiu Fen*, it travels west along the white path; at *Li Dong* and *Dong Zhi*, it travels north along the black path; at *Li Xia* and *Xia Zhi*, it travels south along the red path. Yellow is the true color. It is also said: "Green and red emerge from the yang path; white and black emerge from the yin path." If the moon deviates from its proper course, traveling the yang path brings drought and wind, while traveling the yin path brings rain and water. Traveling the ecliptic is correct.

Thus, the *Xing Bei* says: "When a wise ruler is on the throne, the sun, moon, and five planets all follow the ecliptic." Though the moon’s paths—white, black, red, and green—differ, they never stray beyond the east, west, south, and north of the ecliptic. The sun travels the center of the ecliptic, with its inner part to the north, from *Kui* to *Jiao*, *hai* to *si*. The outer part is to the south, from *Jiao* and *Kang* to *Shi* and *Bi*, *chen* to *xu*. In the east, between *Jiao*, lies the Gate of Heaven; *Fang* is the Celestial Street. The ecliptic passes through it, where the seven luminaries travel.

Shaozi said: "The mutual eclipses of the sun and moon are the intersections of their cycles. When the sun faces the moon, the moon is eclipsed; when the moon obscures the sun, the sun is eclipsed—like water and fire overcoming each other. Thus, the noble employ wisdom, while the petty rely on force." When the sun and moon face each other, it is called the full moon; when they meet, it is called the new moon.

The solar eclipse occurs at the new moon, when the moon is near the sun and devoid of light, becoming the dark moon. Thus, when petty men draw close in familiarity, they often endanger their lord. The lunar eclipse occurs at the full moon, when the moon opposes the sun and is at its brightest—like petty men abroad, though powerful, ever in peril. Water overcoming water is direct concealment and conquest, the petty employing force. Fire overcoming water is separation by an intervening object, the noble employing wisdom.

The sun and moon meet twelve times in a year. When their twelve full-moon nodes intersect, an eclipse occurs; if they do not intersect, there is no eclipse. Thus, while the sun travels the ecliptic, the moon travels the nine paths, and there are times when they intersect yet no eclipse occurs. At the new moon, the sun and moon occupy the same degree. At the full moon, they are at their farthest and in opposition. At the first and last quarters, the sun and moon are near one and far three—such as when the sun is at *wu* and the moon at *mao* or *you*. At the new moon, though the sun and moon are in the same degree east and west, if the moon’s path lies far to the south or north of the sun’s, there is no eclipse. Even if they are close in the south or north, if the sun is on the inner path and the moon on the outer, there is no eclipse.

Master Zhu said: "Eclipses of the sun and moon are not their regular course, yet lunar eclipses are considered ordinary. Solar eclipses are deemed inauspicious because yin overcomes yang and obscures it—an intolerable reversal. Thus, the *Spring and Autumn Annals* records every solar eclipse but makes no note of lunar eclipses, for this very reason." The sun and moon meet twelve times in a year; when they meet, the moon’s light is entirely extinguished, becoming the dark moon. After the meeting, the moon’s light revives, becoming the new moon. Fifteen days before and after the new moon, the sun and moon are in opposition. When the moon’s light is full at the full moon, and the sun and moon are in opposition, occupying the same degree and path, the sun overpowers the moon, and the moon is eclipsed. At the dark and new moons, when the sun and moon conjoin, sharing the same degree east-west and the same path north-south, the moon obscures the sun, and the sun is eclipsed. These follow regular patterns.

Yet when the ruler cultivates virtue, employs the worthy, and removes the wicked, yang flourishes and yin cannot encroach, so the moon constantly avoids the sun. Though their paths may be irregular, eclipses that should occur do not. Otherwise, if virtuous governance is neglected, ministers betray their lord, petty men encroach upon the noble, and barbarians disrupt the central kingdom—yin flourishes while yang wanes, and eclipses that should not occur do, marking extraordinary omens.

Master Guliang records four kinds of solar eclipses: one where the sun is eclipsed at dusk, as in the third year of Duke Yin; one where the eclipse is total at the new moon, as the next day in the seventeenth year of Duke Xiang; one where the eclipse is total, as in the third year of Duke Xiang; and one where the eclipse occurs at night, as in the eighteenth year of Duke Zhuang. A day and night together make one day; if the sun is not eclipsed by day but is by night, the sun is below the earth, and its form is unseen. By the new moon, when the sun first appears, if its light is diminished, it is known that a nighttime eclipse occurred. Nighttime eclipses belong to the dark moon of the previous month. During a nighttime eclipse, the stars lose their light.

The *Book of Songs* says: "In the meeting of the tenth month." Master Zhu adopted Kong’s commentary in great detail, and I now excerpt it here: "On the new moon of *xinmao*, the sun was eclipsed." Ancient calendars and the *Zhou Bi* both state that the celestial sphere measures 365¼ degrees, with the sun and moon both moving rightward across the heavens. The sun travels one degree per day, while the moon travels 13⁷⁄₁₉ degrees per day. Thus, the sun is slow and the moon swift, and after slightly more than twenty-nine days, the moon completes a circuit, overtakes the sun, and meets it—this is called a node. Each month they meet, but the moon may be outside or inside the sun’s path, so eclipses do not always occur; they only happen when they meet. Moreover, the moon and sun must share the same path for an eclipse to occur—this was in the tenth month of the Zhou and the eighth month of the Xia.

When the sun and moon meet and a solar eclipse occurs, it is a sign of yin encroaching upon yang, a minister encroaching upon the ruler. That it is called a solar eclipse is because the moon eclipses it. He Xiu said: "It does not say the moon eclipses it because its form cannot be seen." Thus, it is ambiguously called "the sun was eclipsed." From *jia* to *gui* are the days; *jia* is firm and *yi* is yielding, with five firm and five yielding among them, the ten days all being stems, thus the sun is the ruler. From *zi* to *hai* are the ministers; *zi* is yang and *chou* is yin, with six yang and six yin among them, the twelve days all being branches, thus the hours are ministers. *Mao* corresponds to ministers, *xin* to the ruler, metal should overcome wood. Now ministers reverse and encroach upon the ruler—this is the five phases acting in opposition. With *xin* as king in autumn, ministers should rest and withdraw, yet they encroach upon the young ruler who should reign.

The celestial degrees of ancient and modern times are the same. Solar and lunar eclipses have no fixed time. Calendar makers calculate the nodes of the sun and moon’s meetings, generally setting a limit of slightly more than 173 days. The sun and moon each have their own paths across the heavens. Even when they meet at the new moon, their paths may be outer or inner. If the moon is first on the inner path, eclipses occur more frequently within the limit; if the moon is first on the outer path, eclipses occur less frequently even within the limit. Du Yu, observing these irregularities, said: "Though the sun and moon are moving bodies with measurable paths, they cannot avoid slight excesses and deficiencies. Thus, even when they meet, there may be no eclipse." Only on the day of the summer solstice do the noble take heed.

Yet eclipses have no fixed time; they may occur even in months other than the solstices and equinoxes. It is merely that at the two equinoxes, day and night are equal, resembling shared paths, while at the two solstices, length and shortness are at their extremes. If they coincide, it is named to convey meaning, not because it is necessarily so. In truth, all solar eclipses are anomalies. If the ruler reforms his faults and cultivates goodness, even in the month of the summer solstice, disaster can be averted. If he persists in evil and wrongdoing, even in the months of the solstices and equinoxes, misfortune will follow. How can the two solstices and two equinoxes alone be free from calamity?

When the radiant and great luminous Dao/Way shines upon the earth below, should it suddenly dim and vanish, turning day into night, nothing could be more monstrous or strange. Thus, there are established rites of drumming, offering silk, demoting the victorious, and removing music—all to emphasize celestial omens and warn the ruler. Yet the Way of Heaven is profound and distant, and its verifications may come only in time. Or perhaps human misfortune and transgression merely coincide with them. Therefore, the sage seizes upon these changes, often invoking the numinous to serve as admonitions. This allows the wise and discerning to grasp the profound intent of the ancient sages, while those of middling or lesser capacity may believe in omens and portents, thus fostering self-restraint.

Yet the numinous may aid teaching but cannot itself be the teaching. To rely solely on the numinous is to delude the masses; to discard it entirely is to harm what is fitting. Thus, its words seem both present and absent, its affairs both credible and doubtful—all to achieve a grand harmony.

Du Yu said: "The sun’s light, at the time of full moon, obscures the moon’s light, hence a lunar eclipse. When the sun and moon meet, the moon obscures the sun, hence a solar eclipse."

Su Shi said: "Confucius, in compiling the *Spring and Autumn Annals* over 242 years, recorded every celestial anomaly involving the sun and stars, yet never noted lunar eclipses. Commentators explain: the moon represents the way of the feudal lords and the image of the barbarians. When it wanes, it signifies the ruler of the Central Kingdom prevailing. Thus, it is not considered a disaster."

The diviner of Chuqiu in the *Zuozhuan* said: "The sun has ten divisions, corresponding to ten times and ten ranks. Noon corresponds to the king, the meal hour to the duke, dawn to the minister, cockcrow to the officer, midnight to the commoner, the time of settling to the charioteer, dusk to the servant, sunset to the subordinate, the afternoon to the attendant, and the time of dim light to the menial. The time of the corner sun at sunrise is omitted out of respect for the king and duke."

Du Yu of Jin said: "The time when the sun is at its zenith corresponds to the king; the meal hour to the duke; dawn to the minister; cockcrow to the officer; midnight to the commoner; the time of settling to the charioteer; dusk to the servant; sunset to the subordinate; the afternoon to the attendant; and the time of dim light to the menial. The corner sun at sunrise (mao) and the time of the hidden sun (si) are omitted—both times of sunrise (mao) and emergence (si)—out of reverence for the king and duke." This is akin to the five hexagrams of a month, where the first corresponds to the feudal lord, the second to the grandee, the third to the minister, the fourth to the duke, and the fifth to the ruler.

This fool says: *The Book of Changes* is a work that exalts the yang and suppresses the yin. The sun is the image of the ruler, thus it is revered and exalted. The Upper Canon concludes with thirty hexagrams, ending in *Li* (Fire). *Li* in the heavens is the sun, so all living beings look up to its light. The Lower Canon concludes with thirty-four hexagrams, ending in *Ji Ji* (After Completion), with *Li* present throughout. Without it, the people cannot live, for it sustains their daily needs.

*Qian* (Heaven) is the image of the ruler; *Kun* (Earth) comes to interact with *Qian* to form *Li*. This embodies the principle of ministers serving the ruler with loyalty. Thus, when *Li* ascends to the heavens, it becomes *Dayou* (Great Possession), where the yielding attains a noble position. If reversed, it descends to become *Tongren* (Fellowship with Men). When *Li* emerges above the earth, it becomes *Jin* (Progress), where the sun is received thrice in a day. If reversed, it enters the earth to become *Mingyi* (Darkening of the Light). All this serves to exalt the yang and suppress the yin, ever fearful that the brilliance of the yang might fail to rise in the heavens.

Its other manifestations scatter among the hexagrams, each fulfilling its function: as the cauldron for cooking, as the nobility of human culture, as the abundance of the sun at its zenith, or as the reform of the calendar and the reckoning of time. But in *Kui* (Opposition), *Shi* (Biting Through), *Lü* (The Wanderer), and *Jiaren* (The Family), *Li* is inverted. Its image is named "Great Yang," the primal source of all things. Even the moon and stars borrow its light to adorn the heavens—how much more so for the myriad beings?

King Wen was imprisoned at Youli and suffered the misfortune of *Mingyi* (Darkening of the Light). Yet he was able to expound the *Changes*, laying the foundation for the Ten Wings transmitted by Confucius. Thus, Confucius’ sun followed King Wen’s, succeeding one another to illuminate the world, establishing the constant order of day and night—was this not the great fortune of all under heaven and later generations?

*Tian Yuan Fa Wei*, Scroll Six, concludes.

**Tian Yuan Fa Wei, Scroll Seven**
*Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai; Collated by Fang Hui Wanli of Xugu*

**The Great Yin (Taiyin)**

*The Shuo Gua* states: "Kan represents water and the moon; the moon is the essence of water."

*The Hetu: Di Lan* says: "The essence of metal is Kan, with one yang residing within. Internally bright and externally dark, it becomes the moon in heaven and water on earth."

Hu Shi remarks: "The moon’s body is Kan, its yang employed by yin. Yang moves swiftly, while yin governs the night."

*The Liben Yi* states: "When the sun appears at dawn, it is called *na* (reception); when it appears at dusk, it is called *tiao* (leaping)."

*The Wu Xing Zhuan* says: "When the moon appears in the west at dusk, it is called *tiao*. When it appears in the east at dawn, it is called *ce* (sidelong). Concealment is also named *tiao-na*. When *tiao* occurs, the lords and kings relax; when *ce* or concealment occurs, they hasten."

*The Tai Xuan* commentary notes: "*Tiao* describes the appearance of change. *Tiao* means visibility. The moon appearing in the east at dawn is called *ce*; appearing in the west at dusk is called *tiao*. The sun is male, the moon female—they traverse the heavens without cease."

*The Sitian Kao* states: "When the moon departs in *tiao* or *pu*, it is adjusted according to the calendar. When the sun’s path involves *tiao-na*, it is applied with *han* (shouting)." It further says: "The sun’s path and moon’s departure determine the fixed numbers of *tiao-na*, with *tiao-han* and *na-jia* (addition)."

*The Zhou Pi* says: "The moon is born where the sun shines; its *po* (lunar essence) arises where the sun is obscured. When aligned with the sun, its light is full; when approaching the sun, its light is exhausted."

Jing Fang observes: "Though it has form, it has no light; only when illuminated by the sun does it shine. Where the sun shines, it is bright; where it does not, it remains dark. The moon is *que* (deficient)—when full, it wanes. In the *Xiantian* system, Qian is one and Dui is two; the moon begins from Dui because its motion falls short of the sun’s count."

Both the sun and moon experience waxing and waning. When the sun waxes and the moon wanes, the full moon comes after the midpoint and before the new moon. When the moon waxes and the sun wanes, the full moon comes before the midpoint and after the new moon.

Master Zhang states: "The moon is yin essence, the reverse of yang. Where the moon is positioned in yang, it receives the sun’s light but not its essence. At opposition and quadrature, its light is eclipsed—essence cannot be divided."

He further says: "The essences of the sun and moon mutually reside in each other’s domains."

Zhu Hanshang remarks: "*Tai* (Peace) signifies the union of heaven and earth, through which the myriad things communicate. *Pi* (Stagnation) means no union, no communication. *Tai* corresponds to Kan’s nine-five, where water qi ascends. Kan is the body of Kun, and the yang within Kan is two-five. Yang flows into yin, so the moon retreats swiftly. The moon’s body is not bright; it awaits the sun to shine. Brightness is Qian. Qian speaks of the sun and moon’s combined brilliance—Kan and Li mutually employed. *Yu* (Enthusiasm) speaks of the sun and moon not transgressing: Kan is the moon, Li the sun, and they meet in the north. *Heng* (Constancy) speaks of the sun and moon attaining heaven: Qian’s nine-four transforms into Li and Kan."

In *Xiaoxu*’s top nine, *Zhongfu*’s six-four, and *Guimei*’s six-five, the moon nears fullness. In *Xiaoxu*, four conceals Kan, and Xun conceals Zhen. The moon is in the east, the sun in the west—this is fullness. In *Fusong*, Kan transforms into Zhen: the moon in the east is also fullness. In *Guimei*’s six-five, the moon is in the east, the sun in the west—also fullness. In *Xiaoxu* and *Zhongfu*, Kan is in four; in *Guimei*’s six-five, the line is in five. When Kan is in four or five, it is centered. Thus, when the sun opposes the moon, the moon is eclipsed; when the moon obscures the sun, the sun is eclipsed—Kan and Li contend for dominance.

Someone asks: "Kan is water, Li is fire—how can fire cling to water?" The answer: "Li is not bright without water, having no place to attach. Kan is not bright without Li, having no source of generation. When water gathers, essence gathers; when essence gathers, spirit arises. Burning firewood produces charcoal; withered wood becomes ash. Rotten wood glows at night, but when moisture is exhausted, the light dims. Blood becomes *zoulin* (will-o’-the-wisp), seen on dark, rainy nights. Thus it is said: *Li* means *li* (attachment). When Kan’s water is exhausted, Li has nothing to cling to."

Hengqu states: "The sun, moon, and five planets move contrary to heaven while encompassing the earth. The moon is yin essence, the reverse of yang, so its rightward motion is swift. The sun’s substance is fundamentally yin; the moon’s substance is fundamentally yang. At the new and full moons, their essences and spirits reverse and exchange, causing eclipses and the laws of waxing and waning. The moon is closer to humans; the sun is distant. Thus, the moon receives the sun’s light always on its outer side. When viewed from earth, its beginning and end appear curved like a hook, and at its zenith, like a half-jade disk—proof of its waxing and waning. Metal and water have inner light, able to repel and receive the essences of yin and yang. Mutually residing in each other’s domains, each attains its proper place. Thus, the forms of the sun and moon remain unchanged through the ages."

Someone asked Master Cheng: "The moon has a fixed *po*, and the sun is farther than the moon. Does the moon’s reception of the sun’s light cause its waxing and waning as seen by humans?" He replied: "The sun and moon are one—how could the sun be higher than the moon? If the moon had no waxing and waning, how would the year be completed? When the moon gains one part of light, its *po* wanes by one part. The moon does not receive the sun’s light, hence the eclipse. When it does not receive the sun’s light, the moon is precisely aligned with yin opposing yang. Drumming assists yang—thus, the green of the sun and moon can both be drummed. The moon does not descend below the sun; when directly opposite, it eclipses the sun. A slight eclipse quickly recovers because it cannot overcome its constant nature. Between the sun and moon, there is a distinction of yin and yang, honor and humility. Near the ruler, its power diminishes; far from the ruler, its influence grows. Thus, when the moon is far from the sun, its light is full; when near, its brightness wanes. Before fullness, it rises in the west; after fullness, it rises in the east."

Yang Xiong’s *Tai Xuan Jing* states: "Before fullness, the moon carries its *po* in the west; after fullness, it completes its *po* in the east." This means that when the moon is newly born, the sun’s light is cast upon the *po*’s west, filling its east—only at fullness does it become round.

After fullness, the moon’s light fully occupies the east of its *po*, gradually waning in the west until it vanishes at the dark of the moon. The moon borrows the sun’s light to shine; before fullness, the sun lies to its right, and after fullness, the sun lies to its left—thus each receives light according to its position.

Master Zhu remarked that Yang Xiong’s two lines were mistakenly phrased. The *Book of Documents* states: “In the first month, on *renchen*, the *po* dies at its side.” It also says: “In the fourth month, the light begins to be born.” The *Record of Rites* states: “Three and five to fullness, three and five to waning.” Cai Jiufeng said: “The first month establishes *yin*; this is the first month of the year. It is not called the ‘proper’ month but the ‘first’ because the Shang dynasty established *chou*. Here, ‘the first month’ refers to the first month. The second day is the death of the *po*—the new moon. The *po* dying at its side is the second day. The first light of the moon appears on the third day.” The *Treatise on the Calendar* states: “When the moon is nearly new but not yet visible, the day is called the death of the *po*. When it forms light in the evening, it is called *chu*.” Master Chen said: “Some take the ‘beginning of light’ as the third day of the moon. Tang scholar Kong believed that both the birth of light and the death of the *po* occur at the start of the month—how could this be the same as the new moon? The moon’s light begins faintly; as light is born, the *po* dies. Thus, it is the first death of the *po*, for when the *po* dies, light is born. The first day of the moon is the birth of light; the second day is the *po* dying at its side. By the full moon, light is fully born and the *po* fully dead. From the day after fullness, the moon’s *po* begins to be born. As the *po* is born, light dies. By the dark of the moon, light is fully dead and the *po* fully born. Thus, the end of the month is called *hui* (darkness), for the *po* is entirely dark. The beginning of the month is called *shuo* (new moon), for its light is first seen. Observing the dark and new moons, Zheng Xuan’s interpretation should be considered correct. Various scholars take ‘the beginning of the *po*’ as the sixteenth day. Yet the *po* begins to be born the day after fullness, and fullness may fall on the fifteenth, sixteenth, or fourteenth day—it cannot be fixed. The sixteenth day is not necessarily the birth of the *po*.”

Master Kaoting said: “The length of the month is calculated as twenty-nine and twenty-nine four-hundred-ninetieths days. Observe when the new moon occurs. When the month is long, the third day sees the first light; when short, the second day. From the first light to the first quarter is fifteen days, and the full moon’s light is complete—this is the birth

Master Kaoting further stated: “From the last quarter to the dark moon, the moon and sun become indistinct. The moon lies behind the sun, its light entirely concealed, its body hidden. When the *po* overlays the sun, a solar eclipse occurs; when it lies behind the sun, there is no eclipse. At the dark and new moons, the sun and moon are conjoined.”

The *Chu Ci* asks: “What virtue does the night’s light possess, that in death it revives? What benefit does it seek, that the hare ever dwells within its belly?” This questions what virtue the moon holds to die and be reborn, and what advantage it gains by gazing back, that the hare constantly resides within it.

The answer states: “According to the old theories of calendar scholars, at the new moon the moon gradually moves away from the sun, so the *po* dies and brightness is born. After the full moon, it gradually approaches the sun, so the *po* is born and brightness dies. By the dark moon and new moon, it moves away from the sun again, and brightness is reborn. This is what is meant by ‘dying and reviving.’” This explanation is mistaken. If it were true, then before the full moon, the west would be near and the east far, and the newly born brightness should appear on the moon’s eastern side. After the full moon, the east would be near and the west far, and the not-yet-dead brightness should appear on the moon’s western side. How could it be that before the full moon the *po* is borne on the west, and after the full moon the *po* ends on the east, while brightness is distant from the sun? Thus, Shen Kuo’s explanation is the correct one.

He said: “The moon itself has no light; it is like a silver pellet, which shines only when illuminated by the sun. When the light first appears, the sun lies beside it, so the light is slanted, and what is seen is but a hook. As it moves farther away, the light shines obliquely and gradually fills out. Generally, it is like a pellet of nine measures, with powder applied to half: viewed from the side, the powdered part appears like a hook; viewed directly, it is perfectly round.”

Wang Pu of later times further developed this theory, saying: “On the evening when the moon’s brightness is born, only a hook is seen. When the sun and moon are in opposition, a person standing between them sees its fullness. Yet at the quarters and dark moon, it is no different from the full moon—people simply do not see it. From this, we know the moon’s light is always full; its waxing and waning arise from the observer’s position and perspective, not from death and revival.”

The moon is a yin essence, its form round and substance clear. When illuminated by the sun’s light, its brightness is seen; when not illuminated, it is but *po*. At the full moon, when the sun and moon are in opposition and a person stands between them, the moon’s brightness is fully visible, and its form is round. At the first and last quarters, the sun illuminates its side, and a person observes it from the side, so half is bright and half is *po*. On the day of the dark moon, the sun shines upon its outer surface, while a person stands within, so it is not seen.

He further said: “Within the moon’s brightness lies a dark void, its darkness exceedingly subtle. At the full moon, the moon and sun are directly opposite, without the slightest deviation. The moon is struck by the dark void, hence the eclipse. Though this is yin overcoming yang, it is ultimately inauspicious. If yin had the intention to retreat, it would not reach this point; it is their direct opposition that causes the eclipse.”

Zhang Heng of the Han said: “Fire emits light outward, while water contains reflections. The moon’s light arises from where the sun shines, and its *po* arises from where the sun is obscured. When facing the sun, its light is full; when approaching the sun, its light is exhausted. The stars are illuminated by its light, which is turned by water. When opposite the sun, its light is never fully united because it is obscured by the earth—this is called the dark void. When the stars or a faint moon encounter it, they cause the sun’s old eclipse. Its brightness: looking from darkness to light, the light is unobstructed, so it appears like fire. When at mid-heaven, heaven and earth share its brightness. Looking from brightness to union, union still has its奪 (seizure), so it appears like water. Fire blazes at night but is not bright in daylight.”

I say: “The theory of the dark void originates here. The so-called dark void is yin embracing yang to form the moon. *Kan* is the moon, and the single yang line within *Kan* is originally the single yin line within *Li*. *Qian* enters *Kun* to become the moon, and *Kun* enters *Qian* to become the sun. At the full moon, the sun and moon are in opposition, and the pure essences meet, so *Li* faces the sun with the body of *Qian*, seeking to absorb the single yang line within the moon—hence the moon is eclipsed. What is called the dark void is precisely the single yin line within *Li*. This is an evident matter, yet no one in ancient times ever spoke of it.”

He further said: “When the moon is near the sun, its light appears as a single crescent. As it moves farther away, its light gradually increases. For example, when the sun is at noon and the moon is at *you*, it is near one and far three, forming a quarter. At the full moon, the sun is in the west and the moon in the east; a person below gazes up and sees its light in full.”

The *Hun Yi Lüe Shuo* states: “The moon moves swiftly while the sun moves leisurely; it takes slightly more than twenty-nine days for them to meet. Thus, in one year they conjoin twelve times. Their conjunction forms the dark moon, and from the dark moon, revival begins—brightness is born anew. This is called the swift movement of the new moon, gradually distancing itself from the sun. In terms of the celestial sphere, its nearness to the sun is slightly more than ninety-one degrees, and its distance from the sun is slightly more than 374 degrees. This is what is meant by ‘near one, far three’ forming the quarter—this is the first quarter. As it moves farther and opposes the sun, the distance is slightly more than 182 degrees and 62 minutes—this is called mutual opposition. Dividing the center of heaven forms the full moon, for the sun and moon are in opposition. After passing the center, it is distant from the sun by slightly more than 274 degrees. This is what is meant by ‘near one, far three’ forming the quarter—this is the last quarter. The first quarter typically occurs on the eighth day, the last quarter on the twenty-second day, and the full moon on the fifteenth day—this is the norm. The first quarter may occasionally retreat to the seventh day or advance to the ninth; the last quarter may retreat to the twenty-first day or advance to the twenty-third. The full moon may retreat to the fourteenth day or advance to the sixteenth—these are its variations.”

The first quarter is when the moon waxes to half, resembling the upper string of a bow. The last quarter is when the moon wanes by half, resembling the lower string of a bow.

It is also said: The *Xiantian Diagram* depicts the phases of a month, progressing from *Fu* to *Zhen*. On the third day, the moon begins to brighten. By *Dui*, on the eighth day, the moon reaches its first quarter. On the fifteenth day, at *Qian*, it is the full moon. At *Xun*, the moon begins to wane, on the eighteenth day. At *Gen*, it is the last quarter, on the twenty-third day. At *Kun*, the moon becomes new, on the thirtieth day. In a single breath, there are the new moon, crescent, quarter, and full moon.

The first quarter is when qi begins to grow, descending from above. The last quarter is when qi begins to wane, ascending from below. The full moon is when qi reaches its fullness below, and the moon becomes round above. Between the new moon and the crescent, the sun and moon conjoin above—this is like "using water to extinguish fire" or "metal returning to its original nature."

The terms *jia*, *yi*, *bing*, *ding*, *geng*, and *xin* refer to the sun’s rising and setting at dusk and dawn, not to the division of the six trigrams’ directions.

It is further said: The phases—waxing brightness, waning darkness, quarters, full moon, new moon, and crescent—are all named according to their orientation toward or away from the sun. From the waxing brightness to the first quarter, as the sun sets in the west, the moon follows and begins to grow. The dark part generates brightness, facing downward to meet the sun, and light arises from below. By the time it reaches the midpoint, how could it not form the upper string, appearing before the full moon?

After the full moon until the last quarter, as the sun rises in the east, the moon follows and begins to wane. The bright part generates darkness, facing upward to distance itself from the sun. The light from before the full moon entirely transforms into darkness. Brightness retreats and dies from below, and when it reaches the quarter again, it becomes like metal and water. As before, it reaches the midpoint, how could it not form the lower string, appearing after the full moon?

It is also said: *Zhen* is one, *Dui* one, *Qian* three, *Xun* four, *Gen* five, and *Kun* six—each five days form a node. The beginning of the crescent marks the start of the moon’s activity, the initial interaction of the sun and moon’s yin and yang. After the twenty-sixth day, until the second day, it is *Kun*. When the sun moves to *Zhen*, the moon gains one part of light. At *Dui*, the moon gains two parts of light. At *Qian*, the moon’s entire body is illuminated.

After the sixteenth day until *Xun*, the moon’s light gradually wanes. By the time of the new moon and crescent, the sun and moon converge, and no light is visible. The sun and moon meet and separate at the new moon. The moon is obscured, revealing its dark side, so its yin body is fully visible—this is the new moon. Representing *Kun*, at this time the moon is in the *yi* direction, and the sun is in *yi*; since they conjoin, *Kun* corresponds to *yi*. When the moon reaches the new moon, it moves from east to north to meet the sun. East is *yi*; north is *gui*. *Yi* diminishes into *gui*, converging at *Qian* and *Ren*. *Ren* and *gui* are where the qi of the north gathers. After twelve meetings, all things flourish, and the moon reappears at *Zhen* and *Dui*.

Only after heaven and earth interact does *Zhen* arise. *Zhen* is the beginning of heaven and earth’s interaction and the flourishing of all things. When *Kun* ends at *yi* and *gui*, *Qian* begins at *Zhen* and *geng*. The full moon, new moon, and crescent follow one another endlessly.

In the first node, on the third day, it is pure yin. When the first yang begins to interact in the center, a single line of light appears, and the moon takes the form of *Zhen*. At this time, the moon is faintly visible in the west at the *geng* position, while the sun is just setting below the earth, so *Zhen* corresponds to *geng*. After six days, in the second node, two yangs generate brightness, half illuminating the dark part—this is the first quarter. The moon takes the form of *Dui*, faintly visible in the south at the *xin* position.

After another six days, in the middle of the third node, on the fifteenth day, it is full and pure yang, and the moon takes the form of *Qian*. It is faintly visible in the east at the *jia* position, receiving light as the sun aligns with the earth’s surface. After the full moon, at the beginning of the fourth node on the sixteenth day, the *Qian* body begins to receive the first yin, becoming *Xun* and generating darkness. The moon takes the form of *Xun*, setting in the west at the *xin* position as the sun aligns with it.

After another six days, in the middle of the fifth node, on the twenty-second day, one yang is restored. The first yin in the center forms *Gen*, and the moon takes the form of *Gen*, setting at dawn in the south at the *bing* position. On the thirtieth day, the sixth node ends, all three yangs transform, and the light is entirely extinguished as the moon’s body hides in the northwest. After the six nodes of a month are complete, they pass to the next month, and the *Zhen* trigram reappears.

Chapter Fourteen of the *Cantong Qi* states:
*Gen* aligns with the south at *bing*, the last quarter on the twenty-third day.
*Kun* at *yi* on the thirtieth day, in the northeast its light is lost.
When the nodes are exhausted, they pass and the body continues, the dragon is reborn thus.

Regarding the *Na Jia* system: The father *Qian* corresponds to *jia-zi* and *ren-wu*. *Zhen*, the eldest son, inherits the father’s body and corresponds to *geng-zi* and *geng-wu*—*zi* and *wu* are the same as *Qian*. The mother *Kun* corresponds to *yi-wei* and *gui-chou*. *Xun*, the eldest daughter, inherits the mother’s body and corresponds to *xin-chou* and *xin-wei*—*chou* and *wei* are the same as *Kun*. *Kan*, the middle son, corresponds to *wu-yin* and *wu-shen*. *Gen*, the youngest son, corresponds to *bing-xu* and *bing-chen*. *Li*, the middle daughter, corresponds to *ji-mao* and *ji-you*. *Dui*, the youngest daughter, corresponds to *ding-si* and *ding-hai*.

*Ren*, *gui*, *geng*, *xin*, *wu*, *ji*, *bing*, *ding*, *jia*, and *yi* proceed from below to above. *Qian* and *Kun* each use two, encompassing the six children within, enabling generation and transformation. *Wu* and *ji* are the central earth. *Kan* yang corresponds to *wu*, and *Li* yin corresponds to *ji*—yin and yang each reside in their proper place at the center. *Ren* is the essence of yang, *jia* the head of yang, *gui* the blood of yin, and *yi* the beginning of yin. The two elders envelop and conceal, with *Kan* and *Li* acting at the center—this is the divine work of heaven and earth’s creation.

Three hundred eighty-four lines form the number of a complete celestial cycle. Each line governs one day, and four trigrams govern twenty-four lines to correspond to the twenty-four qi.

A single month encompasses the crescent, full, waning, and new moons; a single day encompasses *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*. Speaking of one day, then one month and one year are all contained within it. From *zi* to *si*, light is born, and within this lies the first quarter leading to the full moon. From *wu* to *hai*, the lunar essence is born, and within this lies the last quarter returning to darkness. The twelve hours correspond to the twelve sovereign hexagrams, and the seventy-two lines correspond to the seventy-two seasonal nodes.

Speaking of one month, the two crescents above and below correspond to the two eights of yin and yang, and the three hundred sixty hours correspond to the three hundred sixty lines—further mirroring the three hundred sixty days. Speaking of one year, the six lines of the *na jia* system divide into the twelve sovereign hexagrams, doubled. The work of the year is thus completed, the labor of a full year condensed within a single month. Observing the year through the month, the month through the day, the day through the hours, and the hours through the *ke*—even within a single *ke* of effort, the qi and seasonal signs of an entire year are present. The subtlety of creation’s workings may be minutely examined thus.

Master Shao said: The moon embraces yang as yin—it is yang within yin. As yang wanes, yin arises, so the sun descends while the moon rises in the west. When yin flourishes, it contends with yang, so at the full moon, the moon rises in the east. Heaven is the father, the sun the child—thus heaven revolves leftward. The sun is the husband, the moon the wife—thus the sun rises in the east while the moon is born in the west. When the moon is distant from the sun, its light is born and its motion slows; when near, its essence is born and its motion quickens—this is the principle of ruler and minister.

Though the sun moves rightward, it follows heaven’s leftward turn. Though the moon moves swiftly, it meets the sun only after the sun has passed, ever trailing behind. That stars follow the moon is seen at night. According to various calendrical theories: from the first to the fourth day, the moon moves fastest, traversing over fourteen degrees in a day and night. From the twenty-fourth day to the new moon, it is again swift, moving at the same rate. From the fifth to the eighth day, its motion is next fastest, traversing over thirteen degrees daily. From the twentieth to the twenty-third day, it moves slightly faster, at the same rate. From the ninth to the nineteenth day, its motion slows, traversing over twelve degrees daily. Averaged over a month, it moves thirteen and seven-nineteenths degrees per day. Distant from the sun, its light is born and its motion slows; near, its essence is born and its motion quickens—here too lies the principle of ruler and minister.

The moon has no light of its own but borrows from the sun. When it waxes full, it contends with yang—how cautious must a ruler be! Heaven revolves leftward, the sun moves rightward. The sun rises in the east, the moon is born in the west—this is the principle of father and son, husband and wife. That the full moon also rises in the east is to contend with yang, an irregular path.

I offer this explanation: Heaven is greatest, the sun next, the moon next still. Thus the sun’s motion does not match heaven’s, nor the moon’s the sun’s. One exalted, one humble—each has its measure, and this is heaven’s teaching. King Wen expounded the *Yi* at *Kan*, speaking of practice through images, saying the moon has one yang at its center, encircled by two yins. In a year’s twelve solar terms, the moon always follows the sun and dares not precede yang—its meaning is profound. Yin assists yang to complete the year’s work, and the moon’s aid is indispensable. Yet when yin overreaches yang to encroach upon the noble, vigilance is all the more essential.

Thus in *Qian*, the text speaks of sun and moon, then adds "their light conjoined." In *Jia*, it speaks of sun and moon, then adds "not transgressing." In *Da*, it speaks of sun and moon, then adds "attaining heaven." Though sun and moon are mentioned together, the moon is never placed before the sun—all to convey the intent of exalting yang and restraining yin. Where the moon alone is taken as a warning, the meaning is especially urgent. In *Xiaoxu*, the moon nears fullness and danger arises, for it harbors hidden *Kan* and *Xun* contains hidden *Zhen*. The moon is in the east, the sun in the west. *Xiaoxu*’s single yin overreaches the yang of the ninth line—hence the omen is dire. In *Guimei*, the sixth line, the moon also nears fullness yet brings fortune, for *Kan* moon is in the east, *Li* sun in the west. Yin and yang reside at the center, and though sun and moon face each other, nearing fullness they do not contend—hence the omen is auspicious.

Thus the moon may govern night but not day—such is the Way of Heaven, how much more so for man! When the moon opposes the sun, a lunar eclipse occurs; when the moon obscures the sun, a solar eclipse occurs. Yin must not contend with yang, ministers must not overreach rulers, wives must not overreach husbands, the petty must not overreach the noble. Master Cheng said: A minister occupying the ruler’s position—Wang Mang and Dong Zhuo are examples—is still speakable. A woman occupying the ruler’s position—Nüwa and Empress Wu are examples—is an extraordinary aberration, unspeakable.

The *Han Annals* states: The moon revolves rightward, aligning with the *Xiantian* diagram’s eight trigrams. The *Treatise on Pitch Pipes and Calendars* says: The sun has dark and new moons, the moon has crescents and fullness. The sun is yang, the moon yin—yin qi is ever present, while yang is consumed and refined. After the new moon, the moon moves rightward, gradually departing the sun and light is born. Around the seventh or eighth day, it reaches the first quarter, half-illuminated. By the fifteenth day, the moon is farthest from the sun, fully bright. After the full moon, it gradually nears the sun’s left side, and brightness wanes. Around the twenty-second or twenty-third day, the moon is half-bright again—this is the last quarter. On the thirtieth day, sun and moon conjoin, the moon’s light consumed by the sun—this is the dark moon.

Among various theories, only this is credible. I have compared the hexagrams with the twelve earthly branches: on the dark moon, the hexagram is *Kun*, sun and moon meet at *zi*, and the moon’s light is extinguished. Between the dark and new moons, it revolves rightward from *Kun*, and the hexagram becomes *Gen*—yang is born, light appearing externally. Between *xu* and *hai*, it turns due west to the *Kan* hexagram—one yang resides at the center, far from the sun at mid-heaven—this is the first quarter. At midnight, it is at *you*; the sun rises in the east, illuminating the western half of the moon—hence it is said: "Within it is the cock."

You and *mao* stand opposite each other. Advancing further, the hexagram becomes *Xun*—two yang lines shine externally, while the lunar essence (*po*) remains only within. At midnight, it resides between *wei* and *shen*, midway between the first quarter and full moon. As the sun’s illumination grows more direct, the moon turns to *Qian* at noon, while the sun stands directly above or below *Kun* at *zi*, their opposition creating full illumination—hence its name: the full moon.

Then, turning left from *Li* at *wu* toward the interval between *chen* and *si*, what once faced *xu* and *hai* to generate brightness now generates the lunar essence. As the sun’s light shines obliquely, one yin arises externally, forming the *Dui* hexagram. *Dui* turns to *Li*, and the moon stands due east, half the sky distant from the sun; the lunar essence returns to the center—this is the last quarter. At midnight, it is at *mao*; the moon rises in the west, illuminating the eastern half—hence it is said: "Within it is the hare."

Proceeding to *Zhen*, it again stands opposite *Xun*. In *Xun*, one yin gradually wanes and the moon waxes full; in *Zhen*, two yin lines grow strong and the moon wanes. The sun draws nearer, brightness remaining only within; at midnight, it resides between *chou* and *yin*, midway between the last quarter and dark moon, until at *Kun* the moon is extinguished.

The sequence of hexagrams begins in the north and proceeds west because the moon’s motion retreats in degree and turns rightward. The sequence of lines proceeds from external to internal because the moon receives the sun’s light from without to within. This explanation accords well with the *Han Annals* and the *Xiantian* hexagrams.

Some say: the sun is measured at *wu*, the moon at *zi*, and the stellar lodges at dusk and dawn. The author of the *Cantong Qi* failed to grasp the moon’s retrograde motion and the principle of receiving light from without, thus seizing upon *Kan* and *Li* as central while taking *Zhen* as the birth of brightness and *Dui* as the first quarter—this is entirely backward. Though he overturned the explanations of earlier scholars and deemed it a revelation of the moon’s ancient secrets, I consider this but one interpretation within the *Book of Changes*. Yet Master Zhu’s investigations in the *Cantong Qi Kaozhu* were most precise. Moreover, what Wei Boyang transmitted has endured from the Han to the present for over a thousand years, and no former worthy has ever refuted it—how could one lightly dismiss it?

*Tianyuan Fawei*, Scroll Seven, concludes.

The Lesser Yang’s stellar phenomena are manifold and defy exhaustive exposition. Stars are the essence of primordial *qi*. Master Shao said: “The Lesser Yang gives rise to stars.” Zhang Lingxian stated: “The celestial offices within and beyond the central palace number one hundred twenty-four in constant brightness, with three hundred twenty named. The stars themselves total two thousand five hundred, while the faint stars number eleven thousand five hundred twenty.” Master Luo often remarked to me that astrologers, like the *aili* people, leave one utterly bewildered. To grasp the myriad forms of the cosmos, one need not look beyond the finger-signs of the Five Phases. From the Supreme Ultimate’s division into Yin and Yang, Yin and Yang unfold into the Five Phases. The Five Stars are the essence of the Five Phases. The sun, moon, and Five Stars constitute the Seven Governors. Scattered across the four directions, each direction has its seven lodges. Including the Northern Dipper at the center, there are five times seven—thirty-five names—all inseparable from the Five Phases. Thus, Ban Gu said: “The Supreme Ultimate moves the Three Luminaries and Five Stars above at the *shangyuan*; *qi* revolves the Three Sequences and Five Phases below among men. The imperial throne embodies the Three Powers and Five Affairs at the center, dividing into the Three Talents—what lies beyond the Five Phases?” Master Ouyang said: “Yao commanded Xi and He to observe the central stars and rectify the four seasons, keeping the Dao simple. Descending to later ages, the methods grew increasingly refined, accumulating the wisdom of many before attaining their utmost subtlety. During the Three Dynasties, lost texts lay scattered, and the Six Classics offered no confusion—yet the affairs of Heaven and man are hard to speak of. What remains today takes the Five Phases as its foundation, not daring to compare with the starry chronicles of history.”

Master Shao said: “The theory of the Five Stars began with Gan Gong and Shi Gong.” The Five Stars’ theory was unheard of in antiquity. The *Book of Yu* merely states: “They pacified the Five Luminaries.” By the time of Gan and Shi, it was fully revealed. Shi Shen was a man of Wei who composed the *Star Canon*. Gan De was his contemporary. Stars have three colors to distinguish the three schools’ differences: those from Shi appear red, those from Gan appear black, and those from Wu Xian appear yellow. The purple palace and all stars within and beyond it also derive from the three schools, totaling three hundred eighty-three names and seven hundred eighty-three stars. What is applied in the sphere-school are only the celestial pole, Northern Dipper, and twenty-eight lodges, essential for divination. The rest are recorded merely to complete the celestial body’s full image.

Master Zhang said: “The Five Wefts are the essence of the Five Stars.” Ban Gu said: “The Five Stars move eastward while the heavens turn west. The constant stars serve as the warp, the Five Stars as the weft. Interweaving warp and weft, they form celestial patterns.” The refined *qi* of the Five Phases, when forming shapes on earth, coalesce into wood, fire, soil, metal, and water. When forming images in the heavens, wood aligns with Jupiter in the east, fire with Mars in the south, metal with Venus in the west, water with Mercury in the north, and soil with Saturn at the center. In man, wood’s spirit is benevolence, associated with appearance; fire’s spirit is propriety, associated with vision; metal’s spirit is righteousness, associated with speech; water’s spirit is wisdom, associated with hearing; and soil’s spirit is trust, associated with thought and the heart. Distributed across the four seasons, wood flourishes in spring, fire in summer, metal in autumn, and water in winter, each dominating for seventy-two days. Soil flourishes in the four seasons—during the months of *chen*, *xu*, *chou*, and *wei*—for eighteen days each, totaling three hundred sixty. Their colors are: wood green, fire red, metal white, water black, and soil yellow. Their terrestrial correspondences each have their place, further varying in dominance, mutual generation, rest, and decline. Their colors differ: when dominant, they shine with radiance; when mutually generating, they are inwardly full; when at rest, their radiance lacks edges and does not sway; when in decline, their light diminishes in color. A white halo portends mourning; a red halo, war; a green halo, summer floods; a black halo, plagues and many deaths; a yellow halo, good fortune. White horns herald sounds of weeping; red horns, assaults on our cities; black horns, floods and exhausted armies.

The Grand Historian said: “When the Five Stars share the same color, the world lays down its arms, the people live in peace, the five grains flourish, spring winds and autumn rains come in season, winter is cold and summer hot, the sun does not eclipse at the new moon, nor the moon at the full. This is a state with the Dao, and a sage must occupy its throne.”

**Jupiter**—Wood’s nature is gentle and upright. Historians say it oversees the faults of the world’s rulers. It governs the year’s five grains, dividing the four seven lodges into twelve stations. It traverses one station per year, with the Great Year in *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*. At the four cardinal points, it moves through three lodges per year, with the Great Year in *yin*, *shen*, *si*, and *hai*. At the four initial and final points—*chen*, *xu*, *chou*, and *wei*—it moves through two lodges per year. Two times eight is sixteen, three times four is twelve, totaling twenty-eight lodges. Jupiter completes one celestial circuit in twelve years, forming one cycle. The Great Year is Yin, moving left in *yin*. Jupiter is Yang, turning right in *chou*. When the Great Year is in *mao*, Jupiter resides in *zi*; when in *chen*, Jupiter resides in *hai*, and so forth. Thus, when the Great Year is in *yin*, Jupiter first appears in the east in the first month; when in *mao*, it appears in the east at dawn in the second month. From this, the rest can be deduced. The land where Jupiter resides enjoys good fortune. If it deviates from its station, the opposite land suffers misfortune. Greater calamity follows greater deviation, lesser calamity follows lesser deviation, and no fault arises if it does not deviate.

Master Zhang said: “Wood embodies the waxing and waning of a year. The *chen* station is where it transitions each month, bearing the image of a year.”

**Mars**—Fire’s nature is fierce and violent, thus it governs the execution of laws. It regularly enters the Supreme Subtlety in the tenth month, receives restraint, and departs. The stellar lodges oversee the lawless; its movements are irregular, completing one celestial circuit every two years.

Master Zhang said: “Fire is Yin in substance yet Yang in its gathering. Its *qi* is akin to the sun’s, hence its slowness is twice that of the sun.”

The Fire Star moves from presence to absence, from absence to presence, governed by the transformative *qi* of Heaven and Earth.

**Saturn**—Earth’s nature is weighty and profound, residing in *wu-si* during late summer. Among the four seasons, it is like the heart in a person, governing the four limbs and all organs. Thus, when the four stars err, Saturn is the first to move. One account says: it governs the feminine image. Another says: it is the Star of the Son of Heaven. When the Son of Heaven loses trust, Saturn stirs greatly. It regularly advances one stellar lodge in the year of *jia-chen* at the origin of the Dipper, completing one celestial circuit every twenty-eight years. Where it lingers long, that land’s virtue is deep.

Master Zhang said: “Saturn’s motion is the slowest, yet it is not entirely bound to Earth.”

**Venus**—Metal’s nature is firm and unyielding, governing military affairs as a Yin star. When it emerges in the east, it should conceal itself in the east; when it emerges in the west, it should conceal itself in the west.

Ban said: “It regularly emerges in the east with Mars on *jia-yin* of the first month, remaining visible for two hundred forty days before withdrawing for forty days. It then reappears in the east, emerging at *yin* and *xu*, and withdrawing at *chou* and *wei*. Generally, it completes one celestial circuit each year, nearly matching the sun and moon.”

**Mercury**—Water’s nature is placid and mild, governing the gains and losses of form and law, and rectifying the four seasons. It regularly appears at *Kui* and *Lou* at the spring equinox, at *Dongjing* at the summer solstice, at *Jiao* and *Kang* at the autumn equinox, and at *Qianniu* at the winter solstice. It emerges at *chen* and *xu*, and withdraws at *chou* and *wei*. It is observed in the east at dawn and in the west at dusk. If it fails to appear for one season, that season is disharmonious. If it fails to appear for all four seasons, the realm suffers great famine. It also completes one celestial circuit each year.

Some say: Mercury is the Hour Star, with eleven hours in a season and twelve meetings in a month. Scattered between Heaven and Earth, it acts everywhere without exception. When its moisture and nourishment appear untimely, cold and heat lose their rhythm, thus it embodies the image of the Great One. For the Water and Fire Stars depend on each other: Fire may be present or absent, Water may be full or parched, both receiving the transformative *qi* of Heaven and Earth.

Master Zhang said: “Metal and Water cling to the sun, advancing and retreating before and after it. The principle is profound, residing in things, and its resonance may be known.”

The *Star Classic* says: “Venus and Mercury follow the sun in their course. When swift, they precede the sun; when slow, they trail it. When swift and preceding the sun, they appear at dusk in the west. When slow and trailing the sun, they appear at dawn in the east.”

The *Odes* commentary states: “Before the sun rises, it opens the way for the sun’s brightness, hence the Bright Star is called *Qiming* (Starter of Dawn). After the sun sets, it continues the sun’s light, hence the Bright Star is called *Chang Geng* (Long Twilight).”

The *Han Odes Outer Commentary* further says: “When Venus appears at dawn in the east, it is *Qiming*; when it appears at dusk in the west, it is *Chang Geng*.” It may be that Metal and Water, these two stars, can be spoken of interchangeably.

Sun Yan said: “The Bright Star is Venus. When it emerges in the east at dawn, three lodges high, it is called the Bright Star of today. When it emerges in the west at dusk, three lodges high, it is called Venus. Thus *Qiming* is Venus, but what star is *Chang Geng*? It may be that one star appears in both east and west under different names, or that the two are distinct stars—this cannot be determined with certainty.”

Master Zhu resolved it, saying: “*Chang Geng* is Mercury; *Qiming* is Venus. When Metal is west of the sun, as the sun is about to rise, it appears in the east. When Water is east of the sun, as the sun is about to set, it appears in the west. This saying exhausts the matter.”

Zhang Pingzi said: “When *Sheti* and Mars appear at dawn, they cling to the sun; when Venus and Mercury appear at dusk, they cling to the moon. This requires separate investigation.”

Master Cheng said: “The true primordial *qi* of Heaven and Earth converges within the celestial reckoning, so that the sun and moon form a *bi* (jade disk), and the five stars string like pearls—thus giving birth to the Sage. According to the calendar, at the origin of the primordial chaos of high antiquity, the year was named *Yanfeng Jia Shetige Yin*. At the *jiazi* new moon, midnight of the winter solstice, the sun, moon, and five stars all converged at *Zi*, hence the auspicious signs of *bi* and *lian zhu* (connected pearls) appeared, corresponding to the epoch when Emperor Zhuan established the calendar.

Again, in the first year of the Han dynasty, the ten-month month, the five stars gathered at *Dongjing*, serving as the omen of Emperor Gao’s receipt of the Mandate. Again, in the fifth year of the Qiande reign of the Song, the third month, the five stars aligned like strung pearls, scattered across *Jiang Lou*, signifying the brilliance of culture throughout the realm.

As Master Cheng said: ‘The true primordial *qi* of Heaven and Earth cannot be mingled with wandering *qi*, hence it converges to give birth to the Sage.’ Such was the case with Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, Wu, the Zhou, and Confucius. Here I cite three examples to verify the signs of creation; others need not be exhaustively listed.

I once considered that Emperor Wu changed the reign title to *Taichu* out of admiration for Emperor Zhuan, altering the era to align with that auspicious sign—though in truth, no such celestial configuration occurred in Emperor Wu’s time.

Su Shi of the Dongpo said: “Metal and Water always remain near the sun, no more than ten degrees away. When the sun is at *Ji* and *Wei*, this casts doubt on the claim.” In my view, the Qin took the tenth month as the first. The tenth month corresponds to the present eighth month, and at the seventh-month node, the sun still lingers between *Jiao* and *Yi*. Thus, the gathering of Metal and Water at *Jing* is not so distant. Dongpo’s argument is ultimately based on Han annotations.

The gathering of the five stars at *Kui* occurred in the domain of Lu, marking the beginning of great peace throughout the realm, as noted by Dou Yi.

Master Zhu said: “The *wei* stars are Yang within Yin; the *jing* stars are Yin within Yang. The five stars are the *qi* of Earth’s Wood, Water, Fire, Soil, and Metal, ascending and coalescing, yet they receive the sun’s light. The *jing* stars are the surplus of Yang *qi*, congealed and flickering, their light unstable. The *wei* stars have rays and points, their inherent light also unmoving, and all receive the sun’s light.”

Master Zhu also said: “The *jing* stars revolve leftward day and night, completing slightly more than one circuit. Heaven has twelve stations, and now the Weaving Girl star beside the Milky Way ‘seven times daily’—from *mao* to *you*—passes through seven stations.”

The *Erya* annotation states: The Five Stars all move rightward through the twenty-eight lunar lodges, while the celestial body remains fixed, hence they are called *jing* stars. The Five Stars serve as *wei*, like the warp and weft of woven fabric. However, among the Five *wei*, only the Chen star is difficult to observe, just as the flow of blood through the interstices of flesh and muscle is most subtle. The Chen star belongs to Kan, the Water trigram, which excels in concealment. The Yuan, Ji, Tuo, and Qian rivers flow hidden for over a thousand *li* before emerging. The Celestial Palace and the Emperor’s Mansion are all central *jing* stars. The *wei* stars, being mobile, are Yang, yet Venus and the Chen star are Yin. The *jing* stars, being immobile, are Yin, yet the *Ximu* and *Chunshou* regions are Yang.

Shaozi said: The stars that are Yang within Yin are the most minute, like dust and sand, and when they fall, they form mounds. He also said: When stars fall to earth, they become stone. The surplus of stellar Yang, the Five Stars correspond to the human Five Viscera, while the various stars correspond to the human limbs and bones. When essence, blood, and spirit are preserved, essence abides, fulfilling its office and manifesting its luminosity. When spirit wanes and essence scatters, it is like a person’s death. Thus, when stars fall, they become stone.

Zhu Hanshang said: When essence scatters and *qi* wanes, Kan reaches its extreme and Li appears, giving rise to falling stars. Their light illuminates the earth like Li. When they fall as stone, mounds, or dust and sand, it is Gen. Once their radiance disperses, *qi* congeals into stone, much like a person’s corporeal form descending to earth.

The sun, moon, and Five Stars all originate from the beginning of the Oxherd constellation. This corresponds to the Han *Taichu* calendar’s eleventh month, the first day of the new moon, and the winter solstice. The Han scholar Liu stated: Pointing to the beginning of the Oxherd to mark the sun and moon is called *Xingji* (Star Cycle). The Five Stars begin at the start of the Oxherd, while the sun and moon commence from its midpoint, traversing nearly twelve stations. When they reach the beginning, it marks a solar term; when they reach the midpoint, the Dipper’s handle points below, forming the twelve earthly branches. Observing its position reveals the station. The Han scholar Song Zhong said: The winter solstice begins at the first degree of the Oxherd lodge, with the Dipper’s handle pointing to the *zi* position. Du Yu said: The twelve stations begin with *Xingji* and rotate rightward.

The *Erya* states: *Xingji* begins with the Oxherd, the Dipper and Oxherd constellations. Guo’s annotation says: The Oxherd and Dipper are where the sun, moon, and Five Stars begin and end. The *Zuo Tradition* does not say “winter solstice” but “the sun’s southernmost point,” referring to the sun’s arrival at the Oxherd from *Chunwei*. At noon, the shadow is longest, revealing its southernmost point at the tip of the Dipper’s handle, threading through the Celestial Palace and marking the Weaving Girl’s station.

The *Lost Books of Zhou* states: In the eleventh month, when the southern culmination occurs, twilight shifts, the sun treads the long path, and faint stars stir in the Yellow Springs. In this month, the Dipper’s handle begins to point north at dusk, while the sun and moon both rise from the Oxherd, moving rightward. The moon completes a circuit of the heavens, beginning a new station, and meets the sun. The sun traverses one station per month, completing a circuit through the twelve earthly branches, ending only to begin anew—this is called the origin of the sun and moon.

I humbly suggest that the profundities of creation are too subtle to be illuminated without synthesizing multiple explanations. The *Star Canon* poses a difficulty: When observing the canopy’s ribs and wheel spokes, those near the hub are dense, while those farther away grow sparse. Now, the North Pole serves as the celestial hub, and the twenty-eight lodges as the canopy’s ribs. Why, then, are they unevenly spaced? The ribs are modeled after the *Zhou Rituals’* carriage-maker, who crafted twenty-eight canopy bows to represent the stars and wheel spokes to symbolize the sun and moon. The sun and moon meet once a month, completing a circuit of the heavens. The moon then moves to the next station, eventually catching up to the sun and sharing a lodge.

The heavens are round like two covers joined together, with the North and South Poles as the hubs of the two covers, and the twenty-eight lodges as the canopy’s ribs. The celestial equator encircles the heavens’ midsection like the seam where the two covers meet. The region north of the equator forms the inner enclosure, like the upper cover, while the region south forms the outer enclosure, like the lower cover. The equator lies at the heavens’ center, like the seam. The ecliptic crosses obliquely through the equator. Thus, the spacing of the ribs is narrow near the hubs and widens with distance, just as the degrees of the lodges are narrow near the poles and widen toward the equator, reaching their maximum breadth there.

In a circular chart, the southern stars appear progressively narrower, yet the opposite occurs. In a horizontal chart, the regions far from both poles appear broad, distorting the true shape of the heavens. Now, if we carve the celestial sphere into two circular charts—one facing upward, the other downward—with the chart’s center as the pole, the region north of the equator becomes the *Neiguan* star chart, while the region south becomes the *Waiguan* star chart. When the two charts are joined, they form a complete celestial sphere, accurately reflecting the varying breadth of the star degrees and ensuring precise divination.

The North Pole is called the upper circle, the South Pole the lower circle, and the equator encircling the heavens is called the middle circle. The middle circle is broad, while the regions near the two poles are extremely narrow.
