Ouzi said: In the canopy-heavens theory, southern degrees gradually narrow, while in the celestial-sphere theory, the North Pole is elevated. Both explanations may be left unresolved.

The *Zhou Rituals*’ *Xingtu* distinguishes the nine provinces by their corresponding stars and boundaries, all of which are recorded in the ritual classics and cannot be erased. There are three interpretations: The *Ling Zhoujiu* states: The station where Jupiter resides determines our *fenye* (field allocation). After the loss of the *Gu Kan* and *Yu Shu*, the regions assigned to commanderies and states no longer align with Jupiter’s ancient positions. Some lie north, others west, differing from the original locations of enfeoffment.

During the Tang, Yu, Xia, Shang, and Zhou dynasties, the ten thousand states and 1,773 polities were all affiliated with the twelve principal states, linking them to the twelve celestial stations. This system honored the ancient kings’ intent to prioritize ancestral sacrifices. For example, Ban Bo was enfeoffed at Shangqiu, which governs the Chen station, hence the Shang star, from which the Shang people derived their name. Shi Shen was enfeoffed at Greater Xia, which governs the Shen station, hence the Xia star, from which the Tang people derived their name. Later, Tang became Jin, and Shen became the Jin star—this is the second interpretation.

Now, consolidating the *fenye* stations and lodges: Qingzhou lies in the east, Xuan’gan in the north, Yong in the west, and *Chunshou* in the south.

Extending to the southeast, Yangzhou aligns with the *Xingji* station in the north; Jizhou in the northeast with *Daliang* in the west; Xuzhou in the east with *Jianglou*; and Yuzhou and the Three Rivers occupy the center, while *Dahuo* resides in the due east. This constitutes the third and most divergent arrangement of the celestial stations and lodges. With three interpretations differing, the discerning must choose for themselves.

The *Traditions* states: "The five planets heralded the rise of Zhou." *Discourses of the States* records: "When King Wu campaigned against Shang, Jupiter was in *Chunhuo*, the moon in *Tianxi*, the sun in *Ximu*, the hour in the initial *Dou*, and the star in *Tiandian*." I have examined this: when Zhou arose, *Chunhuo* stood directly above the void of *Xuanyuan*, where the *Ji* star was bound. The *Fang* mansion and Jupiter intersected as warp and weft, linking to the divine power of the revered *Shen*—Hou Ji was conceived through this influence. *Chunshou* lay to the right of the mountains and rivers, where King Tai flourished and Hou Ji was enfeoffed. When the Zhou army set forth, the sun stood at ten degrees of *Ji*, the ford of *Ximu*; the moon at four degrees of *Fang*, the chariot of *Shengyang*. Three days later, it was the Zhou’s first month, *gengyin*. On the new moon, sun and moon conjoined at one degree of the Southern Dipper, placing the hour at the Dipper’s handle. At that time, Mars advanced with the Zhou army, while Mercury lay concealed in *Tiandian*, signaling the end of the water phase’s dominion to Emperor Zhuan. From *Tiandian* to *Ximu*, Jupiter reached *Chunhuo*, then retrograded to sojourn in *Chunshou* before advancing again to *Niaofu*. This cyclical traversal, weaving the destiny of the Zhou house, was surely not by human effort. Thus, it was fitting that Zhou endured for thirty generations and eight hundred years, the longest of the sagely dynasties. The celestial portents are luminous—how could they not be trusted? Later ages sought to overcome them by human force—how could this be possible?

The *Spring and Autumn Annals* states: "The twenty-eight lodges are divided among the four directions, seven lodges to each, forming a single image. As creatures and beasts exist on earth, their counterparts reside in heaven. In the east, the Azure Dragon, its head to the south and tail to the north; in the west, the White Tiger, likewise south-headed and north-tailed. In the south, the Vermilion Bird, its head to the west and tail to the east; in the north, the Dark Warrior, west-headed and east-tailed. They proceed from *Jiao* in a leftward revolution." The *Erya* notes: "*Shouxing* begins with *Jiao* and *Kang*." Guo’s commentary: "The count begins at *Jiao*, the eldest of the lodges, hence *Shouxing*."

The seven lodges per direction are as follows: from *Dou* to *Bi* is the *Xingwu* (Dark Warrior); from *Kui* to *Shen*, the *Xinghu* (White Tiger); from *Jing* to *Zhen*, the *Xingniao* (Vermilion Bird); from *Jiao* to *Ji*, the *Xinglong* (Azure Dragon). Arrayed around the four directions, they revolve westward with the heavens. Each direction has its fixed stars, though the stars themselves have no fixed abode, appearing and vanishing in their due seasons.

Master Lin states: "*Niaohuo*, *Xu*, and *Xu* mark the solstices and equinoxes at dusk, appearing in the southern sky as the culmination stars of the meridian. In the second month of spring, the seven lodges occupy their respective positions: *Xinghuo* in the east, *Niao* in the south, *Xu* in the west, and *Xu* in the north. When the sun is in *Xu* and enters the *You* quadrant at dusk, *Chunhuo* appears in the southern meridian. At this time, day and night are each fifty *ke*—this is the *qi* of the vernal equinox. By the second month of summer, *Niao* turns west, *Huo* turns south, *Xu* turns east, and *Mao* turns north. When the sun is in *Xing* and enters the *Xin* quadrant at dusk, *Dahuo* (*Fang* and *Xin*) appears in the southern meridian. At this time, days are long and nights short—sixty *ke* for day, forty for night—marking the *qi* of the summer solstice. By the second month of autumn, *Huo* turns west, *Xu* turns south, *Mao* turns east, and *Niao* turns north. When the sun is in *Xin* and enters the *You* quadrant at dusk, *Xu* appears in the meridian. Day and night are again equal—fifty *ke* each—this is the *qi* of the autumnal equinox. By the second month of winter, *Xu* turns west, *Mao* turns south, *Niao* turns east, and *Huo* turns north. When the sun is in *Xu* and enters the *Shen* quadrant at dusk, *Mao* appears in the meridian. Days are short and nights long—forty *ke* for day, sixty for night—marking the *qi* of the winter solstice. Once the *qi* of the solstices and equinoxes are fixed, the *qi* of the twelve months are likewise determined. *Xingniao* refers to the image, *Xinghuo* to the station, *Xu* and *Mao* to the lodges—they complement one another."

I observe: the *qi* of heaven, earth, and the four seasons all emanate from the center. *Zi* and *Wu* are the midpoints of the two solstices, the center of heaven and earth. *Mao* and *You* are the midpoints of the two equinoxes, the center of yin and yang. The culmination stars of the twenty-eight lodges reach their zenith at the center. When the sage emerges to harmonize and position heaven and earth, it is simply a matter of holding to the center.

The star counts of the seven lodges: *Xinglong* has thirty-two stars, *Xingwu* thirty-five, *Xinghu* fifty-one, and *Xingniao* sixty-four. Together, they total 182 stars.

The degrees of the seven lodges: *Xinglong* spans seventy-five degrees, *Xingwu* ninety-eight and one-quarter degrees, *Xinghu* eighty degrees, and *Xingniao* one hundred twenty degrees. Combined, they form the celestial circle of 365¼ degrees. Each degree measures 2,933 *li*, divided into twelve stations.

The *fenye* divisions of the seven lodges:
From eleven degrees of *Dou* to seven degrees of *Nü* (also called *Xunü*) is the station *Xingji*. The celestial branch is *Chou*, called *Chifenif*, corresponding to the pitch *Huangzhong*. *Dou* points to *Zi*—this is the division of present-day Wu and Yue.
From eight degrees of *Nü* to sixteen degrees of *Wei* is the station *Xuangan* (also called *Tiandian*). The celestial branch is *Zi*, called *Kundun*, corresponding to the pitch *Dalü*. *Dou* points to *Chou*—this is the division of present-day Qi.
From seventeen degrees of *Wei* to four degrees of *Kui* is the station *Shiwei* (also called *Zouzi*). The celestial branch is *Hai*, called *Dayuanxian*, corresponding to the pitch *Taicou*.

From the fifth degree of *Kui* to the sixth degree of *Wei* is the station *Jianglou*. The celestial branch is *Xu*, called *Kuomao*, corresponding to the pitch *Jiazhong*. *Dou* points to *Yin*—this is the division of present-day Wei.

From the seventh degree of *Wei* to the eleventh degree of *Bi* is the station *Daliang*. The celestial branch is *You*, called *Zuoe*, corresponding to the pitch *Guxi*. *Dou* points to *Chen*—this is the division of Zhao.

From the twelfth degree of *Bi* to the fifteenth degree of *Dongjing* is the station *Shichen*. The celestial branch is *Shen*, called *Huotan*, corresponding to the pitch *Zhonglü*. *Dou* points to *Si*—this is the division of Jin and Wei.

From the sixteenth degree of *Jing* to the eighth degree of *Liu* is the station *Chunshou*. The celestial branch is *Wei*, called *Hongqia*, corresponding to the pitch *Rui Bin*. *Dou* points to *Wu*—this is the division of Qin.

From the ninth degree of *Liu* to the seventeenth degree of *Zhang* is the station *Chunhuo*. The celestial branch is *Wu*, called *Dunpang* (also named *Dalü*). In terms of pitch, this corresponds to *Linzhong*. *Dou* points to *Wei*—this is the division of Zhou.

From the eighteenth degree of *Zhang* to the eleventh degree of *Jiao* is the station *Chunwei*. The celestial branch is *Si*, called *Dahuangluo*, corresponding to the pitch *Yize*. *Dou* points to *Shen*—this is the division of Chu.

From the twelfth degree of *Jiao* to the fourth degree of *Di* is the station *Shouxing*. The celestial branch is *Chen*, called *Zhixu*, corresponding to the pitch *Nanlü*. *Dou* points to *You*—this is the division of Han.

From the fifth degree of *Di* to the ninth degree of *Wei* is the station *Dahuo*. The celestial branch is *Mao*, called *Danban*, corresponding to the pitch *Wuyi*. *Dou* points to *Xu*—this is the division of Song.

From the tenth degree of *Wei* to the tenth degree of *Dou*, comprising 135 divisions, concludes the station *Ximu*. The celestial branch is *Yin*, called *Shetige*, corresponding to the pitch *Yingzhong*. *Dou* points to *Hai*—this is the division of present-day Yan.

I humbly note: The Minister of Agriculture, Master Zheng, stated: "Heaven has twelve stations, where the sun and moon are entwined. Earth has twelve divisions, where kings and lords establish their domains." Thus, the twenty-eight lodges are allocated to the twelve celestial branches, interwoven with the seven luminaries. One moves left, the other right, forming the warp and weft. This duality of the Five Phases completes the creation of heaven, earth, and the four seasons.

The Eastern Palace, the Azure Dragon, begins with *Jiao* as the court of the Five Emperors. The first lodge is the ancestral temple, *Di* is the root of heaven, *Fang* is the celestial palace, *Xin* is the Hall of Light, *Wei* is the filial son, and *Ji* is the guest. The *Ling Xian* states: "The Azure Dragon coils on the left." The left is the Green Dragon, belonging to Wood and residing in the East. Further divided into lesser images: in the *Seven Stars* system, *Jiao* of Wood is the *Jiao* dragon, *Kang* of Metal is the dragon, *Di* of Earth is the *Mo*, *Fang* of the Sun is the hare, *Xin* of the Moon is the fox, *Wei* of Fire is the tiger, and *Ji* of Water is the leopard. The sun and moon reside within, while the five planets weave without. *Ximu* converges at *Yin*, *Dahuo* at *Mao*, and *Shouxing* at *Chen*.

*Jiao*: Two stars, serving as the Celestial Gate. Between them lies the Gate of Heaven, within which is the Celestial Court. The ecliptic passes through its midst, where the seven luminaries traverse. The left horn of the dragon is the Celestial Field, governing law and punishment. The right horn is the general, commanding the army. When the stars are bright and large, the king’s Way flourishes, and the worthy hold court. If *Yinghuo* (Mars) trespasses the Celestial Field, drought ensues. As recorded in the *Treatise on Sacrifices*: "The Han founder established the shrine of the Spirit Star."

*Kang*: Four stars, the inner court of the Son of Heaven. It serves as the ancestral temple, governing epidemics and overseeing all memorials, lawsuits, trials, and the recording of merits. *Di*: Four stars, the lodging of the king and the palace of the queen and consorts. The two front stars are the primary wives, the two rear stars the concubines. The *Shanzi* states: "When the Root of Heaven appears, the waters dry up." The *Erya* says: "*Di* is the Root of Heaven." Below *Jiao* and *Kang*, it connects to the people like the root of a tree. This is the division of Song.

*Fang*...

**Fang**: Four stars, the Hall of Light, the office from which the Son of Heaven promulgates governance. The central space is the Celestial Thoroughfare; when the Seven Luminaries pass through it, the Son of Heaven is at peace. It is also the Celestial Steed and Heavenly Horses, governing chariots and carriages. The *Guoyu* states: "The Agricultural Auspice." At dawn, when the sun and moon align with the Celestial Temple, the earth begins to stir. The *Sui Treatise* says: "When the Five Weft Stars enter Fang, the rise of King Wen of Zhou commences."

**Xin**: Three stars, the true seat of the Celestial King. The central star is the Hall of Light, where the Son of Heaven, as the Great Star, administers rewards and punishments. The front star is the Crown Prince, the rear star the lesser sons. The Great Fire is the Great Star; only when the great cold and heat recede does it retreat. The *Treatise on Pitch Pipes* says: "Xin signifies the flowering of the myriad things." The *Tang Treatise* states: "In the *Book of Changes*, 'Thunder follows Qian' is called *Great Strength*, symbolized by Fang." Xin is the essence of Qian, just as Fang is the ascending yang steed. Fang is where the sun and moon reside. The *Divine Mandate in Sequence* says: "When Jupiter guards Xin, the year’s grain is abundant." The *Hongfan* states: "Zhonghua refers to Jupiter in Xin." Jupiter is also called *Sheti* and *Zhonghua*. The *Zuozhuan* says: "Xin is Fire." In the fifth month, fire first appears at dusk. The *Book of Songs* says: "Three stars are in the heavens."

**Wei**: The first of nine stars in Wei is the Empress; the next three stars are the consorts, and the following star represents the secondary concubines. The adjacent star is called the Divine Palace. The tail of the Azure Dragon represents the nine sons, their colors uniform and bright. When the rear palace is harmonious, there are many descendants. On *bingzi* and *chen* days, the Dragon’s Tail lies hidden to the left of *chen*. Wei signifies the life and death of the myriad things, like a tail.

**Ji**: Four stars, the rear palace and the residence of the Empress and consorts. It is also the Dragon’s Tail, the Guest of Ceremony, governing speech and disputes. It is also called the Celestial Ford, ruling the Eight Winds. When the sun and moon lodge in Ji, they are northeast of the Eastern Wall. The *Yi* and *Si* winds arise above, and the Quail constellation follows. It is also said: "Fu Yue rides the tail of Ji, ranked among the stars." The *Jin Treatise* states: "Fu Yue is one star behind Wei." The *Mao Commentary on the Book of Songs* says: "Ji is in the south and the Dipper in the north, hence 'Southern Ji, Northern Dipper.'" I have once compared the hexagrams: the dragon belongs to the east, to *Zhen*. *Zhen* moves beneath the heavy yin, like a tortoise exhaling qi. The serpent rises—none are as adept at transformation as the dragon. The hexagrams align after the Spring Equinox, when *chen* is the station of the Azure Dragon. Movement brings change, so the dragon ascends at the Spring Equinox, rising as thunder, bursting forth from the earth. At the Autumn Equinox, it descends into the Thunder Marsh, embodying the image of *Guimei*. In midsummer, fierce thunder uproots trees and the dragon ascends. *Zhen* Wood occupies the position of *mao*. *Kang* is called the Dragon, akin to thunder. In the profound, the third in sequence is the dragon. Diviners take *jia*, *yi*, *yin*, and *mao* as the dragon. In celestial patterns, *Jiao* is the flood dragon, *Kang* the dragon, and *Yi* the earthworm. *Jiao* and *Kang* are *chen*; *Yi* is *si*. From the Spring Equinox to the Grain in Ear, *Zhen* governs, and *chen* and *si* are called *Yi*: the dispersal of qi. Fang is the Celestial Steed and the station of the Azure Dragon, hence horses are also called dragon-horses. The *Treatise* says: "When the Five Weft Stars enter Fang, the rise of King Wen of Zhou begins"—this too is an auspicious omen of a rising king. The saying that five horses transform into a dragon also originates here. Its symbols—the Celestial Field, the Agricultural Auspice, and many sons—all correspond to the spring of the east.

The Western Palace’s *Xianchi*:
- **Kui** is the Ditch and Dike.
- **Lou** is the Gathering Spring.
- **Wei** is the Celestial Granary.
- **Mao** is the White Robe.
- **Bi** and **Hui** are the Frontier Troops.
- **Zi** and **Nao** are the Tiger’s Head.
- **Shen** is the Reaping.

The *Ling Xian* says: "The White Tiger fiercely occupies the right." The White Tiger, belonging to Metal, resides in the west and is further divided into lesser images. The Seven Stars of the household:
- **Kui Wood** is the Wolf.
- **Lou Metal** is the Dog.
- **Wei Earth** is the Pheasant.
- **Mao Sun** is the Rooster.
- **Bi Moon** is the Crow.
- **Zi Fire** is the Monkey.
- **Shen Water** is the Ape.

The sun and moon dwell at the center, the Five Stars weave outside. *Jiang Lou* meets *xu*, *Da Liang* meets *you*, and *Shi Shen* meets *shen*.

**Kui**: Sixteen stars, the Celestial Armory. Also called the Celestial Boar or the Sealed Boar. It governs military affairs and suppresses violence, as well as canals and ditches. The *Monthly Ordinances* state: "In the second spring month, the moon is in Kui; in late summer, Kui culminates at dawn."

**Lou**: Three stars, the Celestial Prince, governing parks, livestock, and sacrificial offerings for suburban rites and grand feasts. When bright, the realm is at peace. The *Tang Treatise* says: "When the sun meets Lou, great ministers are in distress."

**Wei**: Three stars, the Celestial Kitchen, the granary of the Five Grains. When active, there are matters of transport; when bright, the realm is at peace. In late spring, the sun is in Wei.

**Mao**: Seven stars, the Celestial Ear, governing judicial affairs in the west. Also the Hairy-Headed Hu Star; when bright, the realm’s prisons are just. The *Book of Documents* says: "The day is short at the Mao star."

**Bi**: Eight stars, the Net of Bi governs frontier troops, hunting, and distant military campaigns. The Yellow Road passes through it; when the Son of Heaven travels, he raises the banner. The Net of Bi leads the way—this is its significance.

**The *Justice* states:** The preferences of the *Ji* and *Bi* constellations align with those of their consorts. The central earth-*qi* generates wind, while the eastern wood-*qi* generates rain. Wood overcomes earth as a consort, thus the *Ji* star favors wind—this is what its consort prefers. *Bi* belongs to the western quadrant, where metal-*qi* is yin; overcoming eastern wood as a consort, it thus favors rain and aligns with the preferences of its wife.

Moreover, *Shen* and *Yin* oppose each other in mutual clash: when *Shen* is broken, it counters *Yin*; when *Yin* is countered, it becomes a howling gale. When *Yin* breaks *Shen*, *Shen* is countered, and violent rains arise.

**Zi**: Three stars, the Tiger’s Head, the hidden treasury of the Three Armies. When bright, military provisions are abundant, and the general’s authority is secure. The *Monthly Ordinances* states: "In mid-autumn, *Zi* culminates at dawn."

**Shen**: Ten stars. Also called *Shenfa*, *Great Constellation*, *Celestial Market*, or *Iron Blade*. It governs execution, slaughter, and military campaigns. It is also the Balance, ensuring justice and equilibrium, and oversees frontier cities. The ten stars of *Shen* form the body of the White Tiger. The three central stars in horizontal alignment are the Three Generals. The three slanted stars below are called *Fa*, the Celestial Commandant, ruling over the Hu, Xianbei, Rong, and Di states—thus they must not shine brightly. The four outer stars are the shoulders and thighs: the northeast is the Left Shoulder, governing the Left General; the northwest is the Right Shoulder, governing the Right General; the southeast is the Left Foot, governing the Rear General; the southwest is the Right Foot, governing the Deputy General. Thus the *Yellow Emperor’s Divination* says: "*Shen* corresponds to seven generals; if all seven are bright, and the *Star of Punitive Expeditions* shines as brilliantly as *Shen*, great ministers will conspire to raise arms."

*Shen* is the third of the White Tiger. *Fang* is the Celestial Steed, also the second mansion of the Azure Dragon—hence horses are also called "dragon-horses." The *Treatise* states: "When the five planets enter *Fang*, it portends the rise of a king who will expand the frontiers—this is an auspicious omen of a flourishing dynasty." The saying that "five horses transform into a dragon" also originates from this. Its symbols include the *Celestial Field*, the *Agricultural Auspice*, and *Abundant Offspring*, all corresponding to the spring of the eastern quadrant.

In the Western Palace, *Xianchi* is the *Pool of Harmony*; *Kui* governs canals and ditches; *Lou* governs the gathering of springs; *Wei* is the *Celestial Granary*; *Mao* is the *White Garment*; *Bi* commands frontier troops; *Zi* is the *Tiger’s Head*; and *Shen* governs reaping and execution.

The *Spiritual Constitution* says: "The White Tiger fiercely crouches on the right." The White Tiger, belonging to metal, resides in the west and is further divided into lesser symbols: the seven stars of *Kui* are the Wolf (Wood); *Lou* is the Dog (Metal); *Wei* is the Pheasant (Earth); *Mao* is the Rooster (Sun); *Bi* is the Crow (Moon); *Zi* is the Monkey (Fire); and *Shen* is the Ape (Water). The sun and moon dwell at the center, while the five planets weave their paths beyond. *Jianglou* meets *Xu*; *Daliang* meets *You*; and *Shichen* meets *Shen*.

**Kui**: Sixteen stars, the Celestial Armory. Also called the *Celestial Boar* or *Sealed Boar*. It governs military affairs and suppresses violence, as well as canals and waterways. The *Monthly Ordinances* states: "In mid-spring, the sun is in *Kui*; in late summer, *Kui* culminates at dawn."

**Lou**: Three stars, the Celestial Son, governing imperial parks, sacrificial animals, and offerings for suburban rites and grand feasts. When bright, the realm is at peace, and descendants are numerous. The *Tang Treatise* says: "When the sun meets *Lou*, great ministers are in distress."

**Wei**: Three stars, the Celestial Kitchen, the granary of the Five Grains. When active, there are matters of transport; when bright, the realm is at peace. In late spring, the sun is in *Wei*.

**Mao**: Seven stars, the Celestial Ear, governing judicial affairs in the west. Also the Hairy-Headed Hu Star; when bright, the realm’s prisons are just. The *Book of Documents* says: "The day is short at the *Mao* star."

**Bi**: Eight stars, the Net of *Bi* governs frontier troops, hunting, and distant military campaigns. The Yellow Road passes through it; when the Son of Heaven travels, he raises the banner. The Net of *Bi* leads the way—this is its significance.

The *Justice* states: The *Ji* and *Bi* constellations favor the preferences of wives. The central earth *qi* manifests as wind, while the eastern wood *qi* manifests as rain. Wood overcomes earth to become the consort, thus the *Ji* star favors wind—this is aligning with the consort’s preference. *Bi* belongs to the western quadrant, where metal *qi* is yin; overcoming eastern wood to become the consort, it thus favors rain and aligns with the wife’s preference. Moreover, *Shen* and *Yin* clash in opposition: when *Shen* breaks *Yin*, it comes in reverse, and *Yin* being reversed produces whirlwinds; when *Yin* breaks *Shen*, *Shen* being reversed produces violent storms.

**Zi**: Three stars, the Tiger’s Head, serving as the hidden treasury for the Three Armies’ scouts. When bright, military provisions are abundant, and the general’s authority is secure. The *Monthly Ordinances* states: "In mid-autumn, at dawn, *Zi* culminates."

**Shen**: Ten stars: also called *Shenfa*, *Da Chen*, *Tian Shi*, or *Tiefan*. It governs execution, slaughter, and military campaigns. It also serves as the balance scale, ensuring fairness and order, and oversees frontier cities. The ten stars of *Shen* form the body of the White Tiger. The three central stars arranged horizontally represent the Three Generals. The three slanted stars below are called *Fa*, the Celestial Commandant, governing the Hu, Xianbei, Rong, and Di states—thus they should not shine brightly. The four outer stars are the left and right shoulders and thighs. The northeast is the Left Shoulder, governing the Left General; the northwest is the Right Shoulder, governing the Right General; the southeast is the Left Foot, governing the Rear General; the southwest is the Right Foot, governing the Deputy General. Thus the Yellow Emperor’s divination states: "*Shen* corresponds to the Seven Generals; when all seven are bright, and the *Fa* star shines equally with *Shen*, great ministers plot rebellion." *Shen* as the White Tiger’s three stars includes one called the Balance. To the west lie nine curved stars: the first is *Tianqi*, the second *Tianyuan*, and the third *Jiuyou*. To the east is a great star called *Lang* (Wolf). When the Wolf’s horn changes color, banditry abounds.

In my humble opinion, the *Book of Changes* associates *Shen*’s tiger with the western quadrant, residing in *Dui*. *Dui*’s metal embodies the *qi* of contraction, severity, and killing, bearing the likeness of a tiger. The *Lü* hexagram speaks of "treading on a tiger’s tail"—its inner trigram is *Dui*. The *Ge* hexagram speaks of "the tiger’s transformation"—its outer trigram is *Dui*. The *Yi* hexagram speaks of "the tiger’s gaze, fixed and fierce"—it contains a hidden *Dui*. The *Image* states: "*Shen* is the White Tiger"—*Shen* is *Shen* (the zodiacal sign). *Shen* occupies the Tiger’s head, wielding the power of execution and slaughter. To the east lies the Great Wolf, and the wolf is also of the tiger kind—thus it is considered a celestial general star. Among the Seven Mansions, some correspond to the Armory or Celestial Granary, aligning with the autumn of gathering and contraction.

In the Southern Palace, the Vermilion Bird: *Jing* governs water affairs, *Gui* governs sacrificial rites, *Liu* is the Bird, governing plants and trees, *Xing* is the Great Fire, governing urgent matters, *Zhang* is the Plain Kitchen, governing feasts and guests, *Yi* is the Wings, governing distant guests, and *Zhen* is the Chariot, governing wind.

The *Spiritual Constitution* states: "The Vermilion Bird spreads its wings in the front." The front is the Vermilion Bird, belonging to fire and residing in the south. Further subdividing its minor images: among the Seven Stars’ household, *Jing*’s wood is the *Jie* (reed), *Gui*’s metal is the sheep, *Liu*’s earth is the river deer, *Xing*’s sun is the horse, *Zhang*’s moon is the deer, and *Yi*’s fire is the sun. In late autumn, at dawn, *Liu* culminates. The sun and moon reside in the center, the Five Planets weave externally, the Quail’s Tail meets *Si*, the Quail’s Fire meets *Wu*, and the Quail’s Head meets *Wei*.

**Jing**: Eight stars, the Celestial Watchtower, governing waterways and legal standards for fairness. The *Justice* states: "East of *Shen* lies the King’s Well." Thus it is said: "When the Eastern Well’s Dragon King upholds the law with fairness, the Well stars shine brightly and align properly, governing the capital’s jurisdiction." It is also said: "When *Yinghuo* (Mars) trespasses the Eastern Well, drought follows."

**Gui**: Five stars, the Celestial Eye, governing keen insight and the detection of treachery. When bright, the Five Grains flourish. It is also said to govern death and mourning.

**Liu**: Eight stars, the Celestial Steward, governing imperial cuisine and the discernment of flavors. It also governs thunder and rain, being the Vermilion Bird’s beak—thus it is called the Bird’s Peck. The *Erya* states: "The *Wei* (flavor) is called *Liu* (willow), which is the beak." It is also written as *Zhou* (啄, peck). The *Zuozhuan* states: "*Wei* is the Great Fire." The *Treatise on the Celestial Offices* states: "It is the Bird’s Beak, governing plants and trees." Moreover, in late summer, the sun resides in *Liu*; in late autumn, at dawn, *Liu* culminates.

**Xing**: Seven stars, also called the Celestial Capital, governing garments and embroidered patterns. The seven stars also form the neck.

**Zhang**: Six stars, the Plain Kitchen, the bird’s feeding ground, governing treasures and ancestral temple offerings. It oversees imperial banquets, rewards, and punishments—when bright, the kingly Way prospers. The *Han Records* state: "It governs feasts and guests." The *Jin Records* state: "*Zhu Zhang* is the Crow Star, thus it governs insects."

**Yi**: Twenty-two stars, the Celestial Music Bureau, governing entertainers and distant guests from the barbarian lands beyond the seas. When bright, grand rituals and music flourish; when active, envoys from the Four Barbarians arrive, or the Son of Heaven mobilizes troops. *Yi* governs feathers and elegance, overseeing distant matters.

**Zhen**: Four stars, the Chariot, governing cavalry and carriages, as well as burdens and tasks. When armies march or return, divination is performed at the *Jiao* (linchpin). It also governs the Prime Minister and assisting ministers, and is called the Bird’s Tail. It further governs wind, sharing the same position as *Xun*. Another name is *Yi* (轶), the head.

In my humble opinion, the *Book of Changes* associates *Li* with the southern quadrant, forming the image of the Flying Bird—the Vermilion Bird. The histories speak of "flowing fire as the bird" and "the crow in the sun." *Wu* is the Quail’s Fire, *Wei* is the Quail’s Head, and *Si* is the Quail’s Tail. Its flavor resides in *Liu*, and its wings in *Yi*. *Liu* is *Wu* (the zodiacal sign), *Li*; *Yi* is *Si*, *Xun*. Fu Chuqiu, in his commentary on the *Mingyi* hexagram’s *Modesty*, states: "The arriving bird is the Vermilion Bird." The *Gui Cang*’s initial *Xun* states: "A bird will come, drooping its wings." *Yi* is the Quail’s Tail, hence it is called the Flying Bird. Birds of the quail and pheasant kind, such as the peacock of Lingnan, cling to the grass when they fly.

The Seven Stars form the Horse, corresponding to *chen* (the fifth earthly branch) as *wu* (noon), thus the Horse belongs to fire. Domestic animals born under *wu* are of the fire phase, and the silkworm is the Horse’s head—the essence of the Dragon Star. Therefore, horses and silkworms share the same *qi*, just as crabs, turtles, and soft-shelled turtles all bear yellow markings, which pertain to *Li* (fire). Among the Seven Mansions, there is the Ghost Chariot. The Seven Stars of the Celestial Eye govern embroidery and patterned fabrics, all corresponding to the image of *Li* in the southern quadrant, radiant and luminous.

The Northern Palace’s Dark Warrior (*Xuanwu*) has the Southern Dipper as its temple, the Oxherd as its sacrificial offering, and the Weaving Maiden as the Celestial Granddaughter. The Girl (of the *Xu* mansion) governs matters of weeping and lamentation, *Wei* (the Rooftop) governs roofing, the Encampment governs the ancestral temple, and the Eastern Wall governs literature and scholarship. The *Ling Xian* states: "The sacred turtle coils its head behind, and what lies behind is *Xuanwu*, which belongs to water and resides in the north." Further subdivisions of the constellations include: in the Seven Stars mansion, *Doumu* is the cricket; *Niuxin* is the ox; *Nü* is the bat; *Xu* is the rat; *Wei* is the swallow; and *Yingshi* is the wall lizard.

The sun and moon reside at the center, while the five planets weave their paths beyond. *Xuanleng* converges at *zi* (midnight), *Xingji* at *chou* (the ox), and *Zouzi* at *hai* (the pig). The Southern Dipper consists of six stars, the Celestial Temple, and also marks the span of longevity. It further represents the positions of the Prime Minister and Grand Steward, governing rewards, promotions, the advancement of the worthy, and the bestowal of ranks and emoluments. It also commands military affairs. The two southern stars are the Celestial Beam, the two central stars the Celestial Minister, and the two northern stars the Celestial Treasury. When the stars of the Dipper shine brightly, the king’s Way is harmonious and peaceful. As the tradition states: "When the *chen* (celestial stem) aligns with the Dipper’s handle, the sun and moon meet at the Southern Dipper."

The Ox consists of six stars, the Celestial Pass and Bridge, governing sacrificial affairs. The *Hou Zhi* states: "The movement of the seven luminaries begins with the Oxherd." These three stars are the River Drum, also called the Oxherd. The *Lü Shu* says of the Oxherd: "Yang *qi* moves, and the myriad things emerge." The *Bo Wu Zhi* records: "Zhang Qian rode a raft to explore the source of the Yellow River." Yan Junping divined that a guest star trespassed upon the Ox and Dipper. It is also said: "When Venus invades the Oxherd, the general meets with misfortune."

The Maid consists of four stars, also called the Bearded Maiden, governing cloth, silk, tailoring, and marriage. *Xu* is the term for a lowly concubine, the most humble of women’s duties. To its north lies the Weaving Maiden, the Celestial Granddaughter. The Void governs the northern settlements, the ancestral temple, and matters of sacrificial prayers and invocations. It also governs weeping and lamentation, as well as the office of the Chief Minister. The *Book of Documents* states: "At the first watch, the star *Xu* culminates." The Rooftop consists of three stars governing the Celestial Treasury and Celestial Market, with *Wei* as the Black Ridgepole—a spirit of depletion, as is the Void.

The Encampment consists of two stars, the palace of the Son of Heaven. It also serves as the granary for military provisions and governs earthworks. When its stars shine brightly, the nation prospers. One name for it is the Dark Palace, another the Pure Temple. The two stars of the Encampment are called *Ding* (Stability). *Ding* means "correct," governing earthworks. The *Book of Songs* says: "*Ding* is rightly in the center." The Eastern Wall consists of two stars governing literature and the secret treasury of the world’s books. The *Commentary on the Book of Songs* states: "The Wall is the outer courtyard of the Encampment." When the Winnowing Basket is in the south, the Wall lies to the east of the Encampment, hence it is called the Eastern Wall. When its stars shine brightly, the king’s rule flourishes, the Way of the state is luminous, and the land abounds with noble men. If Jupiter guards it, the five grains suffer damage from water. The *Monthly Ordinances* state: "In mid-winter, the Eastern Wall culminates at dusk."

Comparing this with the *Book of Changes*, the Dipper originally belongs to the north as *Kan* (Water), and the Northern Palace’s turtle shape is its fundamental image. It is further divided into two creatures, the turtle and the snake, within the treasure, with *Kan* as the fox. Yu Fan takes *Gen* (the mountain) as the fox, while the *Tian Wen* takes the Heart as the fox—these interpretations diverge. The *Yun Dou Shu* states: "The Jade Pivot scatters to become the rat." The Jade Pivot is the star of the Dipper, also belonging to *Kan*. The Oxherd lies in *chou* (the ox), its northern star the River Drum, also called the Oxherd. It signifies the movement of yang *qi* and the emergence of the myriad things. The four stars of the Bearded Maiden are base, while the three stars of the Weaving Maiden are noble—the concubine and the Celestial Granddaughter belong to different ranks.

The Celestial Prison consists of six stars beneath the Dipper’s bowl, the prison of the noble. The Chain of Nine Stars lies before the Summoning Star, the prison of the common people. Among the seven northern mansions, many pertain to ancestral temples and sacrificial prayers, for the north is the dark and hidden realm, the dwelling place of ghosts and spirits. The governance of palaces, women’s work, and the correspondence to winter’s season reflect the extreme of yin giving rise to yang, which is why the constellations all begin with the Oxherd’s inception.

The Central Palace’s Celestial Pole consists of five stars, and the Curved Array of six stars, all residing within the Purple Palace as the most exalted. The *Ling Xian* states: "The Yellow Spirit, Xuanyuan, resides at the center." The fifth of the ten celestial stems, *wu* and *ji*, belongs to earth and corresponds to the five stars of the Celestial Pole. The sixth of the twelve earthly branches, *chen*, *xu*, *chou*, and *wei*, belongs to earth and corresponds to the six stars of the Curved Array. In heaven, it is the celestial stem; on earth, it is soil, which is why Master Zhang refers to it as the Yellow Spirit. Its days are the five offices, five times seven making thirty-five.

The names derive from the seven stars of the Northern Dipper as the Emperor’s Chariot, which steers the revolutions of creation. The five stars of the Celestial Pole—astrologers speak of the Four Emperors flanking the Yellow Emperor’s central seat: the Eastern Emperor, *Weilingyang*; the Southern Emperor, *Chipi’ao*; the Western Emperor, *Zizhaoqu*#18; and the Northern Emperor, *Xuguangji*. These correspond to the *Monthly Ordinances*’ wood, fire, metal, and water, with central earth making five. Some ask: "Heaven is one, yet there are five emperors—why?" The answer is: This merely borrows the names of rulers to describe the *qi* of the five phases, each with its own governance.

The Curved Array consists of six stars, an earthly image, for the number of *kun* (earth) is six. The single star in its mouth is called the Heavenly August Emperor, governing the numinous. The four stars embracing the Pole’s pivot are called the Four Assistants. The *Sui Zhi* states: "They reside within the Purple Tenuity Palace." Ban Gu says: "The Zhou dynasty took the position of the Curved Array, for earth resides at the center of the five phases, and the *qi* of the four seasons is complete within it. The Curved Array dwells amid the Dragon, Tiger, Quail, and Turtle, and thus governs creatures of fur, feathers, scales, and shells from all four directions. Is this not why it serves as the guardian of the Central Palace?" The *Ling Xian* states: "In court, it resembles officials; in man, it resembles affairs."

The Three Terraces Six stars rise in pairs, called the Heavenly Pillars. In man, they are the Three Dukes; in heaven, they are the Three Terraces. The first two stars of Wenchang are the Upper Terrace, serving as the Controller of Destiny and governing longevity. The next two stars are the Middle Terrace, serving as the Controller of the Center and governing the imperial clan. The last two stars to the east are the Lower Terrace, serving as the Controller of Emoluments and governing the military.

They are also called the Heavenly Stairway, by which the Great One ascends and descends. Another name is the Grand Stairway. The upper star of the upper step is the Son of Heaven, and the lower star is the queen. The upper star of the middle step is the feudal lords, and the lower star is the ministers and grand masters. The upper star of the lower step is the scholar-officials, and the lower star is the common people.

It is also said: the Upper Terrace’s Controller of Destiny is the Grand Commandant, the Middle Terrace’s Controller of the Center is the Minister of Education, and the Lower Terrace’s Controller of Emoluments is the Minister of Works. When the colors of the Three Terraces are uniform, lord and minister are in harmony; when uneven, discord and perversity arise. When the Three Steps are level, yin and yang are harmonized, wind and rain are timely, the spirits of soil and grain receive their due, and the world enjoys great peace.

The Six Signs are the signs and proofs of the six stars. The Wenchang Constellation consists of six stars located before the Dipper’s *kui*. They are the Six Signs of Heaven, governing the gathering and reckoning of the heavenly Dao. The *Historical Records* states: "The Dipper’s *kui* bears the two generals, and the three nobles assist." The Controller is the same as the Three Terraces’ Controller of the Center.

The Five Stars of the *Shangshu* serve as the Heavenly Throat and Tongue, measuring and harmonizing the primordial *qi*, regulating the four seasons, and bestowing unified governance upon the four seas to jointly rule the world.

The Four Stars of Shaowei occupy the position of the scholar-officials in the Supreme Subtlety. The first is called the Recluse, or sometimes the Erudite. The first star south of the office is the Recluse, the second is the Counselor, and the third is the Grand Master. When bright and yellow, worthy talent is promoted.

The Fifteen Stars of the Gentlemen-Officials lie northeast of the Imperial Throne, also called the Office of the Raven-Dependent Gentlemen. In the *Rites of Zhou*, they are the primary officers; in the Han, they are the Gentlemen of the Palace, the Dispersed Gentlemen, and the Gentlemen of Remonstrance—such are their duties.

The Star of Transmitted Sayings lies behind the Winnowing Basket and Tail, governing prayers and invocations, and is the office of shamans. I humbly suggest that one star corresponds to those in court, and the star preceding the office is the Grand Invocator. The Invocator is the shaman’s office.

Thus, the Three Terraces correspond to the Three Dukes, the six stars of Wenchang to the *Shangshu*, the six stars north of the Dipper to the Heavenly Throat and Tongue, and the *Shangshu* likewise to the king’s throat and tongue. Heaven has the Star of Transmitted Sayings, and man has the minister of transmitted sayings; heaven has Wang Liang driving the horses, and man has Wang Liang the skilled charioteer. Such parallels are too numerous to list exhaustively. The Literary Star...

The Eastern Wall: two stars, the celestial repository of the world’s literary arts and written works. When these stars shine brightly, the king’s Way prevails, and the land abounds with noble men.

The Five Planets Converging in Kui: see previous note on the Five Planets in Alignment.

The Chronicler at the Pillar: the easternmost star governs the recording of transgressions, bearing the likeness of the left-side historian.

The Six Jia: six stars beside the Canopy of Flowers, overseeing governance and instruction.

The Canopy of Flowers: it divides yin and yang, matches the seasons to their lords. The *Jin Treatise* states: above, nine stars form the canopy; below, nine stars form the handle of the canopy, which shelters the imperial throne. The *Book of Songs* says: “It makes its pattern in the heavens.”

The Weaving Girl: three stars, the Celestial Granddaughter, governing fruits, ferns, silk, precious treasures, and marriage rites. The *Book of Songs* says: “All day she moves seven times.” The Celestial Granddaughter weaves cloud-brocade robes.

The Martial Star: the Celestial General. Twelve stars north of Lou, governing military affairs. The central great star is the Celestial Grand Commander; the outer small stars are his officers and soldiers. When the Grand General trembles, armies rise and the great commander marches forth. When the small stars are incomplete, troops are not dispatched.

The Gentleman of the Court: one star, in the Gentleman’s Station, with the north as the armory.

The Cavalry Office: twenty-seven stars, in the region of Di. The southern stars represent the Son of Heaven; the Tiger Nobles govern the night watch.

The Tiger Warriors: one star, north of the Supreme Subtlety, the mounted guardsmen at the palace gate.

The Feathered Forest: forty-five stars, south of the Encampment, also called the Celestial Army.

The Ramparts: twelve stars, north of the Feathered Forest, the walls of the Feathered Forest. The positions of the Three Armies form the Encampment.

The Banner of Orion: nine stars, west of Shen. Also called the Celestial Banner, the bow. It governs the drawing of crossbows and watches for omens.

The Nine Pennants: nine stars to the southwest, the Son of Heaven’s banner.

The Left and Right Banners: nine stars each, north of Qianniu, the Celestial Drums. Also called the Three Martial Grand Generals, stationed between the left and right commanders. The nine stars beside the drums serve as standards. The River Drum stars are also called Qianniu—this is incorrect. The *Sui Treatise* states: the River Drum comprises three stars. The *Tang Astral Treatise* says: the River Drum is the symbol of the general.

The Celestial Lance: three stars, east of the Dipper’s handle, also called the Celestial Halberd, the celestial armory.

The Bow: nine stars, southeast of the Wolf, the Celestial Bow. It governs defense against bandits.

The Celestial Clubs: five stars, the Son of Heaven’s vanguard. They quell strife, repel adversity, and guard against the unforeseen. If one star is missing, the nation’s armies rise.

The Beckoning Star: one star, corresponding to the Dipper, through which the Hu receive the Mandate from the Central Kingdom. If it shines brightly but not upright, the Hu will not receive the Mandate.

The Celestial Stables: ten stars of the Eastern Wall, called the Celestial Trough. They govern the office of horses, post stations, and water clocks, running in tandem with sundials.

Wang Liang: the Celestial Team of Four, one star called Wang Liang, filling the fields with honored chariots and cavalry. The *Jin Treatise* states: Wang Liang is also called the Celestial Horse; Liang is also the Celestial Bridge. Thus, it may portend chariots and cavalry, or fords and bridges. I humbly say: the concurrent use of civil and martial arts is the Way of long endurance. Yet in the court, the Gentlemen of the Court number fifteen stars, while in the wilds, the Gentleman General is but one star—such is the celestial instruction to mankind.

The People’s Star: the Chamber Star as the Agricultural Auspice. Among the seven stars in the east, when the sun stands at midday on the day of the Beginning of Spring, the star is centered—this is the Agricultural Auspice. Its omen is the ripening of all grains.

The Agricultural Minister: southwest of the Southern Dipper, the Elder Farmer governs cultivation.

The Celestial Rooster: it heralds the seasons to hasten plowing.

Qianniu: Zhang Qian, riding a raft, saw the Cowherd plowing east of the river and the Weaving Girl weaving west of it. Though the tale is not attested, it benefits the world’s teachings—how much more so, as the Seven Luminaries rise in Qianniu. The Dao brings forth yang qi, as stated in the *Erya*. The River Drum and Qianniu are one star—so say Li Xun and Sun Yan. Li says: the name of the twenty-eight lunar lodgings; Sun says: the River Drum lies north of Qianniu.

The Plow’s Curve: nine stars. Six are large and bright; the remaining three are small and dim. When plowing, the handle points upward.

The Celestial Fields: nine stars, south of the Oxherd. Also said: the left horn of the Azure Dragon is the Celestial Fields.

The Water Office: southwest of the Eastern Well, governing the office of waters.

The Net Dam: nine stars east of Qianniu, damming floodwaters for irrigation.

The Four Rivers: the stars of the Jiang, the He, and the Ji.

The Dipper: five stars, south of the Palace, governing balanced measures. When it tilts upward, the world’s grain measures are uneven; when inverted, the year requires expiation.

The Inner Pestle and Mortar: seven stars governing the provision of military rations. If a guest star enters, war arises in the Eastern Prefecture. The southern three stars are the Inner Pestle; the four stars are the Inner Mortar.

The Celestial River: north of the Tail. If the four stars are incomplete or unsettled, great floods emerge.

The Celestial Boat: nine stars, also called the Chariot or the Accumulated Waters, used to ferry across impassable waters. The central star is called Accumulated Waters, for divining water disasters and governing floods and droughts. When evenly bright, the world is at peace.

The Celestial Coins: ten stars, resembling strung coins, located in the north, northwest of the Falling Gate.

The Celestial Lock: west of the Dipper’s handle, governing closure and sealing.

The Celestial Market Enclosure: According to the *Sui Treatise*, the enclosure comprises twenty-two stars, northeast of the Chamber, governing weights and measures and the gathering of crowds in markets. When the stars are numerous and radiant, the year is abundant; when sparse, the year is barren. If Mars lingers here, disloyal ministers are executed. If a comet or broom star lingers, markets are relocated and prefectures altered. The Emperor’s Seat: one star in the market’s center, the Celestial Court. When bright and lustrous, the Son of Heaven is auspicious, and his commands prevail. The Observer: one star northeast of the Emperor’s Seat, governing the surveillance of yin-yang and eunuchs. Four stars in the southwest—if not overly bright, then the assisting ministers are strong. As the *Yi* says: “At midday, there is a market; exchange and withdraw.” The people of the world follow this, as Shennong took from *Shi He* for this purpose.

The Human Stars: five southern stars called the Human Stars, governing tranquility and the harmony of the multitude near and far. Also called the Outer Stars, governing the prevention of licentiousness and dissipation.

The Old Man Star.

The South Pole Star appears at *bing* on the autumnal equinox and sets at *ding* on the vernal equinox. When visible, it heralds governance in peace and prosperity; when absent, it foretells the outbreak of war.

**Celestial Nipple**
One star north of *Kang* is called the Celestial Nipple, governing sweet dew.

**Gezai**
It resembles a flame of fire, yellow-white, rising from the earth, broad below and tapering above. When it appears, there will be harvests without plowing, yet great misfortune will follow if no earthworks are undertaken.

**Auspicious Star**
A star of virtue, also called the Celestial Gleam. It regularly emerges over states of the Dao, born at the dark and new moons, aiding the moon’s brightness. *Li* means radiance: a red directional *qi* connects with a green directional *qi*. Within the red *qi* are two yellow stars; within the green *qi* is one yellow star. These three stars together form the Auspicious Star.

**Celestial Altar**
Six stars south of *Yu Gui* are called the Celestial Altar. Its position is *kun*, its *qi* is *wei*, and its spirit is the son of Gonggong. Gou Long pacified water and earth, so sacrifices were made to his essence, which became a star.

**Celestial Millet Star**
Five stars south of the Seven Stars. Millet is the minister of agriculture, taking the chief of the five grains as its name.

**Celestial Temple**
Fifteen stars south of *Zhang* are called the Celestial Temple, the ancestral temple of the Son of Heaven. *Xu* and *Wei* govern the *qi* of ancestral temples and descendants.

I say: The merit of the people begins with Hou Ji. By exerting effort in agriculture to match Heaven, the Auspicious Star appears and sweet dew descends. The celestial signs are clear—how can this not be true?

Shaozi said: Stars belong to the day.
*The Essential Meaning* states: Day and night divide the clepsydra’s marks.
Ma Shi said: Fifty marks for day, fifty for night, based on sunrise and sunset.
Cai Yong said: Stars appear at night. The three marks after sunset and before sunrise belong to the day. Thus, day has fifty-six marks, night forty-four.
Zheng said: The sun at midday is taken as the day; when visible, the clepsydra marks fifty-three, when not, forty-five—differing from Cai by one mark.

I observe: Shaozi takes *li* as stars, stars as day, and *chen* as night. When the sun rises, stars fade; when the sun sets, stars emerge—this is plain to all. Thus, this defines day and night.

**Celestial Changes**
The five planets move in degrees, intertwining with the seven luminaries. Distributed across the four directions, they proclaim fortune and misfortune. Thus it is said: When the constant order is disrupted, stars and planets move erratically.

Jupiter moves one-twelfth of a degree per day, completing a celestial cycle in twelve years.
Mars moves one-thirty-third of a degree per day, completing a cycle in thirty-three years.
Saturn moves one-twenty-eighth of a degree per day, completing a cycle in twenty-eight years.
Venus moves one-eighth of a degree per day, completing a cycle in eight years.
Mercury moves one degree per day, completing a cycle in one year.

These are the degrees traversed by the five planets, which together with the twenty-eight lodges form the celestial body. Any deviation brings floods and droughts in the heavens, calamities among people, and the rise and fall of states. If one cultivates virtue to avert them, one may yet escape harm. Otherwise, the accumulation of minute errors leads to great consequences—how can this not be feared?

Now, the celestial changes are briefly outlined below.

**Comet Changes**
*The Gongyang Commentary* states: A star that trails at the Great Fire is a comet.
He Xiu said: It is the *qi* of disorder, symbolizing the sweeping away of the old and the establishment of the new.
The *Apocrypha* states: Its form is a *zhang* long, and comets have five colors.
If green, the feudal lords will be broken;
if red, powerful states will act wantonly;
if white, great wars will erupt.

I have examined their changes in detail:
In the time of Duke Wen of Lu, a comet appeared in the Northern Dipper, and later Chu, as a barbarian, deeply invaded the Central States.
In the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, the Marquis of Qi performed a comet-averting ritual.
Yanzi said: It cannot be done. This is Heaven’s teaching to the people; the people will scatter and flee.

During the fifteenth year of the First Emperor of Qin, comets appeared four times, some stretching across the sky. He then conquered the six states, repelled the four barbarians, and the dead piled like hemp.
In the fifth year of Emperor Wu’s *Yuanguang* reign (130 BCE), in the seventh month, a comet entered the Eastern Palace, reaching the favored ministers, and lingered for over fifty days—an omen of the eunuch Zhao Zhong’s treacherous disorder.
In the second year of Emperor Ai’s *Jianping* reign (5 BCE), in the third month, a comet emerged in *Qianniu* and remained for over seventy days, ultimately foreshadowing Wang Mang’s usurpation of the state.

In the *Jiazi* year of Emperor Lizong’s *Jingding* reign (1264), a comet appeared in *Liu* Lodge, with the pitch matching *Kuibin*, in the *Chunhuo* mansion. Its baleful flames stretched halfway across the sky for over seventy days.
Only the Secretariat Drafter again memorialized: The comet’s trespass on *Liu* Lodge is an extraordinary and ominous sign. The administrative path and the prison bureau—this is the star of the gate and its attendants. Now, with Kong Guang and Cui Lie transformed into a comet, the *Ganquan* method follows, and the stars of *Wenchang* and the Canopy are implicated. Now, with Yuan Ji and Gong Yu transformed into a comet, the stars of the Remonstrance Bureau and the Censorate are implicated. Now, with the phoenix’s song turning to the cicada’s chirp, and the steeds of the imperial stables fed with moss, they are transformed into a comet—yet there is an even greater comet. For Shang Yang’s ministerial achievements and emphasis on merit and profit, for Wang Anshi’s monopolization of power and claim that celestial changes are not to be feared—this cuts to the heart of the times’ maladies. Alas! The words were not heeded, and within a decade, the state perished. How lamentable!

**Chi You’s Banner Changes**
Resembling a comet but curved like a banner, yellow above and white below, it is the essence of Mars. When seen, the Son of Heaven will campaign against the four directions.

**Heavenly Dog Falling to Earth**
Its form is like a great meteor, with a sound like thunder, its light resembling fire, blazing in the sky. When it falls to earth, it resembles a dog. Its base is round, like a field of several *qing*. Its top is sharp, and within a thousand *li*, armies are broken and generals slain.
Meng said: It is also the essence of Venus.

Venus’s passage across the sky has variations of excess and deficiency. Excess and deficiency mean: when the sun is in the south, Venus is in the south; when the sun is in the north, Venus is in the north. When in excess, feudal lords are unsettled; in warfare, advance is auspicious, retreat inauspicious.

When the sun is in the south, Venus is in the north; when the sun is in the north, Venus is in the south. This is called "deficiency." The lord and ruler will have worries; in warfare, retreat is auspicious, advance inauspicious.

Moreover, when Venus is in the south and Jupiter in the north, it is called "the male and female." The year’s grain will be abundant. If it should emerge but does not, or should enter but does not, it will not break the state but will cause its downfall.

When Venus crosses the sky, it is the yang of the sun. When the sun rises, the stars vanish; if it appears at noon, it is called "crossing the sky." Contending with the sun for brightness is called "disordering the celestial order." The world will undergo revolution, the people will change rulers, and the populace will flee. The strong will weaken, the small will grow strong. The female ruler will prosper—Wang Mang, the Earthly Emperor—when Venus is in the Supreme Subtlety, illuminating the earth like moonlight. The Supreme Subtlety is the court of the Son of Heaven. In that year, great armies entered the Son of Heaven’s court, advancing to the Gradual Terrace, where Wang Mang’s head was struck off.

I say: These two stars do not necessarily retreat far. In the autumn of the *gengwu* year, the Heavenly Dog star fell. Soon after, a great general was slain. In the summer of the *jichou* year, Venus crossed the sky. The following year, the White Wave Rebellion arose, along with the previous *jiazi* year’s three great comet-like omens. At seventy years of age, I witnessed all these with my own eyes, and so I record them here.

Venus governs warfare; Mars governs internal strife. The moon governs punishment, and its changes have three forms. When these three lose their proper measure, there will be treacherous ministers and murderous subjects, with corpses and bloodshed in war.

The Crooked Arrow star’s omen: Nothing is straighter than an arrow, yet one who wields a crooked arrow is a wicked person. Formerly, when Xiang Yu rescued Julu, a crooked arrow flowed westward. He then buried the Qin soldiers alive and massacred Xianyang, using chaos to punish chaos.

The omen of stars gathering as portents: When water, wood, and fire combine in the Eastern Well, the prognostication says there will be external warfare and mourning. When the five planets enter the Ghost Cart, or when wood, fire, and metal combine, appearing like linked pearls in the Void, it signifies death and mourning. When three stars gather in the Dipper, generals and ministers will be executed. When fire, metal, and water combine in the *miao* asterism, or when metal and water combine in the Eastern Well, it signifies a gathering of white-clad mourners. In the time of Emperor Wen of Han, the Son of Heaven wore white four times and visited the imperial residences—this was the omen. During the Tianbao era of Tang, the five planets gathered in the Winnowing Basket and Tail. The prognostication said: "With virtue, there is celebration; without virtue, calamity." In the Zhide era, wood, fire, metal, and water gathered in the Quail’s Head, following Jupiter. Wood and fire, as yang, govern the central states; metal and water, as yin, govern the outer states. When yin and yang combine, the inner and outer states will be linked by warfare. From this, we see that the gathering of the five planets may be auspicious or inauspicious; one cannot be rigid.

The omen of meteors: During the Yuanyan era of Han, at the hour of *ru*, a meteor appeared with a head as large as a jar, over ten *zhang* long, red and white, descending from the sun toward the southeast. Some were as large as basins, others like chicken eggs, shining brightly like rain, falling until dusk. The prognostication was that the Son of Heaven would lose power, and later Wang Mang usurped the state. During the Jianwu era, over a hundred small meteors appeared, some moving from southwest to northeast in all directions. The prognostication was that the common people would flee, and later the rebellion of Gongsun Shu caused the people to scatter. A meteor as large as a cup moved westward from the Weaving Maiden; later, Empress Guanglie passed away. A meteor emerged from the Literary Prosperity asterism, its white light illuminating the earth for four *zhang*, swaying like a dragon or serpent. Soon after, Wang Shang took his own life.

The *Jin Treatise* says: It is the messenger of Heaven. Large stars are messengers of great matters; small stars, of minor matters. Those descending from above are called "flowing"; those ascending from below are called "flying." Large ones are called "rushing," which are also meteors. A rumbling sound is a sign of anger. Small meteors represent the common people. Those as large as the moon represent the ruler. When all stars shake, it is a sign of the people’s toil.

The Whispering Ear star’s omen: Beside the great star of the Western Net, a small star stirs and moves, indicating slanderous disorder at the ruler’s side.

The Celestial Slander star’s omen: It is also said that the six stars of the Curled Tongue in the north govern speech and discern flattery and slander. The star above the Curled Tongue is called the Celestial Slander.

The Ominous Spear and Withered Comet’s omen: Four stars of unusual form bring calamity—one state will be broken, and its ruler endangered. The remaining calamities will be drought, famine, and violent epidemics.

Mars’s essence as an omen: At the end of the Daye era of Sui, Mars moved retrograde into the Southern Dipper, its color red like blood, its rays flashing and shining seven or eight *chi* long. Later, Yang Xuangan rebelled, and the world fell into great chaos. During the Changshou era of Tang, Mars trespassed against the Five Feudal Lords. Hun Yi, the director of the armillary sphere, memorialized: "My fate is in metal, and the Five Feudal Lords’ position is in fire. Fire overcomes metal; I will die." Empress Wu said: "Let us perform exorcisms." She transferred him to the post of Director of the Waterways Bureau, where water generates metal, and also removed him from the position of Grand Astrologer. "You need not worry." That autumn, Hun Yi died.

Venus’s essence as an omen: The Six Bandit stars are the essence of Venus. They appear in the due south, about six *zhang* above the earth, large and red, shaped like a comet with nine rays. The Heavenly Dog is also Venus, governing warfare. The Amnesty star: A star over three *zhang* long appears south of the Dipper’s handle, extending to the Xuanyuan asterism before vanishing. The prognostication is that there will be an amnesty.

Saturn’s essence as an omen: The Xianhan star is Saturn’s essence. It appears in the due north, about six *zhang* above the earth, large and red with a blue center, or blue with a red center. Below its tail, three comets lie horizontally; it is also called the Five Remnants. A star appearing in the due east, resembling the celestial timekeeper, about six *zhang* above the earth, large and yellow.

The Wolf’s Horn omen: East of the Nine Wandering stars is a great star called the Wolf. When its color changes, there will be many bandits. The Coiled Rope: It is the prison of the lowly. It is also called the Linked Rope. Linked to the Celestial Prison, it governs the suppression of violence. When all nine stars are bright, prisons will be crowded. When seven stars are bright, there will be a great amnesty. The Axe and Drill are in use. The Returning Evil omen: It is like a star but not a star, like a cloud but not a cloud. When the Returning Serpent appears, there will be one who returns to their state.

Its omens may also be auspicious or inauspicious. The above is all recorded in the *Treatise on Astronomy*. Celestial changes correspond to the rise and fall of the times. Though stars are one in kind, their forms and colors, their constancy and mutations, their seasons and divisions—each has its own significance and interpretation. Their omens differ, yet only Yan Ziling was worthy of the omen of the visiting star, and only Chen Zhonggong could match the gathering of virtue-stars. Fu Yue was born when the asterism *Jiwei* hosted a guest star, and Zhuge Liang died when a star fell into the central encampment. Noble persons and gentlemen have never been unconnected to celestial phenomena.

Visiting stars may also undergo transformations: when their form is as large as a melon and their color is greenish-white, they portend either great floods or severe famine. When a white *qi* arises in the southwest of *Tianyuan*, cattle and horses will perish or be injured—how inauspicious indeed! During the Spring and Autumn period, comets appeared three times. At night, the constant stars would vanish, and stars would fall like rain in the darkness. Thirty-six rulers were slain, twenty-five states perished, and lords fled in such numbers that they could not preserve their altars of soil and grain.

The *Commentary* states: "When stars appear at night without clouds yet heaven weeps, what is the cause?" It is because heaven cannot bear the inversion of the noble and the base, and grieves that the common people suffer such calamities. How could celestial signs be empty correspondences?

Thus, the recluses of ancient times who concealed their virtue would observe the heavens by night and examine human affairs by day, harboring hidden concerns. The *Book of Changes* says: "The Way of Heaven diminishes the full and augments the modest; the Way of Earth alters the full and flows to the modest; ghosts and spirits harm the full and bless the modest; the Way of Man detests the full and favors the modest." Ouyang Zi said: "This is the sage’s ultimate discourse on the relationship between heaven and man."

Though the *Spring and Autumn Annals* records solar eclipses and celestial changes, Confucius never explained their causes, saying: "The minds of heaven, earth, and ghosts and spirits cannot be known; what can be known is only man." When the sun reaches its zenith, the bell tolls in reverse; prosperity and decline alternate. Heaven—I do not know it; I see only its waxing and waning in things. When plants and trees reach maturity, they wither and fall; when things are lowly, they advance and flow onward. Earth—I do not know it; I see only its transformations and flows in things. Those among men who are arrogant and excessive meet with misfortune, while those who are restrained and frugal enjoy good fortune. Ghosts and spirits—I do not know them; I see only the fortunes and misfortunes of men.

Since the minds of heaven, earth, and ghosts and spirits cannot be known, we infer them from their manifestations in things. Thus, we base our words on what is visible in their movements: we speak of waxing and waning, of transformation and flow, of fortune and misfortune. But for man, what can be known is straightforward—we speak directly of likes and dislikes. The known and the unknown are expressed in different terms, yet when weighed together, they differ not from man. Because they are unknowable, we should revere and keep them at a distance. Because they differ not from man, we need only cultivate our human affairs. Never has a man’s heart been content yet heaven’s intent angered; never has human principle been perverse yet the Way of Heaven been compliant.

Alas! After the sages passed, heterodoxies arose. Since the Qin and Han dynasties, scholars have long been deluded by theories of calamities and anomalies. The doctrines of celestial patterns and the Five Phases have proliferated endlessly. My confusion cannot but differ from that of the *Spring and Autumn Annals*. Ouyang [Xiu] held that what is knowable in man is one, while the unknowable in heaven, earth, and ghosts and spirits are three. When the one is exhausted, the three are contained within. How, then, should the noble person conduct themselves? Only by cultivating the self and awaiting destiny.

*Tian Yuan Fa Wei*, Scroll Eight, concludes.

#25 "Water Palace" (*Shuǐfǔ*), in the *Siku Quanshu* edition: "Water Palace Star" (*Shuǐfǔ xīng*).
#26 "Net Weir" (*Luóyàn*), in the *Siku Quanshu* edition: "Net Weir Star" (*Luóyàn xīng*).
#27 "Four Waterways" (*Sìdú*), in the *Siku Quanshu* edition: "Four Waterways Star" (*Sìdú xīng*).
#28 "Dipper" (*Dǒu*), in the *Siku Quanshu* edition: "Dipper Star" (*Dǒu xīng*).
#29 "Palace" (*Guān*), in the *Siku Quanshu* edition: "Palace" (*Gōng*).
#30 "Auspicious" (*Jí*), originally "Ancient" (*Gǔ*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#31 "Dross" (*Gézǐ*), in the *Siku Quanshu* edition: "Luminous Vapor" (*Gézé*); "Misfortune" (*Jiù*), originally "Guest" (*Kè*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#32 "Regret" (*Xī*), originally "Borrow" (*Jiè*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#33 "Qin" (*Qín*), originally "Offer" (*Fèng*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#34 "Five" (*Wǔ*), originally "King" (*Wáng*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#35 "Constrained" (*Jū*), originally "Dogwood" (*Jǔ*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#36 "Heaven" (*Tiān*), originally "Foot" (*Chǐ*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
#37 "Pierce" (*Zuān*), in the *Siku Quanshu* edition: "Gild" (*Dù*).

*Tian Yuan Fa Wei*, Scroll Nine.
Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai; collated by Fang Hui Wanli of Xugu.

**Lesser Yin**

In the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, Duke of Jin asked Bo Xia: "What is meant by *chén*?" He replied: "*Chén* is the conjunction of the sun and moon. *Chén* is time." I humbly observe: From the eleventh month to the tenth month, from *zǐ* to *hài*, the sun and moon converge at fixed times, hence the term *chén*. In the heavens, this manifests as the twenty-eight constellations, visible to the eye, thus described in terms of stars. Yet their circulation through the four seasons and eight nodes is formless *qi*, dispersed across the great void—seemingly tangible yet intangible—all governed by *chén*.

Master Shao said: "*Chén* are the stars in the sky, divided into twelve segments, which are the twelve *chén*, the celestial firmament. The sun, moon, and stars are four distinct entities. Here, *chén* refers to the degrees within each segment. For instance, when the sun and moon lodge in *Jiǎo* at a certain degree, that lodging place is *chén*. Thus it is said: 'The conjunction of the sun and moon is called *chén*.'"

Master Wei of the Xiang clan said: "If referring to the stellar bodies themselves, they are called *stars*. When the sun and moon meet at a star, it is named *constellation*, *chén*, *station*, *mansion*, or *conjunction*. If not based on their meeting with a constellation, then referring to the stellar bodies themselves, they are simply *stars*."

The *Essential Meaning of the Book of Documents* states: "The celestial pole is called the Northern *Chén*; the five planets include the *Chén* star; there are also the twelve stations and the solar *chén*." It further says: "*Chén* number twelve; the conjunction of the sun and moon is called *chén*. *Chén* is the body of Heaven, the *qi* of no-thing."

Master Zhang said: "*Chén* are the twelve counted from Earth. The *qi* of no-thing is invisible, yet it becomes manifest through the conjunction of the sun and moon. Because it is invisible, it is *yin*; the *yin* of Heaven is Heaven’s body, the foundation of Heaven’s existence. Counted from Earth, it is Heaven’s Earth."

It also says: "The *yin* within *yin* is *chén*, the celestial firmament. *Qi* is one, yet its ruler is *qián*. *Chén* is the *qi* of no-thing, the body of Heaven. Thus it is called the celestial firmament, for *chén* is to Heaven as soil is to Earth. *Chén* is the body of Heaven; soil is the body of Earth. *Chén* is the *qi* of no-thing, invisible. Yet by observing the stars, we know its vast emptiness can contain all things. Soil is a tangible substance, visible. By observing mountains, we further know its capacity to bear all things. Soil is the great substance; *chén* is the great void. The sun, moon, and stars are lodged within it. Though *chén* is invisible, Heaven is ever visible day and night, hence the one that is not used is the one that is revered."

It further says: "*Chén* reaching the sun is generation; the sun reaching *chén* is function. Following is generation, while reversing is function. *Chén* reaching the sun refers to Heaven’s leftward movement, which is harmonious, distributing *qi* to generate things. The sun reaching *chén* refers to the sun’s rightward movement, which is contrary, transforming *qi* to serve the seasons. Thus the seasons can be retroactively deduced, and things naturally come to fruition."

Master Zi Yun said: "Patrolling the six *jiǎ*, encountering the Dipper—this speaks of the correspondence between Heaven and the sun." It also says: "Stars are the surplus essence of the sun; *chén* are the surplus *qi* of the moon. The sun is the essence of *yang*, the spirit-soul of Heaven. The moon is the essence of *yin*, the *qi*-po of Heaven. Stars are the surplus essence of *yang*; *chén* are the surplus *qi* of *yin*. Thus stars are the spirits of Heaven, while *chén* are the body of Heaven. The sun and moon in the sky are like a person’s true heart and life-gate, the root of *yin* and *yang*. The five planets are like the five viscera; the myriad stars are like the limbs, bones, essence, and blood. *Chén* to Heaven is as the body and *po* are to humans."

It further says: "Heaven has the sun, moon, stars, and *chén*, making five. Earth has the five phases—metal, wood, water, and fire—making five. *Chén*, the *yin* within *yang*, cannot change; thus a day has twelve *chén*, which are invisible. *Chén* is the body of Heaven. *Chén* to Heaven is as soil is to Earth. Heaven governs function—does it have spirit? *Chén* cannot exhaust Heaven, unlike soil, which exhausts Earth. Beyond *chén* is another name for Heaven; soil is what constitutes Earth."

Master Hanshang said: "The moon is born from the sun’s illumination; the multitude of stars are bathed in its radiance, their light transformed by water. The Three Luminaries share the same form, *yin* and *yang* paired, their body being *chén*." It also says: "Heaven transforms one into four. The four have form, while the one is formless."

Master Zhang said: "The sun, moon, and stars constitute Heaven’s body. The four completed form the body of the Great Ultimate, which retreats and hides among the four yet remains invisible. Thus the sun, moon, stars, and Heaven make five; without the sun, moon, and stars, there is no Heaven. The four have form, thus constituting shape. The one is formless, retreating into concealment. Speaking of five, it must return to Heaven; speaking of ten, it must return to Earth. Five corresponds to the Ultimate of Non-Being; ten corresponds to the Ultimate of Being. It may also be said: remove the one, and the four cease to exist."

Heaven’s body numbers four, with one unused. The invisibility of Heaven’s *chén* arises because *chén* is Heaven’s body, hence unseen. Its color is deep blue; its form is vast and undifferentiated. Among the four, only the sun, moon, and stars are distinctly visible. Thus we know that among the five, one is retained as the body, hence four are used. Among the four, one is retained as the body, hence three are used. In this way, five becomes the formless one, exemplifying spontaneity. *Chén* is the unused one, exemplifying the Dao."

The *Great Mystery* uses one *yuan* to govern three directions, called Heaven, Earth, and Human—this conveys the same idea. One season consists of three months; one month consists of thirty days, both excluding the *chén* count. A season originally has four months but uses only three; a month originally has forty days but uses only thirty. Both exclude one *chén*, using three while leaving one unused. Heaven has three *chén*; Heaven is three, Earth is four. Heaven encompasses Earth’s surplus; Earth has the four phases.

Dividing thus, seven are employed. This is why celestial *chén* are unseen, and earthly waters remain ever concealed. Master Zhu said: "The void where no stars appear is called *chén*." I humbly observe: The *Book of Changes* speaks of five celestial numbers, while Master Shao speaks of four celestial images. From whence do these four arise? Fundamentally, from one yin and one yang. One yang divides into two, yielding the images of Greater Yang and Lesser Yang—these are the sun and stars. One yin divides into two, yielding the images of Greater Yin and Lesser Yin—these are the moon and *chén*. Sun, moon, stars, and *chén* together form the four celestial images, none of which may be lacking. Yet Master Shao calls *chén* the celestial body, while Master Zhu says *chén* is the starless void that does not move. If so, then one *chén* serves two functions: as Lesser Yin and as the celestial body. Is this permissible? The answer is no. The celestial numbers originally comprise five, one being the Ultimate Non-Polar, and four □ forming the four images. Within these four images, one is embodied while three are employed. Lesser Yin becomes the body, upon which the sun, moon, and stars depend. As for the claim that *chén* is the starless void: *chén* is originally undifferentiated and unfathomable, the ultimate of embodiment. The sages designated the places where the sun and moon conjoin as *chén*. Thus, within the formless, no image is unencompassed. Its immobility, residing in the north as the pivot of the Pole Star, is called the Northern *Chén*. Therefore, while Lesser Yin is *chén* and constitutes the celestial body, the Northern *Chén* resides at the center of the celestial body and presides over the twelve *chén*. Hence, *chén* are used to distinguish directions, called the twelve stations; *chén* encompass stars and lands, called the twelve fields; *chén* encompass the twelve branches, twelve years, and twelve months—all are *chén*. Throughout the cosmos, vast or minute, high or low, none is not enclosed within them. For details, see the following section.

**Celestial Pivot**
