In the north there is *chén*, serving as the hub and pivot. Abiding at the center, unmoving, it revolves the Dipper’s handle externally to establish the four seasons and align the seven governances. The *Western Record* states: "Among the central palace’s stars of the celestial pole, the brightest one is the constant abode of the Great One." I say: The One is the beginning of qi and numbers, without material form. That it "constantly abides" signifies the principle of residing at the center, unmoving. The *Eastern Record* states: "The Northern *Chén* Star unites primordial radiance, erecting the imperial form, operating the mechanism, conferring measures, and unfurling the hundred spirits. The three steps and nine ranks, the Dipper’s balance and the Supreme Subtlety, the *shetis* and their kind—one hundred twenty constellations—along with the twenty-eight lodges, each arrayed in their places, correspond below to the twelve branches. Heaven and Earth establish their positions, and the images of the stars and constellations are complete." I say: The nine words—unite, suspend, erect, operate, confer, unfurl, belong, array, and correspond—are most worthy of contemplation. They signify that the *chén*-pole encompasses all images of Heaven, Earth, stars, and constellations within it, hence it is said to be complete. When condensed, it is primordial qi; when dispersed, it becomes stellar radiance; when unfurled, it is the hundred spirits; when erected, it is the Dipper’s balance. Ban Gu called it *sheti*. Directly aligned with the Dipper’s handle, it establishes the seasonal nodes, hence it is also called the Celestial Pivot. The twenty-eight lodges are arrayed within the twelve *chén*, and the twelve *chén* are governed by the Northern *Chén*. Thus it is said: "Abiding in its place, all stars share it." The *Er Ya* commentary states: "The Dipper’s handle establishes the *chén* to rectify the four seasons, hence it is called *chén*. The Northern *Chén* encompasses the twelve *chén* within it. The Dipper’s handle points to the twelve *chén*." Many classics speak of the Northern Dipper but not the Northern *Chén*, for *chén* resides at the center, wu-wei, concealed within function. Thus, there is no visible marker to point to. What can be pointed to and spoken of is merely the Dipper’s handle as it establishes the *chén*. When the Northern Dipper is mentioned, the Northern *Chén* is thereby understood. Master Cheng said: "The Northern *Chén* is inherently unmoving and serves as the master of qi and the most venerable of stars. Therefore, all stars encircle and orient toward it from all sides." I have examined and weighed the discourses of former masters and arrived at this understanding: The Northern *Chén*, unmoving, is the pivot of Heaven. Master Zhu said: "The ancients took this as the pole and could not do without a marker. Thus, they selected a small star nearby and called it the Pole Star." Someone asked: "Does the Pole Star move?" He replied: "It does move, but as it is near the *chén*, though it moves, the motion is imperceptible. Now, if one observes the Pole Star through a tube, one sees it move, but it remains within the tube, never moving beyond. Formerly, people said the Northern Pole was unmoving. By the Song dynasty, people had determined that the Northern Pole was merely near the *chén*, while the Pole Star still moved." Former accounts all took the "silken star" as the celestial pole, residing due north as the unmoving heart of Heaven. Now, upon verification, the celestial pole also revolves day and night. The immovable point lies within one and a half degrees of the Pole Star. Thus, in an armillary sphere, the central axle is fixed, unmoving, to represent the heart of Heaven. I observe: The five stars of the Northern Pole reside within the Purple Tenuity Enclosure. The northernmost star lies at the celestial heart. Ninety-one degrees separate it from each of the four directions. Ninety-one degrees of Heaven total three hundred sixty-five and one-quarter degrees. The four directions converge like spokes, and the *chén*-star resides at the center—within the northernmost star, where there is no star. All stars share it: the Northern *Chén* is to Heaven as the heart is to the human body—the master of the entire form. Hands, feet, ears, eyes, blood, vessels, skin, and flesh—none is unrelated. The world is nothing but people, yet the lord faces south to serve as their master. The celestial body is nothing but *chén*, yet the Northern *Chén* resides at the center to serve as its master. Even the degrees of the celestial sphere and the myriad beings in their multitude—none is not interconnected through it.

Thus, the unmoving *chén* serves as the root of all movement. Hence it is said: the formless governs the formed. This "unused one" is the ultimate of the limitless, descending to reside within us. Ah! How profound!

Master Zhu said: The North Pole is the pivot of Heaven, for it dwells at the center—hence it is called the North Pole. The South Pole lies at the center beneath the earth, directly opposite the North Pole. Though Heaven revolves, Earth remains unmoving at the center. Heaven’s form turns like a potter’s wheel, with the Pole as a crossbar fixed within it, its two ends firmly set. One end lies above the north—this is the North Pole. The other lies above the south—this is the South Pole.

The Great One, like an imperial throne within the Purple Tenuity, bears the title of Northern *Chén*. At its very center, in the starless void, lies the slightest point of stillness, which observers take as the Pole. Like the hub of a wheel, the navel of a millstone, Heaven circles Earth in ceaseless leftward rotation. Only this, its pivot, remains unmoving—fixed at the two ends of south and north. The South Pole dips thirty-six degrees below the earth, so that within its seventy-two-degree circuit, it is perpetually hidden from view. The *Old Tang History* records: travelers at sea once saw several bright stars beneath the South Pole, all within its seventy-two-degree span. The North Pole rises thirty-six degrees above the earth, so that within its seventy-two-degree circuit, it is perpetually visible. The star of the North Pole lies precisely within this ever-visible seventy-two-degree span, steadfast in its place, unmoving. Around it, the circumpolar stars revolve leftward with the turning sky. The sun and moon, as weft threads, move rightward in alternation, appearing and vanishing as if circling back toward it.

Monk Yixing of the Tang observed: the distance between north and south spans over eighty thousand *li*. In the kingdom of Linyi to the south, the North Pole stands seventeen degrees high; at the Protectorate of Annan, it stands twenty-one degrees high. Other regions differ. The *Great History of the South* records... and others who reached the sea, gazing southward beneath the Old Man Star, saw a dazzling array of stars—none of which bore names in ancient texts. Someone asked: "When the South Pole appears, the Old Man Star brings longevity—is this the South Pole?" Master Zhu’s reply explains: the South Pole is not visible; rather, there is a distinct Old Man Star in the south. When the South Pole rises high, the Old Man Star emerges into view.

The Great One is the imperial seat, like a sovereign enthroned at the North Pole. Like an imperial capital within the Purple Tenuity, it lies within the seventy-two-degree span that is ever visible and never hidden—hence its title of Northern *Chén*. The *Former Records* state: of the stars at the celestial pole, the brightest is where the Great One eternally resides. The Great One, Heavenly August, Great Emperor of the North *Chén*—by these names it establishes the celestial measures, and it is also the Purple Tenuity Palace. The Celestial Emperor dwells at the center; "purple" signifies this central palace. Within this palace, the divine diagrams and the opening and closing of yin and yang all reside.

Master Zhu said: this *chén* remains ever in its place. Heaven’s form revolves day and night without cease, and this serves as its pivot. Like the hub of a wheel or the navel of a millstone, though it may seem to move, it cannot—it is not that it wills itself to be unmoving. Zhu Hanshang said: the *chén* is Heaven’s pivot, yet the unmoving point lies beyond it. Beneath the Pole Star, the sages spoke of it as the North *Chén*. Astronomers, however, refer to the point beneath the Pole Star—this is but a difference in precision.

Someone asked: "Why is the North *Chén* called Heaven’s pivot?" The answer: Heaven is round and moves, enveloping Earth. Earth is square and still, resting at Heaven’s center. Thus, Heaven’s form half covers the earth above and half encircles it below, revolving leftward without end. Only this point remains unmoving—hence it is called the pivot. All other stars, along with the twenty-eight lunar lodges, share the same motion.

Master Zhu replied: consider the Supreme Subtlety in Wings, the Celestial Market in Tail, or the Dragging Stars in Neck—their southern distance from the celestial equator is slight, while their northern distance from the celestial pole is vast. Thus, they cannot remain unmoving but must revolve with the circumpolar stars. Their movements—east or west, hidden or visible—follow fixed degrees. Gazing upward, one sees not a moment’s pause. Yet those within the Purple Tenuity remain in their places, unmoving.

If this were so, then one Heaven would have four pivots, one wheel four hubs, one millstone four navels. The slightest shift in position would shatter it like broken spokes or shattered tiles—such a day would come swiftly. How then could motion endure without end?

Hu Wufeng remarked: there are said to be three unmoving Pole Stars—this is quite incomprehensible. If Heaven’s motion is like a turning millstone, then the Pole Star is merely the central stem, which does not move. If there were three unmoving stars, it could not turn.

Master Cai of Xishan asked: "The Pole Star lies at Heaven’s center, and all directions—east, west, south, north—are aligned by it. Yet why does the Pole Star always lie above them?" Master Zhu had no answer at first. Later, he reflected: it is merely that one faces away from the Pole Star. If the Pole Star is north, then south has no fixed position.

The *Gongyang Commentary* states: the North *Chén* is the Great *Chén*, ever abiding in its place. Those who are lost, not knowing east from west, look to it. Yongjia’s Master Zheng said: the North Pole resides at Heaven’s center yet always lies to the north of people, for Heaven’s form inclines northward. Some say: the Dipper’s handle can point to the cardinal directions, but the *chén* serves no such purpose for the sun’s observation. Yet by its position, one knows where the *chén* lies.

The *Book of Documents* commentary says: the sun and moon are Heaven’s messengers; the stars and constellations are Heaven’s markers. One moves left, the other right, weaving the warp and weft. From the Dipper’s handle’s direction, it is called *jian*.

From where the sun and moon conjoin, it is called *chén*. As the Dipper’s handle shifts left and the sun and moon drift right, the *chén* and *jiàn* thus invariably align. When the moon’s motion reaches the sun, their conjunction forms the *chén*. The *chén* itself has no fixed form but is revealed through the stars—hence the celestial standard’s auspiciousness. When the *chén* lies in *Xingji*, moving from north to west, the earthly standard aligns with Heaven, and the *Xuangan* corrects it at *Shiwei*. The western quadrant’s *Jianglou*, *Daliang*, and *Shishen*, the southern quadrant’s *Chunshou*, *Chunhuo*, and *Chunwei*, and the eastern quadrant’s *Shouxing*, *Dahuo*, and *Ximu*—their new moons may thus be known.

The *Chunqiu Wai Zhuan* states: "When the sun and moon lodge in the Celestial Temple," it refers to the month of *jiàn-yín*. When the *chén* is in *Kezī*, it is the month of *jiàn-hài*. When the *chén* is in the *Tianhan*, the sun and moon conjoin at *Longtang*. Thus, by the new moon’s position, one knows the *chén*; by the *chén*’s conjunction, one knows the Dipper’s *jiàn*.

In my humble opinion: the Dipper’s handle and the North *Chén* are interconnected. The North *Chén* governs the twelve *chén*, while the Dipper’s handle points to them. Moreover, the *Gongyang Commentary* states: *Dahuo* is the Great *Chén*—here, *Jiazhong* arises from the *qi* of *Fang* and *Xin*, serving as the Celestial Emperor’s Bright Hall, also called the Celestial Palace. This is not comparable to the North *Chén*’s Great *Chén*. For instance, in the first month of *jiàn-yín*, the *chén* lies in the northeast, while the sun and moon conjoin in the northwest at *hài*—their *qi* harmonize because *yín* and *hài* align. The sun and moon always follow this pattern.

The Dipper’s monthly pointer is called *jiàn-dǒu*. Its first star is the *Kui*, the fourth the *Heng*, and the seventh the *Biao*. The *jiàn* at dusk is determined by the *Biao*, which belongs to *yin*; at midnight, the *jiàn* is determined by the *Heng*, which stands at the center of the three *jiàn*; the *Kui* belongs to *yang*. Calendar scholars use the sequence of *jiàn*, *chú*, *mǎn*, *píng*, *dìng*, *zhí*, *pò*, *wēi*, *chéng*, *shōu*, *kāi*, and *bì*—twelve days in total—repeating in cycles, observing their correspondences to determine auspiciousness and inauspiciousness. With each month’s transition, the solar terms alternate, and two values align with the day. For example, in the first month, the *yín* day corresponds to *jiàn*, the *mǎo* day to the second, and the *chén* day to the third, aligning with the Dipper’s pointer.

The *Yi Zhuan* states: "The number of the Great Expansion is fifty; its use is forty-nine. The one is the Supreme Ultimate." (Han scholar Ma Rong said: "The *Yi* contains the Supreme Ultimate, referring to the North *Chén*. The Supreme Ultimate generates the Two Modes; the Two Modes generate the sun and moon; the sun and moon generate the four seasons; the four seasons generate the Five Phases; the Five Phases generate the twelve months; the twelve months generate the twenty-four *qi*. The North *Chén* abides at the center, unmoving.")

Someone objects: "If so, then the Supreme Ultimate has this North Star to which it may be pointed. Yet Master Zhou [Dunyi] speaks of the Ultimateless (*wuji*) and yet the Supreme Ultimate—this seems not to be the case."

I respond: The Supreme Ultimate is without sound or scent, the most subtle within which resides the most manifest principle. The North Star is the most central and ultimate, within its visible form lies the most subtle principle. Substance and function share a single origin; the manifest and the subtle are without separation. Thus the North Star, abiding at the center unmoving, can generate the Two Modes, the sun and moon, the four seasons, the Five Phases, the twelve months, and the twenty-four *qi*. The Supreme Ultimate, in its utmost quiescence, moves and can generate the Two Modes, the Four Images, the Eight Trigrams—each thing possesses its own Supreme Ultimate.

The North Star of Heaven subtly possesses form, resembling the Supreme Ultimate of the Master [Confucius]. The Supreme Ultimate of the Master is thus formless yet possesses form. The North Star of Heaven is the progenitor of *qi*, yet principle courses within it. The Supreme Ultimate is the progenitor of principle, yet *qi* courses within it. These may be of a single Dao.

Master Shao [Yong] said: "Heaven swirls indistinct above, beyond measure—thus we observe the Dipper’s numbers to divine the heavens. The Dipper’s establishment is Heaven’s movement. The *kui* establishes *zǐ*, the handle establishes *yín*; the stars take *yín* as day." The Dipper has seven stars, so day and night are divided into no more than seven parts.

Master Zhang said: "That the stars take *yín* as day is central. The stars take *yín* as dawn and *xū* as dusk. The sun takes *mǎo* and *yǒu* as midpoints, thus twelve divisions using seven. The stars take *yín* and *xū* as limits, thus ten divisions using seven."

I elaborate: The Way of Heaven revolves leftward, taking the celestial stems as its body. The *qi* of no-thing is not visible—within its indistinctness, only the stars may be pointed to. The sun, moon, and five planets move rightward from the earth. The Dipper’s handle establishes the four seasons in balance.

The number of the Great Expansion is fifty; one is the Supreme Ultimate. Forty-nine is seven times seven, divided and used, each entering its proper place. One is the body of Heaven, one is the Seven Governors, one resides at the center—this is the Northern Dipper. Four arrayed in the four directions—these are the seven lodges. Thus Heaven and Earth, the four directions, take the Dipper as their pivot. Heaven’s movement through the four seasons begins from the Dipper and points forth. When the Dipper is correct, the seasons are correct; when the seasons are correct, the Dipper is correct. Thus calendars have methods of adjustment, but the Dipper has no deviation in its degrees. Those who skillfully regulate the calendar verify it against the Dipper alone.

The Northern Dipper’s seven stars lie within the Supreme Subtlety. The four stars of the *kui* form the *zōngjī*, the three stars of the handle form the Jade Balance. The Dipper is the symbol of the ruler, the master of decrees. The Assistant Star aids *Kāiyáng*, assisting the Dipper’s success—it is the symbol of the prime minister.

From one to four is the *kui*; from five to seven is the handle. The first is Celestial Pivot; the second, *Zōng*; the third, *Jī*; the fourth, *Quán*; the fifth, Jade Balance; the sixth, *Kāiyáng*; the seventh, *Yáoguāng*.

The Pivot is Heaven, symbol of the Son of Heaven, the power of yang. *Zōng* is Earth, symbol of the female ruler, the punishment of yin. *Jī* is Man, the Decree Star, governing fire. *Quán* is Time, the Punitive Star, governing water. The Jade Balance is Sound, the Killing Star, governing earth. *Kāiyáng* is the Pitchpipe, the Perilous Star, governing wood and the Celestial Granary of the Five Grains. *Yáoguāng* is the Star, also called the Responsive Star, governing metal.

The Assistant Star, one in number, aids *Kāiyáng*, assisting the Dipper’s success and governing the correction of peril and the rectification of imbalance.

Someone asks: "The Dipper has seven stars, and with the Assistant Star, eight. Yet astrologers speak of the Dipper having nine stars, governing the Nine Provinces—why is this?" Another says: "The *Treatise on Celestial Offices* mentions *Mèngkè* and *Shètí*. *Shètí* is a star’s name, following the Dipper’s handle to establish the twelve months. If this star is included to make nine, is this acceptable?"

I reply: There is no clear evidence for this; it cannot be followed. Master Zhang Heng, profound in his knowledge of Heaven, stated that the Northern Dipper and the four palace stars together make five sevens—thus the seven stars are the Dipper, and this cannot be increased.

The learning of blind musicians and historians in the *Yi* has degenerated into absurdity.

Scholars may well suspend judgment where doubt remains. Master Ouyang said: "The relationship between Heaven and humanity is distant indeed. To have a practitioner of a single art deploy calculations and accumulated fractions, abandoning the classics to follow the historians in seeking concordance—is this not arduous?"

The *Treatise on Celestial Offices* states: "The so-called *xuanji* and jade balance align the Seven Governors. The handle connects to *Dragon’s Horn*, the balance rests upon *Southern Dipper*, and the *Fish-trap* leans upon *Triaster’s* head." The *Former Records* say: "The handle is used for dusk alignment, the handle extending from *Huá* to the southwest. The balance is used for midnight alignment, the balance centered between the *Yellow River* and *Jǐ* in the Central States. The *Fish-trap* is used for dawn alignment, the *Fish-trap* in the lands east of the *Sea* and *Dài*. *Dān* means ‘to connect.’ *Dragon’s Horn* is *Celestial Fields*. The seven eastern lodges together form the shape of a dragon. *Yīn* means ‘center.’ The jade balance resides in the middle, with the northern and southern dippers aligned. The seventh star of the Dipper’s handle is the *Tail*, which belongs to *yin*. At dusk, *yin* occupies its position, thus governing the southwest. The balance star resides in the center; at dusk, the handle aligns with *yín*, and at midnight, the balance also aligns with *yín*. The first star of the *Fish-trap* belongs to *yang*, thus governing the northeast. This is why the Dipper’s handle connects to the star *Dragon’s Horn* in the east."

The *Essential Principles* states: "What the Dipper aligns with are the earthly branches. Where the branches converge are the celestial stations. The Dipper and the branches unite." Note: The Dipper’s handle aligns with the twelve earthly branches, while the sun’s body moves through the twelve months, revolving rightward in conjunction with the lunar lodges. However, what the Dipper aligns with are the twelve earthly branches on earth, hence the terms *zǐ*, *chǒu*, and so forth. The branches are the conjunctions of the sun and moon, which occur in the twelve celestial stations, hence the terms *Kuízī*, *Jiànglóu*, and so forth. The twelve pitch-pipes serve as tubes for awaiting *qi*. The *yin* and *yang* of sound each have their correspondences, as with *Huángzhōng* aligning with the eleventh month, *jiànzǐ*, and *Dàlǚ* with the twelfth month, *jiànchǒu*, and so on. Thus, the Dipper and the branches unite with the lodges to complete the conjunctions of the sun and moon. The Dipper’s stars also revolve with the turning of Heaven."

Master Kong said: "The Dipper’s stars, in one day and night, also revolve with Heaven, completing one circuit and moving one degree across the sky." The sage-kings observed the Dipper’s alignment to command the four seasons and divide the twelve monthly conjunctions. The *Book of Songs* says: "In the north there is the Dipper, its western handle raised." Master Zhu said: "The Northern Dipper is ever visible, never concealed. The Southern Dipper’s handle invariably points west. If the Northern Dipper’s handle points west, it is also autumn." Master Dong said: "The Dipper has four stars forming its bowl, and three stars forming its handle, which hangs down and is raised. The Dipper revolves with the turning of Heaven, and in each of the four seasons, it has its own demarcated region. Thus, the *Spring and Autumn Annals* states: ‘The Dipper has its encircling domain.’"

Master Li Zijian said: "The Northern Dipper is Heaven’s tongue and throat, measuring and harmonizing the primordial *qi* to regulate the four seasons." The *Great Mystery* also says: "The *yin* substance is the Northern Dipper; at night, it measures *yin*. It speaks of the *yin* night substance being rectified by the Northern Dipper, which calendars the sun and moon to determine the seasons and complete the year." Moreover, the North Pole and South Pole stand opposite each other as the *Pivot Stars*. The South Pole is concealed while the North Pole is visible, and people often speak of the visible one because it resides at the center of Heaven. The Northern Dipper has seven stars, an odd number, while the Southern Dipper has six stars, an even number—thus does Heaven display such artistry.

The *Commentary* states: "When the branch is at the Dipper’s handle, the sun and moon conjoin at the Southern Dipper." Its stars are also divided into north and south: the two southern stars are called *Great Bridge*, the two central stars *Celestial Minister*, and the two northern stars *Celestial Court*. When the Dipper’s stars shine brightly, the kingly *Way* is harmonious and peaceful. Liu Xiang said: "The Northern Dipper is the noble star, the image of the ruler." *Heaven’s Origins Unfolded*, Scroll Nine, concludes.

**The Supreme Ultimate, Scroll Two: Movement and Stillness, Stillness and Movement**
**Discerning Directions, Scroll Three**
**The Mysterious and Undifferentiated, Scroll Four**
**Division into Two, Scroll Five**
**Derivation of the Five, Scroll Six**
**Observing Images, Scroll Seven**
**The Greater Yin, Scroll Eight**
**The Lesser Yang, Scroll Nine**
**The Lesser Yin, Scroll Ten**
**The Celestial Pivot, Scroll Eleven**
**Yearly Conjunctions and Qi Governance, Scroll Twelve**
**Hexagram Qi and the Mysterious Essence, Scroll Thirteen**
**Waxing and Waning, Scroll Fourteen**
**Images and Numbers, Scroll Fifteen**
**Prior and Posterior, Left and Right, Scroll Sixteen**
**The Two Centers and Yang’s Return, Scroll Seventeen**
**The Origin of Numbers, Scroll Eighteen**
**Ghosts and Spirits, Scroll Nineteen**
**Transformations**
**Unfolding the Subtleties of the Celestial Origin, Scroll Twenty**
*Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai; Collated by Xu Gu Fang Hui and Wan Li*

The yearly conjunctions begin with twelve months, for six and six pair to form twelve. According to Shao Kangjie’s numerology, the daily, yearly, and epochal cycles all employ twelve: thus, one day has twelve hours, one year twelve months, one epoch twelve generations, and one great era twelve conjunctions. This encompasses past and present without exception. From one hour, one may infer a day; from one day, a month; from one month, a year; and from one year, one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred years. Extending this upward, how could it be limited merely to the twelve months of a year?

The twelve sovereign hexagrams outline the general principles, while the *Supreme Principles of the Sublime World* (*Huangji jingshi*) exhausts their ultimate depths. The *Sublime World Through the Ages* is a work of calendrical computation, using the twelve sovereign hexagrams to govern the twelve conjunctions, thereby fixing the seasonal nodes. Within this framework, one deduces the waxing and waning of fortune, simply by doubling the calculations. The hexagram qi of Jing Fang employs six days and seven fen, and Shao Kangjie likewise uses six days and seven fen. Kangjie’s and Jing Fang’s *Changes* are one and the same work, unrelated to the *Book of Changes*.

Master Zheng said: "The movements of the sun and moon form twelve conjunctions in a year. The sage-kings followed these conjunctions to establish the great reckoning, observing their meetings to designate the four seasons." The hexagram qi system outlines its essentials, the *Great Mystery* (*Taixuan*) refines its details, and the *Sublime World* elaborates its wonders. Thereafter, the workings of Heaven and Earth, sun and moon, stars and epochs, and the rise and fall of emperors and kings may all be deduced.

In years, it is reckoned by waxing and waning; in months, by leaps and bounds; in days, by division into day and night.

The Grand Historian said: "The great cycle undergoes a minor change every thirty years, a medium change every hundred years, and a major change every five hundred years. Three major changes make one epoch, and three epochs complete the great reckoning—such are the grand numbers."

Master Shao said: "One great era governs twelve conjunctions, three hundred sixty days, and four thousand three hundred twenty generations, just as one year governs twelve months, three hundred sixty days, and four thousand three hundred twenty hours. The day is the era, the origin—its number is one. One year completes one cycle. The month is the conjunction, the meeting—its number is twelve. Twelve years complete twelve cycles. The star is the epoch, the movement of time, revolving leftward with the heavens, completing three hundred sixty cycles in a year. The hour is the generation, the end of change. One day has twelve hours, accumulating to four thousand three hundred twenty hours in a year. One generation spans thirty years, so twelve generations total one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred years—this is the number of one great era. Within the great transformation, one era is like one year. From the era of the era to the hour of the era, from the era of the hour to the hour of the hour, the numbers are exhausted, and exhaustion brings change. The *Sublime World* records only the numbers of one era, yet from this, the numbers of Heaven and Earth may be known.

One era has one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred years; one conjunction, one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred months; one epoch, one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred days; one generation, one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred hours—all are natural numbers, not artificially contrived.

It is also said: The first six conjunctions are waxing, the latter six waning. This is like a year, where from *zi* to *si* is waxing, and from *wu* to *hai* is waning. Things open at the seventy-fifth star, like the *Jingzhe* solar term of the year. Things close at the three hundred fifteenth star, like the *Lidong* solar term of the year.

Some ask: "The qi overflows at three hundred sixty-six days, while the lunar phases are deficient at three hundred fifty-four. Why does the *Sublime World* consistently use three hundred sixty as its standard?" The answer: "This is to conceal its application within. From *zi* to *si*, waxing occurs as yang advances and yin retreats. By the end of the *si* month, at the two thousand one hundred sixtieth hour, yang reaches its peak, leaving a remainder of six for both yin and yang. From *wu* to *hai*, waning occurs as yin advances and yang retreats. By the end of the *hai* month, at the four thousand three hundred twentieth hour, yin reaches its peak, again leaving a remainder of six for both yin and yang. These total twenty-four, corresponding to the three hundred eighty-four lines of the sixty-four hexagrams in the *Book of Changes*. Excluding the four cardinal hexagrams, there are six lines per hexagram. Four times six is twenty-four; subtracting twenty-four from three hundred eighty-four leaves three hundred sixty. *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li* are the four cardinal hexagrams, occupying the four directions and remaining unchanged in reversal. The *Sublime World*’s reckoning of one era’s cycles takes the rounded numbers, with the methods of waxing, waning, fullness, and emptiness concealed within—this is how it hides its application.

Emperor Yao began at the first *gui* star, the one hundred eightieth day, and the two thousand one hundred fifty-seventh hour. Why? It is said: "By projecting today’s movements of Heaven and Earth, the sun, moon, and Five Phases backward, this was derived."

It is also said: "Divided into twelve, the first six limits are waxing, the latter six waning. Advancing and retreating by three hundred sixty days corresponds to the era’s passage through the conjunctions. The twelve months are the twelve conjunctions; the three hundred sixty days are the three hundred sixty epochs."

The count of the *Primordial Era*’s *primordial* divisions takes one as its unit. Each *conjunction* comprises ten thousand eighty *primordial* divisions; each *epoch* comprises three hundred sixty *primordial* divisions. Summing the divisions of one *era* yields one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred *primordial* divisions of the *Primordial Era*.

Yang’s three hundred sixty are advance; yin’s three hundred sixty are retreat. Combined, three hundred sixty form seven hundred twenty.

It is also said: divided into thirty, this corresponds to the thirty days of one month. Following the waxing and waning of the *Great Epoch*, advance and retreat span sixty days. This accords with the reckoning of *conjunctions* through *epochs*: one month of thirty days is one *conjunction* of thirty *epochs*.

From the perspective of months, leaps and intercalations alternate in application—one advance, one retreat—waxing and waning each yielding sixty *epochs*. From the perspective of days, day and night are further divided in application. One advance, one retreat, leaps and intercalations each yielding their counts, then one month’s count becomes one hundred twenty days, and one *conjunction*’s count becomes one hundred twenty *epochs*.

It is further said: one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred, divided into twelve, corresponds to the twelve hours of one day, with an advance and retreat of six days. This accords with the reckoning of *epochs* through *eras*: when reckoning *eras* through *conjunctions*, these are the counts of *Year Hexagrams* and *Month Hexagrams*; when reckoning *conjunctions* through *epochs*, these are the counts of *Qi Hexagrams* and *Phase Hexagrams*; when reckoning *epochs* through *eras*, these are the counts of *Day Hexagrams* and *Hour Hexagrams*.

One day of twelve hours is one *epoch* of twelve *eras*. One is one *second*; twelve *seconds* make one *minute*; thirty *minutes* make one *hour*. In total, one day comprises four thousand three hundred twenty *seconds*. One hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred are thus the *seconds* of thirty days.

Accumulating the years of one *epoch* yields nearly five hundred fifty-nine million eight hundred seventy-two thousand *seconds*—this is the count of *Tai*.

The advance and retreat of six days represent the count of one transformation. When reckoned as years, six days become six years; advancing to sixty years, in the *Lesser Epoch* this is ten transformations, while in the *Great Epoch* it is one transformation. For the Dao of Heaven transforms through six, yet always with a remainder.

The lesser six days traverse six celestial stems: *jiazi* and *jiawu* each mark one *era*. Thus, the *Great Epoch* transforms once every sixty years—this is the count of the Five *Epochs*. Heaven begins at *jia* and governs at *zi*; Earth begins at *ji* and governs at *mao*. Between *jia* and *ji*, the central *Earth Epoch* is seen. Earth, Metal, Water, Wood, and Fire succeed one another in turn. Heaven ends at *guihai*; Earth ends at *wuyin*. Spoken separately, each has sixty; spoken together, they total sixty. Thus, one great transformation completes one celestial cycle.

The *Lesser Epoch* transforms once every six years—this is the count of the Six *Qi*. The Six *Qi* begin with Heaven at *zi* and end at *si*; Earth responds by beginning at *mao* and ending at *shen*. Heaven begins at *wu* and ends at *hai*; Earth responds by beginning at *you* and ending at *yin*. The *Celestial Controller* and *Terrestrial Controller*, combined, form the Six *Qi*; separated, they are twelve, and when twenty-four are combined, they become twelve and six. Thus, sixty transformations also complete one celestial cycle.

Therefore, the *Great Epoch* transforms through sixty, and six transformations, with their remainders, yield three hundred sixty-six. The *Lesser Epoch* transforms through six, and sixty transformations, with their remainders, also yield three hundred sixty-six.

It is also said: three hundred sixty corresponds to the count of one *hour*. Following the advance and retreat of the *Lesser Epoch*, this corresponds to the hours of day and night. One *hour* is one *era*. From the perspective of *hours*, three hundred sixty are *seconds*; from the perspective of *eras*, three hundred sixty are months. Three hundred sixty months are thus the years of one *era*.

When corresponding to the hours of day and night, one *hour* becomes one *hour*. One *hour* comprises one hundred eighty *seconds*; accumulating one day yields two thousand one hundred sixty *seconds*—half of the four thousand three hundred twenty *minutes* in one day.

Following the waxing and waning of the *Great* and *Lesser Epochs*: the six months after *zi* are waxing; the six months after *wu* are waning. Following the advance and retreat of the *Lesser Epoch*: the six hours after *zi* are advance; the six hours after *wu* are retreat. The *Great Epoch* has waxing and waning but no advance or retreat; the *Lesser Epoch* has advance and retreat but no waxing or waning. Waxing and waning are the accumulation of advance and retreat. From *zi* to *si* is waxing; from *wu* to *hai* is waning.

This exhaustively presents the counts of one *era*, encompassing the counts of retreat and intercalation within. As for the sun and moon, within their waxing and waning, each has its own advance and retreat. One year contains only seven hundred thirty day-and-night cycles. The *Tai Xuan* takes one day-and-night as one day, combining odd and even to make three hundred sixty-five days. The *Canon of Eras* takes day and night as separate days, adding the surplus of yang and the deficit of yin, totaling twelve days to form one hundred twenty days. Combining one hundred twenty with seven hundred twenty yields eight hundred forty days—this is one year of three hundred sixty days, divided into two to include both day and night, and accounting for intercalation.

The remainder of six days is dispersed among the six *jia*, yielding sixty *jiazi*. The intercalary count of six days, combined to make one hundred twenty, serves the use of humans and all beings.

Within the six counts, days are divided into day and night, numbers into leaps and intercalations, each forming twelve. One becomes two, each encompassing waxing and waning; two becomes four, revealing one *limit*. Advancing sixty days, retreating sixty days—six *limits* advance and retreat, totaling three hundred sixty days. Another *limit* advances six days and retreats six days, together forming seven aspects of waxing and waning. Thus, fourteen *conjunctions* yield seven hundred twenty days, all using half-counts.

Some say: advancing twelve days makes one hundred twenty; retreating twelve days also makes one hundred twenty.

Combining the surplus of yang’s fullness, dividing the six intersecting counts of the new moon, six voids also suffice, and two hundred fifty-two likewise accords. It is also said: the establishment of the great and small cyclical counts.

The structural count numbers three hundred eighty-four, yet the operational count stops at three hundred sixty-one years, with the standard count yielding three hundred sixty. Applying the *Scripture of Epochs* to deduce it, using nine of twelve conjunctions yields two hundred seventy days. Now, within one *yuan* of three hundred sixty cycles, only two hundred forty cycles are used, and the intercalary count does not exceed two hundred fifty-two, for eight conjunctions are left unused. This is because *yin* opens at *yin* and closes at *xu*, and *xu* is also unused.

Heaven has its seasons for generating beings, and Earth has its counts for generating beings. Speaking in terms of Heaven, one year uses three hundred sixty. Speaking in terms of Earth, from the sprouting of grasses and trees to the first freezing of the ground, only two hundred fifty-two are used. Thus, the *Diagram of Hexagram Qi* in days numbers three hundred eighty-four; in hours, it numbers two hundred fifty-six. There are only three thousand seventy-two hours—this is how the great and small cycles are distinguished.

Taking the full twelve *limit*-counts, advancing and retreating three hundred sixty days, another *limit* advances six days and retreats likewise. Yin and yang continue in succession, dividing governance within one *yuan*. Speaking separately, each has three hundred sixty yin. Dividing the yang and analyzing the half-counts—if speaking in combination, yang advances while yin retreats, together forming three hundred sixty, for yang encompasses yin, totaling the full count.

Thus, this method of the great cycle, when distinguished from the intercalary count, clarifies the numbers of Heaven and Earth’s yin and yang, where standard and intercalary counts mutually generate, divide halves for common use, share a root yet bear different names.

It is also said: establishing the small cycle method at one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred. Removing the three of intersecting counts, taking the seven of operational counts. The operational method forms six from three, adding surplus fractions, hence seven. The great cycle method focuses on clarifying structure, so the structure of the small cycle may be known. The small cycle method focuses on clarifying function, so the function of the great cycle may be known. They mutually reveal one another.

In structure, it is the operational count of structure: three hundred sixty. In function, it is the operational count of function: two hundred fifty-two. Intersecting counts are unused counts. Operational counts manifest yang; intersecting counts conceal yin. Heaven unifies structure; Earth divides function.

Speaking of the count of one *yuan*, operational counts are seven and intersecting counts are three—yang prevails over yin. Seven parts of Heaven lie above Earth, while three parts intersect below Earth, referring to the division of day and night in *kan*, *li*, *mao*, and *you*. Yang encroaches upon yin, day encroaches upon night—this is the principle of three Heavens and two Earths.

Speaking of one day, from *yin* to *xu*. Speaking of twelve conjunctions, from *Opening Beings* to *Closing Beings*, within one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred, nine myriads seven hundred twenty counts are taken for use. Speaking of day counts, within three hundred sixty days, seven-tenths are taken, yielding two hundred fifty-two for use. Speaking of one year, three-tenths of winter are unused. Speaking of one day, three-tenths of night are unused.

At the two solstices and two equinoxes, dividing three hundred sixty yields one hundred eighty. This is the count of their separation.

Master Zhu said: From the winter solstice to the spring equinox is advancing to the halfway point, hence called *equinox*. From the spring equinox to the summer solstice is advancing to the extreme, hence called *solstice*. Advancing past the extreme leads to retreating to the solstice; the autumn equinox is retreating halfway. Reaching the winter solstice is retreating to the extreme.

The *Nine Yangs of One Hundred Six* cycle. In years of flood and drought, the cyclical movements have constancy. According to the *Treatise on Pitch Pipes and Calendars*: nineteen years make one *zhang*. Four *zhang* make one *bu*, twenty *bu* make one *tong*. Three *tong* make one *yuan*, totaling four thousand five hundred sixty years. Entering the *yuan* at one hundred six years, there is *Yang Nine*, referring to nine years of drought. Next, at three hundred seventy-four years, there is *Yin Nine*, referring to nine years of flood. Combining one hundred six years and three hundred seventy-four years yields four hundred eighty years. The annotation states: the number of six multiplied by eight.

This is from Wei Heshān’s *Essential Meanings*. In my humble opinion: history records that the Han dynasty suffered the calamity of *Yang Nine*. Yao endured nine years of flood, and Tang endured seven years of drought. From this, it may be deduced. Yet Yao and Shun, through virtue, mitigated change, for floods and droughts arise from Heaven’s counts and thus could not cause harm. The Han dynasty’s calamity, however, arose from human action and cannot be wholly attributed to Heaven. Thus it is said: “Heaven-made disasters may yet be avoided; self-made disasters cannot be escaped.” This is what it means.

The middle count is called *sui*; the new-moon count is called *nian*. The Grand Scribe oversees the correction of *sui* and *nian* to order affairs. The middle count refers to twelve months, with the total of midpoints numbering three hundred sixty-five and one-quarter days, called *sui*. The new-moon count refers to the new moons of twelve months, one cycle numbering three hundred fifty-four days, called *nian*. This is the contrast between *sui* and *nian*, hence the distinction between new moon and midpoint. Yet if spoken of separately, they are one.

The *Erya*’s *Explanation of Heaven* states: In the Tang and Yu dynasties, it was called *zai*; in the Xia, *sui*; in the Shang, *si*; in the Zhou, *nian*.

Master Zhu said: Shao Kangjie’s twelve conjunctions of one hundred twenty-nine thousand six hundred years, when deduced from small to large, are all uniform, meaning years, months, days, and hours all align. The first conjunction of ten thousand eighty years is when Heaven first opens. The second conjunction of ten thousand eighty years is when Earth first congeals. The third conjunction is when beings first arise, called *Opening Beings*. This time belongs to *yin*; at *xu*, it is *Closing Beings*, the time when beings are extinguished. For the most part, it is half-bright and half-dark, resembling day and night.

When the first *yuan* reaches its end, Heaven and Earth undergo another unfolding. Taking the single *qi* of Heaven and Earth as an analogy, one day and one night suffice to illustrate this. From this winter solstice to the next, the *qi* completes one cycle. Dividing it into two segments, the first is *yang*, the latter *yin*. Further dividing it into four yields the four seasons. Spring and summer exhale, autumn and winter inhale—it is but a single *qi*. Once *yang* *qi* ascends to its peak, it nears exhaustion, and *yin* arises. As *yin* *qi* nears its end, *yang* is born anew. The *qi* that has been expended disperses—this is what is meant by waxing and waning.

Heaven’s revolution in a month sees the sun complete one rotation, yet there are also times of greater turning that cannot be confined to a single pattern.

In my humble observation: *Qian* and *Kun* are the gates of the *Yi*. The *Fu* and *Gou* hexagrams serve as the hinges and bolts of these gates, opening and closing them. From *zi*, when *yang* is born, the gate of *Qian* opens. Its movement is also called *Heaven opens at zi and yin*. Thus, the Dipper’s handle points here, and the unfolding begins. Its name is *Zhen*, and the ten thousand things emerge. *Qian* divides them, and so it continues until *si*. From *wu*, when the first *yin* is born, the gate of *Kun* closes. Its stillness is contraction. *Xun* causes *yin* to diminish and *yang* to grow, and the ten thousand things enter. *Kun* conceals them, and so it continues until *hai*. One opening, one closing—like two doors. When one opens, the other closes. When *yang* comes, it opens; when *yang* departs, it closes.

Viewed thus, the earlier scholars’ saying that when expansion prevails, Heaven and Earth transform and the grasses and trees flourish, while when expansion fails, Heaven and Earth close and the worthy withdraw—this too depends on humanity. When Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu ruled above, the eras of Tang, Yu, Shang, and Zhou were bright with *yang*. Otherwise, upright scholars were imprisoned as slaves, poison and pain spread across the four seas, books were burned and scholars buried, the world dissolved, stones moved, Zhu Wen defiled human relations, and Heaven and Earth were overturned. At such times, without waiting, the sun and moon lost their light, the cosmos darkened, and beings could not but be consumed. Alas! Such things are hard to speak of.

It is further said: Heaven opens at *zi*, Earth unfolds at *chou*, and humans are born in the month of the Three Luminaries—all may serve as the beginning of the year, and the Three Dynasties successively adopted them. The Xia took *yin* as the human standard, the Shang took *chou* as the earthly standard, and the Zhou took *zi* as the heavenly standard. Yet when it comes to conducting affairs by the seasons, the year and months ought naturally to be reckoned by the human standard. Thus Confucius once said, “I have obtained the Xia calendar,” and commentators took this to refer to works like the *Xia Xiao Zheng*, for he valued its seasonal correctness and the excellence of its ordinances.

Someone asked: Why did the Shang and Zhou, in changing the first month of the year, not attain the correctness of the Xia calendar? The answer is: Though *yang* qi begins at *huangzhong*, its month is *jianzi*. Yet it still lies concealed within the earth, and its power to generate life remains unseen. When the calendar turns from *chou* to *yin*, the three *yang* are complete. Then the harmonious winds arrive, the great *power* resides in Wood, and the spring *qi* responds. The sages of antiquity took this as the beginning of life’s generation and the starting point of the year’s renewal—based on what all people could observe. □ speaking of it, none knew its true origin.

As for the Shang and Zhou, they first gained the world through military conquest. Thereupon they altered the first month of the year, establishing it as an institution for their dynasties, to renew the perceptions of the world, and thus arose the theory of the Three Sequences. Yet in terms of Heaven, the power to generate life was not yet manifest; in terms of Earth, the meaning of the year’s renewal was unclear. Moreover, the order of the four seasons and five phases could not attain central correctness. This is why Confucius examined the institutions of the Three Kings and chose to follow the Xia calendar.

Upon reviewing various transmitted records, though the Three Dynasties differed in their first month of the year, all began their reckoning with *jianyin*. For court assemblies and the distribution of calendars to mark the seasons, they conducted affairs by the first month of the year. Yet in counting the months, all took *yin* as the first. The *Yi Xun* speaks of “the twelfth month,” and the date “yichou” refers to the Shang, which took *jianchou* as the first month, thus making the twelfth month the first. *Yichou* is the day, not tied to the new moon, for it is not the first day of the month. As for changing the first month of the year without altering the count of months, this can still be verified in the classics and histories.

The Zhou took *jianzi* as the first month, yet the *Book of Songs* speaks of “the fourth month” and “the sixth month of summer.” The *Zuozhuan* counts months from *yin*, never altering it. The Qin took *jianhai* as the first month, yet the *Records of the Grand Historian* states that in the thirty-first year of the First Emperor, “the twelfth month was renamed *La*, called *Jiaping*.” Now, *La* is the *jianchou* month. If the Qin took *hai* as the first month, then *La* would be the third month. Saying it was the eleventh month shows that the Qin, too, counted months from *yin* and never altered it. In the thirty-seventh year, it records: “In the tenth month, *guichou*, the First Emperor went on a tour. In the eleventh month, he reached Yunmeng.” Then it continues: “In the seventh month, *bingyin*, the First Emperor died. In the ninth month, he was buried at Mount Li.” That it first records the tenth and eleventh months, then the seventh and ninth, shows that it took the tenth month as the first of the year but still counted months from *yin*, never altering it.

The Qin official records state that the year’s beginning was changed, and court congratulations were all held on the first day of the tenth month. The Qin succeeded the Zhou; if it had changed the count of months, then the Zhou’s tenth month would have been *jianyou*. How could it then be *jianhai*? The Han followed the Qin first month, and the histories record: “In the first year, winter, the tenth month,” making it clear that the first month of the year was changed but the count of months was not.

Moreover, according to the Han scholar Kong, spring was taken as the *jianzi* month, for he held that the Three Dynasties, in changing the first month of the year, also altered the count of months and took their first month as the head of the four seasons. The *Tai Shi* speaks of “the first month, *wuwu*,” where “one” is a corruption of “three.” Since he took the first month as *jianzi*, and the classic also associates it with spring, he thus took the *jianzi* month as spring. Yet in reality, the first month is early spring. If this were so, the four seasons would be disordered, which is even more unreasonable. Winter cannot be made spring, nor cold made warm—this is evident without need for argument.

Someone said: Zheng’s commentary on the *Book of Songs*, “Wei Mo’s spring,” also refers to the Zhou’s late spring, which in the Xia calendar is early spring. The answer is: This is an error inherited by the Han scholars. The “Ministers and Workers” poem speaks of late spring, when they should prepare their new offerings. Yet how is it now? The wheat is about to ripen, and they can receive the bright gifts of the Supreme Lord. When wheat is about to ripen, it is the *jianchen* month, clearly the late spring of the Xia calendar. Zheng’s commentary on the *Book of Songs* fails to grasp its meaning and interprets it inaccurately. Otherwise, the Shang would take late winter as spring, and the Zhou would take mid-winter as spring, reversing the four seasons and losing their correctness. How could the sages of the Three Dynasties, in following Heaven’s governance, have done this?

It is further said: Though the Zhou took *zi* as the first month and thus attained the heavenly sequence, Confucius’ discussion of governing a state took the Xia calendar as correct, for it clearly follows the beginning and end of *yin* and *yang*. *Yang* begins at *zi* and ends at *si*; *yin* begins at *wu* and ends at *hai*. In terms of the *qi* of the four seasons, *yang* begins at *yin* and ends at *wei*, while *yin* begins at *shen* and ends at *chou*. Though these two accounts seem to differ slightly, the discrepancy is only two positions.

At the *zi* position, though one *yang* is born, it has not yet emerged from the earth. At the *yin* position, the *Tai* hexagram, the three *yang* have just emerged above the earth, and the warm, generous *qi* begins from this point. At the *si* position, the *Qian* hexagram, though the six *yang* are at their peak, the warm, generous *qi* has not yet ended. Thus, though one *yin* is born at *wu*, it does not yet harm the *yang*. Only at the *wei* position, the *Dun* hexagram, does the warm, generous *qi* finally dissipate. At the *wu* position, though *yin* has been born, the severe, congealing *qi* only begins at *shen*.

At the *hai* position, though the six *yin* are at their peak, the severe, congealing *qi* only dissipates at *chou*. The principle here follows the same pattern.

Shao Kangjie’s *Twelve Conjunctions* states that at *zi*, Heaven exists but Earth has not yet formed; at *chou*, Earth exists but humanity has not yet appeared; and only at *yin* does humanity emerge—all marking the beginnings of Heaven, Earth, and humanity. Thus, the Three Dynasties took these starting points and established them as their first months. Hence it is said: the establishment of *zi*, *chou*, and *yin* as the first months all belong to the three *yang* months. If the Qin had taken *hai* as the first month, it would have been entirely baseless.

It is further said: Within the *Xiantian* Diagram, the twelve *bi* hexagrams are also contained. On the left side, from *zi* to *si*, there are thirty-two *yang* hexagrams. Beginning with *Zhen*, *Fu* divides into sixteen hexagrams. The second *yang* ascends to *Dui*, forming *Lin*; from *Lin* upward through eight hexagrams, it enters the *Qian* palace and ascends. The third *yang* forms *Tai*, the fourth *Zhuang*, the fifth *Da Zhuang*, and the sixth *Qian*, concluding the fourth month. On the right side, from *wu* to *hai*, there are thirty-two *yin* hexagrams. *Xun* generates *Sheng*, dividing into sixteen hexagrams. The second *yin* descends to *Gen*, forming *Dun*; from *Dun* downward through eight hexagrams, it enters the *Kun* palace and descends. The third *yin* forms *Pi*, the fourth *Guan*, the fifth *Bo*, and the sixth *Kun*, concluding the tenth month. The *yang* hexagrams take one from the *Zhen* palace and four from the *Qian* palace. The *yin* hexagrams take one from the *Xun* palace and four from the *Kun* palace. This illustrates that the two *qi* of *yin* and *yang*—at their inception, they are subtle and gradual; at their culmination, they are swift and intense. Subtle and gradual, they root and nourish the source of the myriad things. Swift and intense, they generate and destroy the myriad things, accomplishing their work.

The *Kan* and *Li* palaces are not taken, for they show that the two *qi* of water and fire flow between Heaven and Earth. What is not used here is in fact used everywhere without exception. The *bi* hexagrams, laid out flat, correspond to the four seasons in pairs, so that the twenty-four solar terms and seventy-two pentads are arranged within them without disorder. The eight palaces of the *Xiantian* Diagram stand in fixed opposition. Among the twelve *bi* hexagrams, *Qian* and *Kun* alone govern eight of them. Thus, the father and mother of the myriad things—birth, growth, harvest, and storage—all proceed from these.

Master Shao said: The Dao began with Fuxi, was perfected under Yao, reformed by the Three Kings, reached its limit with the Five Hegemons, and was severed by the Qin. The order and chaos of the myriad things in recent times cannot escape this pattern. It began with Fuxi, who opened things at *yin*. It was perfected under Yao, when *yang* was pure at *si*. It was reformed by the Three Kings, when *yin* was born at *wu*. It reached its limit with the Five Hegemons, when the *yang* Dao was exhausted. It was severed by the Qin, which imposed a barrier. What Master Shao called the principles of emperors, kings, rulers, and hegemons—father and son, lord and minister—were all bounded by the Qin. The boundary was cut off at the Qin, and the Dao could no longer be practiced.

It is further said: The *Book of Changes* began with the Three Sovereigns, the *Book of Documents* with the Two Emperors, the *Book of Songs* with the Three Kings, and the *Spring and Autumn Annals* with the Five Hegemons. This theory holds that the era of the Two Sovereigns was like spring, the era of the Five Emperors like summer, the era of the Three Kings like autumn, and the era of the Five Hegemons like winter. It is also said: The Seven States were the lingering embers of winter. The Han was a king but fell short; the Jin was a hegemon but had excess. The Three Kingdoms were the heroes of hegemony; the Sixteen Kingdoms were a thicket of hegemons. The Southern Five Dynasties were hegemons riding on borrowed power; the Five Dynasties were a relay station for hegemons. The Sui was the younger brother of the Tang; the late Sui’s various commanderies were hegemons, the last ripples of the Yangzi and Han. The late Tang’s various warlords were hegemons, the lingering light of the sun and moon. The Later Five Dynasties were hegemons, stars at dawn.

In my humble opinion: Before Yao, there were also those like the Five Hegemons. Within the great cycles, there are smaller cycles to distinguish them in detail. Only the closer eras are visible, as earlier records have not been transmitted. Master Shao’s statement that within the eras of emperors, kings, rulers, and hegemons, there are further emperors, kings, rulers, and hegemons is correct. Taking the Han dynasty as an example, even within it there were emperors, kings, and hegemons among the shorn barbarians. Those who ruled by non-action were emperors like Gaozu and Empress Lü; those who ruled by benevolence and trust were emperors like Emperor Wen; those who ruled by cunning and force were hegemons like Emperor Wu. Emperor Xuan was a king among hegemons; Emperor Wu was a hegemon among kings. This is like the four seasons: spring, autumn, winter, summer. Hegemons who were barbarians ranked even lower.

Alas! How fortunate it would be to have lived in the time of the Three Sovereigns.

It is further said: The *qi* of Heaven and Earth moves from north to south to bring order, and from south to north to bring chaos. When chaos has lasted long, it returns from north to south. This is true of both the Dao of Heaven and human affairs. Extending this to successive dynasties reveals the principle of waxing and waning.

It is also said: *Yin* affairs exceed half, for *yang* is one while *yin* is two. Thus, ordered eras are few while chaotic eras are many; the noble are few while the petty are many. In my humble opinion: The numbers of *yuan*, *hui*, *yun*, and *shi* cycles—one *yun* spans 360 years—can be used to infer the order and chaos of successive dynasties. From *zi* to *mao* is *yin* within *yang*, heralding order. From *mao* to *wu* is *yang* within *yang*, the height of order. From *wu* to *you* is *yang* within *yin*, heralding chaos. From *you* to *zi* is *yin* within *yin*, the height of chaos.

In the *Xiantian* Diagram, from *Tai* through *Gu* to *Pi*, and from *Pi* through *Sui* to *Tai*, this is the cycle of north and south. For *Tai* and *Pi* stand opposite each other, as do *Gu* and *Sui*. Thus it is said: from *Tai* to *Pi*, *Gu* lies between them. Who brings about *Gu*? It is when *yin* prevails, *yang* is halted by *Gen*, and the *yin* evil of *Xun* enters—then *Pi* arrives. From *Pi* to *Tai*, *Sui* lies between them. Who brings about *Sui*? It is when *yang* of *Zhen* moves in accord, and the *yin* of *Dui* follows—then the people rejoice without bound, and *Tai* is fitting in all ways. These *Pi*, *Tai*, *Gu*, and *Sui* are perhaps the gates of Heaven and Earth, the paths of humanity and spirits, the intersections of entry and exit.

Numbers flow forward in sequence: from *Zi* to *Wu*, through *Zhen*, *Li*, *Dui*, and *Qian*—this is the image of order. To foresee what comes, one traces backward: from *Wu* to *Zi*, through *Xun*, *Kan*, *Gen*, and *Kun*—this is the image of chaos. Observe it facing south with your back to the north, and you will understand the reversal.

From the Tang through the Five Dynasties, spanning six *jiazi* cycles, there was an equal measure of order and chaos. From the *Qiande* reign of the Song to the present, another six *jiazi* cycles have passed. Midway, southerners held power, and the southern creatures followed the *qi*, overstepping the north and bringing chaos. Master Kangjie deduced this through numerology: six *jiazi* cycles make three hundred and sixty years, corresponding to the twelve hours of a day. The reckoning begins with the *jiachen* year of Yao, with the months *si*, *chen*, *wei*, and the star *gui*. Now, the moon still resides in *Wu*, while the celestial stem has just passed *You*. This accounts for three thousand six hundred and sixty years, yet only one hundred and twenty-two hours have elapsed. How swift it is! The span from ancient times to the present, within Heaven and Earth, is but a single day and night.

The sage comprehends the Dao of day and night and thus knows how to observe a single hour to understand all hours, a single generation to understand all generations. I have recorded the world’s fortunes across the twelve epochs. When an epoch ends, how could one not feel moved?

**The Governance of *Qi***

The earthly numbers begin with two. Two multiplied by six makes the twelve months; two multiplied by four makes the twenty-four *qi*; two multiplied by three makes the seventy-two *hou*. Within these twelve sovereign hexagrams lie the seventy-two *hou*. Within the four cardinal hexagrams lie the twenty-four *qi*. Within the sixty hexagrams lie the three hundred and sixty days.

The Han initially designated *Jingzhe* as the central *qi* of the first month and *Yushui* as the first month’s solar term. This was later revised at the end of the Former Han. Thus, the *Lüli Zhi* states: "In the first month, *Lichun* is the solar term and *Yushui* the central *qi*; in the second month, *Jingzhe* is the solar term and *Chunfen* the central *qi*." It speaks of hibernating creatures: in the first month, they first stir; in the second, they stir greatly. Hence, the later text states: "In the *Santong* calendar, *Guyu* is the third month’s solar term and *Qingming* the central *qi*." According to the *Tonggua Yan* and the current calendar, *Qingming* is the third month’s solar term and *Guyu* the central *qi*; the rest align with the *Lüli Zhi*.

The *Yueling* records the seasonal phenomena of the twelve months, though its structure varies. Some *qi* and *hou* are listed in order, others out of sequence. For the months of the two solstices and two equinoxes, phenomena are recorded twice, for these are the beginnings and endings of *yin* and *yang*, the intersections of their exchange. These are the great nodes, which the sage-kings treated with utmost care.

The *Yi* says: "*Kan* is the hexagram of the true north." It also says: "*Dui* is the true autumn." And again: "The former kings, on the solstices, closed the gates." The later generations’ *qi*, seasons, and sequences were already fully contained in the *Yi*. Since *Kan* is called the hexagram of the true north, then *Zhen* as east, *Dui* as west, and *Li* as south are self-evident. When *Dui* is called the true autumn, it refers to the autumnal equinox; thus, *Zhen* corresponds to the vernal equinox, *Li* to the summer solstice, and *Kan* to the winter solstice—these are self-evident. The *Daxiang* of *Fu* states that on the solstice, the gates are closed, referring to the winter solstice; thus, *Gou* corresponds to the summer solstice—this, too, is self-evident. One who understands the *Yi* can grasp the waxing and waning of *yin* and *yang* *qi* through analogy.

Master Wei of Heshān said: "Autumn is the *yin* within *yin*; spring is the *yang* within *yang*. The *yin* *qi* begins in the fifth month and ends in the tenth, so the seventh and eighth months are the *yin* within *yin*. Though *yang* is present within *yin*, *yin* dominates. The *yang* *qi* begins in the eleventh month and ends in the fourth, so the first and second months are the *yang* within *yang*. Though *yin* is present within *yang*, *yang* dominates."

Master Zhu said: "From this year’s winter solstice to the next, it is but a single cycle of *qi*. Divided in two, spring and summer are *yang*, autumn and winter are *yin*. Divided into four, they become the four seasons. Further divided, they become the twenty-four *qi* and seventy-two *hou*—all originating from this."

**The Twenty-Four *Qi***

As Kongzi said:
- First month: *Lichun* (solar term), *Yushui* (central *qi*)
- Second month: *Jingzhe* (solar term), *Chunfen* (central *qi*)
- Third month: *Guyu* (solar term), *Qingming* (central *qi*)
- Fourth month: *Lixia* (solar term), *Xiaoman* (central *qi*)
- Fifth month: *Mangzhong* (solar term), *Xiazhi* (central *qi*)
- Sixth month: *Xiaoshu* (solar term), *Dashu* (central *qi*)
- Seventh month: *Liqiu* (solar term), *Chushu* (central *qi*)
- Eighth month: *Bailu* (solar term), *Qiufen* (central *qi*)
- Ninth month: *Hanlu* (solar term), *Shuangjiang* (central *qi*)
- Tenth month: *Lidong* (solar term), *Xiaoxue* (central *qi*)
- Eleventh month: *Daxue* (solar term), *Dongzhi* (central *qi*)
- Twelfth month: *Xiaohan* (solar term), *Dahan* (central *qi*)

*Yushui* (Rain Water): Snow disperses into rain. Falling from above is called rain; the north wind freezes it into snow, and the east wind thaws it into water.
*Jingzhe* (Awakening of Insects): Hibernating creatures stir and emerge.
*Guyu* (Grain Rain): Rain nurtures the hundred grains.
*Qingming* (Pure Brightness): All things grow pure, bright, and clean.
*Xiaoman* (Lesser Fullness): Things grow to a small measure of fullness.
*Mangzhong* (Grain in Beard): Grains with beards may be sown.
*Xiaoshu* and *Dashu* (Lesser and Greater Heat): The peak of heat is divided into lesser and greater, with the former at the month’s beginning and the latter at its midpoint.
*Chushu* (End of Heat): Heat retreats and hides.
*Bailu* (White Dew): *Yin* *qi* gradually intensifies, and dew congeals, turning white.
*Hanlu* (Cold Dew): The *qi* of dew grows cold, nearing congelation.
*Xiaoxue* and *Daxue* (Lesser and Greater Snow): In the tenth month, snow is light; in the eleventh, it grows heavy.
*Xiaohan* and *Dahan* (Lesser and Greater Cold): In the twelfth month, cold is light at the beginning and severe at the midpoint.

Each of the twenty-four *qi* spans slightly more than fifteen days. Dividing each *qi* in half yields forty-eight *qi*, each lasting slightly more than seven and a half days. The *Zhouli* speaks of eighteen arrows for the sun, meaning one arrow is changed with each *qi*. Between the twenty-four *qi*, dividing the *qi* into three parts yields slightly more than five days, hence the seventy-two *hou* in a year.

Master Ou said: "For the seventy-two *hou*, each is placed at the midpoint of a solar term. The beginning of a term is a *hou*. By accumulating the *hou* in the hexagrams, one arrives at the central *qi*, which corresponds to the next hexagram. Naming them according to the Five Phases’ governance yields the hexagrams of the dukes."

By accumulating the counting rods, one arrives at the inception of spring wood, summer fire, autumn metal, and winter water. Placing the nodes of the four seasons yields the governance of earth. The lines of the twelve sovereign hexagrams correspond to the midpoints of the seventy-two *hou*. The first *hou* precedes the new *qi* by three days, while the latter follows the new *qi* by three days. As the *hou* divide the inner and outer, sixty hexagrams correspond to the three hundred sixty days of the year. With twelve months and twelve central *qi*, the subsequent *hou* of sixty-four are placed at the four *li*. The nodes of winter are reckoned by the *wei* counting rods. Adding these yields the twelve duke hexagrams to govern them.

For example, in the eleventh month, the initial six of *Kan* (坎) is the central *qi* of sincerity, corresponding to the sovereign hexagram *Fu* (復) and the *hou* of *Zhun* (屯), which spans three days. The nine-two of *Kan* corresponds to the *hou* of *Zhun* externally, the minister hexagram *Qian* (謙), and the *qing* hexagram *Kui* (睽), which is the subsequent hexagram three days after the *qi*.

The four *li*—wood at *Lixia* (立夏), fire at *Lixia* (立夏, seasonal month), the month of the winter solstice as the duke’s central inner *qi* preceding the new *qi* of the twelfth month—along with *Lidong* (立冬) for water and *Liqiu* (立秋) for metal, are all the initial nodes of the four *meng* seasons where the Five Phases govern. *Gen* (艮) and *Xun* (巽) occupy the four corner positions, dividing the six *qi*. The four seasons—*chen*, *xu*, *chou*, and *wei*—are the times when earth governs. *Kan* (坎), *Li* (離), *Zhen* (震), and *Dui* (兌) are the four cardinal hexagrams.

Each hexagram’s two lines govern five hexagrams, completing a month’s governance as the new moon. One line governs two and a half hexagrams. Six lines govern fifteen hexagrams, completing a season of three months. Taken together, the four seasons yield four times six, totaling twenty-four. Examined in detail, there are nodes, *qi*, and *hou*, each ordered without confusion.

I have tentatively interpreted this according to my understanding—whether it aligns with Master Ou’s intent remains uncertain. Some say: in the first month of *jia* and *si*, there are six changes; in twelve months, seventy-two changes. Calling these *hou* is also plausible.

The *Kao of the Celestial Controller* states: seventy-two is the number of the Five Phases’ transformations and completions. This is the canonical method. The counting rods of yang: thirty-six, doubled. The counting rods of yin: twenty-four, tripled. The numbers of yin and yang never fail to harmonize. The seventy-two *hou* are likewise derived from this principle. The seventy-two *hou* were employed by the royal astronomer in the calendar, reaching the utmost precision. I once sought its rationale in vain, pondering through the night until at last I grasped it.

This refers to the counting rods of the *Qian* and *Kun* hexagrams. For *Qian*: one line is thirty-six, two lines seventy-two, and six lines two hundred sixteen. For *Kun*: one line is twenty-four, three lines seventy-two, and six lines one hundred forty-four. Applied to one year, this exhausts the number of three hundred sixty days. Further dividing by the flourishing of the Five Phases: Water, Wood, Fire, and Metal each govern one season, totaling four times seven, or two hundred eighty-eight days. Seventy-two days remain to complete a full cycle. Thus, Earth governs the four seasons, with eighteen days per season corresponding to the months of *chen*, *xu*, *chou*, and *wei*. Additionally, six days each month belong to Earth, aligning with the six lines of a hexagram in a month, demonstrating that the Five Phases cannot be separated from Earth. In all four seasons of the year and all six days of a month, Earth is lodged within them to govern. Therefore, between Heaven and Earth, the myriad people, things, affairs, and transformations all rely upon it as the ground for birth, growth, harvest, and storage—hence the term *hou*.

Readers should appreciate the unceasing effort in contemplation, for it concerns nothing more than the transformations of *jia* and *si*.

Master Zhang of *Guanwu* states: The four seasons and eight solar terms each undergo three changes, with ten days constituting a *xun*. Three *xun* make a month, nine *xun* a season, and thirty-six *xun* complete the four seasons. Five days make a *hou*. Three *hou* form a *qi*, nine *hou* a solar term, and seventy-two *hou* complete the eight solar terms. Heaven has four seasons; each season has four months, and each month forty days. Four times four is sixteen, but one is subtracted from each, leaving three months per season and thirty days per month. Though the numerical structure is four, one is consistently unused. The operative numbers are three and nine. Thus, three *xun* make a month, and three months—ninety days—make a season. Five days make a *hou*, so one month has six *hou*, and five times six is thirty days. Three *hou* make a *qi*, totaling fifteen days. Nine *hou* make a solar term, and eight times nine *hou* complete the cycle of *qi* and solar terms.

I have long lamented that today’s calendar officials merely record the names of the *hou* without delving into their meanings. Let me enumerate them one by one to prevent them from falling into the realm of what the common people use daily yet remain unaware of.

**The Names and Meanings of the Seventy-Two *Hou***

The *hou* begins with "earthworms coil" in the eleventh month, corresponding to the winter solstice and the first day of the *jiazi* superior epoch. The *hou* "east wind thaws" begins with the first month, as the Five Dynasties’ Ma Chongxu’s new calendar prioritized the Rain Water solar term in the first month.

**First Month**
- *East wind thaws*: The spring wind disperses the cold and frozen *qi*.
- *Hibernating insects stir*: They begin to move but have not yet emerged; by the second month, they awaken in great alarm.
- *Fish rise to the ice*: During the height of cold, they lurk beneath the ice, seeking warmth. By the first month, as yang *qi* ascends, they swim near the surface beneath the ice.
- *Otters sacrifice fish*: At this time, fish are plump and delicious; otters first offer them in sacrifice before eating.
- *Wild geese arrive*: The great *hong* and lesser *li* return from abroad to China, soon to fly north to their nests.

The *Tongshu Yan* states: At the Start of Spring, pheasants call and hens lay eggs; at Rain Water, the *tiao* wind descends. The *tiao* wind is the east wind. The *meng* wind is the fiercest of winds.

The rustling of grasses and trees produces sound, hence called "the sprouting of grasses and trees." This marks the season for plowing.

In the second month, the peach trees begin to blossom, responding to the *Jing Zhen* season. After another five days, the *cang geng* sings, its plumage turning yellow. It is also called *huang li liu*, or *wu huang*. In *Shang Geng*, it is known as the *chu que*; in Qi, people call it *bo shu*. Today, it is named the *bu gu*, as its call resembles *bo shu*. The *hong jiu* and *gu hong* are among its names. The hawk transforms into the turtledove, and by autumn, the turtledove reverts to a hawk.

The *xuan* is the swallow. It is also called the *yi* bird, arriving when life flourishes. It nests in the eaves of houses to breed, symbolizing marriage—thus, in ancient times, *Shang Jian Di* swallowed an egg and gave birth to *Qi*. Yang lies trapped within yin and cannot emerge; when it strikes forth, thunder resounds. The sound of yang is thunder, while its light is lightning. Yang is external, and yin clings to it, thus flashing and forming lightning. It is also said: lightning is the light of yang, and when yang is weak, its light is unseen. As yang gradually strengthens and strikes yin, its light becomes visible—hence, it is called the "beginning of lightning."

In the third month, the paulownia begins to blossom. The field mouse transforms into the *jia*—the cuckoo. Another account states: the *mu wu* mouse, a creature of yin, transforms into the *jia* when yang *qi* prevails. When yin *qi* dominates, the *jia* reverts to a mouse.

The rainbow is called *fu lian*. The bright and vivid one is the male rainbow; the faint and subdued one is the female rainbow. The rainbow is the *qi* of yin and yang in union. When pure yin overcomes yang, the rainbow does not appear. If clouds thin and sunlight breaks through, illuminating raindrops, a rainbow forms.

The *ping* is floating duckweed. It is also called *ping* or *zao*, a water plant. When the *wu jiu* preens its feathers, silkworms are about to emerge. Its flight and wing-beating urge haste in farming. The *hong jiu*, also called the *que gu*, resembles a small mountain magpie. Dark blue-black with a short tail and many calls, it is thus named the *ming jiu*.

The *dai sheng* descends upon the mulberry—this is the *zhi wei* bird, also called the *ya jiu*. Its descent upon the mulberry signals that women should attend to their fundamental duties. The *Nei Zai* states: In the second month of spring, the matrons are commanded to tend the mulberry trees, bathe silkworm eggs, and invoke the essence of the *Long Jing*. When the moon aligns with *Da Huo*, the eggs are bathed.

In the fourth month, the *lou gu* calls—this is the croaking frog. The *Zhou Li*’s *Guo Shi* annotation states: Frogs croak with furious voices when summer *qi* is at its peak. The earthworm, a creature of yin, emerges in response to the pure yang *qi*. The *wang gua* grows red, born of the fire *qi*. The *ku cai* blooms, its bitter taste formed by the fire *qi*. *Mi cao* refers to delicate grasses, their slender stems and leaves giving them this name. By the sixth month of yang, grasses of yin cannot withstand the yang and perish.

In the fifth month, the *tang lang* is born. It is also called *cheng hao*, or *bu ying*. Its young are called *piao qiao*. In Yan and Zhao, it is eaten as *li*; in Qi and Qi’s east, it is called *ma gu zhe*. Its first call in the seventh month heralds the approach of cold. In the late-cold lands of Bin, it calls in the fifth month.

The *fan she* is the *bai she*, today called the frog. Its tongue is fixed at the base to the side of its mouth, with the tip curling inward. Some claim to have dissected it and found its tongue curled backward. Zheng disputes this, noting that the *fan she* begins calling in spring and gradually ceases by the fifth month. Its voice frequently shifts, hence the name *fan she*. Alternatively, the frog’s tongue may naturally curl, not necessarily making it the *fan she*.
