The deer, a creature of yin, sheds its antlers in response to yin *qi*—hence, physicians use deer antler velvet to supplement yin. The cicada, also of yin, calls in response. The *ban xia* sprouts—this is the name of a medicinal herb. At the extreme of yin, yang is born.

In the sixth month, the warm wind begins to arrive, its gentle and abundant *qi* reaching its peak in late summer. The *xi shuai*, also called *can* or *cu shi*, is born in the earth. By late summer, its wings have nearly formed, though it cannot yet fly far, so it dwells in walls. By the seventh month, it flies far into the wild.

When the hawk senses the dual yin *qi*, it develops a killing intent and begins to practice striking and grappling.

Jiao Wen asks: "In mid-autumn, the turtledove transforms into a hawk, and in mid-spring, the hawk transforms into a turtledove. Why then does the hawk practice in the sixth month?"
Zhang Yi answers: "Though the hawk may become a turtledove, there remain true hawks from which to learn."

Rotten grass, receiving the *qi* of summer dampness, becomes fireflies. It is not said to "transform" because the firefly does not revert to rotten grass.

The earth, moistened by summer heat, sees frequent heavy rains.

In the seventh month,
the cool wind arrives—this is the sign of cold.
White dew descends—this is the color of metal.
The cold cicada calls, attuned to the essence of yin *qi*.
The *han tiao*, also called *han xie*, resembles a cicada but is smaller.
The azure-red hawk kills the crow, yet dares not taste it first, demonstrating to the people the principle of repaying origins.
It further shows: without martial prowess, Heaven and Earth begin to turn solemn, and the grain ripens.

In the eighth month,
the swan-goose arrives.
In early spring, it was said to come from without into within; here, it comes from the north to the south.
The dark crow returns, marking mid-autumn.
From spring to autumn it returns, retreating to its original nesting place, where flocks of crows prepare provisions.
They gather and store fine food, nourishing it well to prepare for winter storage.
Hibernating creatures open their doors when thunder first sounds, and so they seal their doors when thunder’s voice recedes.
Sealing their doors means filling the gaps. They reinforce the four sides of their burrows, narrowing the openings to let in light, for the season remains warm and they must still venture out. Only in the tenth month, when the cold grows severe, do they close them entirely.
Thunder’s voice emerges in the second month amid yang, and recedes in the eighth month amid yin.
As yin contracts, waters begin to dry up.
The *Guo Yu* states: "When the Horn of Chen appears, the rains cease; when the Root of Heaven appears, the waters dry." The rains cease and the yin path is established; the waters dry and bridges form.
The eighth-month lunar lodge aligns with the Pleiades and the Net, which govern rain.
The Root of Heaven lies between the chambers of Fang.
When the Horn of Chen appears...
When the Root of Heaven appears at the ninth month’s end, the interval between root and end spans twenty-one days and some remainder.

In the ninth month,
the swan-goose arrives as a guest. It is said that those arriving in mid-autumn are hosts, while those arriving in late autumn are guests.
Alternatively, those arriving in mid-autumn depart, while those arriving in late autumn remain as visitors.
The *wei* bird, entering deep waters, transforms into clams—this is the "flying transformation into concealment."
The chrysanthemum blooms with yellow flowers. Its color alone is noted, for its blossoms respond to the height of yin.
I say: the five yin cannot strip away the one yang, so it expresses its beauty as flowers.
The dhole offers sacrifice to Heaven, then hunts and eats its prey.
In early autumn, the hawk sacrifices the crow—flying creatures are small, and their transformations swift.
In late autumn, the dhole sacrifices beasts—running creatures are large, and their transformations slow.
Grasses and trees yellow and fall, returning to their roots.
Hibernating creatures all bow low, their heads facing downward to follow the yang *qi* now within.

In the tenth month,
waters begin to freeze.
From the descent of frost in late autumn to this point, ice begins to form—walking on frost, solid ice follows.
The earth begins to freeze; when water ices, the ground freezes.
Thus it is known that the pheasant enters deep waters to become a *shen*—the great clam is called *shen*, and this is the "flying transformation into concealment."
The rainbow hides and is no longer seen.
In early spring, yang overcomes yin, so the rainbow first appears.
In early winter, yin overcomes yang, so the rainbow hides and is no longer seen.
Heaven’s *qi* ascends: in the fifth month, the first yin is born.
Heaven’s *qi* ascends, and by the tenth month, the six yin have all risen, while the six yang have faded.
The celestial body resides above, and yang returns to emptiness.
Thus it is said: "Heaven’s *qi* ascends; earth’s *qi*, with the six yin below, takes charge." Hence it is said: "descends."
The closing and sealing that form winter occur as yang *qi* hides beneath the earth, and yin *qi* seals and solidifies to create winter.

In the eleventh month,
the magpie does not call, for the crow of Yan cries at night seeking dawn—it is a yin creature yearning for yang.
Thus, sensing the first yang, it falls silent.
The tiger begins to mate, for it too is a yin creature responding to the first yang.
The *li* plant sprouts—this is a fragrant herb responding to yang with fragrance.
The horse is a yang creature.
The earthworm coils, emerging from its burrow with its head bent downward.
As yang *qi* stirs, it desires to curl and raise its head, so it knots and bends.
The elk sheds its antlers: the deer is a yang creature, and at the summer solstice, receiving the first yin, it sheds its antlers. The elk is a yin creature, and at the winter solstice, receiving the first yang, it sheds its antlers.
Springs and streams stir—this is the work of Kan, the yang born of Heaven’s One.

In the twelfth month,
the wild goose faces north, turning from south to north.
The early ones face north this month; the late ones do so in the second month.
The magpie begins to nest, for it knows the year’s position and anticipates the coming year’s *qi*, hence it builds its nest.
The early ones do so in the eleventh month.
The *Poetry* weft states: "On the day of return, the magpie begins its nest"—this is the case.
The pheasant crows—it is a fire creature, and sensing yang, it calls.
The chicken breeds—it is a wood creature, and clinging to yang, it takes form, hence it breeds at the advent of the Spring Commencement node, for the Spring Commencement lies within this month.
The hawk grows fierce and swift, for the season’s killing *qi* reaches its peak, and so the hawk’s ferocity intensifies, seizing birds with speed.
*Yan* means "fierce."
The marshes and lakes freeze to their depths—the ice grows thick within, meaning the depths are solid.
This occurs in the lunar lodge of the Dark Dripping Maiden, the Void, and the Rooftop.
The earth-ox is brought forth—this is the beginning of labor.

The month is aligned with *chou* (丑), which corresponds to earth, and earth can overcome water. Thus, the earth-ox is fashioned to dispel the cold *qi* and prevent the yin *qi* from harming the coming year.

These seventy-two seasonal indicators each bear their own significance. Gazing upon the scene stirs reflection, allowing one to lodge laments for the times and the movements of creatures—hence they are recorded here in detail.

The *Commentary on the Spring and Autumn Annals* states: "The divisions, solstices, commencements, and closures must be recorded along with the clouds and phenomena as a precaution." The *divisions* refer to the spring and autumn equinoxes; the *solstices*, to the summer and winter solstices; the *commencements*, to the beginnings of spring and summer; and the *closures*, to the beginnings of autumn and winter.

Kong Yingda of the Tang remarked: "The two solstices mark the beginning and end of yin and yang; the two equinoxes, the convergence of yin and yang. These are the greatest of the seasonal nodes." Thus, the ancients observed the clouds and phenomena at the two solstices and two equinoxes. *Phenomena* here refers to color.

The *Baozhang* official states: "By discerning the colors of the five clouds and phenomena, one may distinguish good fortune, misfortune, floods, and droughts. The portents of abundance and famine are revealed as follows: green signifies insects, white signifies mourning, red signifies warfare and famine, black signifies floods, and yellow signifies abundance. All are discerned from the colors of the clouds beside the sun, verifying the portents and knowing the twelve phenomena’s corresponding regions. The lands upon which these portents descend will experience abundance or famine."

The *Baozhang* official further states: "By means of the twelve phenomena, one examines the harmony of heaven and earth. Wind is *qi*." In ancient times, the twelve pitch-pipes were blown to gauge the *qi* of the twelve earthly branches, distinguishing omens of good and ill. Today, the method of blowing the pitch-pipes has been lost, and thus its Way has perished.

The *Commentary on the Spring and Autumn Annals* records: When the Chu army attacked Zheng, Music Master Kuang said, "Five times the song of the north wind was played, and again the song of the south wind. The south wind was weak and full of deathly tones—Chu will achieve nothing." My humble observation: Music Master Kuang blew the pitch-pipes to assess Chu’s strength. The north wind corresponds to *wuyi* and *jiazhong* in the north; the south wind, to *guxi*. Since the south wind was weak, he knew Chu would fail.

Wang Zhaoyu said: "The twelve winds arise from the positions of the twelve earthly branches. The six *qi* of heaven and earth combine to generate wind. *Zhen* produces the *ming shu* wind; *li*, the *jing* wind; *dui*, the *yan he* wind; *kan*, the *guang mo* wind—these are the winds of the four cardinal hexagrams. *Gen* produces the *tiao* wind (also called the *tiao* wind at the beginning of spring); *xun*, the *qing ming* wind (also called the *qing ming* wind at the beginning of summer); *kun*, the *dong* wind (also called the *liang* wind at the beginning of autumn); *qian*, the *bu zhou* wind (also called the *bu zhou* wind at the beginning of winter)—these are the winds of the four intercardinal hexagrams. Including the four commencements, the eight hexagrams yield twelve winds." The eight winds govern the eight hexagrams.

The *Commentary* states: "Dance is used to enact the eight winds." It also says: "The twelve winds correspond to the twelve pitch-pipes." On the day of a military campaign, the pitch-pipes are blown to harmonize the sounds. By observing the enemy, one knows good or ill fortune; by hearing the sounds, one predicts victory or defeat. Further details appear in the later discussion on blowing the pitch-pipes.

Master Zhu said: "Heaven and earth are but a single *qi*. At the outset of generation, it is the *qi* of spring. When it grows to excess, it becomes summer; when it contracts, autumn; when it recedes, winter. The next year, it begins again with spring. Benevolence and righteousness are like yin and yang; the four beginnings, like the four seasons. Dividing the four seasons and four beginnings yields the eight nodes. It is but a single *qi* undergoing growth and decline. One divides into two, two into four, three times four into twelve, twice twelve into twenty-four, three times twenty-four into seventy-two. Scattered, they become ten, a hundred, a thousand, ten thousand—nothing more than heaven’s three and earth’s two."

The sounds of the pitch-pipes are appended.

Master Zhu said: "The *qi* of heaven all rises from within the earth. This *qi*’s ascent and descent should be divided into six. From the first layer below the winter solstice, it begins to generate; by the fourth month, the six yang are complete and begin to wane, while the yin *qi* below begins to arise. The pitch-pipes correspond to the visible signs of the *qi* and seasons. The Grand Music Master oversees the six *lü* and six *tong*. The six *lü* correspond to the yang sounds; the six *tong*, to the yin sounds. The yin and yang of sound each have their correspondences."

*Huangzhong* is the *qi* of *zi*, established in the eleventh month, with its celestial branch in *Xingji*. *Dalü* is the *qi* of *chou*, established in the twelfth month, with its celestial branch in *Xuangan*. *Taicou* is the *qi* of *yin*, established in the first month, with its celestial branch in *Yuezi*. *Yingzhong* is the *qi* of *hai*, established in the tenth month, with its celestial branch in *Ximu*.

For example, *Taicou* governs the *qian* palace, the second yang sound, and is paired with the nine-two of *qian*. The *kun* palace corresponds to *Yingzhong* six-three—these yang *lü* and yin *lü* all take yang as primary, with yin coming to pair with it. The same applies thereafter. Thus, *jiazhong* is established in *mao*; *wuyi*, in *xu*; *guxi* and *nanlü*, in *chen* and *you*, descending through the lodges of *Jiao*, *Dahuo*, *Shouxing*, and *Daliang*, intertwining further.

The establishment in *si* is *zhonglü*; in *shen*, *yize*; in *wu* and *wei*, *ruibin* and *linzhong*—these are the pipes of the fourth, fifth, sixth, and seventh months, with their celestial branches in *Shichen*. The stars of *Chunshou* and *Weihuo* intertwine and pair to circulate their *qi*. The stars weave through the twelve lodges, the pitch-pipes correspond to the twelve months, and together they harmonize heaven and humanity. First, the *qi* is distributed within the four seasons to assist the sage-king in positioning heaven and earth, establishing the ultimate of central harmony.

It is also said: "The three systems are as follows: establishing *yin* is the human system; establishing *chou*, the earth system; establishing *zi*, the heavenly system. The heavenly system is the *Huangzhong* pitch-pipe, nine inches long, the number of *qian*. The earth system is the *Linzhong* pitch-pipe, six inches long, the number of *kun*. The human system is the *Taicou* pitch-pipe, eight inches long, the number of the eight trigrams. The three pitch-pipes have no remaining fractions. *Linzhong* occupies the *wei* position, where the *qi* of *Huangzhong* is exhausted. At *Linzhong*, the pattern of heaven and earth becomes manifest; by *si* in the southeast, it achieves full expression, with no remaining fractions."

The pitch-pipes take the yin and yang of nine and six as their method. At nine times nine, eighty-one, the system of one epoch is complete and its pattern fully revealed.

Moreover, accumulating nineteen years—seven intercalary months—brings the great pattern to full completion, with no remaining fractions. Thus the sage kings of the Three Dynasties established these three pitch-pipes as the beginnings of the year, aligning them with the celestial cycles, terrestrial transformations, and human affairs. That later ages adopted the Xia calendar as the standard was because human harmony elicits the harmony of heaven and earth in response.

The Three Palaces are: *Yuanzhong*, the Celestial Palace; *Hanzhong*, the Terrestrial Palace; and *Huangzhong*, the Human Palace. *Yuanzhong* is *Jiazhong*. It arises from the *qi* of the *Fang* and *Xin* constellations, known as the Great Star, the Bright Hall of the Celestial Emperor—hence the Celestial Palace. As a yin tone derived from a yang pitch-pipe, sacrifices to heaven employ four tones, excluding *Huang*, *Cou*, *Gu*, and the like.

*Hanzhong* is *Linzhong*. It arises from the *qi* of *wei*, occupying the *kun* position, where the Celestial Altar and Earthly Spirits reside. Located beyond the *Dongjing* and *Yugui* constellations, it is called the Terrestrial Palace. Sacrifices to earth employ four tones: *Lin*, *Cou*, *Dalü*, *Gu*, and the like.

*Huangzhong* arises from *zi*. Above *zi* lies the *qi* of *Xu* and *Wei*, governing ancestral temples—hence the Human Palace. Sacrifices to ancestral temples employ four tones: *Huang*, *Cou*, *Yingzhong*, *Dalü*, and the like. These three serve as palaces for the sacrificial rites of heaven, earth, and humanity, each generating *jiao*, *zhi*, and *yu* tones from their respective palaces, sought through the classification of sounds.

It is further said: The pitch-pipes and their harmonics await the *qi* without discrepancy. The pitch-pipes are also called *zhong*. *Zhong* means "center" and "resonance." Resonance refers to the blowing of ashes. Each pitch-pipe’s bore is nine *fen* in circumference, with an inner diameter of three *fen*. The *Huangzhong* tube is buried at the *zi* position, nine *cun* above the ground, its head facing south. From its orientation and position, all other pitch-pipes can be fully deduced. The ashes are made from the pith of reeds from Henan. The tubes are crafted from bamboo of Jinyang’s Jinmen Mountain.

The twelve pitch-pipes are buried in a secluded chamber, aligned with the four seasons’ positions: *zi* to *si* and *wu* to *hai* represent the great yin and yang. The six yin and six yang intervals constitute the lesser yin and yang. According to Jing Fang’s twelve pitch-pipes: five pitch-pipes occupy their proper positions, each generating five "children"—*Huang*, *Tai*, *Gu*, *Lin*, *Nan*. When yang resides in yang and yin in yin, they occupy their proper positions. Five times five makes twenty-five, plus the original five, totaling thirty.

Those that lose their positions generate three "children"—also five pitch-pipes: *Dajia*, *Zhonglü*, *Yize*, *Wu*. When yang resides in yin and yin in yang, they lose their positions. Three times five makes fifteen, plus the original five, totaling twenty. Combining two with three yields fifty positions. Some neither lose nor gain their positions, generating four "children." These reside at the junctures of yin and yang. Two times four makes eight, plus the original two, totaling ten. Ten added to fifty makes sixty.

Furthermore, *Huangzhong*, *Taicou*, and the like—seven pitch-pipes—each govern a day as its palace. The remaining fifty-three pitch-pipes follow the days they generate. Six times seven equals forty-two, which governs the days of one cycle. The twelve pitch-pipes generate twelve modes. According to Jing Fang’s method: one pitch-pipe yields five tones, twelve pitch-pipes yield sixty tones, and sixty pitch-pipes further generate three hundred sixty sounds, corresponding to the days of one year.

Alternatively, taking twelve pitch-pipes, each yields seven tones. Each tone forms one mode, totaling eighty-four modes. Modes serve to harmonize sounds. As the *Huainanzi* states: The three hundred sixty pitch-pipes each take the monthly pitch-pipe as their mother, with one solar term as their child. They follow the established day and celestial stem as their divisions, aligning with the seven tones to determine the established day. The sound of the winter solstice—five tones and seven tones—harmonizes therein. Within it, distinctions between primary and secondary tones exist.

One pitch-pipe generates five tones. For example, *Huangzhong* belongs to *zi*, and *zi* has five: *jiazi* as *zhi*, *bingzi* as *yu*, *wuzi* as *gong*, *gengzi* as *jiao*, and *renzhi* as *shang*—these are the five tones of *Huangzhong*. *Dalü* likewise has five: *yichou*, *dingchou*, *jichou*, *xinchou*, *guichou*—five tones, following the same pattern. The remaining pitch-pipes, from *mao* to *hai*, are all thus.

Master Zhu further states: There are twelve pitch-pipes in total, each taking its own pitch-pipe as the *gong* tone and generating four tones. For instance, if *Huangzhong* is *gong*, then *Taicou* is *shang*, *Guxi* is *jiao*, *Linzhong* is *zhi*, and *Nanlü* is *yu*—these are the tones of *Huangzhong*’s one *jun*. If *Linzhong* is *gong*, then *Nanlü* is *shang*, *Yingzhong* is *jiao*, *Dacou* is *zhi*, and *Guxi* is *yu*—these are the tones of *Linzhong*’s one *gong*. Each of the twelve palaces establishes its own *gong* tone and generates four tones, completing the sixty pitch-pipes’ tones.

It is not that *Huangzhong* is fixed as *gong*, *Taicou* as *shang*, *Guxi* as *jiao*, *Linzhong* as *zhi*, and *Nanlü* as *yu*. Rather, *Huang*, *Tai*, *Dajia*, *Gu*, *Zhonglü*, *Rui*, *Lin*, *Yize*, *Nan*, *Wu*, *Ying*—these form the sequence of the twelve pitch-pipes’ lengths. In *Huangzhong*’s one *jun*, the generation of tones—whether ascending or descending—follows the proper order, so each employs the full pitch-pipe’s primary tone. The remaining sixty pitch-pipes each complete one day’s five tones in sequence. On their respective days, each serves as *gong*, rotating through *gong*, *shang*, *jiao*, *zhi*, and *yu*.

Master Zhu further discusses the method of generating rotating *gong* tones: For example, if *Dalü* is *gong*, it uses *Huangzhong*’s number of eighty-one, reducing it by one-third to generate *Yize* below. *Yize* then uses *Linzhong*’s number of fifty-four, increasing it by one-third to generate *Jiazhong* above. The rest follow this pattern. The numbers of the pitch-pipes and harmonics: *zi*, *wu*, *si*, and *mao* belong to yang and generate above, governing growth—hence "increase by one-third." *zi*, *wu*, *si*, and *you* belong to yin and generate below, governing decay—hence "reduce by one-third." Yang generates yin below, long tubes generate short tubes. Reducing by one-third shortens them. Yin generates yang above, short tubes generate long tubes. Increasing by one-third lengthens them. For example, *Huangzhong* is nine *cun*; dividing nine into three parts and combining two yields six *cun*, which is the generation below.

The *Linzhong* pipe, corresponding to the sixth month, takes the length of the *Lin* pipe in *cun*, divides it into three parts, and adds two to six to yield eight *cun*, thus generating above. The *Taicu* pipe, corresponding to the first month, follows the same pattern for the remaining pitch-pipes.

Counting by the standard numbers: nine nines are eighty-one, the *gong* tone. Dividing it into three parts and removing one yields twenty-seven, which then becomes fifty-four, the *zhi* tone. Adding eighteen to fifty-four yields seventy-two, the *shang* tone. Within this, removing one part of twenty-four yields forty-eight, the *yu* tone. Adding one part of sixteen to it yields sixty-four, the *jiao* tone. These numbers of the five tones, derived through the method of "reducing and increasing by one-third," all arise naturally.

The pitch-pipes (*lü*) proceed left while the harmonics (*lü*) proceed right, their courses differing like the method of divination. From *Huangzhong* to *Zhonglü*, all generate below. From *zi* to *si*, yang ascends and yin recedes. Thus, when a pitch-pipe generates a harmonic, it is called "generating below," and when a harmonic generates a pitch-pipe, it is called "generating above." From *Rui Bin* to *Yingzhong*, all generate above. From *wu* to *hai*, yin ascends and yang recedes. Thus, when a pitch-pipe generates a harmonic, it is called "generating above," and when a harmonic generates a pitch-pipe, it is called "generating below." At *wu*, the pattern changes, so *Yi Bin* generates above again. The methods of Jing Fang, Ma Rong, and Zheng Xuan all follow this principle. Ban Gu arranged them in descending order. The *Jiazhong* pipe, at three *cun* and seven *fen* with a fraction, is too short to correspond to the first or second month. As spring embodies the qi of growth and nurturing, Zheng Xuan takes yang’s generation as ascent. But where does descending yang reside? It is better to discuss this through the method of divination: *Qian* (乾), *jia* (甲), and *ren* (壬) proceed left, while *Kun* (坤), *yi* (乙), and *gui* (癸) proceed right. The six yin and six yang follow their courses, and the true nature of *Rui Bin* is the seventh note. Generating above to *Dalü* yields *zhi*, generating below to *Yize* yields *shang*. Generating above to *Jiazhong* yields *yu*, generating below to *Wuyi* yields *jiao*.

Using the sixty pitch-pipes and sixty hexagrams, from *Huangzhong* proceeding left to *Zhishi* is "generating above," while from *Linzhong* proceeding right to *Chishi* is "generating below." The sixty hexagrams: *Qian* is aligned with *zi* and proceeds left, *Kun* is aligned with *wei* and proceeds right, *Zhun* is aligned with *chou* and the *wenshi* interval and proceeds left, *Meng* is aligned with *yin* and the *wenshi* interval and proceeds right, *Tai* is aligned with *yin* and proceeds left, *Pi* is aligned with *shen* and proceeds right, and *Xiaoguo* is aligned with *wei* and proceeds right. Seven hexagrams proceed in alternating directions, and the pitch-pipes empirically validate this. For a quicker method, one need not look beyond *Qian* and *Kun*, the six yang and six yin. *Zi*, *yin*, *chen*, *wu*, *shen*, and *xu*—*Huang*, *Tai*, *Gu*, *Mo*, *Yi*, and *Wu*—follow *Qian*’s leftward rotation, which is called "pitch-pipes generating below." *Wei*, *si*, *mao*, *chou*, *hai*, and *you*—*Lin*, *Zhong*, *Jia*, *Da*, *Ying*, and *Nan*—follow *Kun*’s rightward turn, which is called "harmonics generating above." These are the words of Zheng Xuan’s divination method, derived from the *Tai Xuan*.

Jing Fang’s method holds that one pitch-pipe contains the variations of the five tones, forming sixty hexagrams, which actually begin with *Zhong Fu*. After seven days, it returns to correspond with the pitch-pipe of the winter solstice, *Huangzhong*. Its true origin lies in *Zhishi*, which precedes the winter solstice—this is the origin of the pitch-pipe and calendar systems. Yang Xiong and Fang Shi understood this. The Northern Asterism does not move, serving as the pivot of the celestial axis. Yet the true immovable point lies at the final degree of the pivot star. Those who are not skilled in observing the heavens cannot grasp this. The numbers of the *gong* tone begin and end here.

"Huangzhong is the first, Linzhong the second, Taicu the third, Nanlü the fourth, Guxi the fifth, Yingzhong the sixth, Ruibin the seventh, Dalü the eighth, Yize the ninth, Jiazhong the tenth, Wuyi the eleventh, and Zhonglü the twelfth *gong*. Thus, yang descends to generate yin, and yin ascends to generate yang, culminating in Zhonglü, where the twelve pitch-pipes complete their cycle. Ascending generates Huangzhong as *zhi*, descending generates Linzhong as *shang*. Ascending generates Taicu as *yu*, descending generates Nanlü as *jiao*. These twelve *gong* each possess five tones, totaling sixty tones. Nanlü occupies the final position, hence it is said: 'culminating in Nanlü.' It is also said: 'Zhonglü ascends to generate Zhishi, Zhishi descends to generate Qumie. Ascending and descending generate one another, culminating in Nanshi.' Nanshi is Nanlü. Thus it is said: 'culminating in Nanshi, where the sixty pitch-pipes complete their cycle.'

The Three Powers and Seven Beginnings: The Three Powers begin with Huangzhong for Heaven, Linzhong for Earth, and Taicu for Man. The Seven Beginnings are Guxi for the beginning of spring, Ruibin for summer, Nanlü for autumn, and Yingzhong for winter—these are the Four Beginnings, which together with the Three make Seven. If one uses the two transpositions to adjust the melody, the sounds of winter and summer will be lacking, and the four seasons incomplete. Therefore, each *gong* has five modes, with the addition of the transposed *gong* and transposed *zhi*—two modes—making seven.

The *Monthly Ordinances* records only the five tones, without mentioning the transpositions of *gong* and *zhi*. The six yang lines of *Qian* generate the six yang pitch-pipes: *Qian*’s initial nine is Huangzhong as *Fu*, the second nine is Dalü as *Lin*, the third nine is Taicu as *Tai*, the fourth nine is Jiazhong as *Zhuang*, the fifth nine is Guxi as *Da You*, and the top nine is Zhonglü as *Qian*. From Huangzhong, the first yang arises in the eleventh month, and the descent of yang generates yin as 'descending generation.' The six yin lines of *Kun* generate the six yin pitch-pipes: *Kun*’s initial six is Ruibin as *Shen*, the second six is Linzhong as *Dun*, the third six is Yize as *Pi*, the fourth six is Nanlü as *Guan*, the fifth six is Wuyi as *Bo*, and the top six is Yingzhong as *Kun*. From Ruibin, the first yin arises in the fifth month, and the descent of yin generates yang, also termed 'descending generation,' though it may also be called 'ascending generation.'

Eight eights correspond to the eight winds; same positions represent husband and wife, different positions represent mother and child. Thus it is said: 'The pitch-pipe takes a wife, and the mother gives birth to a child.' The twelve pipes generate one another, all following the pattern of eight eights. Ascending and descending generation exhausts itself in Zhonglü. The mutual generation of yin and yang begins with Huangzhong and proceeds leftward. For example, Huangzhong generates Linzhong, passing through eight celestial stems. This pattern applies to all subsequent generations, all following the method of 'three heavens and two earths.' Three threes make nine, two threes make six. Nine and six embody the Dao of yin and yang, husband and wife, mother and child.

Huangzhong’s initial nine and Linzhong’s initial six both occupy the first position of the first image. Husband and wife are one body, sharing the same position, and together reside in Linzhong, which ascends to generate Taicu. The second nine, second in the first position, represents mother and child generating one another in different positions. Thus it is said: 'What the pitch-pipe generates are husband and wife in the same position; what the *lü* generates are mother and child in different positions.'

Blowing the pitch-pipes reveals matters of good and ill fortune. The Grand Master holds the unison pitch-pipe to listen to the sounds of the army and proclaim good or ill fortune. In the records of King Wu’s military campaigns, it is said: on the day the king led his troops into battle, the soldiers arrayed their ranks, the generals raised their banners, and the Grand Master blew the pitch-pipes to harmonize the sounds. If *shang* prevailed, the battle was won, and the soldiers were strong—this pertains to the west, where metal governs firm decisiveness, hence strength. If *jiao* prevailed, the army was disordered, with many changes and loss of the soldiers’ morale—wood governs bending and straightening, hence disorder. If *gong* prevailed, the army was harmonious, and the soldiers were of one mind—earth governs growth and nurturing, hence harmony. If *zhi* prevailed, the general was hasty and prone to anger—fire governs fierceness and wrath, hence haste. If *yu* prevailed, the troops were weak, lacking awe and clarity—water governs softness and weakness, hence obscurity.

Master Kuang said: 'I sang the *North Wind*, then sang the *South Wind*. The *South Wind* was not strong, and many died—Chu also achieved nothing.' The *North Wind* refers to Jiazhong and Wuyi to the north; the *South Wind* refers to Guxi and Nanlü to the south. The qi of the southern pitch-pipes did not arrive, hence many deaths—all were discerned by blowing the pitch-pipes and singing the winds. Sound emitted is called song.

Master Shao said: 'Primal wine is bland and flavorless; grand sound is pure and rare.' At the winter solstice, the first yang sprouts in Huang*gong*, like the brewing of primal wine. Its flavor is hidden in tastelessness—this is the music of no sound. Master Zhou said: 'Music is pure and not excessive.' Purity calms desire, harmony releases restlessness. This is the central sound of Huangzhong, the root of the pitch-pipes, which naturally sighs without *gong* or *shang*.

I once admired Wan Baochang, who, while dining with others, struck a miscellaneous jar with his chopsticks and spontaneously produced musical tones. The gradations of high and low were exquisite—it was only regrettable that no one at the time understood. Su Shi said: 'If the world had no Master Nan Guo, ears would never have heard the earth’s breath—how much less the breath of heaven?' Ouyang Xiu said: 'Instruments may decay, but sound cannot be transmitted through words.' Alas! Sound—can it truly be without transmission? Within the universe, where can one go that is not governed by tone and pitch? They constantly move with the sun and moon, cold and heat, darkness and light, wind and rain, traversing between heaven and earth. From ancient times to the present, from dawn to dusk, the clamor of voices and the resonance of apertures—all are my lofty mountains and flowing waters, my yellow reed pipes and clay drums. Must one await the performance of metal, stone, silk, and bamboo before sound is deemed transmissible?

It is also said: 'The *lü* resonates with the *lü* to generate sound.' Sound is *lü*, and *lü* is yang. The *lü* has opening and closing; with each opening and closing, myriad sounds arise. Ten sounds correspond to ten pitch-pipes. The ten celestial stems—five are yang, and the five tones are the conjunction of the ten stems. Thus, in the hexagrams, they correspond to *Qian*, *Dui*, *Li*, and *Zhen*; in time, to the primal epoch, kalpa, cycle, and generation; in the pitch-pipes, to the sounds of the sun, moon, and stars."

Voices intone tones, and pitch-pipes intone *lü*. Their reckoning distinguishes the Greater Yang, Lesser Yang, Greater Rigidity, and Lesser Rigidity. Sound arises from the sun, corresponding to *jia* through *gui*, and initiates the twelve tones below on earth. Of the one hundred sixty sounds, one hundred twelve are employed.

*Lü* resonates with *lü* to generate tone. Tone is *lü*, and *lü* is yin. *Lü* has call and response; with each call and response, myriad tones arise. The twelve tones correspond to the twelve *lü*. Of the twelve earthly branches, six are yin, comprising the six *lü*, which are the conjunction of the twelve cyclical signs. Thus, in the hexagrams, they correspond to *Kun*, *Gen*, *Kan*, and *Xun*; in time, to the year, month, day, and cyclical hour; in *lü*, to the sounds of water, fire, earth, and stone.

Tones harmonize sounds, and *lü* harmonize pitch-pipes. Their reckoning distinguishes the Greater Softness, Lesser Softness, Greater Yin, and Lesser Yin. Tone arises from the cyclical hour, corresponding to *yin* through *chou*, and responds above with ten sounds to heaven. Of the one hundred ninety-two tones, one hundred fifty-two are employed.

Master Shao’s *lü-lü* system reveals the untransmitted secrets of the Primordial, differing from the *Tai Xuan* and *Tai Chu*. The *Xuan* takes heaven’s three odd numbers as its measure. Three multiplied by three yields nine, forming the three sequences of heaven, earth, and humanity. All commence with the *huangzhong* pitch-pipe, following Fan’s reckoning. Master Shao’s *Jingshi Lü-Lü* takes earth’s four even numbers as its measure. Four multiplied by four yields sixteen, arising from the Two Modes generating the Four Images, following the reckoning of the *Yi*. It divides sound and *lü-lü* by the old and young of yin and yang. Four serves as the body, five and six as function. Five is sound, and six is *lü-lü*. If within the body one distinguishes body and function, then sound is the body, and tone is function. If within function one distinguishes body and function, then *lü-lü* is the body, and sound and tone are function.

The *Tai Xuan* and *Tai Chu* exclusively take *zi* through *si* as yang pitch-pipes and *wu* through *hai* as yin *lü*. Sound and tone merge into one. Master Shao’s Primordial reckoning exclusively takes the ten celestial stems as yang pitch-pipes and the twelve earthly branches as yin *lü*. Sound and tone correspond to *lü-lü* only when one comprehends the method of three multiplied by three and four multiplied by four, after which the study of *lü-lü* may be mastered. Those who have discoursed on sound from ancient times to the present have conflated them into one path. Only with Master Shao’s teachings does precision emerge.

Those skilled in bell and pitch-pipe discern order and chaos by examining sound. When Kangjie traveled to Longmen Mountain, he rested in a grove of gravel, where a withered branch suddenly fell nearby. He said to Fu Han’gong, “This tree will soon be felled.” On his return journey, he saw the land as far as the eye could reach had been cleared. Upon verification, it was discovered that the capital’s construction had felled it, and his words were confirmed. Other examples, such as the prosperity and decline of peonies or the cuckoo’s call foretelling order and chaos, all belong to the same study. Here, I record a few instances below.

During the Han, Cai Yong, styled Bojie, passed through Gaoqian Pavilion in Kuaiji and saw the bamboo eaves of its roof. Sixteen stalks from the eastern side could be made into flutes. Indeed, they produced an extraordinary sound, and the world called them “Keting Flutes.” This is knowing sound by form. He also took scorched paulownia from a traveler’s lodge to fashion the “Jiaowei Zither.” This is knowing sound by movement. A neighbor played the zither, and he observed a mantis stalking a cicada. Listening to the sound, he knew it contained a killing intent. This is knowing a person’s heart by the sound of things.

During the Sui, Wan Baochang was profoundly versed in music and told Emperor Wen, “The music established by Zheng Yi is the sound of a fallen state—how could it befit Your Majesty’s ears?” He also once listened to the music performed by the Court of Imperial Sacrifices and wept profusely. When asked why, he did not reply.

He said: “The music is lewd and fierce, and it bodes mourning for the realm—before long, there will be mutual slaughter.” At the time, the four seas were in full prosperity, and those who heard this all deemed it unlikely. Yet by the end of the Daye era, his words proved true.

There was a man named Wang Lingyan who, overhearing his son playing the pipa outside the door and performing the tune *Fan Angongzi*, was greatly startled. He asked, “When did this melody first arise?” The son replied, “It has appeared recently.” Lingyan wept and said to his son, “You must not accompany the Emperor on his journey to Jiangdu—the Emperor will never return.” He explained, “This tune is in the *guan* mode, signifying departure without return. That is how I know.” And so it came to pass.

During the Tang, Li Sizhen, listening to music, remarked, “The *gong* mode does not summon the *shang*—this betrays a rift between ruler and minister. The *jue* and *zhi* modes are in discord—this foretells estrangement between father and son. The prevalence of deathly, mournful tones suggests that if the state remains at peace, the Crown Prince will bear the blame.” Soon after, the Crown Prince was deposed. Li Sizhen also divined the day of his own death, prepared his coffin and burial clothes, and died as predicted.

In the first month of the first year of the Shenlong era, Pei Zhigu was present when the ancestral temple music commenced. He said to Yuan Xingchong, “The harmony of bells and chimes is exquisite—does this not portend great fortune for the Tang imperial line?” That very month, Emperor Zhongzong was restored to the throne.

There was a man who, upon hearing a horse neigh, declared, “The horse’s cry is mournful—its master will surely fall to his death.” Another, hearing the jingling of a bride’s girdle ornaments, said, “This will end in separation.” Upon inquiry, all such predictions proved accurate. Similarly, Zheng Xiangru’s counsel to Zheng Qian foretold glory and disgrace, success and failure, life and death.

Song Shaochangbo Dou Gongyan could calculate the decay of wooden pillars and accurately foretell the lifespans and fortunes of brothers. Dou Wan was deeply versed in musical temperament; his discernment of sounds allowed him to make such predictions. From this, one may infer that those skilled in divination and prognostication have been numerous since the Qin and Han dynasties, yet only the Song Confucian Master Kangjie attained its subtleties.

He held that human nature is quiescent, and that extreme stillness gives rise to movement. This movement is yang, not emotion. It is the first manifestation of the heart of Heaven and Earth at the dawn of creation. When a person lies in the womb, it is a time of creation, the initial yang. At birth, the moment of “opening things and accomplishing affairs,” emotion first stirs, and movement becomes visible.

He arranged two hundred fifty-six positions, calculating from sound. Master Kangjie used two hundred sixty-four characters to encompass the numerical system of *lü* and *lü* tones. Within this, one hundred twelve characters summarized *sheng* (sound), while one hundred fifty-two summarized *yin* (tone). Sound and tone mutually interact through fanqie, yielding seventeen thousand twenty-four distinct sounds. Sound corresponds to rhyme, tone to initials. Sounds are divided into level, rising, departing, and entering tones, while tones are categorized by labial, lingual, dental, molar, and guttural articulation. Sounds distinguish inner and outer, and tones are further refined through eight transformations: opening, developing, gathering, and closing. They are also divided into *pi* (expansion), *qing* (clear), *xi* (contraction), and *zhuo* (turbid). Sounds are represented by trigrams on the left, tones by trigrams on the right. The outer three lines of a hexagram are called *hui* (regret), the inner three *zhen* (constancy). Viewed horizontally, each yields a specific hexagram—these two hexagrams together form *jiji* (already completed), the diagrammatic hexagram. By identifying the hexagram in the *guai* diagram, one determines fortune or misfortune, all calculated from sound. Listening must be precise, without the slightest error.

Master Yichuan said: “One expansion and one contraction encompass the level, rising, departing, and entering tones. One intonation and one response encompass opening, developing, gathering, and closing. When the level, rising, departing, and entering tones are complete, ten thousand sounds arise. When opening, developing, gathering, and closing are complete, ten thousand tones arise. The *lü* follow Heaven and change; the *lü* follow Earth and transform. Expansion follows yang and emerges; contraction follows yin and withdraws. Intonation follows the firm and ascends; response follows the yielding and descends. Only then do the *lü* and *lü* align with sound, and the Dao of *gong*, *zhi*, *yu*, *jue*, and *shang* each attain their proper place.

Yang is the sun, fire; yin is the moon, water. The firm is metal, the stars; the yielding is earth, the celestial stems. When the sun, moon, stars, celestial stems, metal, wood, water, fire, and earth are in harmony, Heaven and Earth are rectified. The sun is the eye, fire, color; the moon is the ear, water, sound; the stars are the nose, metal, qi; the celestial stems are the tongue, earth, taste. When the eye, ear, mouth, and nose—color, sound, qi, and taste—are rectified, the human Dao is rectified. Thus, to understand the Dao of *lü*, *lü*, and sound is to be able to conduct the affairs of Heaven, Earth, and humanity. The mutual resonance of *lü* and *lü* gives rise to sound, through which the emotions of Heaven, Earth, and all things are revealed.” Thus concludes the tenth scroll of *Tian Yuan Fa Wei*.

**Scroll Eleven of *Tian Yuan Fa Wei*: *Tian Yuan Fa Wei Xu Wei***

[Corrections per *Siku Quanshu* edition: #16 "續" emended from "績"; #17 "冰" emended from "水"; #18 "牟無" emended from "鵪母一"; #19 "陽" emended from "陰"; #20–21 "虹" emended from "蜺"; #22 "鴉" retained (variant: "鷗"); #23 "二" supplied; #24 "始" supplied; #25 "冰" emended from "水"; #26 "鴉" retained; #27 "陽" emended from "陰"; #28 "猛" emended from "猶"; #29 "物" emended from "色"; #30 "辰在" emended from "在辰"; #31 "後" emended from "厚"; #32 "本" (variant: "根本"); #33 "度" emended from "加"; #34–35 "太" emended from "大"; #36 "變徵" emended from "歡變"; #37 "將張兮" (variant: "將弓矢"); #38 "南風" emended from "北風"; #39 "故" emended from "時"; #40 "太" emended from "大"; #41 "傍" (variant: "前"); #42 "之" and "乃" supplied; #43 "治亂" supplied; #44 "胞" (variant: "胎"); #45–46 "姆" (variant: "母"); #47 "一" (variant: "二"); #48 "為" supplied after "名."]

*Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai; Collated by Fang Hui Wugu and Wan Li.*

The Hexagram-Qi System

The hexagram-qi system originates with *Zhong Fu* (Hexagram of Inner Truth). Jing Fang transmitted it from Jiao Gan, who received it from a recluse. It states that the four cardinal hexagrams govern the four directions, serving as regional overseers. The hexagrams *Kan* (Water), *Li* (Fire), *Zhen* (Thunder), and *Dui* (Lake) correspond to the solstices and equinoxes—days when the qi of the four seasons holds sway: wood in spring, fire in summer, metal in autumn, and water in winter. Each hexagram governs its season, and divination on its designated day reveals auspicious or inauspicious omens. The remaining sixty-four hexagrams and their lines each govern a single day, totaling three hundred and sixty days. The remaining five and a quarter days are divided into eighty parts per day, beginning at midnight, so that five days comprise four hundred parts, and the quarter-day is further divided into twenty parts. The sixty hexagrams are apportioned such that forty-two hexagrams each receive seven parts. The *Bo* hexagram (Stripping), where yang qi is exhausted, falls at the end of the ninth month, and the tenth month is governed solely by *Kun* (Earth). *Kun* spans six days and seven parts; when *Kun* is exhausted, the *Fu* hexagram (Return) brings the return of yang qi. From the exhaustion of *Bo* to the return of yang in *Fu*, *Kun* intervenes with six days and seven parts. Rounded to whole numbers, this is referred to as "seven days." In my humble opinion, when speaking of monthly hexagrams, *Bo* to *Fu* spans *Kun*; when speaking of seasonal hexagrams, each month contains five hexagrams. The above is recorded in the *Yi Wei Tu* (Diagrams of the *Weft* Texts of the *Book of Changes*).

Jing Fang’s transmission designates the waxing and waning hexagrams as rulers: waxing hexagrams are called *Tai Yin* (Great Yin), waning hexagrams *Tai Yang* (Great Yang), and the rest are termed *Shao Yin* (Lesser Yin) and *Shao Yang* (Lesser Yang). Cai Shi of Xishan remarked: Shao Yong also employed the six-day-seven-part system, and Yang Xiong adapted it to compose the *Tai Xuan* (Supreme Mystery). The *Tai Xuan* omits *Kan*, *Dui*, *Li*, and *Zhen*, while the *Jing Shi* (Sovereign Generations) omits *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li*. Both exclude the four cardinal hexagrams, adhering to the sixty-hexagram, six-day-seven-part system. Zixia said: "When the six positions are exhausted, they revert to *Fu* within *Kun*; the number is seven." The seventh line begins at the first position, hence the term "seven days." Hu Anding stated: Traversing six lines, each line represents a day, so the sixty hexagrams and their lines correspond to three hundred and sixty days. The interval between any two hexagrams is seven days. Moreover, some hexagrams take a line as a year, others as a month, and others as a day. The phrase "seven days" clarifies the hexagram-qi system. In terms of waxing and waning, from the *Li Dong* (Beginning of Winter) solar term in the tenth month to the *Da Xue* (Great Snow) solar term in the eleventh month, *Kun* transitions to *Fu*, spanning nearly seven days. The explanations of all scholars trace back to Zixia. The Confucians of the two Han dynasties transmitted the Classics using the six-day-seven-part system. By the Song dynasty, Wang Zhaosu, Wang Kui, and Song Xian began to write treatises refuting it.

Zhaosu argued: The commentaries and sub-commentaries both contradict the Master’s meaning. Even in the tenth month, when *Kun* is pure, yang qi still resides within. Thus, the *Qimai* (Millet) Master’s *Kun* hexagram’s top line speaks of "dragons battling." If *Kun* were exhausted after six days and seven parts, then at the end of the tenth month, a single yang would immediately occupy its position, leaving fifteen days until the winter solstice. This demonstrates the difficulty of applying the seven-day principle to the numerical system of the *Yi Wei*. Hu Dan challenged this, saying: In the Western Han, Jing Fang’s hexagram-qi governance consistently proved accurate. In the Eastern Han, Lang Dian’s six-day-seven-part theory was the most refined. The six-day-seven-part system is the actual count of hexagrams and lines, while the days of the year are a nominal count. The remaining days of the month are calculated and added as intercalary days. Zhaosu failed to grasp the subtlety of intercalation, hence his doubt about the fifteen-day gap before the winter solstice. It is regrettable that the *Weft* texts have been lost, and Jing Fang and Lang Dian’s teachings have perished.

Zhu Zifa remarked: Zhaosu only understood the general outline. How could he comprehend that the four cardinal hexagrams govern the four directions, while the sixty hexagrams govern the days of a full year? The solar terms are all regulated by the four cardinal hexagrams, leaving five days and a quarter, which accumulate to form intercalary days. Each hexagram spans six days and seven parts, with the advance and retreat of qi propelling the cycle. For example, in the ninth month, *Bo* is followed by *Gen* (Mountain), *Ji Ji* (After Completion), *Shi He* (Biting Through), and *Da Guo* (Great Exceeding), totaling five hexagrams before *Kun* is complete. In the tenth month, *Wei Ji* (Before Completion), *Jian* (Obstruction), *Yi* (Nourishment), and *Zhong Fu* (Inner Truth) precede *Fu*, again five hexagrams. Thirty days separate *Bo* and *Fu*, with *Fu* governing the winter solstice. The mid-point qi of the winter solstice begins with *Zhong Fu*, and seven days after *Zhong Fu*, *Fu* arrives. Thus it is said: "This is the movement of Heaven." Among the scholars of past dynasties, only the *Xuan* (Mystery) grasped its essence. Hence: *Xuan* is one, *Zhong* (Center) is two, *Xian* (Surplus) is three, *Cong* (Compliance) is four, *Geng* (Renewal) is five, *Mou* (Gaze) is six, *Kuo* (Vastness) is seven, *Han* (Shout) is eight, *Chen* (Submersion) is nine, forming the central image.

*Zhong Fu* corresponds to the winter solstice solar term, when the sun rises at one degree of the *Niu* (Ox) constellation, and the Dipper points to *Zi* (North). The pitch-standard *Huang Zhong* (Yellow Bell) governs the eleventh month of the Xia calendar. The Zhou dynasty entered the *Niu* constellation at five degrees. The Zhou symbol *seven-five-one-return* (七五一复) signifies the seven-day return of yang qi. From Yang Xiong, Ma Rong, Zheng Xuan, Song, Yu, Lu, and Fan, all transmitted this learning, yet Zhaosu opposed it—what could be done? Wang Kui had already refuted Kong Yingda’s interpretation of Wang Bi’s commentary, and Song Xian further denounced Jing Fang, Lang Dian, and Guan Lang for appropriating the *Book of Changes* to practice *Ren Dun* (Six Ren), divination, incantations, and yin-yang numerology, which deviate from the Sage’s intent. Why not simply assign each hexagram to a month and each line to a day? Why does the qi not arise from other hexagrams but uniquely from *Zhong Fu*?

Zhu Shi countered: Kong Yingda and Wang Bi’s *Yi Wei* waxing-and-waning hexagrams cannot be dismissed. *Liu Ren* (Six Ren) divination aligns times and days with the *Kan* and *Li* hexagrams of the *Book of Changes*. *Dun Jia* (Hidden Jia) divides the nine palaces according to the *He Tu* (Yellow River Diagram), both capturing one aspect of the *Book of Changes* but not its entirety. He further argued: Scholars who invoke *Ren Dun* to explain the *Book of Changes* seek to encompass all under heaven, unaware that *Ren Dun* actually derives from the *Book of Changes*. Why, then, should those who expound the *Book of Changes* rely on *Ren Dun*?

The sage deduced the principles of yin-yang waxing and waning to illuminate the images of gain and loss, survival and demise. Those who trust in the advance and retreat of hardness and softness yet reject the *Xiao Xi* (Waxing and Waning) learning of the *Yi Wei* (Apocrypha of the *Book of Changes*), claiming that hexagram qi does not arise from *Zhong Fu*, are like those who count all day yet know not of two and five—how can they be deemed skilled at reading books?

On the winter solstice of the *yimao* year in the *Yuanzhen* reign (1295), Master Fang of Xugu sent me a letter, stating: "Zhu Zifa posits that the winter solstice begins at the first degree of *Niu* (Ox) Mansion—this is an ancient method. In the third year of Emperor Wen of Han (177 BCE), the winter solstice fell on the twenty-second degree of *Niu*. By the first year of the *Xingyuan* reign of Tang (784 CE), it had shifted to the ninth degree of *Dou* (Dipper). Over 961 years, it regressed twelve degrees. Now, in the first year of *Yuanzhen* (1295), on the seventh day of the eleventh month, the winter solstice falls on the eighth degree of *Ji* (Winnowing Basket), having regressed another twelve degrees. Five days after the solstice, it lies at the third degree of *Dou*. How can one still cling to the old saying that the winter solstice enters *Niu* Mansion to symbolize the return of the Zhou?"

I replied: "Examining antiquity, it is difficult to find complete uniformity. The *Calendar* states that in the time of Yao, the winter solstice fell on the first degree of *Xu* (Emptiness), yet He Chengtian claimed it was on the tenth degree of *Nü* (Woman). The *Yuangai Calendar* of the Song placed the solstice at the seventeenth degree of *Dou*, while the *Monthly Ordinances Essential Meaning* placed it at the fourteenth degree. The *Dayan Calendar* of the Tang’s *Kaiyuan* era set it at the tenth degree of *Dou*, and by the Song’s *Tongyuan Calendar*, it had shifted to the second degree. Moreover, ancient calendars marked the start of the day at midnight, yet Cui Zhongrong took the beginning of *zi* (midnight) as the start of the month, resulting in a two-*ke* discrepancy—yet even then, his precision was acknowledged.

For yin and yang qi, uneven and irregular, surge and recede to form a year. Their expansion and contraction, advance and retreat, naturally follow this pattern, shifting only within the bounds of *Ji*, *Dou*, *Niu*, and *Nü*. The *Tai Xuan*’s claim that entering *Niu* Mansion symbolizes return derives from the *Tai Chu* calendar. It speaks of what was empirically validated at the time, approximating the general reckoning as a standard. Though later developments diverged, their foundation remains the same—I dare not dismiss them lightly."

Zhu Xi said: "A skilled calendar-maker must establish broad, flexible parameters to encompass all—this is indeed correct." As for the theory of "six days and seven fen," the various scholars have debated it thoroughly—what need is there to belabor it further?

The *Xiang* (Image) of *Ge* (Revolution) states: "Fire within the marsh—*Ge*. The noble person, in accordance with this, regulates the calendar and clarifies the seasons." Zhu Zifa said: "The winter solstice begins at the first degree of *Qianniu* (Ox Leader), proceeding rightward through the twelve stations, completing at the twenty-sixth degree of *Dou*, then returning to the first degree of *Qianniu*—thus the calendar is renewed. *Qianniu* is the fire position, *Xingji* (Star Marker) the water position; the sun and moon conjoin here, forming the image of fire within the marsh. This is the origin of the *Tai Chu* calendar’s epoch, corresponding in hexagram qi to the third month. The *Tai Xuan* aligns with this, renewing the historian’s daily reckoning. It occupies the forty-ninth position in the sequence of hexagrams, matching the nodal point of the *Tai Yan* (Great Expansion) number, and the sixtieth, aligning with the degrees of the celestial circuit.

The sixty hexagrams and 360 lines, each line governing a day. The upper canon begins with *Qian* at *jiazi*, *Tai* at *jiaxu*, *Shi* at *jiashen*, and concludes at *Li*, thirty hexagrams encompassing 180 days, exhausting the three *jia*. The lower canon begins with *Xian* at *jiawu*, *Sun* at *jiachen*, *Zhen* at *jiayin*, and ends at *Jie* at *guihai*, likewise thirty hexagrams spanning 180 days to complete a year. Thus, Jing and Jiao used them to determine daily nodes, calling them 'heaven-and-earth nodes.' The four seasons achieve revolution—this is also called 'heaven-and-earth revolution.' The four seasons are thus established, or unified in a single Dao of nodal division.

The subsequent hexagrams—*Zhong Fu*, *Xiao Guo*, and *Ji Ji*—serve to divide the *Kan*, *Li*, *Zhen*, and *Dui* hexagrams, corresponding to *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*, representing spring, summer, autumn, and winter. Doubling the four seasons yields the eight nodes, marking the solstices, equinoxes, and the beginnings and closings of the seasons. Each line governs fifteen days, aligning with the seventy-two seasonal markers.

Former scholars claimed that hexagrams begin with *Zhong Fu*—this is incorrect. *Zhong Fu* begins at *jiazi*. *Qian* in the eleventh month starts at *jiazi*, with yang qi culminating at *si* and ending its node. Ten hexagrams yield *guihai* in the tenth month—here, yin is born at *wu*, culminating at *hai* to conclude the reckoning of a year.

Now, it is also said that *Zhong Fu* corresponds to the eleventh month, beginning at *jiazi* and concluding at *Wei Ji*—this is because hexagram qi all arise from the mid-month qi of the preceding month. The *Tai Xuan* aligns *Zhong Fu* with the initial six of *Kan*, corresponding to the eleventh month. The mid-month qi begins to stir, resonating with the *Shang Yuan* (Upper Origin). The *Tai Chu* calendar takes the new moon and winter solstice of the eleventh month as the origin of reckoning, the head of calendar computation.

*Zhong Fu* (upper *Xun*, lower *Dui*), *Xiao Guo* (upper *Zhen*, lower *Gen*), and *Ji Ji* (embodying the forms of *Kan* and *Li*) constitute the six children. The qi of lesser yang and lesser yin, the six children, distribute across the four seasons to complete a year’s seasonal markers."

Shaozi said: "Luo Xiahong reformed the *Zhuan* calendar into the *Tai Chu* calendar, and Yang Xiong modeled the *Tai Xuan* upon the *Tai Chu* calendar. Every sixth hexagram, qi arises from the center. Thus, the first hexagram combines three celestial and two terrestrial numbers—though not the true celestial and terrestrial numbers, it approximates them.

Kangjie said: "Does the *Tai Xuan* not grasp the heart of heaven and earth? The heart is where *Kun*’s extremity generates *Qian*, beginning at the time of the winter solstice—this is the origin of pitch standards and calendars. Its profound and far-reaching insights harmonize with the divine to this extent." Thus, his poem reads: "Were it not for Yangzi’s learning of heaven and man, how could there be Zhuangzi’s inner and outer chapters?"

When the first yang stirs and the myriad things are yet unborn, the sage perceives the heart of heaven and earth in this. Hexagrams begin with *Zhong Fu*, responding in seven days. Its central emptiness images the heart, resonating with the Han *Tai Chu* calendar.

The *Zhuan Emperor’s Calendar* takes the tenth month as its beginning, while the *Lianshan* Classic begins with *Gen*. Ziyun’s *Tai Xuan* comprises eighty-one chapters, each with nine eulogies, totaling seven hundred twenty-nine eulogies. These eulogies are the *yao*. Two eulogies correspond to one day, each eulogy governing six hours; one hour yields six *ce*, so one eulogy yields thirty-six *ce*, aligning with the *yao* of the sixty hexagrams’ qi. Each hexagram spans six and seven-sevenths days, while each *Tai Xuan* chapter spans four and a half days. One chapter of four and a half days leaves a remainder, but two chapters of nine days balance evenly. Yin chapters take yin numbers as their basis, while yang chapters take yang numbers. The number five resides between two and four—five is earth. One, two, three, and four, the generating numbers, precede five; six, seven, eight, and nine, the completing numbers, follow it.

Master Zhu said: "*Tai Xuan* entirely follows Jiao Yanshou’s method of deriving hexagram qi." The explanations of Jiao’s school differ. In King Wen’s eight trigrams, *Qian* lies in the northwest, with twelve hexagrams in the southeast; *Kun* lies in the southwest, with twelve hexagrams in the northwest—their positions are entirely distinct. The *Yi*’s trigrams—*Zhen* in the east, *Dui* in the west, *Li* in the south, and *Kan* in the north—form one system, while the twelve sovereign hexagrams assigned to the twelve earthly branches form another. When Jiao Yanshou devised his method of hexagram qi governing days, he merged the two into one. He not only used the eight trigrams’ *Kan*, *Li*, *Zhen*, and *Dui*—with their twenty-four *yao* governing days and hours—but also assigned the twelve sovereign hexagrams to the twelve months. Further dividing them into forty-eight hexagrams, corresponding to dukes, marquises, ministers, and grandees, the theory of "six days and seven parts" emerged. *Tai Xuan* models itself on hexagram qi, taking *Zhong Fu* as the beginning of the winter solstice and *Yi*’s top *yao* as the end of *Da Xue*. *Tai Xuan* also takes the center as the inception of yang qi, the start of the winter solstice. *Yang* contains the two eulogies *Qi* and *Ying*, marking the end of *Da Xue*. All follow the sequence of the *Yi*’s hexagram qi but alter their names. Hexagram qi employs *Kan*, *Li*, *Zhen*, and *Dui* to govern the twenty-four qi. *Tai Xuan*, however, begins with the center, multiplies by four and then six, to govern the twenty-four qi. In its selection of numbers, it fundamentally follows the *River Chart*: one and six share a lineage, two and seven form a fellowship, three and eight become companions, four and nine follow the same path, and five and ten guard one another—thus uniting the generating and completing numbers. To approximate this, the symbols of robes and garments are derived from three and eight...

The might of halberds and spears derives from four and nine; the system of ruler and minister, from two and seven; the sacrifices to spirits and gods, from one and six; the nourishment of wine and food, from five and five—yet five is unspoken, for five times five makes ten.

It is further said: Yang Xiong’s *Tai Xuan* is entirely modeled on the *Yi*. While the *Yi* employs the number four, he uses the number three throughout. Since he originally imitated the *Yi*, one may glimpse its meaning by examining the very phrases he imitates. Where the *Tai Xuan* aligns with the *Yi*: *Zhou* corresponds to *Fu*, *Tong* to *Meng*, *Zeng* to *Yi*, *Jiao* to *Tai*, and *Yi* to *Yang*. All these names are drawn from the *Yi*’s hexagrams. Sometimes one section matches one hexagram, or two or three sections match a single hexagram—thus clarifying the method of minute divisions.

*Xuan Chong* is the sequence of hexagrams; *Xuan Cuo*, the miscellaneous hexagrams; *Xuan Shu*, the commentary on the hexagrams; *Xuan Wen*, the *Wen Yan*. The *Xi Ci*’s exposition on the *Tui Ying Kang Tu* is its proclamation—all modeled on the *Yi*. The *Yi* begins with one generating two, two generating four, four expanding to eight, and eight times eight yielding sixty-four, where it halts. The *Tai Xuan* begins with one generating three, three generating nine, and nine times nine yielding eighty-one, where it halts. The *Yi* has six lines: metal, wood, water, and fire form one division, the upper line another. Six times six produces sixty-four hexagrams and three hundred eighty-four lines. The *Tai Xuan* has nine eulogies, dividing the generation of metal, wood, water, and fire. These become eight, and with earth’s guarding of one, form nine. Nine times nine extends to seven hundred twenty-nine eulogies, where it is complete. Both the *Yi* and the *Tai Xuan* share the same Five Phases.

The *Yi* multiplies two by four, four by eight, eight by sixty-four, and sixty-four by three hundred eighty-four—rooted in the *River Chart*’s celestial three multiplied by earth’s four. Thus, four is the body of earth, while heaven employs the number three. This is why Fuxi doubled three lines into six to form a hexagram. The numbers of the *Tai Xuan* arise from the pitch of *Huang Zhong*: a nine-inch hollow with a nine-fen circumference. Tripling one yields the Three Powers; tripling heaven’s three yields the Nine Categories; tripling earth’s four yields the twelve sovereign hexagrams; tripling heaven’s five yields the fifteen days of one qi; tripling earth’s six yields the eighteen changes that form a hexagram.

*Huang Zhong* begins at *zi*, heaven’s one. Tripling *zi*’s one yields three at *chou*; tripling *chou*’s three yields nine at *yin*; tripling *yin*’s nine yields twenty-seven at *mao*; tripling *mao*’s twenty-seven yields eighty-one at *chen*; tripling *chen*’s eighty-one yields two hundred forty-three at *si*; tripling *si*’s two hundred forty-three yields seven hundred twenty-nine at *wu*. From *wu* through *wei*, *shen*, *you*, *xu*, and *hai*—the five celestial stems—it reaches one hundred seventy-seven thousand one hundred forty-seven. This is the number of Jing Fang’s sixty pitch-standards, as recorded in the annotations of the *Treatise on Pitch Standards and Calendars* in the Eastern and Western *Han Histories*.

The *Tai Xuan*’s three hundred sixty-five days, each multiplied by eighty-one, yield twenty-nine thousand five hundred sixty-five parts. Adding one-fourth of a day—twenty parts and a fraction—makes twenty-nine thousand five hundred eighty-five parts and a fraction. Every eulogy and every qi aligns precisely in calculation. Thus it is said: "Below they drink, above they drink, issuing and entering the nine voids." The *Xuan* is the chief of spirits.

It is also said: Ziyun (Yang Xiong) was a man of deep thought, who would withdraw to ponder. Like the subtleties of yin and yang’s waxing and waning, he simply sought to deduce them. Zhang Heng praised its profound Dao. Its numbers enable one to debate matters of yin and yang—a book of the Han dynasty’s two hundred years, comparable to the Five Classics.

For example: "The sun’s path errs; yin and yang alternate in succession. Death and life intertwine; the ten thousand things are thus entangled." This speaks of the bright phase’s birth and the dim phase’s death, the dim phase’s death and the bright phase’s birth—death and life passing one to the other, the ten thousand things entwined and accomplished.

Or: "Examine the patterns of dragon and tiger; observe the principles of tortoise and bird. Establish heaven’s warp as yin and yang; establish earth’s weft as vertical and horizontal; establish human conduct as darkness and light." This means heaven is the warp, earth the weft; south and north are the warp, east and west the weft. The division between darkness and light, worthiness and foolishness, lies therein.

Or: "One day and one night make a full day; one yin and one yang generate the ten thousand things." This refers to summer’s sixty *ke* of daylight and winter’s sixty *ke* of night. No night is longer than the winter solstice, no day longer than the summer solstice. The winter solstice’s night does not surpass the summer solstice’s day, hence the day’s count is greater.

Or: "Nothing generates yang like *zi*; nothing generates yin like *wu*. In the northwest, *zi*’s beauty is exhausted; in the southeast, *wu*’s beauty is exhausted." This means yang begins at *zi* and ends at *wu*, yin begins at *wu* and ends at *zi*. The southwest still holds a trace of yang, hence in the northwest its beauty is exhausted. The northeast still holds a trace of yin, hence in the southeast its beauty reaches its peak.

As for the Way of yang being ever abundant and the Way of yin ever deficient, this means yang is fully employed while yin is half used. "South and north fix position; east and west circulate qi." That *zi* and *wu* fix position refers to the storehouses of yin and yang. When east’s wood flourishes, west’s metal dies—qi responds and communicates.

In its discussion of the sun and moon, it says: "The sun has south and north; without south and north, there would be no winter and summer. The moon has going and coming; without going and coming, there would be no new and full moons." The sage observes the changes of concealment and exposure, and regulates the sequence of the sun and moon’s male and female.

The sun reaches its southernmost point at *Niu* (Ox) and its northernmost at *Dongjing* (Eastern Well). The south is the Greater Yang; the north is the Greater Yin. When yang essence reaches the Greater Yang, it is summer; when yin essence reaches the Greater Yin, it is winter. At dusk, the moon appears in the west as *tiao* (exposure); at dawn, it appears in the east as *ce ni* (concealment)—the rest is seen in the *Greater Yang* chapter.

In its discussion of the numbers of yin and yang, it states: *Zi* and *wu* are nine; *chou* and *wei* are eight; *yin* and *shen* are seven; *mao* and *you* are six; *chen* and *xu* are five; *si* and *hai* are four. Thus, the forty-two *lü* and thirty-six *lü* (pitch standards) alternate or not, totaling seventy-eight. *Jia* and *ji* are nine; *yi* and *geng* are eight; *bing* and *xin* are seven; *ding* and *ren* are six; *wu* and *gui* are five. Sound arises from the sun; *lü* arises from the celestial stems. Sound is based on essence and substance; *lü* harmonizes with sound. When sound and *lü* align, the eight tones arise. The Yellow Bell begins at *zi*, as *Qian*’s first yang line starts with nine. *Wu* is the palace of *zi*, hence both are nine. The months from *zi* to *si* correspond to those from *wu* to *hai*. The numbers four, five, six, seven, eight, and nine are divided: *jia* and *ji* pair with *zi* as nine; *yi* and *geng* follow *chou* as eight; *bing* and *xin* follow *yin* as seven; the numbers six and five follow likewise. Yang *lü* are nine, seven, and five, doubled to make forty-two. Yin *lü* are eight, six, and four, doubled to make thirty-six. Combined, they total seventy-eight. At eight, *chou* and *wei* return, obtaining *lü* but not *lü* (pitch standards). The five tones arise from the ten celestial stems: *jia* and *yi* are *jiao* (角); *bing* and *ding* are *zhi* (徵); *geng* and *xin* are *shang* (商); *ren* and *gui* are *yu* (羽). The twelve *lü* arise from the twelve hours, from which they emerge. The eight tones are metal, stone, silk, bamboo, gourd, earth, leather, and wood. This is the source of sound and *lü*, the numbers of the Five Phases. Later generations who cite this to discuss Kangjie’s numbers—how could they know its true origin lies here?

The profound principle: The numbers of the *Tai Xuan* begin with three. *Tai Xuan* is the Supreme Ultimate. It symbolizes the ruler’s position. The three directions are heaven, earth, and humanity, representing the Three Powers. The *Xuan* of heaven, the *Xuan* of earth, and the *Xuan* of humanity. The three directions symbolize the Three Dukes; each direction has three provinces, totaling nine provinces symbolizing the Nine Ministers. Each province has three departments, totaling seventy-two departments symbolizing the grand masters. Heaven’s Way is established as beginning, middle, and end; earth’s Way as upper, middle, and lower; humanity’s Way as contemplating fortune and misfortune.

The *Yi* divines through change; the *Xuan* divines through penetration. The opening eulogies of the *Xuan* are all based on the Five Phases. From *zhong* (center) to *zhou* (cycle), reaching *yang* (nourishment): *zhong* is one, water; *zhou* is two, fire; *zan* is three, wood; *xian* is four, metal; *shao* is five, earth; *li* is six, water (earth’s six completes water). *Shang* is seven, fire; *gan* is eight, wood; *shi* is nine, metal. The numbers end at nine. From *xian* onward, it begins again as one, water. This pattern continues thereafter. Each of the nine eulogies in a section: the first is water, the second is fire, the third is wood, the fourth is metal, the fifth is earth, the sixth is water again, the seventh is fire, the eighth is wood, and the ninth is metal. In all sections, five is the ruler. The primary line of a section is like the fifth line in a *Yi* hexagram. In the *Xuan*, the first section begins with yang, corresponding to the first section of yin. The eighty-one sections all correspond to one another. Odd sections are yang; even sections are yin. Odd corresponds to odd; even to even. Their governing principles differ and oppose one another.

The *Xuan*’s sequence states: "Patrolling the six *jia*, it meets the Dipper." This is the great principle of the *Xuan*’s calendrical origins. The *Xuan* has *qi man* (excess), just as the Dipper has intercalary months. The eighty-one sections begin from the center, and each section must indicate the degree of the sun’s entry at the month’s first day, all governed by the Dipper. For time is easily corrected and visible, while the Dipper’s reckoning is obscure and prone to error. When seasonal qi and the Dipper align without discrepancy, intercalation is correct, and the calendar is accurate.

It also states: "The *Tai Xuan* measures the sun by day and the Dipper by night," omitting the moon, for it constantly waxes to govern the void. The *Xuan* measures the sun by day and the Dipper’s pointer because they are constant and unchanging. The moon waxes and wanes, its size and speed irregular, hence it is not recorded. The degrees of the twenty-eight lodges in the diagram are not the celestial twenty-eight lodges but the positions of seasonal nodes. The degrees of the sun’s entry at the month’s first day and the *Monthly Ordinances*’ solar entries are broadly similar.

The *Xuan*’s diagram states: "From *zi* to *chen*, from *chen* to *shen*, from *shen* to *zi*, crowned with *jia*." Chapters, sessions, unities, and elements vanish together with lunar eclipses—this is the *Xuan*’s Way. Wu Ji of Yulin annotates: In the first year of the *Taichu* epoch, the first month was *jiazi*, the new moon coincided with the winter solstice, with no remainder. After 1,539 years, the first month was *jiachen*, the new moon coincided with the winter solstice, with no remainder. After another 1,539 years, the first month was *jiashen*, the new moon coincided with the winter solstice, with no remainder. Nineteen years make one *zhang*; twenty-seven *zhang* make 513 years. One *hui* is the completion of the sun and moon’s conjunction. Eighty-one *zhang* make 1,539 years, one *tong*. From *zi* to *chen*, from *chen* to *shen*, totaling 4,617 years make one *yuan*. A *yuan* has three *tong*; a *tong* has three *hui*; a *hui* has twenty-seven *zhang*. Nine *hui* have 243 *zhang*, completing the cycle. Calculating a *yuan*’s numbers with *zhang*, *hui*, and *tong*: when the nine *hui*’s *tong* numbers are exhausted, the cycle ends. One *zhang* exhausts intercalary fractions; one *hui* exhausts lunar eclipses; one *tong* exhausts new moon fractions; one *yuan* exhausts the six *jia*—this is the *Xuan*’s Way.

The *Xuan* begins at the Celestial Origin, at the first dawn of *jiazi*, when the winter solstice commences at the start of *Qianniu*. From a span of eight *cun* to a single step, it moves ceaselessly. It revolves through 365¼ degrees, thirty days make a month, twelve months make a year. Intercalary months are added to fix the four seasons, completing 365¼ days—yet it does not fully circle. The *Zhuan* calendar falls short by ¾ of a day, while the *Tai Chu* calendar falls short by half a day. The reason it does not fully circle is that yang numbers overflow while yin numbers are deficient. Thus, two surplus measures are added to fulfill the *Xuan*’s numbers and align with celestial degrees—just as intercalary months are added to reconcile the year’s days, thereby enacting the pitch pipes and calendrical reckoning.

I deem that Shao Kangjie’s *Jingshi* calendar, though differing from this, proceeds by analogy to achieve divine subtlety. Master Shao’s *Xiantian* hexagram qi: *Qian* and *Kun*, *Kan* and *Li*, are apportioned to the four seasons, governing the twenty-four qi. When *Kan* completes its course at the *zi* midpoint, it exchanges with *Li*; its first line marks the winter solstice, its top line the *Jingzhe*. When *Li* completes its course at the *mao* midpoint, it exchanges with *Qian*; its first line marks the spring equinox, its top line the *Mangzhong*. When *Qian* completes its course at the *wu* midpoint, it exchanges with *Kan*; its first line marks the summer solstice, its top line the *Bailu*. When *Kan* completes its course at the *you* midpoint, it exchanges with *Kun*; its first line marks the autumn equinox, its top line the *Daxue*. Spring, summer, autumn, and winter each employ sixty-four hexagram qi, all arising from the *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you* midpoints as the four cardinals—two solstices and two equinoxes. The *yin*, *shen*, *si*, and *hai* positions correspond to the four *Meng* and four *Li*. In the *Jingshi* calendar, the winter solstice is the origin of heaven and earth, the origin of origins. Thus, it omits the four cardinal hexagrams and employs three hundred sixty. The *Guqi Tu* takes the spring equinox as the origin of humanity, also the origin of origins. Thus, it employs sixty-four hexagrams, multiplied fourfold to make two hundred fifty-six positions. The *Guqi Tu* takes the *zi* midpoint of the winter solstice as the origin of the world, the *mao* midpoint of the spring equinox as the origin of origins, the *wu* midpoint of the summer solstice as the origin of conjunctions, and the *you* midpoint of the autumn equinox as the origin of revolutions. Each employs sixty-four hexagrams, alternating according to qi and revolution. The inception of affairs and the unfolding of things begin at the *yin* midpoint, commencing at the *Jingzhe*, the second month’s first qi. The closure of things occurs at the *xu* midpoint, concluding at the *Lichun*, the tenth month’s first qi. Why? The *yin* and *xu* midpoints govern monthly conjunctions in name, but in function, they truly depend on the nodal qi. The earth’s generation of things relies on qi as its mechanism. Heaven’s qi arrives first, and only then do earth’s things respond. The qi’s arrival always precedes by half a month. Qi, by its nature, is ever-expansive and thus constantly overflows, while the moon, by its swiftness, is ever-contracting and thus constantly deficient. Thus, Guan Ziming said: "In the reckoning of appointed times, that which exceeds is called *qi* surplus, while that which falls short is called new moon deficiency." The irregularities of *qi* surplus and new moon deficiency accumulate over time, so that the central qi may occasionally fall on the month’s last day. Intercalation is then applied to restore the original alignment.

The *Jingshi* is rooted in the *Xiantian*, thus its central conjunctions and new moons both arise from hexagram qi. Following the *Xiantian*’s fundamental numbers, it takes the central qi to govern the months. The origins of *yuan*, conjunctions, revolutions, and worlds all begin from the center—this is what is meant by "establishing the correct from the center."
