The *Guqi Tu* states: "The great revolution’s method should follow the *Jingshi*, its numbers beginning at *xing*, *jia*, *chen*, *zi*. The lesser revolution’s method should follow the *Guqi Tu*, beginning at *jia*, *si*, *Meng* days." Heaven unifies the forms. The form of qi arises from the four midpoints. Thus, the great revolution’s *jiazi* corresponds to the winter solstice, and the heads of the twenty-four qi all attain the four midpoints of *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*. The function of qi operates through the four *Li*, thus the lesser revolution’s *jiayin* corresponds to the *Lichun*, and the heads of the twenty-four qi all attain the *yin*, *shen*, *si*, and *hai* positions, reigning over the four *Meng*.

Master Zhu said: In the *Xiantian Tu*, the left side begins with the first line of *Zhen* as the winter solstice, the midpoints of *Li* and *Dui* as the spring equinox, and proceeds to the end of *Qian* to exchange with the summer solstice. The right side begins with the first line of *Xun* as the summer solstice, the midpoints of *Kan* and *Gen* as the autumn equinox, and proceeds to the end of *Kun* to exchange with the winter solstice. The diagram’s reversal and progression, left and right, have been detailed by earlier scholars. *Qian* as one, *Dui* as two, *Li* as three, *Zhen* as four—these are the generated hexagrams, their sequence proceeding from south to north. As for the circulation of hexagram qi, it proceeds from north to south. The first yang arises at the beginning of *Zhen*, thus Master Shao takes the *zi* midpoint of the winter solstice as *Fu*. The eleventh month’s midpoint is the *Xiaohan*, its hexagrams *Kan* and *Zhun*. The twelfth month’s *Chuji* is the *Dahan*, its hexagrams *Zhen*, *Shike*, and *Sui*. The first month’s *Yinchu* is the *Lichun*, its hexagrams *Wu Wang* and *Ming Yi*. The month’s midpoint is the *Yushui*, its hexagrams *Gui*, *Ji Ji*, and *Jia Ren*. The second month’s *Maochu* is the *Jingzhe*, its hexagrams *Feng*, *Li*, and *Ge*. The month’s midpoint is the spring equinox, its hexagrams *Tong Ren* and *Lin*. The third month’s *Chenchu* is the *Qingming*, its hexagrams *Sun*, *Jie*, and *Fu*. The month’s midpoint is the *Guyu*, its hexagrams *Mei*, *Kui*, and *Dui*. The *Sichu* of the fourth month is the *Lixia*, its hexagrams *Lü* and *Tai*. The month’s midpoint is the *Xiaoman*, its hexagrams *Da Xu*, *Xu*, and *Xiao Xu*. The *Wuchu* of the fifth month is the *Mangzhong*, its hexagrams *Da Zhuang*, *Da You*, and *Guai*. At the end of *Qian*, it exchanges with the summer solstice, which is the *wu* midpoint. These thirty-two hexagrams belong to yang and correspond to spring and summer.

The hexagrams *Xun* as five, *Kan* as six, *Gen* as seven, and *Kun* as eight are the ungenerated hexagrams. The diagram proceeds from west to north, but the circulation of hexagram qi begins with the first yin arising at the start of *Xun*, thus the *wu* midpoint of the summer solstice is the beginning. The fifth month’s midpoint is the *Xiazhi*, the sixth month’s *Weichu* is the *Xiaoshu*, its hexagrams *Da Guo*, *Ding*, and *Heng*. The month’s midpoint is the *Dashu*, its hexagrams *Xun*, *Jing*, and *Gu*. The *Shenchu* of the seventh month is the *Liqiu*, its hexagrams *Sheng* and *Song*.

The month’s midpoint is *Chushu*, its hexagrams *Kun*, *Weiji*, and *Xie*. The eighth month’s *You* beginning is *Bailu*, its hexagrams *Yang*, *Kan*, and *Meng*. The month’s midpoint is the autumn equinox, its hexagrams *Shi* and *Dun*. The ninth month’s *Xu* beginning is *Hanlu*, its hexagrams *Xian*, *Lü*, and *Xiaoguo*. The month’s midpoint is *Shuangjiang*, its hexagrams *Jian*, *Jian*, and *Gen*. The tenth month’s *Hai* beginning is *Lidong*, its hexagrams *Qian* and *Pi*. The month’s midpoint is *Xiaoxue*, its hexagrams *Cui*, *Jin*, and *Yu*. The eleventh month’s *Zi* beginning is *Daxue*, its hexagrams *Guan*, *Bi*, and *Bo*. At the end of *Kun*, it exchanges with the winter solstice, which is the *Zi* midpoint. These thirty-two hexagrams belong to yin and correspond to autumn and winter.

From *Zi* to *Si* are *Qian*, *Dui*, *Li*, and *Zhen*. The six yang months have four solar terms: the winter solstice, *Lichun*, the spring equinox, and *Lixia*. From *Wu* to *Hai* are *Xun*, *Kan*, *Gen*, and *Kun*. The six yin months also have four solar terms: the summer solstice, *Liqiu*, the autumn equinox, and *Lidong*. One year has eight solar terms. Doubling these yields sixteen hexagrams, with sixteen additional qi. Tripling these yields forty-eight hexagrams, and combining them makes sixty-four, corresponding to one cycle of seasonal qi. Thus time is fixed and the year completed, and the movements of ghosts and spirits bring about transformation.

Now Master Zhu* takes the two solstices and two equinoxes to grasp the essentials; I, in my foolishness, have inferred the details through hexagram qi.

*Tianyuan Fawei*, Scroll Eleven, concludes.

*Tianyuan Fawei*, Scroll Twelve
Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai
Collated by Fang Hui Wugu of Xu Valley and Wan Li

The Expansion and Contraction of Yang and Yin

Yang expands for six days, also called *qi* expansion. This accounts for the remainder of five and one-quarter days. Yin contracts for six days, also called new moon deficiency. This refers to the six days of lunar emptiness in a short month. Over one year, the total remainder of twelve days is allocated as intercalary months, honoring the supremacy of yang.

This describes the sun, moon, and five planets all revolving leftward with the heavens. The latter system follows the motions of the guards. It describes the sun, moon, and five planets all revolving rightward against the heavens. If the heavens revolve leftward, the sun and moon also revolve leftward. The sun’s motion falls short of the heavens, and the moon’s motion falls short of the sun’s—this sequence follows principle and is thus correct. Confucians regard this explanation as valid, adhering to the *prior heavens*.

If the heavens revolve leftward while the sun and moon revolve rightward against them, the sun moves slowly while the moon moves swiftly. Calendar scholars adopt this explanation for *Yi*-based calculations, adhering to the *posterior heavens*. Confucians do not accept this view but provisionally retain both for reference.

The *Canon of Yao* states: “Three hundred and sixty-six days, with intercalary months to fix the four seasons and complete the year.” The *Book of Documents* commentary says: “From this winter solstice to the next is one cycle. Within this cycle, there are either 366 days or slightly more than 365 days. The heavens and the sun converge above to form one year, comprising twenty-four qi. The interval between two years remains consistent, even when intercalary months occur.”

The *day divisor* is multiplied by 940 parts to measure the celestial circuit. The remainder beyond the full degrees is one-quarter of a day, for a day is divided into 940 parts. Dividing these into four yields 235 parts, which constitutes one degree. Thus, one-quarter of these parts is obtained.

Master Zhu said: “In terms of *qi*, there are 360 days; in terms of lunar phases, there are 354 days. Taking the median of *qi* expansion and lunar deficiency, we speak of 360 days. The remaining variations do not exceed six parts.” The *Canon of Yao* uses round numbers, hence “three hundred and sixty-six days.”

Hu Shenzhen said: “When reckoning the year by the four seasons, each season has ninety-four days and ninety parts. When reckoning by the six *qi*, each has sixty-six days and sixty parts. When reckoning by the five phases, each has seventy-two days and seventy-two parts. All yield 360 days. The two trigrams *Qian* and *Kun* follow the same principle—this is the norm of celestial numbers. Exceeding this is expansion; falling short is contraction.

When expanded, the yang path is ever-abundant, so the sun’s circuit of the celestial degrees constitutes one year. Beyond the 360 days, the year consistently has a remainder of slightly more than five days. When contracted, the yin path is ever-deficient, so the moon’s circuit of the celestial degrees constitutes one month, consistently falling short of thirty days. This is why there are short months and why the year has a remainder of slightly more than five days. Accumulated over the years, this yields the so-called eleven-day deficit. The surplus of accumulated years—once every three years, twice every five, and seven times every nineteen years—completes the great pattern of the heavens.

The sun and moon cannot be without expansion and contraction, and calendar makers cannot avoid discrepancies. Thus, intercalary months are established to stabilize the seasons and complete the year.” The *Supreme Principles Governing the World* also takes 360 as the standard: one *yuan* comprises 360 *yun*, one *hui* comprises 360 *shi*, one *yun* comprises 360 years, one *shi* comprises 360 months, one year comprises 360 days, and one month comprises 360 *chen*. Yang attains 360 through the number six.

Master Zhang of Hengqu said: “The sun and moon both revolve leftward.” His explanation: “The heavens are exceedingly vigorous, completing one circuit of 365¼ degrees in one day and night. They then exceed this by one degree. The sun’s motion, though swift, is secondary to the heavens. In one day and night, it completes 365¼ degrees, precisely matching the heavens’ advance of one degree, which causes the sun to appear to retreat by one degree. Over two days, the heavens advance two degrees, and the sun retreats two degrees. Accumulating over 365¼ days, the heavens’ excess degrees complete a full cycle, and the sun’s retreating degrees also exhaust their original count, reuniting with the heavens to form one year.

The moon moves slowly, completing 365¼ degrees in one day and night but falling short of the heavens by slightly more than thirteen degrees.” Master Zhu said: “If one assumes the heavens complete one circuit daily without excess while the sun falls short by one degree, how can the mid-star positions of the four seasons differ? If this were so, what would define the boundaries of the seasons? As days pass like lamps relayed in succession, noon would soon strike the third watch.”

Here, I cite two passages from the *Monthly Ordinances* commentary that clarify the matter; other calendrical works do not explain it thus. If we consider the heavens’ motion internally, they complete only 365¼ degrees. If we consider them externally, they exceed one degree per day. The sun and moon are discussed within the heavens; if observed from the great void, the heavens themselves are not fixed in their former positions.

He further said: “The sun and moon both begin at *Jiao*. The sun completes one circuit daily, returning to *Jiao*, while the heavens complete one circuit and slightly surpass *Jiao*. This accumulates daily, and after one year, they reunite with the sun.”

In my humble opinion, the explanations of these two masters reveal that the heavens’ motion exceeds, the moon’s falls short, and only the sun’s motion is correct. Thus, one year is reckoned at 360 days. Master Shao said: “Yang *qi* expands upon 360. In one year, the sun’s reunion with the heavens exceeds five days and 235 parts, constituting *qi* expansion. Each day’s *qi* expansion is 13 parts, 7 *li*, 8 *si*, and 3 *hu*.”

Between two *lichun* solar terms, there are in total 4,935 parts, which together constitute the excess of five days and 235 parts. Speaking in whole numbers, the heavens exceed by six days. This is the measure by which the sun’s motion falls short of the heavens.

Master Zhu said: “The celestial body is perfectly round, with a circumference of 365¼ degrees. Revolving leftward around the earth, it completes one full circuit each day while surpassing one degree. The sun, attached to the heavens yet slightly slower, also completes one circuit around the earth daily, but in relation to the heavens, it falls short by one degree. Over 365 days and 940/940 of a day—specifically 235 parts—it reunites with the heavens. This is the measure of the sun’s annual motion.”

According to Kong’s commentary: “The heavens are the great void, inherently formless; we merely designate the revolving stars as ‘heaven.’ The heavens envelop the earth like the white surrounds the yolk. The stars revolve from east to west, completing one cycle in 365¼ days before returning to their original positions. While the stars turn leftward, the sun moves rightward, also taking 365¼ days to return to its original stellar position. These stars are the twenty-eight lunar lodges, distributed across the four directions, forming the celestial body. Each lodge spans 13 degrees and 402 parts, totaling 365¼ degrees. Whether measured in celestial degrees or annual days, the count remains the same. Yet due to the excess or deficiency of *qi*, there arises the waxing and waning of time.” The preceding explanations have thoroughly addressed this.

In my humble opinion: If the heavens’ motion were without excess, matching the sun’s, and the moon’s motion without deficiency, also matching the sun’s—if every day they all returned to *Jiao* and every year consisted of exactly 360 days without remainder—then there would be no irregularity in cold and heat, no variation in the size of days and months, no droughts or floods. Were the heavens to impose such rigid uniformity upon humanity, the workings of creation would lose their pattern. How then could the mechanisms of spirits reward virtue and punish vice operate?

He further said: “Yin *qi* contracts upon 360. Each month’s new moon lacks 441 parts. From *zi* to *chou*, the moon totals 5,292 parts. Together with the deficiency of five days and 592 parts—rounded to six days—this constitutes the measure by which the moon’s motion falls short of the sun’s.”

Master Zhu said: “The moon, attached to the heavens, is even slower. Each day, it falls short of the heavens by 13 degrees and 7/19 of a degree. Over 29 days and 499/940 of a day, it reunites with the sun. Twelve such conjunctions yield 348 full days, with an accumulated remainder of 5,988 parts. Divided by the day divisor of 940, this gives six days and a remainder of 348, totaling 354 days and 348/940 of a day. This is the measure of the moon’s annual motion.”

In my humble analysis: The “7/19 of a degree” means dividing 940 parts into nineteen portions, each portion being 49 parts, 4 *li*, 2 *hao*, 1 *hu*, 5 *si*, 7 *miao*, and 6 *shao*—the daily shortfall by which the moon lags behind the heavens and the sun’s regular motion. Thus, each day, the moon falls short of the sun by 12 degrees and 346 parts. Over 29 days and 499 parts, its accumulated shortfall reaches 365 degrees and 235 parts—the very degrees by which the sun advances. At this point, the sun’s excess precisely completes its full cycle, while the moon’s deficiency is entirely exhausted. They reunite to form one month. Twelve such periods of 29 days yield 348 full days, and twelve accumulations of 499 parts total 5,988. Divided by the day divisor of 940, this results in six days and a remainder of 348.

For yang is whole while yin is half; yang is ever in excess, yin ever in contraction. The sun’s motion constantly surpasses the moon’s, while the moon’s motion constantly falls short of the sun’s. In one year, intercalary days arise from the *qi* excess and new moon deficiency, totaling 10 days and 827 parts—rounded to twelve days. These are used to establish intercalary months. Every three years, one intercalary month is added, accumulating three sets of 10 days and 827 parts—32 days and 601 parts, or thirty-six days in whole numbers. After five years, a second intercalary month is added, accumulating five sets of 10 days and 827 parts—54 days and 275 parts, or sixty days in whole numbers. Generally, an intercalary month occurs every thirty-two days, though not precisely uniform, the variation is not great. Thus, the sages devised the calendar, redistributing the remainders to compensate for the moon’s shortfall relative to the sun and the sun’s shortfall relative to the heavens. By harmonizing these three cycles into one, over twenty-nine years, the *qi*, new moons, and divisions align perfectly with the celestial timing, leaving no discrepancy even as fine as a hair’s breadth. Human affairs, too, proceed in accord with the seasons.

Someone asked about the celestial calendar: “The varying lengths of months are already set in the almanac; one need only calculate them. For instance, if the crescent appears on the second day, the preceding month was long; if on the third, it was short. As the dark bird arrives in the second month and departs in the eighth, so too do things follow this pattern.” An elder once said: “The sun and moon meet between the last and first days of the lunar month. On the first evening, the sight is most splendid: as the sun sets, a faint glow lingers, and the moon follows in its descent. By the second day, they are slightly farther apart. After the third day, as the crescent waxes, the distance gradually increases. By the fifteenth day, when the moon is full and opposite the sun, the sun’s swift motion has carried it halfway across the sky.”

The moon’s motion falls short of the sun’s, yet its retreat also covers half the heavens. From the sixteenth day to the month’s end, the sun’s course fully spans one celestial revolution, while the moon’s shortfall likewise completes one revolution. Thus, the sun advances through its full measure, the moon retreats through its full measure, and they meet again.

Master Shao said: “A year’s intercalation consists of six yin and six yang phases. Three years yield thirty-six days, hence one intercalary month every three years. Five years yield sixty days, hence two intercalary months every five years. Nineteen years yield two hundred twenty-eight days. Thus, seven intercalary months leave no remainder, and the calendar takes nineteen years as one *zhang*. That seven intercalary months yield two hundred twenty-eight days is the origin of the intercalation method.

The method of placing intercalary months arises from the irregular motions of the sun and moon. The sun travels one degree of the heavens per day, while the moon travels thirteen degrees and seven nineteenths of a degree per day. The thirteen degrees account for twelve revolutions of the heavens in one year, while the remaining seven nineteenths form the intercalary fraction. Thus, the intercalation method takes seven and nineteen as its basis: multiplying seven by twelve yields eighty-four, the number of months in seven years; multiplying nineteen by twelve yields two hundred twenty-eight, the number of months in nineteen years. Hence, within a year, one takes months; within a day, one takes hours. Then, taking eighty-four as seven parts and two hundred twenty-eight as nineteen parts, beginning from one hour and accumulating to one day yields a remainder of seven parts. Multiplying this by the thirty days in a month yields two hundred ten parts. Twelve months yield two thousand five hundred twenty parts, with every nineteen parts constituting one hour. A year thus contains one hundred thirty-two hours and eleven days, with a remainder of twelve parts. Multiplying this by the nineteen-year cycle yields forty-seven thousand eight hundred eighty parts. Dividing according to the method, each year yields eleven days and a remainder of twelve parts. Over nineteen years, this totals two hundred nine days and a remainder of two hundred twenty-eight parts, which, when converted into the divisions of a day’s twelve hours, equals two hundred ten days. Thus, nineteen years with seven intercalary months leave no remainder.

To determine the year, set seven parts per year, and when nineteen parts are reached, an intercalary month is added—thus knowing the year to intercalate. Multiplying the twelve months by the number of years yields eighty-four parts per year; when two hundred twenty-eight parts are reached, an intercalary month is added—thus knowing the month to intercalate. To determine the day, set seven intercalary parts per day, and when nineteen parts are reached, one hour of intercalation is gained—thus knowing the day of the intercalary new moon. Multiplying the twelve hours by the number of days yields eighty-four parts; when two hundred twenty-eight parts are reached, one hour of intercalation is gained—thus knowing the time of conjunction.

The mutual reckoning of seven and nineteen is the rough method of intercalation; the mutual reckoning of eighty-four and two hundred twenty-eight is the precise method. Intercalation originates from odd numbers, accumulating to seven and reaching completion at nineteen. Thus, seven and nineteen, when multiplied or divided by each other, both yield one hundred thirty-three. Since the methods for months and hours are derived by multiplying by twelve, they must also be divided by twelve, yielding two hundred twenty-eight. The parts of one month and the days of one *zhang* are both two hundred ten. Hence, the *Xici* speaks of “returning the odd to the beginning to symbolize intercalation,” while the *Xiantian* uses one hundred thirty-three for the number of days and one hundred five for the number of stars.

Intercalation is fundamentally the odd number of heaven, yet it is sought through the moon. Thus, one knows that yang takes yin as its measure, and yin and yang mutually constitute substance and function. Multiplying two hundred twenty-eight by ten and then doubling it yields forty-five million six hundred thousand—the number of one *yuan* in the *Sifen* calendar.

It is also said: the sun advances by slowing, the moon retreats by hastening. When the sun and moon meet, half a day is added and half a day is subtracted, forming the intercalary remainder. The sun advances six days in one great revolution, the moon retreats six days in one great revolution—this is the intercalary difference. The heavens rotate leftward, while the sun and moon revolve rightward; their degrees have been explained earlier. The moon completes one revolution of the heavens each month, yet this is merely its apparent motion. Its actual conjunction with the sun occurs in the final day and a half, always trailing behind the sun. Thus, the sun, though slow, is deemed to advance, while the moon, though swift, is deemed to retreat.

The sun and moon meet every thirty days, though in reality it is twenty-nine and a half days. For the moon’s true period is twenty-nine and a half days, while the sun’s is thirty and a half days, yet both are reckoned as thirty days. Thus, at each conjunction, the sun gains half a day, and the moon loses half a day. The gain of half a day arises because the sun’s yearly course exceeds the moon’s by six days, and an additional six days are added. The loss of half a day arises because the moon’s yearly course falls short of the sun’s by six days, and an additional six days are subtracted. The accumulation of these gains and losses forms the intercalary remainder.

In one great revolution, the sun and moon advance and retreat by twelve days, yielding one intercalary month every three years and two intercalary months every five years—this is the intercalary difference. Three intercalary months in eight years, four in ten years, five in thirteen years, six in sixteen years, and seven in nineteen years. Extending this reckoning endlessly is called *tong*.

Master Zi said: “The year is at peace, yet the *nian* is afflicted.” The year is at peace when its reckoning is complete; the *nian* is afflicted when its reckoning is deficient, and intercalation compensates for it.

Master Zhu said: “Intercalation is determined by the central qi. The central qi belongs to its own month; if it falls after the month’s end, that month is to be intercalated.” The Dipper’s pointer indicates the central qi; the positions of the sun and moon, where the Dipper’s pointer lies between two celestial markers, is the place of their conjunction. If there is no central qi, the month is intercalated. As the Dipper’s handle shifts leftward and the sun and moon revolve rightward, the celestial markers and the established months remain in constant alignment.

The heavens are divided by boundaries, passing through the degrees and minutes of the palaces. Master Wang Yan said: “Using the degrees of the celestial revolution, one allocates them among the twelve palaces.”

The passage through each celestial palace is marked by specific divisions. One hundred seconds make a minute, and one hundred minutes make a degree. The year completes its cycle in 365 days and a quarter, aligning with the full celestial degrees at the advent of spring. The month completes its cycle in 30 days and a fraction, aligning with the degrees of a single palace at each nodal transition. Degrees serve as boundaries, and the celestial wheel is demarcated by these limits. The sun advances one degree per day, thus requiring 365 days and 25 *ke* to traverse the entire celestial boundary. The moon travels slightly more than twelve degrees—astrologers commonly say thirteen degrees for simplicity. The moon completes one full celestial circuit each month, traversing the entire celestial boundary. A month does not precisely comprise thirty days. With months of varying lengths, two months totaling fifty-nine days complete two full cycles. Generally, the moon completes one celestial circuit in slightly more than twenty-nine and a half days, and two circuits in slightly more than fifty-nine days.

I contend that ancient and modern calendar-makers have merely calculated the boundaries between yin and yang. This is why Confucius did not expound on calendrics but simply said, "Follow the Xia calendar’s seasons." It is also said: The theory of the heavens rotating leftward is akin to a large wheel on the outside with a smaller wheel carrying the sun and moon within. The large wheel turns swiftly, while the small wheel turns slowly. Though both rotate leftward, their differing speeds create the illusion that the sun and moon move rightward. The celestial body and its motion, along with the solar and lunar degrees, should be understood through four wheels for clarity.

Now, envision the large wheel as the heavens revolving externally—what is meant by "the heavens envelop the earth"—moving evenly and expansively, covering slightly more than a quarter of the celestial degrees. The smaller wheel carries the sun and moon internally, being smaller than the heavens, thus rotating inwardly and contracting. The moon’s motion lags behind the sun’s by slightly less than a quarter of its degrees. Therefore, the sun falls short of the heavens by one degree, and the moon falls short of the sun by slightly more than twelve degrees. The outer envelops the inner, the outer expansive, the inner contracted. Their speeds and trajectories naturally follow this pattern. Observing this through the four divisions makes it clear.

Years ago, I saw a diagram crafted by my friend Wu Jiuxia that aligned with this understanding. The outermost ring marked the celestial degrees as the celestial body. The second ring showed the heavens beginning at *Jiao*, completing one full revolution each day while advancing one degree past *Jiao*. The third ring depicted the sun also starting at *Jiao*, following the heavens but advancing one degree less per day, returning precisely to *Jiao* without excess. Thus, it falls short of the heavens by one degree. Each day, the sun retreats by one degree, exhausting its full measure. The heavens advance one degree daily, returning to their original position to align with the celestial degrees, completing one cycle. The fourth ring illustrated the moon’s slower motion, falling short of the sun by 12 degrees and 346.5 parts, accumulating to slightly more than twenty-nine days. The moon falls short of the sun’s degrees by 365 degrees and 235 parts, exhausting its full measure before reuniting with the sun, forming one month and twelve conjunctions. Calculating the moon’s deficit to the sun yields 5 days and 592 parts, with new moons, conjunctions, and intercalary adjustments totaling 10 days and 827 parts to establish intercalation. The specifics are not recorded here.

Someone asked: What is the meaning of Kangjie’s theory of "concealed intercalation" and "manifest intercalation"? I replied: The details are not easily explained. Briefly, a year consists of 360 days with an excess of 6 days concealed within the six *jia*—each *jia* spanning two months conceals one day. Six *jia* (360 days) conceal six days. "Manifest intercalation" refers to the period from *kaiwu* to *biwu*, where seven-tenths are utilized, and three-tenths are discarded at the nodal transitions. Of the original 360 days, 240 are used, yielding 252 days. The intercalary number twelve is manifested beyond the 240 days.

Master Zhu said: The *Records of the Grand Historian: Treatise on the Calendar* discusses the *Tai Chu* calendar of Emperor Zhuanxu and the *Four Divisions Calendar*. Liu Xin later revised it into the *Three Sequences Calendar*, while Yi Xing’s *Great Expansion Calendar* is the most detailed. The astronomical records of the Five Dynasties are also concise and rigorous, yet all these calendars—whether adding 720 or subtracting—remained accurate for only two or three years. Among ancient and modern calendars, only Kangjie’s calendar employed 129,600 parts, hence its precision.

I shall now summarize the calendars of previous dynasties:

The *Four Divisions Calendar* was created by Emperor Zhuanxu, based on the *Lianshan*’s *Book of Changes* beginning with *Gen*. It derived from the *River Chart*, expanding ten to one hundred, and dividing by four to yield twenty-five, aligning with the celestial degrees’ quarter division. The Qin dynasty used this calendar, designating the tenth month as the first. Emperor Wu of Han later revised it into the *Tai Chu Calendar*.

The *Tai Chu Calendar* was crafted by Tang Du, Luo Xia Hong, and others during Emperor Wu’s reign. It designated the first month as *yin*, renaming the seventh year of *Yuanfeng* (71 BCE) as the first year of *Tai Chu*, with the year named *Feng Jia She Ti Ge Yin* and the month named *Bi Ju*. The first day was *Jiazi* at midnight, coinciding with the winter solstice. This revision aligned with the *jia* year of Emperor Zhuanxu’s calendar’s inception, matching the auspicious omen of "linked jades and joined pearls." The year was not *jia yin* but the following one.

The *Three Sequences Calendar* was Liu Xin’s revision of the *Tai Chu Calendar*. Three threes make nine, and nine nines make eighty-one, forming the *Sequence Mother*. *Huang Zhong* marks the celestial sequence’s first *jiazi* beginning, *Lin Zhong* the earthly sequence’s second *jiachen* beginning, and *Tai Cu* the human sequence’s third *jia* middle beginning. Its epoch aligns with the *Tai Chu*’s commencement at *Huang Zhong*’s pitch (see previous *Xuan Tu* annotations).

The *Tai Xuan Calendar* was created by Yang Xiong of Han, corresponding to the *Yan Tian Chu* and *Zhuan Calendar*. The *Tai Chu* expanded the *Luoshu*’s nine nines, totaling the *Four Divisions*’ numerical essence.

The *Zhuan Calendar* expands its numbers by ten tens, dividing the *Hetu* into four parts—its core is the "void center" number. The *Tai Chu* takes eighty-one as its *ri fa* (day divisor), being nine nines. The *Tai Xuan* takes seventy-two as its *ri fa*, being eight nines. The *Tai Chu* takes thirty-two as its *chao fa* (fractional divisor), being eight fours. The *Tai Xuan* takes thirty-six as its *chao fa*, being nine fours. Comparing the *Xuan* to the *Chu*, dividing by nine and subtracting one second, or adding one to nine, both yield 2,592 seconds. Though their beginnings differ, their ends converge—see *Gua Qi* for details.

The *Tai Yan Calendar* was created by Tang’s Yi Xing. From Han to Sui, all calendars followed pitch-standards, but Yi Xing relied solely on the *Great Expansion*, rooted in *Gua Qi*. This is the "void center" number of the *Hetu*, taking the sum of one-six, two-seven, three-eight, and four-nine from the four directions to symbolize the four seasons. The void center’s five and ten combine to form the *yan mu* (expansion mother), while the old and young of nine-six and seven-eight serve as its application. The line numbers extend to sixty *ce* (counting rods), and the numbers proceed through 420—this is the *Great Expansion*, the pivot of Heaven and Earth. Later creators could not alter it.

The *Xian De Tong Tian Calendar* was created by Wang Pu of the Five Dynasties. Ouyang Xiu recorded it in his *Astronomical Examination*, establishing it as an independent school. Yang comprises thirty-six *ce*, yin twenty-four *ce*. The union of yin and yang numbers completes transformation, both yielding seventy-two. Doubling this gives 720, doubling again yields 720,000, and once more 72,000,000. When the *yuan* (primary cycle) generates the *yuan* (origin), years, months, days, and hours all align as *jiazi*. The conjunction of the sun, moon, and five planets at *zi* determines excess and deficiency, their mean positions, and the harmony of the seven luminaries.

In my view, among ancient and modern calendars, none surpass the Han *Tai Chu* and the Tang *Tai Yan*. Both originate from the *Hetu*, while intervening calendars merely adjust them. The six pitch-standards employ the *Luoshu*, beginning with odd numbers. The eight trigrams employ the *Hetu*, beginning with even numbers. Though their applications differ, all follow the *Posterior Heaven* *Yi*. At that time, the *Prior Heaven Diagram* remained hidden and unemerged.

The *Prior Heaven Calendar* was the foundation of Shao Yong’s *Huang Ji Jing Shi*. Its method derives from the multiplication of Heavenly Stems and Earthly Branches: the sun as *jia*, the moon as *zi*, the stars as *jiachen* and *zichou*, called the *Four Images*. In grand reckoning, these are termed *yuan*, *hui*, *yun*, and *shi*; in smaller reckoning, years, months, days, and hours. The sun’s *jia* governs one *tong*, the moon’s *zi* twelve; the stars’ *jia* governs 360 *tong*, and the *chen*’s *zi* 4,320. Altogether, thirty-six *jia* are evenly distributed among the twelve *chen*, each *chen* receiving 360.

The *Prior Heaven Calendar* differs from the *Posterior Heaven Calendar*. Excluding intercalary calculations, one year comprises twelve *hui*, 360 *yun*, 4,320 *shi*, and 129,600 years. This is why Master Zhu highly praised Kangjie’s calendar. I conclude this account of various calendars here.

Master Cheng also said: "In calendrical studies, if one grasps the underlying principles, much becomes clear. Only Shao Yaofu established a method for differences at the juncture of mutual influence, unmatched in antiquity or modernity. Yet in seeking the sun and moon’s yang excess and yin deficiency, he erred precisely where precision was most critical."

*The Heavenly Origin Explored*, Scroll Twelve, concludes.
*The Heavenly Origin Explored*, Scroll Thirteen
Compiled and annotated by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai
Collated and proofread by Fang Hui Wuli of Xugu

The Symbolic Forms and Numerical Patterns

The Yellow River yielded the *Diagram*, and the Eight Trigrams were drawn—thus yin and yang first took form. The Luo River yielded the *Script*, and the Nine Categories were arranged—thus the Five Phases first took number. Form cannot stand without the even; number cannot proceed without the odd. These two may possess each other, but cannot be without each other.

Master Zhu said: "Heaven and Earth merely cannot speak; it is the sage who speaks for them. If Heaven and Earth could speak, one imagines they would say it even better. The *River Diagram* and *Luo Script* are the *Changes* that Heaven and Earth have drawn." Fu Xi, gazing above and below, saw that all between Heaven and Earth was the *Changes*. The emergence of the *River Diagram* precisely matched his heart, so he followed it to draw the trigrams. Later generations’ failure to comprehend the study of symbolic forms and numerical patterns had its reasons.

Zhu Shiyu of Hanshang said: "Shang Qu learned from the Master; from Ding Kuan onward, his lineage flowed into Meng Xi and Jing Fang. Even in the Tang, their *Changes* could still be examined. Yixing compiled the *Changes* of these two schools, discussing hexagram qi, the absorption of the celestial stems into the Five Phases, and the like—all derived from the *Great Commentary* of the *Zhou Changes*, the *Appended Remarks*, and the *Discussion of the Trigrams*. Later, Ma [Rong], Zheng [Xuan], Xun [Shuang], and Yu [Fan] each established their own schools, yet they did not stray far from the source of symbolic forms and numerical patterns. In the Wei, Wang Bi and Zhong Hui, though fellow students, entirely discarded the old explanations, mingling them with Lao-Zhuang thought. They prized literary expression alone, no longer tracing back to origins. The Way of Heaven and humanity thus split. The *Great Commentary* states: 'The Yellow River yielded the *Diagram*; the Luo River yielded the *Script*—the sage took them as models.'"

Master Zhu said: "Kong Anguo said the *River Diagram* refers to when Fu Xi ruled the world and a dragon-horse emerged from the Yellow River; he then followed its markings to draw the Eight Trigrams. The *Luo Script* refers to when Yu controlled the floods and a divine turtle bore markings on its back, with numbers up to nine. Yu then arranged them into nine categories."

I humbly observe: At the dawn of Heaven and Earth, the Great Harmony was undifferentiated, and symbolic forms and numerical patterns were not yet manifest. These *River Diagram* and *Luo Script* were how Heaven opened the sage’s understanding. The *Analects* says: "The Yellow River does not yield the *Diagram*." The *Changes* says: "The Yellow River and Luo River yield the *Diagram* and *Script*." The *Documents* says: "The Heavenly Jade and *River Diagram*." Thus, the *Diagram* and *Script* are the natural patterns of Heaven and Earth, treasured objects through the ages, not the work of human effort. How could Masters Ma and Ou have further explanations?

Yet some indulge in the bizarre and absurd: the *Huainanzi* speaks of cinnabar writings and green diagrams; the *Spring and Autumn* apocrypha speak of nine chapters and six chapters. Gu Yewang took the eighteen characters of "agricultural use" and "reverent use" from the *Luo Script* as what the divine turtle bore. Ban Gu took the sixty-five characters from "one" to "six extremes" as the original text of the *Luo Script*. Kong Yingda of the Tang said: "Heaven’s words are concise and essential; they should not be so verbose and convoluted." Only Kong Anguo’s words are most apt, which is why Master Zhu adopted them.

It further states: "Heaven one, Earth two; Heaven three, Earth four; Heaven five, Earth six; Heaven seven, Earth eight; Heaven nine, Earth ten. The numbers of Heaven are five; the numbers of Earth are five. The five positions interact, each having its pair. The numbers of Heaven total twenty-five; the numbers of Earth total thirty—this is how transformations are accomplished and the spirits operate."

Master Zhu said: "This is how the Master elucidated the numbers of the *River Diagram*. Its positions: one and six below, two and seven above, three and eight to the left, four and nine to the right. Five at the center is the generating mother; ten next is the generating child. Then one, two, three, four—the positions of the Four Images. Then six, seven, eight, nine—the numbers of the Four Images. The two elders occupy the northwest; the two youths occupy the southwest. Their numbers each interact by category externally. Moreover, the phrase 'each having its pair' encompasses all the numbers of the *River Diagram*."

I humbly observe: The numbers of Heaven are the sum of the five odd numbers; the numbers of Earth are the sum of the five even numbers. One pairs with two to make an even; three pairs with four to make an even; five pairs with six to make an even; seven pairs with eight to make an even; nine pairs with ten to make an even. Each follows its odd or even counterpart, pairing like brothers—thus they follow their sequence without disorder. One pairs with six as Water; two pairs with seven as Fire; three pairs with eight as Wood; four pairs with nine as Metal; five pairs with ten as Earth. Each matches yin and yang like husband and wife—thus they unite in harmony without separation.

Ten Celestial Stems are likewise: Yang *jia* pairs with yin *yi* as Wood; *jia* departing to pair with *ji* transforms into Earth. Yang *bing* pairs with yin *ding* as Fire; *bing* departing to pair with *xin* transforms into Water. Yang *wu* pairs with yin *ji* as Earth; *wu* departing to pair with *gui* transforms into Fire. Yang *geng* pairs with yin *xin* as Metal; *yi* coming to pair with *geng* transforms into Metal. Yang *ren* pairs with yin *gui* as Water; *ding* coming to pair with *ren* transforms into Wood. All are the doubling of the Five Phases uniting into one. There must be pairing, and within pairing, there is mutual generation and overcoming, similarity and difference—unevenness. This is how the Five Constants—ruler and minister, father and son, elder and younger brothers, husband and wife, senior and junior—interact with the Five Phases and cannot be separated.

Heaven and Earth are the greatest pairing: one, three, five, seven, nine combine as the numbers of Heaven, yet do not depart from five. Two, four, six, eight, ten combine as the numbers of Earth, yet do not depart from five. The wondrous union of two and five generates humans and all things, totaling fifty-five. Five is the center. Center means change; change means transformation—its Dao is inexhaustible. One changes and six transforms; two transforms and seven changes; three becomes eight; four becomes nine; five becomes ten.

Transformation gives rise to change, and change gives rise to transformation. The interplay of odd and even generates all things—flexion and extension, coming and going—all emerge from the *Hetu* (River Diagram). Within the five numbers of Heaven and Earth, how could they be likened to the later generations’ talk of ghosts and spirits, of transformations and changes, which drifts into absurdity?

Master Zhu said: "The *Hetu* governs the five generating numbers to unify the five completing numbers. The *Luoshu* (Luo Writing) governs the five odd numbers to unify the four even numbers." Master Zhu further stated: "The generating numbers all reside within; the generating is the heart-mind of Heaven and Earth. The completing numbers all reside without; the completing is the achievement of Heaven and Earth." This saying is most profound.

In my humble observation: The *Hetu* divides yin and yang by generation and completion. The generating numbers—one, two, three, four, five—belong to yang and reside within. The completing numbers—six, seven, eight, nine, ten—belong to yin and reside without. The generating governs the completing, the inner harmonizes with the outer. The center is the host, while the outer is the guest. This is what is meant by "the constant of the body, each residing in its proper place."

The *Luoshu* divides yin and yang by odd and even. One, three, seven, and nine occupy the four cardinal positions, paired with the four yang trigrams. Two, four, six, eight, and ten occupy the four diagonal positions, paired with the four yin trigrams. The odd governs the even, the noble oversees the humble. The cardinal is the ruler, while the diagonal is the minister—this is what is meant by "through the changes of numbers, each resides in its proper place."

Yet the two diagrams, in their constancy and change, cannot be rigidly confined. In the *Hetu*, young yin and old yang remain unmoving, while young yang resides in the south, corresponding to the *Qian* position, and old yang resides in the west, corresponding to the *Kan* position—this does not align with the sequence of trigram generation. In the *Luoshu*, old yang resides in the south, corresponding to *Qian*, and young yang resides in the west, corresponding to *Kan*—this, in turn, aligns with the sequence of trigram generation. This is what is meant by "within constancy there is change, within change there is constancy"—its subtlety is beyond fathoming.

Master Shao said: "The round is the stars. Could the reckoning of calendrical cycles originate from this?" The *Tang Treatise on Astronomy* states in its "Discussion on Calendar Origins": "The numbers of Heaven begin with one, the numbers of Earth begin with two. Combining the two beginnings determines hardness and softness. The numbers of Heaven center on five, the numbers of Earth center on six. Combining the two centers unifies pitch standards and calendrical reckoning. Heaven has five notes to govern the days, Earth has six pitches to govern the hours." Master Zhu’s explanation of this is detailed; it need not be repeated here.

Master Shao said: "*Li* is the stars, *Zhen* is the hours. The stars combine with the ten of the sun—ten of the sun is five. The hours are the twelve branches—twelve is six. Five and six nurture the center of yin and yang; the *Scriptures of the Ages* use this to establish the calendar. Nine and ten interact; calculation methods arise from this interval. Thus it has been since the inception of calendrical and celestial observation."

The round is the stars. The *Hetu* lacks those four corners, conforming to its round shape. The numbers of the *Hetu* never fail to align with the numbers of the stars. Zhang said: "The celestial body’s number is seven, the Northern Dipper has seven stars. The sun, moon, and five planets number seven, and the twenty-eight lunar mansions total four times seven, making seven sevens. Including the six stars of the Southern Dipper, this sums to fifty-five numbers. From this, we know that in the interactions of Heaven, Earth, and human culture, all correspond to the numbers of the *Hetu*."

He further said: "The square is the earth. The method of dividing land into nine fields may derive from this." Master Zhu said: "A province has nine fields of nine hundred *mu*—this is the method of dividing land into nine fields." In my humble observation: The square is the pattern of the *Luoshu*. The earth has four directions; the *Book of Documents* places yang numbers in the four cardinal positions, symbolizing the division of land into well-fields. The *Book of Documents* takes nine as its foundation, also symbolizing the nine hundred *mu* of the well-fields. Heaven bestowed the Nine Categories; Yu received this *Luoshu* while regulating the waters. Thus, he opened the way for distinguishing the nine provinces, assessing the land, determining tribute, and exhaustively applying effort to irrigation channels. Yu accomplished the Nine Achievements, bringing enduring benefit to all under Heaven for myriad generations—from this, we know the greatness of the nine-chaptered *Luoshu*.

He further said: "The round is the numbers of the *Hetu*; the square is the pattern of the *Luoshu*. Thus, Fuxi and King Wen relied on them to create the *Yijing*, while Yu and Jizhong used them to compose the *Great Plan*. Numbers begin with one yin and one yang. The image of yang is round, so the *Hetu* is round in form yet square in number. The image of yin is square, so the *Luoshu* is square in form yet round in number. This is what is meant by 'three for Heaven, two for Earth.'"

The *Hetu* is ten and square, hence the *Great Commentary* contains the passage on "fifty-five." The *Luoshu* is nine and round, hence the *Great Plan* contains the section on "Heaven’s bestowal of the Nine Categories." In the primordial era, Heaven and Earth, yin and yang, each had their images, but initially, there were no numbers. With the *Hetu*, the fifty-five numbers emerged. The interplay of odd and even, generation and completion, became clearly visible. This is how it profoundly manifests the unique wisdom of the sages.

Master Zhu said: "One to ten form the *Hetu*; its center is left empty to create the *Yijing*. The *Hetu* is round in form, symbolizing Heaven, and ten and square in number, symbolizing Earth. The yang numbers total twenty-five, the yin numbers thirty, together making fifty-five—thus the even is paired with the odd. Leaving ten empty yields the *Luoshu*’s forty-five. Leaving five empty yields the *Dayan*’s fifty. Accumulating five and ten produces the *Luoshu*’s vertical and horizontal numbers. Multiplying five by ten and ten by five both yield the *Dayan*’s numbers."

"The *Hetu* follows the generating numbers, rotating leftward from north and east: water generates wood, wood generates fire, fire generates earth, earth generates metal, metal generates water again. Yet, in terms of opposing positions: the north’s one and six (water) overcome the south’s *bing* and *ding* (fire); the west’s four and nine (metal) overcome the east’s *jia* and *yi* (wood). Thus, the overcoming is already latent within the generating."

The ceaseless generation of life is called the *Yi*. When its center is emptied, the Supreme Ultimate gives rise to the Two Modes, the Four Images, and the Eight Trigrams. Thus it is said: "One generates two, two generates four, four generates eight." The *Hetu* also contains the purport of the *Luoshu*. Between Heaven and Earth, if there were only generation without overcoming, how could generation be accomplished? If there were only overcoming without generation, the human kind would perish. This is why the workings of creation are revealed through the *Hetu* and *Luoshu*.

It is also said: from one to nine forms the *Luoshu*; filling its center makes it the *Fan*. The *Luoshu* is square in form, resembling Earth, and its numbers total nine, round like Heaven. The yang numbers sum to twenty-five, the yin numbers to twenty, together forty-five—thus the odd exceeds while the even is deficient. When its center is emptied, it is also the Supreme Ultimate. The odd numbers each total twenty, forming the Two Modes. One, two, three, four contain six, seven, eight, nine; their vertical and horizontal sums of fifteen also form the Four Images. The central five, combined with the forty, likewise yields the *Dayan*’s number. Accumulating five and ten gives fifteen, which together form the *Hetu*’s numbers. The *Shu* and the *Tu* mutually complement each other.

The *Shu* takes the completed numbers, rotating rightward from north and west: water overcomes fire, fire overcomes metal, metal overcomes wood, wood overcomes earth, earth overcomes water, and water overcomes fire again. Yet, in terms of opposing positions: the southeast’s four and nine (metal) generate the northwest’s one and six (water); the northeast’s three and eight (wood) generate the southwest’s two and seven (fire). Thus, the generating is already latent within the overcoming. Overcoming is the means by which generation is accomplished. That which fills the center is five, which the *Huangji* takes as its foundation.

Master Kong of the Tang said: The *Huangji* does not speak of numbers because it encompasses the Nine Categories, unifying eight with one and standing at the center of Heaven. The Five Phases, Five Affairs, each five; the Eight Governances, Eight and Five Regulations, five. The *Huangji* alone occupies one and three; virtue three, divination seven, all governance ten, the utmost blessings born of the ultimate—these nine categories, too, encompass the numbers of the *Hetu*.

It is also said: Both the *Tu* and the *Shu* place five at the center. Five is the number that balances Heaven’s three and Earth’s two. Master Zhu said: The *Hetu* takes the five generating numbers as its essence, and thus the five at its center also embodies the image of the five generating numbers. Its lower point is the image of Heaven’s one, one combining with four. Its left point is the image of Heaven’s three, three combining with eight. Its right point is the image of Earth’s four, four combining with nine. Its central point is the image of Heaven’s five, five combining with ten. The five numbers reside at the center, the generating numbers on the outside, and the completed numbers beyond the generating numbers. One gains five to become six, two gains five to become seven, three gains five to become eight, four gains five to become nine. One, two, three, four generate through five, hence they are called generating numbers. Six, seven, eight, nine are completed through five, hence they are called completed numbers. Though the numbers are paired, they are never without interpenetration. Thus, the *Hetu*’s fifty-five numbers are inherently contained within the five central points.

The *Luoshu* takes the odd numbers as its essence, and thus the five at its center also embodies the image of the five odd numbers. Its lower point is also the image of Heaven’s one, one paired with two. Its left point is also the image of Heaven’s three, three linked with four. Its central point is also the image of Heaven’s five, five necessarily paired with ten. Its right point is also the image of Heaven’s seven, seven linked with six. Its upper point is the image of Heaven’s nine, nine necessarily paired with eight. The five numbers reside at the center, the odd numbers occupy the cardinal directions, and the even numbers are arranged at the four corners. One gains five to become six at the rear right, two gains five to become seven at the right, three gains five to become eight at the rear left, four gains five to become nine at the front. One, three, seven, nine are the odd numbers derived from five, hence they are called odd numbers. Two, four, six, eight are the even numbers of five, hence they are called even numbers. Though the numbers crisscross vertically and horizontally, each follows its own order without confusion. Thus, the *Luoshu*’s forty-five numbers are also inherently contained within the five central points.

Master Zhu said: The old and young of yin and yang mutually conceal their abodes. He also said: The numbers seven, eight, nine, and six differ. The *Hetu* positions the numbers one, two, three, four, combining them with the images and numbers of six, seven, eight, nine. Within the forty, five contains five; within the fifteen, five connects to fifty-five—this mutual concealment was elaborated in detail by Dong Panjian. One is the position of old yang, six the number of old yin, yet within one is contained nine, already concealing the number of old yang. Four is the position of old yin, nine the number of old yang, yet within four is contained six, already concealing the number of old yin. The young two is likewise. If yin and yang did not mutually root and conceal their abodes, the mechanism of creation would cease.

Moreover, seven and nine are yang. Yang governs advance: from young yang seven, advancing to the position above eight, it advances to the utmost and becomes old yang, nine, with nowhere further to go. When yang reaches its extreme, it generates yin, hence six and eight are yin. Yin governs retreat: from young yin eight, retreating to the position below seven, it retreats to the utmost and becomes old yin, six, with nowhere further to go. Thus, when yin reaches its extreme, yang is born again. The young become old, the old transform, and the young advance once more—this is the inexhaustible mechanism of creation. Yet when yang advances, it is abundant: old yang abounds at eight, young yang at six, and yang numbers are ever full.

Yin retreats and thus is deficient: old yin is deficient at seven, young yin at nine, and yin numbers are ever diminishing. The *Book of Changes* states: "The fullness and emptiness of Heaven and Earth wax and wane with the seasons." One who understands this understands the Dao.

It further states: "The *Luoshu*’s rows and columns sum to fifteen, alternating in waxing and waning." The *Book of Documents* takes one, two, three, and four, containing seven, eight, nine, and six—these are called the yin and yang of the Four Images. From them, the eight trigrams of old and young arise. Their alternation in waxing and waning never departs from seven, eight, nine, and six.

Master Shao cited the *Commentary on the Changes*, saying: "Zhen initiates growth, Qian divides, Xun diminishes, Kun contracts." This speaks to the general principle of waxing and waning. Thus, Zhen begins by uniting with yin to generate yang, reaching Qian’s nine where yang ages. Xun begins by diminishing yang to generate yin, reaching Kun’s six where yin ages.

Master Zhang said: "Seven grows six to nine, then divides eight to diminish nine, contracting at six." He also said: "Yang’s growth proceeds from seven through eight to nine, while old yin’s diminution proceeds from eight through seven to six." This broadly describes the waxing and waning of a single qi, designating Zhen and Xun as seven and eight. Yet if one seeks this in the *Luoshu*’s trigram positions, Zhen is instead eight, and Xun occupies nine. Their alternation in waxing and waning is such that one plus five becomes six, alternating with the nine of the south; four plus five becomes nine, alternating with the six of the northeast. When six advances to nine, nine waxes and six wanes; when nine retreats to six, nine wanes in turn and six waxes again. Three plus five becomes eight, alternating with the seven of the west; two plus five becomes seven, alternating with the eight of the northeast. When seven advances to eight, eight waxes and seven wanes; when eight retreats to seven, eight wanes in turn and seven waxes again. Generally, numbers advance to wax and retreat to wane. What waxes retreats and wanes, and what wanes advances and waxes. The numbers of the four cardinal and four diagonal directions, whether counted horizontally or vertically, all sum to fifteen. With the central five as void, the numbers sum to ten in all directions. Dividing into ten parts, Master Zhu said: "Nine is the remainder of one-tenth, eight of two-tenths, seven of three-tenths, six of four-tenths." Interweaving and interlacing, they meet without fail. Such is the union of seven, eight, nine, and six.

Someone asked Master Zhu: "The *Hetu* does not resemble the trigrams—why did Fuxi follow it to draw the trigrams?" In my humble opinion, the *Great Commentary* states: "The *Changes* possesses the Supreme Ultimate, which generates the Two Modes. The Two Modes generate the Four Images, and the Four Images generate the eight trigrams." This is how the Master elucidated Fuxi’s use of the *Hetu* to draw the eight trigrams. That the Supreme Ultimate is said to be "possessed by the *Changes*" refers to the five and ten concealed within the *Hetu*, the image of the central palace. That it "generates the Two Modes" refers to one, three, seven, and nine as the yang mode, and two, four, six, and eight as the yin mode. That it "generates the Four Images" refers to one, two, three, and four as the images of generating numbers, containing six, seven, eight, and nine as the images of completing numbers. That it "generates the eight trigrams" refers to seven, six, eight, and nine as the trigrams Qian, Kun, Li, and Kan, occupying the four cardinal positions, while one, two, three, and four as the trigrams Gen, Dui, Zhen, and Xun fill the four diagonal gaps. Thus, what Fuxi followed did not depart from a single diagram, yet the modes, images, and trigrams were all complete.

One is the Supreme Ultimate. Only because it exists does generation occur: one generates two, two generates four, four generates eight, and eight generates sixty-four. Generation is inexhaustible, all arising from this center.

I have also heard from my teacher: "These words are indeed the foundation of the *Changes*. But how do they function as numbers?" He replied: "Do not seek vague explanations—look to the beginning of Heaven and Earth, and the profound essence of the sages’ *Changes* is entirely here. One is the beginning of yang, so the sages took the single yang line to draw Qian as one, written as a single unbroken line. All trigrams belonging to yang are Qian. For example, Zhen is written as one yang over two yin, Kan as two yin over one yang, Gen as two yang over one yin—all take one as their essence. Two is the beginning of yin, so the sages took the double yin line to draw Kun as two, written as two broken lines. All trigrams belonging to yin are Kun. For example, Xun is written as two yang over one yin, Li as one yang over two yin, Dui as one yin over two yang—all take two as their essence. Thus the eight trigrams were drawn. When King Wen doubled them, he merely rearranged the lines above and below. Taking Qian’s one to exchange with Kun, Kun is transformed into Kan and placed in the north. Taking Kun’s two to exchange with Qian, Qian is transformed into Li and placed in the south. Zhen is placed in the east with one yang inside and two yin outside; Dui is placed in the west with two yang inside and one yin outside. The northwest is Qian, with three yang; the southwest is Kun, with three yin. The northeast is Gen, with two yin and one yang; the southeast is Xun, with one yin and two yang. The four cardinal and four diagonal directions are all arranged by the alternation of one and two. Fuxi drew the trigrams of the Earlier Heaven based on the *Hetu*, while King Wen followed Fuxi to expound the *Changes* of the Later Heaven. Yu bestowed the Nine Categories to first regulate the waters, and the earthly way was leveled. Jizi presented the *Great Plan*, beginning with one water and two fire, and the constant norms were established. All were derived from the numbers of Heaven as one and Earth as two."

Without the *Hetu*, where shall I place my foundation?

The *Commentary* states: "The *Hetu* and *Luoshu* serve as warp and weft to one another. The eight trigrams and nine categories serve as outer and inner to one another."

Master Zhu said: "Fifty-five is the essence, and the variations of forty-five may be deduced therefrom." Taken together, the *Hetu* embodies the image of the Nine Categories, while the *Luoshu* embodies the image of the Five Phases.

The *Hetu* is the constant number, the *Luoshu* the variable number. The generative numbers govern the completed numbers, serving as the master of the constant numbers. The odd numbers govern the even numbers, serving as the function of the variable numbers. The *Hetu* takes the generative numbers as its master, while the *Luoshu* takes the odd numbers as its master.

The outer aspect of the *Hetu* may be used to draw the trigrams, while its inner aspect may be used to arrange the Nine Categories. The outer aspect of the *Luoshu* may be used to arrange the Nine Categories, while its inner aspect may be used to draw the trigrams.

The *Hetu* unites generation and completion to harmonize yin and yang. Though united, they are never without distinction; thus, in its inner and outer patterns, there is a differentiation between host and guest. The *Luoshu* divides odd and even to distinguish yin and yang. Though divided, they are never without union; thus, within their mutual opposition, there is the subtlety of flowing transformation.

When it speaks of "warp and weft," it does not mean that above and below are the warp and left and right the weft. Rather, the warp refers to what is constant, while the weft refers to what is variable. When it speaks of "outer and inner," it does not mean that this is the inner and that the outer. Rather, it means that within the *Tu* there is the *Shu*, and within the *Shu* there is the *Tu*.

The *Tu* and *Shu* mutually serve as constant and variable, depending on what is taken as primary. If the *Tu* is taken as the basis, then the *Tu* is the constant and the *Shu* the variable. If the *Shu* is taken as the basis, then the *Shu* is the constant and the *Tu* the variable. The same applies to outer and inner.

Master Zhu said: "In the numbers and positions of the *Tu* and *Shu*, three are the same while two differ." In the *Hetu*, one and six (water) reside in the north; two and seven (fire) in the south; three and eight (wood) in the east; four and nine (metal) in the west; five and ten (earth) in the center. These five each occupy their proper directional positions. This is the essence of the numbers, yet there is also variation therein. The *Luoshu* serves to convey this variation.

In the *Luoshu*, the positions of earth, water, and wood remain the same and do not change, for heaven-five resides in the center, generating earth as the Great Ultimate. When it first moves and emerges in the north, it becomes heaven-one generating water. When it emerges again in the east, it becomes heaven-three generating wood. This is the yang qi, which lies dormant in winter and germinates in spring. Its essence cannot be altered; it extends its roots and sprouts, speaking in terms of the inner generative numbers.

As for two and seven, which originally occupy the southern position of fire, when the season enters summer, then Qian metal generates the yang of Si, residing in the height of summer to nurture the myriad things, expanding its generative power. This is like a king emerging to preside over the Bright Hall, wielding great governance. Thus, the lesser yang of Xun and Kan, generated from two and seven, cannot but yield to the dignity of the father Qian, retreating to the west.

When the season enters autumn, then Qian and Dui once again reside in the west, while Xun and Kan return to the south. Each follows its essence, maintaining its constancy. In general, the principle of supporting yang and restraining yin is the fundamental intent of heaven and earth to foster life.

The *Hetu* takes ten as its master, with yang in surplus and odd numbers lacking. Heaven and earth oppose each other to form its essence, and once the essence is established, its function is enacted. Thus, the *Luoshu* takes nine as its master, with odd numbers in surplus and even numbers lacking. Heaven envelops earth to enact its function. Therefore, it restrains the yin numbers, reducing them to twenty, and supports the yang completed numbers externally, so that seven and nine vary while six and eight remain unchanged.

Heaven and earth embed these numbers within the *Hetu* and *Luoshu*, and the sages follow them to unfold their subtleties. The discussions of earlier scholars have been thorough; I shall not repeat them here. I merely set forth my own insights.

(See the extended explanation on "one water, three wood, five earth.")

Master Jiezhai Cai said: "*Yi* governs images, while *Fan* governs numbers. The *Hetu* has even numbers; evenness implies mutual opposition. Thus, *Yi* is based on the two qi. The *Luoshu* has odd numbers; oddness implies flowing transformation. Thus, *Fan* is based on the Five Phases.

Evenness is quiescent, and quiescence employs movement as its function; thus, the lines of the *Hetu* unite in odd numbers: one unites with six, two with seven, three with eight, four with nine, five with ten. Oddness is dynamic, and dynamism employs quiescence as its function; thus, the positions of the *Luoshu* unite in even numbers: one unites with nine, two with eight, three with seven, four with six.

Therefore, in the *Yi*, good and ill fortune arise from movement, and the *Hetu* is the movement that originates from within oneself. Thus, one may perceive the subtle and anticipate good fortune; hence, it is called 'Earlier Heaven.' In the *Fan*, good and ill fortune are seen in quiescence. Quiescence is only completed through movement, so the *Luoshu* is the movement that follows heaven. One can only clarify good and ill fortune; hence, it is called 'Later Heaven.'

Qi has two aspects, while the phases have five. One, three, five, seven, and nine are the movements of yang. Thus, when the Master summed the five numbers of heaven, he obtained twenty-five. Two, four, six, eight, and ten are the movements of yin. Thus, when he summed the five numbers of earth, he obtained thirty.

Two cannot transform without five, and the five talents cannot act on their own without two. This speaks of how yin and yang accomplish transformation. That which enacts the spirits and gods lies in the five. The five are the Five Phases.

The fixed essence of heaven and earth’s yin-yang opposition, from one to ten, is the sequence of yin-yang’s flowing transformation. Divided into odd and even, one, three, five, seven, and nine are yang, while two, four, six, eight, and ten are yin. Arranged in order of priority, one, two, three, four, and five—the generative numbers—are yang, while six, seven, eight, nine, and ten—the completed numbers—are yin."

Numbers flow within yin and yang before form is settled; images manifest within yin and yang once form is complete. The *Yijing* speaks of the Supreme Ultimate, which embodies the principles of yin and yang yet remains formless. Image corresponds to yin’s stillness, while number corresponds to yang’s movement. The Supreme Ultimate is neither still nor moving, yet it governs stillness. Before the Supreme Ultimate, images and numbers cannot be distinguished by priority. Only when movement arises and yang numbers begin does the image also commence. When stillness arises and the yin image takes shape, the numbers are then completed. Yin and yang diverge and unite; movement and stillness differ. Speaking in terms of establishment: stillness is the time of dual opposition, while movement is the time of singular flow. Speaking in terms of progression: stillness is when yin and yang unite in virtue, while movement is when the two—yang leading, yin following—advance. This passage is subtly profound; readers ought to reflect deeply upon it.

Fu Xi based his work on the *Hetu*, first drawing the Primordial Horizontal Diagram. In the horizontal diagram, the sequence from one to eight proceeds in order, while the arrangement of trigrams runs from right to left. The numbers and the circular diagram differ—one moving in reverse, the other in sequence. Greater yang, residing in the first position, divides into Qian and Dui; lesser yin, in the second position, divides into Li and Zhen; lesser yang, in the third position, divides into Xun and Kan; greater yin, in the fourth position, divides into Gen and Kun. This proceeds from top to bottom. Then, from bottom to top and left to right: Gen and Kun emerge from greater yin’s one and six; Xun and Kan emerge from lesser yang’s two and seven; Li and Zhen emerge from lesser yin’s three and eight; Qian and Dui emerge from greater yang’s four and nine. Above the greater yang’s one, generating one odd and one even yields Qian and Dui. Above the lesser yin’s two, generating one odd and one even yields Li and Zhen. Above the lesser yang’s three, generating one odd and one even yields Xun and Kan. Above the greater yin’s four, generating one odd and one even yields Gen and Kun. This is the Primordial Horizontal Diagram. From one to eight, it serves merely as the sequence for generating trigrams.

Fu Xi also based his work on the *Hetu* to create the Primordial Circular Diagram. Master Zhu said: In the sixty-four-trigram horizontal diagram, Zhen, Xun, Fu, and Gou occupy the center. Folding the center to form a circular diagram, one begins from Zhen and Fu, moving backward to Qian, then proceeds from Xun and Gou forward to Kun, thus completing the circle. The sequence of spring, summer, autumn, and winter, as well as the phases of the moon—new moon, full moon, first quarter, and last quarter—and the alternation of day and night, dawn and dusk, all follow in order. This is the essential principle of diagram construction. He further stated: The horizontal diagram follows a forward sequence, while the square and circular diagrams consistently reverse direction, though the circular diagram reverses only halfway. For details, see the left and right chapters.

The Primordial Eight Trigrams align with the numbers of the *Luoshu*. The Primordial Eight Trigrams correspond to the *Luoshu*: greater yang’s nine is Qian in the due south, four is Dui in the southeast; lesser yin’s three is Li in the due east, eight is Zhen in the northeast; lesser yang’s two is Xun in the southwest, seven is Kan in the due west; greater yin’s one is Kun in the due north, six is Gen in the northwest. In the circular arrangement, Qian, Dui, Li, and Zhen—four yang trigrams—occupy the left, while Xun, Kan, Gen, and Kun—four yin trigrams—occupy the right. The *Book of Documents* also places Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li at the four cardinal directions, with Zhen, Dui, Xun, and Gen at the four corners. The diagram and the *Book* align in their trigram positions. The difference lies in the interchange of greater yang and lesser yang in the southwest, creating variation.

The Later Heaven Eight Trigrams align with the numbers of the *Hetu*. The Later Heaven Eight Trigrams also correspond to the *Hetu*’s numbers: one and six, water and Kan, reside in the north; two and seven, fire and Li, reside in the south; three and eight, wood and Zhen, occupy the due east, while Xun occupies the southeast; four and nine, metal and Dui, occupy the due west, while Qian occupies the northwest. Five and ten—Gen at five in the northeast and Kun at ten in the southwest—permeate the central palace as earth. The Later Heaven numbers invariably align with the *Hetu*. Some, observing that two and seven reside in the south as Li, and four and nine in the northwest as Qian and Dui, mistakenly claim ten belongs to the *Luoshu*. Others, seeing nine placed in the south as Qian and two and seven in the southwest as Xun and Kan, erroneously assert nine belongs to the *Hetu*. They claim King Wen modeled this to create the Later Heaven *Yijing*, thus conflating the two diagrams and propagating Liu Mu’s theories. Scholars must be discerning.

In the *Hetu* and *Luoshu*, yin and yang, odd and even, interweave. The *Hetu* combines one, two, three, and four with seven, eight, nine, and six to form ten. The *Luoshu* opposes one, two, three, and four to seven, eight, nine, and six to form ten. Ten is the product of two and five. The numbers of the two are each multiplied by five to generate and complete five. Greater yang’s position is one in the north, yet it contains nine, and its completed number lies in the west. Lesser yin’s position is two in the south, yet it contains eight, and its completed number lies in the east. Lesser yang’s position is three in the east, yet it contains seven, and its completed number lies in the south. Greater yin’s position is four in the west, yet it contains six, and its completed number lies in the north. Thus, they mutually generate and complete one another. In the *Luoshu*, however, they simply oppose each other: one opposes nine in the north and south, three opposes seven in the east and west. The four cardinal directions are thus positioned, while the four corners each form their own oppositions: the northeast corner opposes the southwest corner with two and eight, and the southeast corner opposes the northwest corner with four and six. They also interweave like warp and weft. Both the *Luoshu* and *Hetu* each conceal ten within, doubling the Five Phases to achieve completion. Master Dong’s explanation, focusing solely on the positions of the Four Images and their numbers, is also apt.

Zhu Xi said: *The Book of Changes* was first derived by Fuxi from the *Hetu*, with no reliance on the *Luoshu*. The *Hongfan* chapter, however, was uniquely received by Yu from the *Luoshu*, without necessarily tracing back to the *Hetu*. Zheng Xuan said: The sages drew the Eight Trigrams based on the *Hetu* and arranged the Nine Categories based on the *Luoshu*. How could this be likened to drafting a circle with a compass or squaring with a carpenter’s square? There is principle in one, and there is fact in the other. When Fuxi drew the trigrams according to the *Hetu*, he did not foresee the *Luoshu*, yet their numbers coincidentally aligned. When Yu arranged the Nine Categories according to the *Luoshu*, he did not retroactively seek the numbers of the *Hetu* to match them. In my humble opinion, the positions of the *Hetu* correspond to the Primordial Heaven, while those of the *Luoshu* correspond to the Manifested Heaven. Though their positions differ in sequence, and their numbers vary between ten and nine, both are fundamentally rooted in the Five Phases, with five residing at the center—this alone is their unity.

The transmission of Master Chen Xiyi of Pu Shang: Master Xiyi, whose given name was Chuan, taught numerology to Mu Xiubozhang. Xiubozhang taught Li Zhicai, and Zhicai taught Shao Kangjie, who then composed the *Supreme Principles Governing the World*. Xiubozhang also transmitted the *Taiji Diagram* to Zhou Dunyi. Zhu Hanshang said: Dunyi taught the Cheng brothers. At that time, Zhang Zai lectured among the Cheng brothers and Shao Yong. Thus, Dunyi wrote the *Comprehensive Treatise*, Cheng Yi composed the *Commentary on the Book of Changes*, and Zhang Zai authored the *Great Harmony* in three sections. He also taught Xiang Zong on symbolic studies, and Zong taught Xu Jian of Lujiang—this branch was transmitted in the south.

Some say the two diagrams of the Primordial Heaven (square and circular) originated from Ma Shi’s enlightenment. Zhu Xi clarified this was not so, stating that the diagrams were all created by Fuxi, containing only trigrams without words or text. After the passing of Confucius and Mencius, they were concealed by esoteric practitioners, only to be transmitted by Xiyi.

Cai Xishan said: The images of the *Hetu* and *Luoshu* were described as such by Han scholars Kong Anguo and Liu Xiang, Wei scholar Guan Lang Ziming, and Song masters Shao Kangjie and Shao Yaofu. It was Liu Mu who first reversed their names, and later scholars followed suit. Thus, they are now restored to their original designations. Ancient records, from Kong Anguo, Liu Xiang and his son Liu Xin, to Ban Gu, all state that the *Hetu* was bestowed upon Fuxi and the *Luoshu* upon Yu. Guan Ziming and Shao Kangjie both identified ten as the *Hetu* and nine as the *Luoshu*. The numbers of the Nine Palaces—nine atop, one below, three on the left, seven on the right, two and four as the shoulders, six and eight as the feet—precisely mirror the pattern of a turtle’s shell.

Zhu Xi said: Reading the *Book of Rites* by Dai the Elder yields one clear piece of evidence. Its *Mingtang* chapter contains the phrase “two, nine, four, seven, five, three, six, eight, one.” Zheng’s commentary states: “This follows the pattern of a turtle’s markings.” Thus, the Han scholars indeed took nine as the *Luoshu*. Why then did Liu Mu, with his peculiar views, reverse this, taking nine as the *Hetu* and ten as the *Luoshu*, completely overturning the teachings of earlier scholars? He falsely claimed his diagrams originated from Xiyi, lacking any written record, and that Xu Jian transmitted them to Kuang Chang, who then passed them to Liu Mu. Many adopted his theory, spreading it widely. Later, Zhu Hanshang composed his *Commentary on the Book of Changes*, and Zhang Xingcheng of Shu wrote the *Seven Eases*. Others, like Zheng of Puyang, all followed Liu’s interpretation, none of which had been corrected before. Only with the emergence of Master Zhu and Cai Yijun of Xishan were these errors rectified, restoring ten as the *Hetu* and nine as the *Luoshu*. The world came to trust this. Yet Liu Mu’s theory should not be entirely dismissed.
