Here, I cite a few points: He quoted the *Great Commentary*, asserting that both originated in Fuxi’s time, not that they were bestowed upon him. Had later generations not seen the text of the thirteen hexagrams in the *Appended Judgments*, they might have attributed the sixty-four hexagrams solely to King Wen. Had they not seen the text of the *Hongfan*’s heavenly bestowal, they might have assumed the Nine Categories were arranged by Jizi. How would they know that the selection of *Yu* and *Guai* was already present before King Wen composed the hexagrams, or that the offices of Minister of Education and Minister of Works were established on the day Yu received the Nine Palaces? Thus, the *Hetu* and *Luoshu* both existed in high antiquity; Fuxi merely drew them, and Yu modeled and arranged them accordingly. In my view, this argument is not without merit. However, to claim the *Luoshu* did not emerge during Yu’s flood control contradicts the statement that “Heaven bestowed upon Yu the *Hongfan* Nine Categories.”

Recently, in the Xinwei imperial examination, Liu Mengjian employed this theory. Though he ranked first in the southern palace, many who followed Master Zhu’s interpretation of the *Hetu* and *Luoshu* were placed behind him. He further argued that the *Yu Gong* speaks of the *Luo* as a path but does not mention obtaining the *Luoshu*, and the *Canon of Shun* refers to “favoring categories” without specifying the Nine Categories. The phrase “Heaven bestowed upon Yu” merely means Heaven inspired him. Liu Mu also said: The fifty-five numbers of Heaven and Earth are forty-five in the *Hetu*, leaving ten unused. This is because the four corners and four cardinal directions each combine to form ten. Adding the central five, both vertically and horizontally, sums to fifteen, totaling fifty-five. The five is unused because: Heaven’s one plus five makes six, Earth’s two plus five makes seven, Heaven’s three plus five makes eight, Earth’s four plus five makes nine, and Heaven’s five plus five makes ten. Thus it is said: “Earth completes the ten.”

Moreover, the numbers of the Eight Trigrams total thirty-six. The nine unused numbers arise because *Qian* and *Kun* each bear the number nine, *Zhen* and *Xun* each bear the number nine, and *Kan*, *Li*, *Gen*, and *Dui* all bear the number nine. Why then doubt that the nine numbers cannot form the *Hetu*, or that the ten numbers cannot form the *Luoshu*? The four cardinal directions are all odd and yang, while the four corners are all even and yin. As the saying goes: “Solitary yin does not generate, solitary yang does not complete,” and only when one odd and one even are paired does creation arise.

It is further said: The number one, as a numerical value, is one in Heaven and *jia* in the celestial stems. As an image, it is the central number of six; as a trigram, it is the central line of *Kan*. In doubled trigrams, it is the *initial nine*; in *Fu*, it is the yang line; in the earthly branches, it is *jianzi*; among the Five Phases, it is Water; in the twelve pitch-pipes, it is *Huangzhong*. One is the fixed position. Subtract one from fifty, and one resides within forty-nine. Subtract one again from forty-nine, and one resides within forty-eight. Wherever there are numbers, one is never absent. Wherever one is present, it is invariably the ancestor of the myriad things. To grasp this without fail is to hold the mechanism of Heaven and Earth.

This reasoning is rather sound. I have recorded these discussions at the conclusion of the section on images and numbers to show that such theories flourished alongside the *Hetu* and *Luoshu* in their time, differing only in their inversion and misplacement. Scholars ought not remain ignorant of this.

*Tianyuan Fawei*, Scroll Thirteen, concludes here.

*Tianyuan Fawei*, Scroll Fourteen
By Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai
Collated and Corrected by Fang Wanli of Xugu

The discourse on "prior and posterior" (先天后天) was refined through the contributions of the Four Sages and Four Worthies before its theory reached completion. The Four Sages are: Fu Xi, who drew the trigrams in the *prior Heaven* arrangement; King Wen, who expanded the *Yijing*, and the Duke of Zhou, who composed the line statements—both in the *posterior Heaven* arrangement; and Confucius, whose Ten Wings encompass both *prior* and *posterior Heaven*. The Four Worthies are: Han Yu of the Han, Cheng Shuzhi, Shaozi (Shao Yong), who alone expounded the learning of *prior Heaven*, and Master Zhu (Zhu Xi), who synthesized them into one. Thus, the Dao of the *Yijing* was greatly illuminated for the world. Without the successive efforts of the Four Sages and Four Worthies, the world would still grope in darkness.

Master Zhu said: Kangjie (Shao Yong) had a poem:
*"If one speaks of the prior Heaven, there is nothing at all;
The posterior Heaven is where effort must be applied."*
He also said:
*"The Will of Heaven is nothing but spontaneity;
Beyond spontaneity, there is no Heaven."*
This conveys the same meaning.

Master Pan said: *"The prior Heaven is nothing at all"*—I arrived at this understanding through the investigation of things. In my youth, I observed chicks hatching from their eggs. At first, they did not peck. Only when their *qi* and allotted time were sufficient did they burst forth, breaking the shell. If one were to assist them even slightly, the chick would emerge weak and fail to thrive. From this, I saw that there is not the slightest room for human interference.

I say: One, two, three, four, five are the generating numbers of Heaven and Earth—this is *prior Heaven*. Six, seven, eight, nine, ten are the completing numbers of Heaven and Earth—this is *posterior Heaven*. Some ask: Is *wuji* becoming *taiji* the *prior Heaven*? Is *taiji* becoming yin and yang the *posterior Heaven*? By analogy, the unmanifest center is *prior Heaven*, while its manifestation in harmony is *posterior Heaven*. The opening of things is *prior Heaven*; their completion is *posterior Heaven*. Uprightness and origin are *prior Heaven*; smoothness and benefit are *posterior Heaven*. Yet even within uprightness, there is a *prior* and *posterior*: the first half of *zi* (子) pertains to yin and is *prior Heaven*, while the latter half pertains to yang and is *posterior Heaven*. *Prior* and *posterior Heaven* apply to all affairs and all things. In silent observation, one grasps more than half the truth—how could this be limited to the arrangement of trigrams alone?

Master Cai said: Cold and heat, day and night, the yin and yang of living things—all undergo contraction and expansion, growth and decline. This is *prior Heaven*. That by which yang is yang is its ceaseless movement without fixed form. The *qi* and form, the hun and po of living things—here, yang can transform while yin cannot. This is *posterior Heaven*. That by which yin is yin is its stillness with fixed form. Fu Xi’s trigrams are *prior Heaven*—the *qi* of Heaven. King Wen’s trigrams are *posterior Heaven*—the things of Earth. The *Spring and Autumn Annals* states: *"To precede Heaven and not violate it is the movement of unified will and *qi*. To follow Heaven and accord with its seasons is the movement of unified *qi* and will."* Master Hengqu (Zhang Zai) said: *Qi* and will, Heaven and humanity—there is mutual overcoming in their alternation. In the *Yijing*, every word and meaning flows from this.

Someone asked: How do the *Taiji* and *Prior Heaven* diagrams compare? Master Zhu said: In terms of their structure, the *Taiji* diagram is not as vast as the *Prior Heaven* diagram. Yet in detailed discussion of their principles, the *Prior Heaven* diagram is not as refined and concise as the *Taiji* diagram. Nevertheless, the *Taiji* ultimately lies within the scope of the *Prior Heaven*. What Shaozi called the learning of *prior Heaven* begins from the moment before a single line is drawn and extends to the completion of the six lines. For example, in Confucius’ Ten Wings, passages such as *"The eight trigrams are arrayed"* and *"The Supreme Polarity generates the Two Modes, the Four Images, the Eight Trigrams"*—Heaven and Earth, thunder and wind, mountain and marsh, water and fire—all derive from Fu Xi’s original trigrams. Before the *prior Heaven* trigrams were drawn, no principle was yet divided. First, one yang and one yin line generate two single lines. Above the Two Modes, each generates one yang and one yin, producing four two-line images. Above the Four Images, each generates one yang and one yin, producing eight three-line trigrams. All arise naturally, without contrivance, and become clear without the need for words or discourse. This is the guiding principle of *Yijing* studies, the first essential truth at the opening of the text. From ancient times until now, none have recognized it—until Kangjie (Shao Yong) transmitted its teaching. This is the *Yijing* of *prior Heaven*.

It is also said: Fu Xi’s four diagrams, as expounded by the Shao lineage, were created when he first drew the trigrams. First, he made the horizontal diagram of the eight trigrams, then the horizontal diagram of the sixty-four hexagrams. Next, he made the circular diagram of the eight trigrams’ positions, followed by the circular diagram of the sixty-four hexagrams’ positions.

It is further said: In the *Prior Heaven Diagram*, the outer circle represents Heaven, and the inner square represents Earth. Master Cai Xishan said: In the circular arrangement of the sixty-four hexagrams, *Qian* culminates at noon, *Kun* at midnight, *Li* at dawn, and *Kan* at dusk. Yang is born at midnight and reaches its peak at noon; yin is born at noon and reaches its peak at midnight. Yang resides in the south, yin in the north. In the square arrangement, *Qian* begins in the northwest, and *Kun* ends in the southeast. Here, yang is in the north, yin in the south. These two arrangements represent the numerical correspondence of yin and yang. The outer circle is yang, the inner square is yin. The circle moves and is Heaven; the square is still and is Earth. In the circular diagram, *Qian* is in the south and *Kun* in the north. In the square diagram, *Kun* is in the south and *Qian* in the north. The position of *Qian* has more yang lines; the position of *Kun* has more yin lines. Yin and yang each gather according to their kind. The diagram encloses the square within the circle, illustrating that Heaven envelops Earth externally while Earth resides within Heaven. Master Zhu said: The square and circle are especially wondrous.

Shaozi had a poem:
*"Heaven and Earth take their positions, *Pi* and *Tai* reverse their categories.
Mountain and marsh exchange *qi*, *Xian* and *Sun* reveal their meaning.
Thunder and wind press upon each other, *Heng* and *Yi* arise in intent.
Water and fire shoot forth, *Jiji* and *Weiji* are achieved.
The Four Images intersect, completing sixteen affairs."*

The eight trigrams interact to form sixty-four. Master Zhu explained: "This elucidates the two intersecting legs of the square and circle diagrams. In the northwest corner stands *Qian*; in the northeast corner, *Kun*—this is Heaven and Earth taking their positions. Opposite them lie *Tai* in the northeast and *Pi* in the southwest. Next to *Qian* is *Dui*; next to *Kun* is *Gen*—this is mountain and marsh exchanging *qi*. Opposite them are *Xian* from *Pi* and *Sun* from *Tai*. The remaining four trigrams follow likewise. Next to *Dui* is *Li*; next to *Gen* is *Kan*—this is water and fire shooting forth. Opposite them are *Jiji* from *Sun* and *Weiji* from *Xian*. Next to *Li* is *Zhen*; next to *Kan* is *Xun*, positioned at the central intersection of the two legs—this is thunder and wind pressing upon each other. Opposite them are *Yi* from *Jiji* and *Heng* from *Weiji*. The Four Images intersect to complete sixteen affairs, precisely because *Qian* and *Kun* transform into *Pi* and *Tai*, *Gen* and *Dui* into *Sun* and *Xian*, *Zhen* and *Xun* into *Sun* and *Yi*, and *Kan* and *Li* into *Jiji* and *Weiji*. Each set of four trigrams stands in mutual opposition, exhausting the square of Earth."

He further stated: "In the *Prior Heaven* circular diagram, four sets of sixteen hexagrams reside externally. In the square diagram, four sets of sixteen hexagrams reside internally. The first sixteen are born of *Qian* and *Kun*; the next sixteen, of *Gen* and *Dui*; the following sixteen, of *Kan* and *Li*; and the last sixteen, of *Zhen* and *Xun*. These four sets of sixteen pairs together complete the square and circular diagrams. The circular diagram represents the yin and yang of the Dao of Heaven. In Heaven, they manifest as the sun, moon, stars, heat, cold, day, and night—termed the *Yi* of ceaseless flow, for it moves with Heaven and Earth through the four seasons without pause. The thirty-two yang hexagrams on the left govern spring’s generation and summer’s growth; the thirty-two yin hexagrams on the right govern autumn’s gathering and winter’s storage. Together, they quadruple the sixteen to form sixty-four hexagrams. Divided by spring, summer, autumn, and winter, each season further quadruples the sixty-four to yield 1,536 hexagram lines of *qi*, circulating through Heaven. The Four Images establish form, the six *jia* cycles revolve, revealing the governance of emperors, kings, and lords near and far. The 366-day rotation is presided over by *Qian* and *Kun*, belonging to Heaven’s creative transformations."

"The square diagram represents the yielding and firmness of the Dao of Earth. On Earth, they manifest as water, fire, soil, stone, rain, wind, dew, and thunder—termed the *Yi* of mutual opposition, for it follows Heaven’s seasons to generate and transform. The inner section of thirty-two yang hexagrams spans from *Qian* in the northwest to *Tai* in the northeast; the outer section of thirty-two yin hexagrams spans from *Pi* in the southwest to *Kun* in the southeast. These also quadruple the sixteen to form sixty-four hexagrams. Divided by *yuan*, *hui*, *yun*, and *shi*, each further quadruples the sixty-four to yield 256 positions of hexagram bodies, giving rise to beings on Earth. The Four Images establish form, the four nines serve as function, revealing the yang chant and yin response of pitch pipes and sounds, the birth and death of creatures moving and still. *Kan* and *Li* preside, belonging to Earth’s creative transformations."

Thus, Quezi said: "Sixteen is the number derived from the Four Images’ mutual dependence. All transformations of Heaven and Earth, all resonances of the myriad things, all continuities and changes, gains and losses of past and present—none lie beyond sixteen. With sixteen, the Dao of Heaven and Earth is complete. Herein lies the opening and closing, waxing and waning, fortune and misfortune, good and evil, each paired in perfect opposition without discrepancy."

In the *Posterior Heaven* *Upper Canon*, eighteen hexagrams reverse their opposites, twelve of which are mutable, while six remain immutable: *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, *Li*, *Yi*, and *Daguo*. In the *Lower Canon*, eighteen hexagrams reverse their opposites, sixteen of which are mutable, while two remain immutable: *Zhongfu* and *Xiaoguo*. The *Yi Wei* states: "The sixty-four hexagrams of the *Yi* are divided by King Wen into the *Upper* and *Lower Canons*." Examining the *Sequence of Hexagrams*, all follow the *Posterior Heaven* order, with reversed opposing hexagrams. Of the eight trigrams, four remain immutable: *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li*. Two reverse: *Zhen* and *Dui*. *Zhen* reverses to *Gen*, *Dui* reverses to *Xun*. Thus, six trigrams transform into eight, and through doubling, the additional hexagrams *Yi*, *Zhongfu*, *Daguo*, and *Xiaoguo* emerge. Four hexagrams remain unchanged: *Tai* above, and *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li* as the eight trigrams. Twenty-eight hexagrams undergo reversal and mutation. Eight paired with eight yield thirty-six transformations, completing the sixty-four hexagrams.

*Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li* remain fundamentally immutable, while *Gen* and *Zhen* combine to form *Yi*; *Zhen* and *Gen* combine to form *Xiaoguo*; *Xun* and *Dui* combine to form *Zhongfu*; *Dui* and *Xun* combine to form *Daguo*—all likewise immutable. Though the eight trigrams remain immutable, when *Qian* fully transforms, it becomes *Kun*; when *Kun* fully transforms, it becomes *Qian*. When *Kan* fully transforms, it becomes *Li*; when *Li* fully transforms, it becomes *Kan*. The full transformations of *Yi* and *Daguo*, *Zhongfu* and *Xiaoguo* follow the same pattern."

Master Li said: "The *Upper* and *Lower Canons* each pair opposites. The *Sequence of Hexagrams* shifts its literary meaning at *Kan* and *Li*, establishing the *Canons* with *Qian* and *Kun* as the two elders standing in opposition, each concealing their names in the sequence. The *Lower Canon* begins with *Xian*, the two youths united in form, yet the sequence uniquely conceals the name *Xian*. *Xu* and *Song* in the *Upper Canon* oppose *Jin* and *Mingyi* in the *Lower Canon*. When *Xu* and *Song* fully transform, they become *Jin*; when *Jin* fully transforms, it becomes *Xu*. *Tai* and *Pi* in the *Upper Canon* oppose *Sun* and *Yi* in the *Lower Canon*. *Tai* and *Pi* arise from the exchange of *Qian* and *Kun*; *Sun* and *Yi* do not exchange. *Xian* and *Heng* exchange, yet do not exchange. In the *Upper Canon*, from *Zhun* and *Meng* to *Lin* and *Guan*; in the *Lower Canon*, from *Dun* and *Zhuang* to *Ge* and *Ding*. When *Zhun* and *Meng* fully transform, they become *Ding* and *Ge*; when *Lin* and *Guan* fully transform, they become *Dun* and *Zhuang*. *Yi* and *Daguo* in the *Upper Canon* appear paired before *Kan* and *Li*; *Zhongfu* and *Xiaoguo* in the *Lower Canon* appear paired before *Jiji* and *Weiji*. The *Upper Canon* concludes with *Kan* and *Li*; the *Lower Canon* concludes with *Jiji* and *Weiji*."

*Jiji* and *Weiji* are the intermingling of *Kan* and *Li*. Yet they do not intermingle. *Yi* resembles *Li*, and *Daguo* resembles *Kan*, yet *Kan* and *Li* follow these two hexagrams. *Zhongfu* has thick lines, and *Xiaoguo* has thick lines like *Li*. *Kan* and *Jiji/Weiji* again follow these two hexagrams, for *Kan* and *Li* serve as the function of *Qian*, *Kun*, *Zhen*, *Xun*, *Gen*, and *Dui*.

The *Upper Canon* contains fifty-two yang lines and fifty-six yin lines. The *Lower Canon* contains fifty-six yang lines and fifty-two yin lines. The division of the *Canon* into upper and lower sections follows a profound principle. It is regrettable that the master’s teachings were not transmitted, and the meaning behind King Wen’s division of the *Canon* has become obscure.

Master Shao said: *Qian* and *Kun* run vertically while the Six Children run horizontally—this is the root of the *Yi*. He also said: *Zhen* and *Dui* run horizontally while the six hexagrams run vertically—this is the function of the *Yi*.

In the *Former Heaven* arrangement of the eight hexagrams, *Qian* and *Kun* are the root of the *Yi*, with the Six Children assisting them. Thus, the Six Children are all horizontal, while *Qian* and *Kun* alone are vertical. The vertical is like the warp in weaving, and the horizontal like the weft. Dividing yin and yang, they interlace to form the weft. *Qian* and *Kun* occupy the south and north to establish the positions of heaven above and earth below. *Qian* has twenty-six yang lines and twelve yin lines; *Kun* has thirty-six yin lines and twelve yang lines. Thus, they plant the warp of heaven and earth, dividing south and north. Then *Li* in the east and *Kan* in the west serve as the sun and moon to form the weft. *Gen* in the northwest opposes *Dui* in the southeast, and *Zhen* in the northeast opposes *Xun* in the southwest—these further serve as the transformations of mountains and marshes, thunder and wind to form the weft.

Moreover, *Xun*, *Li*, *Dui* are yin hexagrams, yet their root in *Qian*’s body paradoxically yields more yang. Thus, their abundance of yang is taken as *Qian*’s weft on the left. *Zhen*, *Gen*, *Kan* are yang hexagrams, yet their root in *Kun*’s body paradoxically yields more yin. Thus, their abundance of yin is taken as *Kun*’s weft on the right. Above and below correspond, one vertical and one horizontal. Yang is odd, yin is even—they stand in mutual opposition. This establishes the structure of the *Yi*, while the *Later Heaven* arrangement serves its function.

In the *Later Heaven* arrangement of the eight hexagrams, *Zhen* and *Dui* serve as the function of the *Yi*, with the six hexagrams assisting them. Thus, the six hexagrams are all vertical, while *Zhen* and *Dui* alone are horizontal. The horizontal is like the beam of a scale, and the six hexagrams are the stars on the beam. Some are light, some are heavy—they advance and retreat to weigh them. *Zhen* and *Dui* occupy east and west to hold the power of spring’s generation and autumn’s harvest. *Zhen*, with its single yang, generates the six yang of *Xun*, *Li*, and *Dui*. Accumulating, they become *Qian*, which resides in the northwest to complete the honor of the father’s way. *Zhen* also contains two yin, which enfold the three yin of *Xun*, *Li*, and *Dui*. Combining the six yin, they become *Kun*, which resides in the southeast to complete the goodness of the mother’s way. The east generates, the west completes—one horizontal, six vertical. The horizontal is weighted, with *Zhen* as wood and *Dui* as metal, each in its proper place. *Kan* as water and *Li* as fire intermingle to fulfill their function. This is how the *Later Heaven* eight hexagrams serve as the *Yi* of the earth’s surface, responding to human use.

Someone asked: In the *Later Heaven* eight hexagrams, *Zhen*, *Dui*, *Kan*, and *Li* occupy the cardinal positions of east, west, south, and north, and their oppositions are taken for their intermingling. *Qian*, *Kun*, *Gen*, and *Xun* occupy the intermediate positions of east, west, south, and north, and their lack of opposition means they do not intermingle. If intermingling serves a function, then non-intermingling serves no function. Yet when I examine this through the numbers of yin and yang, all seem to serve a function—why is this?

The answer: With the four cardinal hexagrams serving their function, the four corner hexagrams do not serve theirs—yet all hexagrams enter into what serves a function.

Master Zhang said: The *Former Heaven* eight hexagrams correspond to heaven’s four seasons; the *Later Heaven* eight hexagrams correspond to earth’s eight directions. Where do they not serve a function?

Master Shao said: The *Former Heaven* *Yi* is its structure; the *Later Heaven* *Yi* is its function. The *Former Heaven* employs *Kan* and *Li* as the sun and moon for the transformations of firmness and softness, day and night. The *Later Heaven* employs *Qian* and *Kun*’s nine and six for the transformations of yin and yang, cold and heat. The *Former Heaven* *Yi*’s structure responds to heaven’s qi; the *Later Heaven* *Yi*’s function responds to earth’s directions. The *Former Heaven* hexagram positions are eightfold and cardinal: *Qian* in the south, *Kun* in the north, *Li* in the east, *Kan* in the west, along with *Yi*, *Zhongfu*, *Daguo*, and *Xiaoguo*. The *Later Heaven* hexagram qi are fourfold and cardinal: *Kan*, *Li*, *Zhen*, *Dui*; the four corners are *Qian*, *Kun*, *Gen*, *Xun*.

In the *Former Heaven*, *Qian* and *Kun* divide heaven and earth to establish the positions of above and below. In the *Later Heaven*, *Qian* and *Kun* become the great father and mother, retiring to the northwest and southwest corners. In the *Former Heaven*, *Kan* and *Li* are the sun and moon, arrayed at the gates of *mao* and *you* on the left and right. In the *Later Heaven*, *Kan* and *Li* are water and fire, dividing south and north in the sequence of generation and emergence. The *Former Heaven* eight hexagrams mark the beginning of creation, encompassing heaven above and earth below—*Qian* and *Kun*’s opening and closing, the sun and moon’s rising and setting, spring, summer, autumn, and winter, waxing and waning, dark and new moons, the lengthening and shortening of day and night, the degrees of celestial motion, and the waxing and waning of the seasons—all arise from this. The *Later Heaven* eight hexagrams mark the time after the generation of beings, concerning only the earth’s surface, and thus speak of *Kan* and *Li*. They stand at the solstices, while *Zhen* and *Dui* occupy the fixed positions of the second and eighth months—unlike the waxing and waning of day and night. Thus, the *Appended Judgments* speak of spring, summer, autumn, winter, south, north, east, and west, yet *Kun* speaks only of earth, and *Kan* only of water, never touching upon what lies beneath the earth.

In the *Former Heaven*, yin generates yang; in the *Later Heaven*, yang generates yin. In the *Former Heaven*, the four images generate the sun and moon, so yang embraces yin to generate the sun, and yin embraces yang to generate the moon. In the *Later Heaven*, the sun and moon generate the ten thousand things, so when yang moves through yang, the ten thousand things flourish; when yang moves through yin, the ten thousand things contract. In the *Former Heaven*, the sun, moon, and five stars all move left with heaven. In the *Later Heaven*, the sun, moon, and five stars all move right against heaven. The *Former Heaven* employs the four images: water, fire, earth, and stone. The *Later Heaven* employs the five phases: wood, fire, earth, metal, and water. In the *Former Heaven*, the thirty-two yang on the left are divided from the hexagram *Guimei*, with *Dui* above and *Zhen* below.

In the *Later Heaven*, *Zhen* (☳) is east and *Dui* (☱) is west, with *Qian* (☰) extending to *Guimei* (䷵) in twelve steps, counting twenty-one hexagrams from below to above. In the *Former Heaven*, the thirty-two yin on the right are divided from the hexagram *Jian* (䷴), with *Xun* (☴) above and *Gen* (☶) below. In the *Later Heaven*, *Gen* (☶) is above and *Xun* (☴) is below, with *Kun* (☷) extending to *Jian* (䷴) in twelve steps, counting thirty-one hexagrams from below to above.

In the *Former Heaven*, one hexagram transforms into seven, with one as the root and seven as its function. In the *Later Heaven*, one hexagram transforms into six, with the hexagram as the root and the lines as its function. In the *Former Heaven*, two and three are the numbers of change, totaling forty-two. In the *Later Heaven*, three and two are the numbers of extension, totaling fifty.

In the *Former Heaven*, *Qian* (☰), *Dui* (☱), *Li* (☲), and *Zhen* (☳) are the four yang hexagrams, belonging to Heaven with eight yang and four yin. *Xun* (☴), *Kan* (☵), *Gen* (☶), and *Kun* (☷) are the four yin hexagrams, belonging to Earth with eight yin and four yang. Their numbers are unequal. In the *Later Heaven*, *Qian* (☰), *Kan* (☵), *Gen* (☶), and *Zhen* (☳) are the four yang hexagrams—one father and three sons—with six yin and six yang. *Xun* (☴), *Li* (☲), *Kun* (☷), and *Dui* (☱) are the four yin hexagrams—one mother and three daughters—with six yin and six yang. Their numbers are equal. *Kun* (☷), *Dui* (☱), *Qian* (☰), and *Kan* (☵) reside in the southwest and north, while *Gen* (☶), *Zhen* (☳), *Xun* (☴), and *Li* (☲) reside in the northeast and southeast, likewise.

In the *Former Heaven*, the beginning of generation and flourishing starts with the winter solstice as the first of the first. In the *Later Heaven*, the beginning of the generation of things starts with the spring equinox as the first of the second. In the *Former Heaven*, Heaven’s two primary hexagrams are *Qian* (☰) and *Li* (☲), combined with *Yi* (䷚) and *Zhongfu* (䷼), doubling the hexagrams to form Heaven’s four primary hexagrams. *Yi* (䷚) resembles *Li* (☲), and *Zhongfu* (䷼) thickens the line of *Li* (☲). In the *Later Heaven*, Earth’s two primary hexagrams are *Kun* (☷) and *Kan* (☵), combined with *Daguo* (䷛) and *Xiaoguo* (䷽), doubling the hexagrams to form Earth’s four primary hexagrams. *Daguo* (䷛) resembles *Kan* (☵), and *Xiaoguo* (䷽) thickens the line of *Kan* (☵).

In the *Former Heaven*, the three palaces of *Xun* (☴), *Li* (☲), and *Dui* (☱) each contain twenty-eight yang. The three palaces of *Kan* (☵), *Gen* (☶), and *Zhen* (☳) each contain twenty-eight yin. In the *Later Heaven*, *Zhen* (☳), *Kan* (☵), and *Gen* (☶) have six yang and twelve yin, while *Xun* (☴), *Li* (☲), and *Dui* (☱) have six yin and twelve yang. The *Upper Canon*’s *Qian* (☰), *Kun* (☷), *Kan* (☵), and *Li* (☲) are the ancestors of thirty-six hexagrams, while the *Lower Canon*’s *Dui* (☱), *Zhen* (☳), *Xun* (☴), and *Gen* (☶) are the ancestors of twenty-eight hexagrams.

In the *Former Heaven*, the greater is employed for governance. The three daughters originally embody *Qian* (☰) and follow their father to the southeast. The three sons originally embody *Kun* (☷) and follow their mother to the northwest. In the *Later Heaven*, the lesser is employed for function. The three sons follow their father to the northeast, and the three daughters follow their mother to the southwest.

Master Zhu said: The *Later Heaven* is the present *Zhouyi*, as arranged by King Wen. After Confucius composed the *Ten Wings* for King Wen’s *Yi*, his discussions should take the *Zhouyi* as primary. Yet without tracing back to Fu Xi’s original drawing of the hexagrams, scholars will surely mistake King Wen’s arrangement for Fu Xi’s original *Yi*, beginning their explanations from the middle and failing to recognize the root source above. Fu Xi’s *Former Heaven Yi* was expanded by King Wen into the *Zhouyi*, which is the study of the *Later Heaven*. Thus, the canon is divided into upper and lower sections: thirty hexagrams in the upper and thirty-four in the lower. Confucius then composed the *Ten Wings* for it, further dividing them into six parts—*Xiang* (Images), *Xici* (Appended Judgments), each with upper and lower sections—and four additional parts: *Wenyan* (seven), *Shuogua* (eight), *Xugua* (nine), and *Zagua* (ten).

To "begin explanations from the middle" means only to know King Wen’s restoration of the *Yi*. One must further study Fu Xi’s *Former Heaven Yi* to thoroughly grasp the root source above. Master Zhu said: The *Former Heaven* and *Later Heaven* each have their own meanings, but the *Later Heaven* contains more interpretive principles. They do not conflict, and one must not cling to one while abandoning the rest. He also said: In the *Later Heaven*, ten hexagrams discuss hexagram transformation, while nine explain the origins of hexagram formation. Whenever the *Xiang* (Image) commentary does not explain the origin of a hexagram’s formation, it does not mention the changing line.

Now, observing the *Yi Commentaries*, we see that Shao Kangjie’s theories on the *Former Heaven* and *Later Heaven* are most insightful. Combining the explanations of Masters Zhu and Cai, we say: Master Cheng focused solely on *Qian* (☰) and *Kun* (☷) when discussing hexagram transformation, which works for hexagrams with upper and lower bodies that can transform, such as *Gu* (䷑), *Xian* (䷞), *Da* (䷎), and *Jian* (䷴). However, it does not apply to hexagrams that transform as a whole, such as *Song* (䷅), *Yu* (䷆), *Meng* (䷃), and *Shi* (䷆). To say that yang comes from outside is problematic. The *Cheng Commentary* on the hexagram *Ze* (䷔) asks: How could *Qian* and *Kun* transform into *Tai* (䷊), and then *Tai* transform into *Ze*? If so, then *Qian* and *Kun* transforming into the six children, and the eight trigrams doubling into sixty-four hexagrams, would all derive from *Qian* and *Kun*. Such an explanation does not hold.

If *Zhen* (☳) obtains a son through the first line, and the following six hexagrams are already formed, then the meanings derived from these hexagrams belong to the *Later Heaven*. The so-called hexagram transformations also occur after the hexagrams exist. The sages perceived these images and thus expounded them in the *Xiang* (Image) commentary. How could one claim that *Qian* and *Kun* transformed into them? Master Cai of Jiezhai said: Hexagram transformations are all composed of three yang and three yin, inherently possessing the principles of *Qian* and *Kun*, with upper and lower interactions. *Qian*’s firmness intermingles with *Kun* to form *Zhen* (☳), *Kan* (☵), and *Gen* (☶), while *Kun*’s softness intermingles with *Qian* to form *Xun* (☴), *Li* (☲), and *Dui* (☱). When it speaks of "firmness coming" or "firmness descending," it clarifies that *Qian* is above and descends to interact with *Kun*. When it speaks of "softness coming" or "softness descending," it means *Kun* is above and descends to interact with *Qian*. If it speaks of "above" in relation to "softness ascending," then it reverses: *Qian*’s firmness is below and ascends to interact with *Kun*, while *Kun*’s softness is below and ascends to interact with *Qian*. All these derive from the mutual interactions of *Qian* and *Kun*.

As for *Song* (䷅) and *Yu* (䷆), which only mention "firmness coming" and "firmness coming from outside," these hexagrams have four yang and two yin, not the result of *Qian* and *Kun*’s upper and lower interactions. *Qian*’s body remains above, unmoved, while the firmness of *Kan* (☵) and *Zhen* (☳) comes from outside. All these meanings arise from the hexagrams of the *Later Heaven*. Master Zhu said: Fu Xi’s circular arrangement of the eight trigrams was created through mutual opposition.

Fu Xi’s sixty-four hexagrams in the horizontal arrangement were created through the principle of *flowing movement*. What governs *opposition* is secretly employed through *flowing movement*. *Opposition* arises from stillness as its body. Fu Xi’s eight trigrams embody *opposition*. When stillness gives rise, then fortune and misfortune, regret and remorse proceed from oneself. Thus it is said: the *Former Heaven* sequence is formed by the waxing and waning of the two *qi*, which is the principle of creation and the generation of beings.

King Wen’s eight trigrams in the circular arrangement were created through *flowing movement*. King Wen’s sixty-four hexagrams in the horizontal arrangement were created through *opposition*. What governs *flowing movement* is necessarily employed through *opposition*. *Flowing movement* arises from motion as its body. King Wen’s eight trigrams embody *flowing movement*. When motion is accomplished, then fortune and misfortune, regret and remorse are received from Heaven. Thus it is said: the *Later Heaven* sequence is formed by the flourishing and decline of the myriad things, which is the principle of creation’s cyclical movement.

Between Heaven and Earth, there is only *opposition* and *flowing movement*. *Qian* and *Kun* are the purest form of *opposition*. *Kan* and *Li* are the intermingling of *opposition*. *Xian* (䷞) and *Dai* (䷟) are the progression of *opposition*. *Jiji* (䷾) and *Weiji* (䷿) are the mixture of *opposition*. The *yin* and *yang* of *opposition* thus have the same function as the *yin* and *yang* of *flowing movement*. Only *yang* is employed in function: when still, it is two; when in motion, it is one. *Opposition* reverts to motion and becomes *flowing movement*. *Flowing movement* reverts to stillness and becomes *opposition*.

Master Zhu said: The *Former Heaven Diagram* possesses the marvels of *transformation* and *exchange*. The 192 lines on the left are fundamentally all *yang*. The 192 lines on the right are fundamentally all *yin*. Within *yin* there is *yang*, and within *yang* there is *yin*—this is *yang* advancing to exchange with *yin*, and *yin* coming to exchange with *yang*. The two sides correspond to each other, mutually transforming to form this diagram. In reality, it is not that one moves toward the other; it is merely that their images appear thus.

On the left side of the diagram, belonging to *yang*: from *Zhen* (one *yang*), to *Li* and *Dui* (two *yang*), to *Qian* (three *yang*). This is *yang* within *yang*, *yang* moving in sequence. On the right side of the diagram, belonging to *yin*: from *Xun* (one *yin*), to *Kan* and *Gen* (two *yin*), to *Kun* (three *yin*). This is *yin* within *yin*, *yin* moving in reverse.

*Kun* has no *yang*; *Gen* and *Kan* have one *yang*. This is *yang* within *yin* moving in reverse. *Qian* has no *yin*; *Li* and *Dui* have one *yin*, *Zhen* has two *yin*. This is *yin* within *yang* moving in reverse. The five lines on the west are the *gou* (intermingling) of the five lines on the east. *Yang* having passed returns to the east, just as the five lines on the west. *Yin* and *yang* mutually transform through exchange, giving rise to the *Yi*’s *transformation*. Though the *Yi*’s *transformations* are manifold, this is the primary *transformation*.

At first, Fu Xi merely saw *yin* and *yang* beneath the Supreme Ultimate, so he drew one *yin* and one *yang*. One became two. On one *yang*, another *yang* and another *yin* were born; on one *yin*, another *yin* and another *yang* were born. Continuing thus in generation: from two to four, from four to eight, until eight times eight made sixty-four, all unfolding naturally. Such perfect order is the marvel of Heaven and Earth. The *Former Heaven Diagram* was merely sketched by the hand of the sage. In a single day, there is a principle of thought; in a month, a principle of lassitude. Extending to the twelve *yuan*, *hui*, *yun*, and *shi*—129,600 years—it is still only this principle. Vastly encompassing the 129,600 years of ancient and modern times, it is still only this cycle. Minutely observing the birth and death of beings, the movements of a day, an hour, a month, a year—it is still only this cycle. All proceed from *Fu* (䷗) and extend outward; it is merely the principle of waxing and waning, fullness and emptiness. What was small transforms into what is large; when it reaches its greatest extent, it transforms again into what is small.

Master Shao said: The *Former Heaven Diagram* is the *ring within*. Master Zhang said: That which is round on the outside is Heaven; that which is square within is Earth. Humans stand upon the Earth and thus are within the *ring*. I say: Observing from above and below, *Qian* in the south is Heaven, *Kun* in the north is Earth. Observing from left and right, from *Zhen* to *Qian* on the left is Heaven. Great *yang*, great *yin*, lesser *yang*, and lesser *yin* arise, and the Dao of Heaven is complete. From *Xun* to *Kun* on the right is Earth. Great firmness, great softness, lesser firmness, and lesser softness arise, and the Dao of Earth is accomplished. Heaven envelops Earth externally. The two *qi* of *yin* and *yang*, though endlessly transforming, follow a single principle, cycling without end. This is why it is called the *ring within*.

Dwelling within the *ring* as human relationships: from *Qian* the ruler and *Kun* the minister, the *Family*, father and son, *Xian* and *Dai*, husband and wife, the six children emerge. Applying the *ring* to verify the square city: the Gate of Heaven has no above, the Door of Earth has no below. Rivers and marshes flow into the southeast, their currents endless. Kunlun rises in the northwest, its height unmatched. The great brightness of dawn emerges from *Li*; the *yin* spirit is conceived in the west within *Kan*. Thunder issues from *Zhen*, and the myriad things awaken. Wind enters *Xun*, and the myriad things wither. The images of the *ring within* may be glimpsed here.

Seeking it in the four seasons: at the winter solstice, the *yang* spirit arrives at midnight; at the summer solstice, the *yin* ghost retreats at noon. The three *yang* and three *yin* divide spring; the three *yin* and three *yang* mark autumn’s midpoint. From crescent to full, from waning to new, the moon has its beginning and end. From dawn to day, from dusk to night, the *qi* has its reversal and sequence. Within the *ring*, the years, months, days, and hours—existence and decay, advance and retreat—may be discerned here.

Seeking it in the opening and closing of *yin* and *yang*: Heaven initiates and Earth responds, giving rise to the sounds of the sun, moon, and stars. Earth harmonizes with Heaven, producing the tones of water, fire, earth, and stone. Opening and closing, the trigger moves and the pipes resound—this is the music of the *ring within*, rooted in Heaven’s truth. Seeking it in detail, it is nameless yet may be named.

Fu Xi released it to form the square and round diagrams. Emperor Shun performed it to bring forth the nine movements of the *Xiao Shao*.

Shào Kāngjié grasped it and composed the *Supreme Principles Governing the World*. Can one stand within the *ring*, bearing Heaven and treading Earth, yet remain unaware of its workings?

Kāngjié’s self-eulogy speaks of "playing with the ring at leisure," where moments come and go. He also said: "Since comprehending the meaning of the *ring within*, not a wisp of stray *qi* lingers in my breast." Thus, within his breast, he naturally possessed a *ring within*—this much is clear.

He further declared: "The learning of *Xiāntiān* is the method of the heart." Hence, all diagrams begin from the center, for the myriad transformations and affairs arise from the heart.

Master Zhū said: "The white space at the center is the *Tàijí*. The thirty-two *yīn* and thirty-two *yáng* form the Two Modes. The sixteen *yīn* and sixteen *yáng* constitute the Four Images. The eight *yīn* and eight *yáng* make up the Eight Trigrams. The two sides arise as *yīn* rooted in *yáng* and *yáng* rooted in *yīn*. This pairing emerges from the center, yet what issues from the *Tàijí* at the center is without counterpart—this is the method of the heart."

In my humble view: In the *Xiāntiān Diagram*, that which arises from *kūn* begins with *fù*, and that which arises from *qián* begins with *gòu*. Both reside within Heaven and Earth. The center is the heart, the *Tàijí*. It transcends the myriad things yet moves within them—hence, it is without counterpart.

He also said: "Though the *Diagram* bears no text, I speak of it all day without ever departing from it. The principles generating Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are all contained within." The *Xiāntiān Diagram* possesses positions and numbers, yet initially, no words or texts could convey it. Yet what lies within encompasses all—from the vastness of Heaven and Earth to the minutest tip of a hair. Above, the sun, moon, and stars; below, water, fire, earth, and stone. Transforming into heat and cold, day and night; transmuting into rain, wind, dew, and thunder. Resonating as temperament, form, and body; responding as creatures that walk, fly, and grow as plants. In humans, manifesting as ears, eyes, nose, and mouth; in things, as colors, sounds, *qi*, and flavors. Extending into epochs, kalpas, eons, and generations; dispersing into years, months, days, and hours. Affairs have substance and function, dividing into the eras of emperors, kings, and hegemons. Endeavors have their paths, branching into the *Book of Changes*, *Book of Documents*, *Book of Songs*, and *Spring and Autumn Annals*. Though principle is one, its manifestations are many—nowhere does it not reside within.

Master Shào’s poem reads:
*The sun, moon, and stars shine brightly above,
The emperors, kings, and hegemons spread grandly below.*
This may be called a pavilion within the breast, open on all sides.

Master Zhū praised him, saying:
*A hero heaven-endowed, peerless in spirit,
Riding wind and lashing thunder, his gaze boundless.
With hands he probes the moon’s cavern, with feet he treads the sky’s root.
In leisure, he spans past and present; in intoxication, he embraces Heaven and Earth.*
Such is the manner in which he vividly portrays one of the Dao—his bearing and presence.

He also said: "In stillness, he deduced the principles of Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things."

Master Zhū remarked: "Kāngjié’s *Nánzǐ Yín* speaks of the *Xiāntiān Diagram*, where numbers take their origin. *Tiāngēn* and *yuèkū* refer to the hexagrams *fù* and *gòu*." His poem reads:
*Ears and eyes keen, the body of a true man,
Endowed by the great potter—how could he be poor?
Only by probing the moon’s cavern can one know things;
Without treading the sky’s root, how can one recognize people?
When *qián* meets *gòu*, the moon’s cavern is born;
When Earth encounters thunder, the sky’s root appears.
*Tiāngēn* and *yuèkū*, idly coming and going,
The thirty-six palaces—all are spring.*

"All are spring" signifies the flowing of Heavenly principle.

He further said: "In the *Diagram*, from *fù* to *qián* is *yáng*; from *gòu* to *kūn* is *yīn*. Since the two—*yīn* and *yáng*—govern good and evil, the beings they endow cannot but bear distinctions of purity and impurity. *Yáng* governs humans, *yīn* governs things. *Gòu* lies above in the *Diagram*, hence the phrase 'hand probes'; *fù* lies below, hence 'foot treads.'"

Cài Xīshān said: "*Tiāngēn* depicts the state of a good person, *yuèkū* that of a petty person. The thirty-six palaces are the lines of *yīn* and *yáng* in the Eight Trigrams." Another said: "The terms 'human' and 'thing' cannot be simply divided into good and evil. 'All are spring'—even *yuèkū* is spring."

Master Zhū said: "In terms of correct principle, *yáng* is good and *yīn* is evil—they form a pair, each with its own governance. Kāngjié likely referred to the source of living things: the upright *qi* becomes human, the biased *qi* becomes things—this is the distinction between *yīn* and *yáng*. Jìtōng’s discussion, however, seems to be an extended interpretation. Is 'all are spring' spoken solely in terms of correctness?"

He asked: "Crows, owls, toads, lizards, noxious weeds, and poisonous herbs—can one say they are not the *qi* of Heaven and Earth’s *yīn* and *yáng*? That which is *yīn* and base is called 'thing'; that which is *yáng* and noble is called 'Heaven.'"

In my humble view: From *fù* to *qián*, there are one hundred twelve *yáng* and eighty *yīn*; from *gòu* to *kūn*, one hundred twelve *yīn* and eighty *yáng*. *Yīn* and *yáng* intermingle, their interactions and encounters all bearing the essence of spring, flowing within them. Within my own body, too, there are these *fù* and *gòu*. On the summer solstice, when the sun is in *Dōngjǐng*, the myriad things turn toward nothingness. I, however, gather inward, returning to the uncarved, withdrawing blossoms to bear fruit—this is the resurgence of life’s intent within. On the winter solstice, when the sun is in *Qiānniú*, the myriad things turn toward being. I then expand outward, reaching to Heaven and coiling upon Earth—this is the flourishing of life’s intent without. Viewed thus, the creative work of the Maker by day is the flow of life’s intent, while human rest at night is the return of life’s intent. Where is there not spring?

Chéng Bózǐ said: "Kāngjié’s learning is the Way of inner sageliness and outer kingship." At the outset of Kāngjié’s entry into the Dao, Lord Qīngshè imparted to him the study of the nature of things and the mandate of life. Later, amid movement and stillness, he perceived it and attained its handle.

Thus his poems declare:
*"The body lies behind Heaven and Earth,
The heart stands before Heaven and Earth."*
And again:
*"From the One Center, creation divides,
From the heart, great plans arise."*
This was his self-attained realization.

Master Zhu said: "Quezi carried this learning within his belly, thus he could encompass the cosmos from beginning to end, past and present." And again: "His marrow was the *Huangji Jingshi* book; his blossoms and grasses were his poetry."

I say: Inner sageliness is the prior Heaven—within the *Xiantian Tu*, each stroke and line, from Heaven above to Earth below, from ancient times to the present, all is encompassed. Outer kingship is the posterior Heaven. Thus, in his advancement, he fully unfolded within the realm of emperors, kings, and hegemons. Past and present order and chaos, the flourishing and withering of people—all were seen within stillness, without the slightest deviation. Therefore, inner and outer form a single thread, prior and posterior leave nothing behind.

Master Lin said: "The *Xiantian Tu* is the study of nature and life. His books and poems all flowed from this diagram." Master Shao’s learning of prior Heaven was not lightly transmitted to unworthy recipients. When Master Zhang was magistrate of Shangluo, he repeatedly visited the master’s dwelling and bowed to him, asking: "How many days does it take to master this learning?" The master replied: "There is nothing much to it—with your talent, it can be grasped in an instant. But you must follow me in the woods for ten years, allowing worldly concerns to scatter, before it can be transmitted."

When Shu He came to study, he ceaselessly cited ancient and modern examples. The master said: "Set that aside for now—prior Heaven has no need for so many words. First, empty your heart, making your breast vast and unburdened, free of any affair, before it is possible."

Thus his poems say:
*"If asked about the primordial One character..."*
And again:
*"Strength to uproot mountains and cover the world is called talent,
Yet here, not even the slightest force avails."*

When the master fell slightly ill, Shu tasted his medicinal porridge and said: "I am no Yellow Stone or foolish old man—kneeling to tie your shoes would suffice to obtain it." The master replied: "Without a sincere heart, the Way is distant." Shu was then convinced in his heart. The hearts of these two disciples had already been grasped by the master. For his breast was crystalline, responding to things as they came—yet he did not transmit to those unworthy. As with Qin Zhen and Ren Yan, or Zheng Yang of Jiangnan, who stole his learning to seek glory and wealth, they ultimately starved to death.

I say: The learning of prior Heaven cannot be attained except by one whose nature is purely of Heaven.

The "thirty-six palaces" have been interpreted in four ways by former scholars:
1. *Qian* as one and *Dui* as two make three; *Li* as three and *Zhen* as four make seven; *Xun* as five and *Kan* as six make eleven; *Gen* as seven and *Kun* as eight make fifteen.
2. The nine lines of *Qian* and *Kun*; the five lines each of *Zhen*, *Kan*, and *Gen* make fifteen; the four lines each of *Xun*, *Li*, and *Dui* make twelve.
3. *Qian* as one pairs with *Kun* as eight, down to *Zhen* as four pairing with *Xun* as five—eight palaces forming four nines, totaling thirty-six.
4. The eight proper hexagrams plus twenty-eight inverted hexagrams make thirty-six, matching the hidden hexagrams of prior Heaven.

I say: This last interpretation is correct.

Master Fang of Xugu criticized them, saying: "The theory of palaces based on the sequence of prior Heaven hexagrams is inappropriate; dividing odd and even hexagrams into daytime palaces is fabricated; the theory of quadrupling nine is unclear. As for the fourth interpretation—adding proper and inverted hexagrams to obtain thirty-six hidden palaces—it is clever but insipid."

In my view: In the *Xiantian Circular Diagram*, *Fu* begins at *zi*, and the left 180 days form eighteen palaces. *Gou* begins at *wu*, and the right 180 days form eighteen palaces. One *xun* (ten-day period) is one palace, and the 360 days of a year complete the cycle of gestation and return.

I say: This interpretation is good but not exhaustive. Let me complete it:
From *zi* to *si*, the six branches correspond to the six hexagrams from *Fu* to *Qian*. From *wu* to *hai*, the six branches correspond to the six hexagrams from *Gou* to *Kun*. *Qian* begins with *initial nine*, a single yang that does not interact. *Kun* begins with *initial six*, which becomes *Fu*—the hexagram of the eleventh month—named by Quezi as the "Heavenly Root," meaning the odd yang is the root, born at *zi*. *Kun* begins with *initial six*, a single yin that rises to interact. *Qian* begins with *initial nine*, which becomes *Gou*—the hexagram of the fifth month—named the "Cavern of the Sun and Moon," meaning the even yin symbol emerges as a cavern at *wu*.

*Qian* and *Kun* are the great parents, thus giving birth to *Fu* and *Gou*. *Fu* and *Gou* are the lesser parents, generating one yin and one yang. The alternation of yin and yang begins here. *Fu* and *Gou* become *Lin* and *Dun*, marking the ebb and flow of two yins and two yangs—this is the month of *chou* and *wei*. From *Lin* and *Dun* to *Pi* and *Tai*, the ebb and flow of three yins and three yangs—this is the month of *yin* and *shen*. Proceeding to four yins and four yangs, forming *Guan* and *Zhuang*; five yins and five yangs, forming *Cui* and *Bo*. Finally, six yins and six yangs intermingle and transform. *Fu* and *Gou* return to *Qian* and *Kun*, and *Qian* and *Kun* return to *Fu* and *Gou*, flowing and opposing within the twelve palaces. Within these, there can be no yin without yang, no yang without yin. Divided, they become thirty-six yins and thirty-six yangs, scattered within the 360 days. Combined, they are but one yin and one yang. Doubling the five phases yields the thirty-six palaces.

Things obtain this and are born and transformed; people obtain this and are harmonious and joyful—how great is this spring!

Extending this further, thirty-six becomes seventy-two, and the numbers are inexhaustible. Contracting it, one day has 36,000 seconds, and one month has 360 hours.

Even in the finest details—whether a single insect, fish, plant, or tree—each possesses its own yin and yang, weaving as warp and weft within. Yet people remain unaware of this.

The noble person who studies and attains this understanding delights in Heaven’s decree and knows fate, free from worry.

Now, regarding the left rotation of the Heavenly Dao and the right revolution of the sun and moon, with yang on the left and yin on the right, day and night succeeding one another—there are three explanations for this:

First, the unity of the Heavenly Dao and the sun and moon;
second, *The River Chart* and *The Writ of the Luo*;
third, *The Precelestial Diagram*.

Their positions are determined by facing south from the north, with east on the left and west on the right.

Yet *The Precelestial Diagram* derives its left and right from *The River Chart* and *The Writ of the Luo*, while *The River Chart* and *The Writ of the Luo* themselves arise from the left rotation of Heaven and the right motion of the sun and moon. Thus it is said: "The hanging images display their brightness—none greater than the sun and moon." Ah! Heaven’s revelation to humanity is most profound.

*The Essential Meaning of Ancient Texts* states: "The celestial wheel rotates left from west to east, while the sun and moon revolve right from east to west." It also says: "The twenty-eight lunar lodges all follow Heaven’s motion in leftward rotation, while the sun, moon, and five planets all move right along the earthly path. The sun travels through the six *jia* of Heaven, meeting the Dipper. The Dipper’s handle points left according to the monthly establishment, while the sun meets the moon and moves right. In the first month, the Dipper points to *yin* in the east, and the sun and moon converge northward in *hai*, aligning with *yin*. In the fourth month, the Dipper points to *si* in the south, and the sun and moon converge westward in *shen*, aligning with *si*. Heaven moves left by one degree, distributing *qi* to generate beings. The sun and moon also shift right by one degree, altering the seasons to respond to Heaven. When Heaven and the sun align, the *qi* and climate harmonize."

Moreover, Jupiter and the Great Year also move left and right. The Great Year corresponds to the sequence from *zi* to *hai*. Jupiter is the essence of wood, called the Year Star; Mars is the essence of fire, called the Sparkling Deluder, and so on. Jupiter, being yang, moves right through the heavens, traversing the twelve celestial stations. The Great Year, being yin, moves left through the earth, passing through the twelve earthly branches. Thus, twelve years complete one cycle, forming a great era. For details, see *Shaoyang*.

Master Shao said: "Heaven rotates left, the sun moves right." He also said: "Left rotation and right motion—this is the intersection of Heaven and the sun." And again: "Kan and Li stand as the gates of left and right, the places where the sun and moon enter and exit."

*The River Chart*’s *Earthly Image* states: "Heaven moves left, beginning at Oxherd; Earth moves right, beginning at Net." The degrees of Heaven and Earth diverge.

It is also said: "*The Writ of the Luo*, in terms of the sequence of movement, begins in the west, then proceeds to the south, east, center, and north—rotating right in a full circle before returning to the west. This symbolizes Earth’s rightward motion to generate beings."

Cai Jiufeng said: "In form, the number is ten; in function, it is nine. Ten cannot change—this is the number of *The River Chart*. Nine can change—this is *The Writ of the Luo*. *The Chart* arranges its numbers in opposition to establish form; *The Writ* interlaces its numbers to enable function. Once form is established, if the numbers do not change, function cannot proceed."

Now, applying the mutable numbers of *The Writ of the Luo*:
In one diagram, the numbers for left and right rotation are complete.
Yang moves left with three:
Heaven is round, its diameter one, circumference three—three is the number of Heaven.
One is in the north.
One multiplied by three is three, which is in the east.
Three times three is nine, residing in the south.
Nine times three is twenty-seven, residing in the west.
Three times twenty-seven is eighty-one, returning to one in the north.
From north to east, east to south, south to west, west back to north—endlessly cycling, perfectly embodying the principle of Heaven’s left rotation.

Earth is square, its diameter one, circumference four—two doubled.
For Earth’s numbers begin with two, taking yin as their origin.
Its position is in the southwest, moving right.
Two doubled is four, residing in the southeast.
Two times four is eight, in the northeast.
Two times eight is sixteen, in the northwest.
Two times sixteen is thirty-two, returning to two in the southwest.
From its original position in the southwest to the southeast, southeast to northeast, northeast to northwest, northwest back to southwest—also endlessly cycling, perfectly harmonizing with the theory of Earth’s rightward motion.

One, three, seven, nine—yang occupies the four cardinal directions.
Two, four, six, eight—yin occupies the four corners.
Left and right rotation interweave as warp and weft.
Such is the marvel of creation.

Between Heaven and Earth, no thing lacks left and right—from our own hands, feet, ears, and eyes to men on the left and women on the right, host on the left and guest on the right—all follow this pattern, rooted in the fixed divisions of creation.

If one examines *The River Chart*, the same principle applies, though yin and yang are arranged in opposition internally and externally, with differences in their interlacing.

*The Original Subtleties of Heaven*, Scroll Fourteen, concludes.

**The Subtleties of Heaven’s Origin, Scroll Fifteen**
*Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai*
*Collated by Fang Hui Wuli of Xugu*

**The Subtleties of Heaven’s Origin, Scroll Fifteen**
*Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai*
*Collated by Fang Hui Wuli of Xugu*

---

**Section Two: The Two Middles**

The numbers one through ten are the complete enumeration of Heaven and Earth. Five and six are the central numbers of Heaven and Earth. Five, residing at the center of Heaven, governs the generating numbers; six, residing at the center of Earth, governs the completing numbers. Five is also called the "center of yang," for it stands amid one, three, seven, and nine. Six is also called the "center of yin," for it stands amid two, four, eight, and ten.

Speaking of the total numbers of Heaven and Earth: five fives make twenty-five, five sixes make thirty, and five tens with five make fifty-five. Omitting the five leaves the Heavenly numbers. Centered on five, the remainder is fifty, which is the number of the *Great Expansion*. Omitting the six leaves the Earthly numbers. Centered on six, the remainder is forty-nine, which is the number of the *Stalks and Milfoil*. These two central numbers circulate between Heaven and Earth. Those who attain the center are upright and auspicious; those who exceed or fall short are #1 lost and inauspicious.

Master Shao said: "The origin of Heaven and Earth lies in the center. Man dwells at the center of Heaven and Earth, and the heart dwells at the center of man. When the sun reaches its zenith, it is at its fullest; when the moon reaches its midpoint, it is full. Thus the noble person values the timely center."

The *Great Ultimate* divides into Heaven and Earth. In Heaven and Earth, it becomes man; in man, it becomes the heart. Man is the heart of Heaven and Earth. Within the human heart lies another Heaven and Earth. When the sage emerges to establish the center and create the ultimate, he can give Heaven and Earth a heart, guiding all under Heaven to return to the center. Thus he assists in #3 completing and harmonizing with Heaven.

Therefore, Heaven takes *wu* (noon) as its center, Earth takes *zi* (midnight) as its center. Yang takes *mao* (dawn) #4 as its center, yin takes *you* (dusk) as its center. Heaven and Earth never deviate from the center for even a moment.

As the saying goes: "When the sun reaches its zenith, it shifts; when the moon is full, it wanes." Excess leads to decline and imbalance—this is why the noble person values the timely center.

The *Great Commentary on the Changes* speaks of the numbers of Heaven and Earth as fifty-five, and the number of the *Great Expansion* as fifty. The Heavenly numbers are twenty-five, five times five. The Earthly numbers are thirty, six times five. The *Lesser Expansion* is ten, two times five. The *Great Expansion* is fifty, ten times five.

In my humble opinion: Dong Zhu once said that all numbers do not exceed five. Five is the ancestral number, corresponding to the three yang and two yin of Heaven and Earth, interwoven so that all numbers resolve to five. Three times three and three times two—both the numbers of old yin and old yang—are five. Two times three and one times two—the number of young yin—is five. Two times two and one times three—the number of young yang—is five. From one five to nine five, the blessings total forty-#5five. The numbers of the nine chapters are all five. Thus, both the *Diagram* and the *Book* take five as the ancestral origin of numbers.

Therefore, qi has the five phases, man has the five constants, Heaven has the five stars, Earth has the five peaks, music has the five tones, and colors have the five hues. Extending further: governance has the five teachings, rituals have the five orders, garments have the five insignia, punishments have the five penalties, food has the five flavors and five grains, and armies have the five formations of troops and chariots. All are governed by the Heavenly center of five—this is evident.

Master Zhu said: The *Canon of Yao* speaks of a full year as three hundred and sixty-six days. *Kang Gao* states that after five or six days, it reaches a full decade. Hexagrams have six lines, and the moon has five or six periods totaling thirty days. Six days after birth, light appears; six days later, the first quarter; six days later, the full moon. Another six days, the waning begins; another six days, its function ceases. From *zi* to *si* are the six yang months; from *wu* to *hai* are the six yin months. Five and six multiply to generate transformation. Thus, two sixes make twelve months; three sixes make eighteen changes; four sixes make the twenty-four solar terms; six sixes make the number of a full year.

Extending further: man has the six virtues, six conducts, and six arts; vessels have the six ritual goblets, six ceremonial wine vessels, six sacrificial dishes, and six offering stands; music has the six pitch standards and six complementary tones; the *Rites of Zhou* has the six canons and six ministries; the queen has the six robes. Also, food uses the six grains, meals use the six livestock, drink uses the six pure beverages, delicacies use one hundred twenty items, sauces use one hundred twenty jars, and tripods use twelve objects—all are six. By governing six with five and pairing six with five, the myriad transformations never lose the Earthly center of six.

Master Zhu said: The numbers of Heaven and Earth take six as their measure. When six yang reaches its extreme, one yin is born to restrain it and prevent excess. When six yin reaches its extreme, one yang is born to restrain it and prevent overgrowth. This restraint is called the "measure of Heaven and Earth," and the four seasons are thereby completed. The division of *zi* and *wu* (midnight and noon) marks the great measures of Heaven and Earth. The four seasons and eight nodes complete a year—these are the greatest measures. Six becomes twelve, twenty-four, and seventy-two—these are the lesser measures. Day divides into day and night, numbers divide into odd and even, each forming twelve. The use of two hundred twenty-four is also measured by six.

The numbers of the *Changes* total sixty: thirty-six for old yang, paired with twenty-four for old yin, together making sixty. Thirty-two for young yin, paired with twenty-eight for young yang, also making sixty. Ten stems and twelve branches combine to make sixty. The five tones and twelve pitches of bells and pipes accumulate to sixty.

Master Shao said: The heavenly stems end with six, the earthly branches end with five. The *Inner Transmission* records that the Yellow Emperor commanded Da Rao to create the *Jia-Zi* system, divining by the handle of the Dipper’s bowl. He named *jia* and *yi* as days—these are the heavenly stems. He named *zi* and *chou* as months—these are the earthly branches. Stems and branches combine to form the sixty-day cycle.

In my humble opinion: That which originates in five ends in six; that which originates in six ends in five. Five is the ten heavenly stems, #6 six is the twelve earthly branches. Two sixes pair to make twelve; five and six multiply to make thirty. Yang numbers begin with thirty, hence a month has thirty days, and a generation has thirty years.

Yin numbers begin with twelve, hence a day has twelve *chen* (double-hours), and a year has twelve months. Multiplying a year’s months by thirty yields three hundred sixty days; multiplying a year’s days by twelve yields four thousand three hundred twenty *shi* (hours). Twelve and thirty, when added or multiplied, are all derived from five and six.

The *Qianxu* takes the central number of heaven: five multiplied by five makes twenty-five, the central number of earth; five multiplied by six makes thirty. Combined, fifty-five forms the *xuanxu* (mysterious void), the numbers of generation and completion. The generating numbers are one, two, three, four, five—five stands at heaven’s center and precedes six. The completing numbers are six, seven, eight, nine, ten—six stands at earth’s center and follows five. One and five form six (water); two and five form seven (fire); three and five form eight (wood); four and five form nine (metal); five and five form ten (earth). Water has source and outflow; fire has flame and radiance; wood has root and branch; metal has edge and blade; earth has foundation and burial mound—all do not exceed five and six.

Within yang, five becomes ten by doubling; within yin, six becomes twelve by doubling. Kangjie (Shao Yong) took these two central numbers to compose the *Huangji Lishi* (Supreme Principles Through the Ages), thereby illuminating the principle of the great mean and perfect correctness. Masters Ma and Shao, morning and evening, expounded and clarified this together, and the learning of the *xuanxu* was thus refined.

Heaven’s movements: in small cycles, they change through five and six; in great cycles, they change through sixty. When correlated with hexagram qi: one month has five hexagrams, one hexagram has six lines—five and six make thirty. Six yang and six yin: the twelve branches course through the twelve months and three hundred sixty days. This is the small cycle advancing and retreating by six days, hence the small cycle changes through six. Sixty changes, with their remaining fractions, also complete three hundred sixty.

Heaven’s Dao changes through six, with residual fractions: six days pass through six *chen*. After *zi* (midnight), the six *shi* advance; after *wu* (noon), the six *shi* retreat. Further correlating with the six *jia*: sometimes change occurs every six months, sometimes every six years, sometimes every sixty years, sometimes every three hundred sixty years. Thus, the great cycle changes through sixty, and six changes, with their residual fractions, yield three hundred sixty-six.

In great cycles, sixty years mark one change—*jiazi* and *jiawu* each constitute one generation. In small cycles, six months mark one change: after *zi*, six months bring growth; after *wu*, six months bring decline. Six years mark one change—the number of the six qi. Sixty years mark one change—the number of the five movements.

Master Cai of Xishan said: Heaven’s numbers center on five; earth’s numbers center on six. Heaven has yin and yang, so doubling five makes ten. Combining three and seven, or one and nine, also yields ten. Earth has hardness and softness, so doubling six makes twelve. Combining four and eight, or two and ten, also yields twelve. The ten heavenly stems arise from the five phases having yin and yang; the twelve earthly branches arise from the six qi having hardness and softness. The five phases and six qi are fundamentally one qi.

The five phases in heaven become the five qi—rain, sunshine, cold, warmth, and wind. On earth, they become the five substances—water, fire, wood, metal, and earth. In heaven, rain corresponds to earth’s water; in heaven, sunshine corresponds to earth’s fire. Water and fire possess qi, while rain and sunshine possess substance. Rain and sunshine are heaven interfacing with earth; water and fire are earth interfacing with heaven. Two transform, while three remain unchanging. Two attain the correctness of yin and yang; three attain the mixture of yin and yang.

The *Zuozhuan* states: The people have the six emotions—love, hate, joy, anger, sorrow, and happiness—born of the six qi. The people receive the qi of yin and yang, wind and rain, darkness and brightness to live: yin is metal, wind is earth, rain is wood, darkness is water, and brightness is fire. Love arises from yang; hate arises from yin; joy arises from wind; anger arises from rain; sorrow arises from darkness; happiness arises from brightness.

Thus, heaven has six qi that descend to generate the five flavors. People consume the five flavors, corresponding to heaven’s six qi. Sorrow gives rise to weeping; happiness gives rise to song and dance; joy gives rise to giving; anger gives rise to conflict. Joy arises from love; anger arises from hate. When sorrow and happiness are not lost, one can harmonize with the nature of heaven and earth and thus endure long.

In my humble opinion: People’s qi endowments are uneven. Those who are hard may err in excessive hardness; those who are soft may err in excessive softness. One must first overcome and refine their biases. Among them, there is a type of person exceptionally fierce and resolute—this is encountering an excess of yang qi. There is a type of person extremely weak and timid—this is encountering an excess of yin qi. Some are restless, violent, and wrathful—this is encountering the malignance of yang qi. Some are cunning, deceitful, and treacherous—this is encountering the malignance of yin qi. Some have a nature that is adaptable, turning with a single touch. Some are stubborn and foolish, unable to accept even a single word of good advice, no different from beasts. All these arise from such qi endowments, not from the qi of yin and yang itself. Evil arises merely from the separation, combination, and transformation of qi. Within the unevenness, differences naturally form, giving rise to fragmented likes and dislikes.

Thus it is said: Heaven, earth, and humanity share one qi. When heaven reverses its seasons, it becomes disaster; when earth reverses its creatures, it becomes omen. When the people reverse virtue, it becomes chaos—this can move heaven and earth to produce omens. When things lose their nature and reverse the norm, they become omens.

The historian states: Omens and disasters include floods, droughts, famines, untimely cold and heat, thunder and hail as calamities, solar and lunar eclipses, comets and meteors, halos, mock suns, intersecting arcs, celestial phenomena like "back holes" and "speech embraces," locust plagues, silkworm anomalies, misaligned constellations, landslides, earthquakes; yang qi suppressed and unable to emerge, yin qi oppressed and unable to ascend. The six qi congeal into ominous leaves, pestilential misfortunes, and blue omens—all reverse the norm to harm the people’s nature.

Thus, to transform inverted qi into harmonious qi lies solely with the ruler, who attains the Mean and Harmony to align heaven and earth. When yang returns, in the fifth month a single yin is born, becoming the initial line of *Kun*. In the sixth month, it retreats, and a second yin arises. In the seventh month, it closes, and a third yin arises, completing the three lines of *Kun* within. In the eighth month, it observes, and a fourth yin arises. In the ninth month, it strips away, and a fifth yin arises. Accumulating until the tenth month, the top line of *Kun* is reached, and the six yins are fully realized—then its number is exhausted and reverts to seven. Again, the initial line of *Kun* transforms into yang, and the hexagram is named *Fu* (Return). From *Shi* (Pregnancy), *Dun* (Retreat), *Pi* (Obstruction), *Guan* (Observation), *Bo* (Stripping), and *Kun* (Earth) to *Fu* (Return), it undergoes nearly seven changes, hence the saying "seven days." This is the fundamental purport of the *Great Changes* and the orthodox interpretation of former scholars.

Yet the *Zhengyi* also cites the theory of "six days and seven parts," aligning with Zheng Sinong’s reference to the *Yi Wei*, thus completing the hexagram qi. It further states: "Confucius’s apocrypha are clear, and Wang Bi’s commentary is as such. Can Zheng Kangcheng’s explanation be reconciled?" How, then, to regard these two theories? It is said: both are valid. The *Changes* encompass all phenomena, adapting with time to follow the Dao without exception. In interpreting the sacred canon, one should adhere to the orthodox interpretations of former scholars. Taking "seven days" as seven months is acceptable. To establish hexagram qi and rectify the degrees of the celestial sphere, using "six days and seven parts" is also unobjectionable. Both are preserved here to await future sages.

However, Kong’s explanation in Wang’s transmission states: "After the yang qi of heaven is extinguished, it revives within seven days." The phrase "extinguished" is somewhat dubious. Though yang qi may be subtle, how could it ever be extinguished? Moreover, Wang Kui’s account, from the fifth to the eleventh month, posits that the sun traverses seven celestial lodges, then abandons yang qi before it returns—this is incorrect. Little does he realize that the celestial sphere comprises twenty-eight lodges; the sun moves one degree per day, traversing one lodge per day, and its conjunction with the moon marks a month. Essentially, if the sun moves through seven lodges, it is seven months—how can one alter months to speak of days? To take the sun’s movement through one lodge as a single day is untenable. The distinction between "seven days" and "seven months" has been thoroughly examined by former scholars.

In my humble view: by the tenth month, one yang is stripped away, and from *Shi* to *Kun*, the six yangs are exhausted. By the eleventh month, at the midpoint of *Zi*, one yang is born. Taking yang as primary and counting it with the preceding numbers, it is said: "In seven days, it returns." "Seven days" is thus seven months. Taking yin as primary, it is called "months." From the fifth to the tenth month, six yins fully mature—this is merely six months. Thus, "seven days" is emphasized to manifest the principle of exalting yang.

There are three interpretations of *Fu* (Return), yet the underlying principle is one. Lianxi, focusing on the point of return in *Kun*, says: "It is beneficial to be steadfast." His explanation of sincere return aligns with Wang Bi’s. Yichuan, focusing on the dynamic aspect at the inception of the *Yuan* character, says: "It is the inception of movement," thereby revealing the heart of heaven and earth. Kangjie, addressing the interplay of movement and stillness, says: "Between one movement and one stillness." Zhu Xi remarks that the principle is the same, though the points of reference differ.

Regarding the *Fu* hexagram, the bottom line is precisely where movement occurs. How can one speak of stillness? Observing the image of thunder beneath the earth, Yichuan’s interpretation is correct. Though *Fu* has three divisions, its lines number six. Zhu Xi says: "Heaven and earth have yin, thus there is return; the multitude have evil, thus there is return; the sage has no return." I also say: the multitude have return, the worthy do not stray far from return, and the petty person is deluded by return.

Someone asked Zhu Xi: "Within utter stillness, if a single thought arises, is this return?" He replied: "Your question is inexhaustible. There is return of good and evil, and return of movement and stillness—these two must be clearly distinguished." In my view, the sage’s heart is a companion to creation. Like an infant, it does not lose the undifferentiated principle of heaven. Without interruption, who can perceive its arising and ceasing? This is called "no return."

Day after day, in ceaseless turmoil, the heart of compassion and shame suddenly stirs in a moment of awakening—this is the division of good and evil into yin and yang. Or, when a single thought arises from utter stillness, this is the division of movement and stillness into yin and yang. The human heart is inherently empty, luminous, and unobscured. When stillness reaches its extreme and movement begins, recognizing it as the good heart and expanding it fulfills its capacity. When stillness is found within movement, discerning and suppressing what is not good eradicates evil by its root. Thus, human desires are entirely eliminated, and heavenly principle is ever-present. One may then progress from having return to aspiring to the sage’s state of no return.

Extending this to the six lines of *Fu*: the first nine, "not straying far from return," Confucius assigns to Yanzi. The remaining five lines do not specify individuals, for admonition and warning are subtly conveyed without explicit words—truth is thus revealed. It is said that Zixia, upon hearing criticism, rested in return; Zengzi reflected daily, Boyu yearly—these are frequent returns. Yi’s solitary return, Zhou Tu of Han’s earnest return—these attributions may be questionable. Lu Qi’s deluded return, however, aptly illustrates its peril.
