As he said: "Before the diagram, there was *zi*; after *wu*, there is no change." If numbers are thus, principle is also thus. I say: exhaust principle, fulfill nature, to reach destiny. Principle is primary, yet numbers are within it. Though the Master did not speak of destiny, he said, "If the River does not yield the Diagram, I am done." If numbers are unclear, principle is also obscured. Thus, Master Shao said: "Learning that does not connect Heaven and humanity cannot be called learning." The noble person should attain its utmost.

I say: the numbers of the world arise from principle; violating principle leads to mere calculation. Master Zhu said: "Though Master Kangjie’s methods differ, in essence, they are the hexagrams drawn by Fuxi." The *Records of the Historian* state: "Those who embody the compositions of Heaven and Earth reach their culmination in *The Book of Changes*." The odd lines of Heaven and even lines of Earth, the yang nine and yin six, the 4,096 hexagrams’ changes, the 11,520 counting rods—what more can be added? Thus, the sun, moon, stars, water, fire, earth, and stone exhaust the substance and function of Heaven and Earth. Cold and heat, day and night, rain, wind, dew, and thunder exhaust the transformations of Heaven and Earth. Nature, emotions, forms, bodies, creatures that run, fly, and plants exhaust the responses of the myriad things. The *yuan*, *hui*, *yun*, *shi*, years, months, days, and *chen* exhaust the beginning and end of Heaven and Earth. Emperors, kings, hegemons, *The Book of Changes*, *The Book of Songs*, *The Book of Documents*, and *The Spring and Autumn Annals* exhaust the achievements of sages and worthies. Since the Qin and Han dynasties, there has been only one such master. The essentials of numerical study:

Heaven as one, Earth as two.
Heaven’s odd: one-one; Earth’s even: two-two.
Heaven has no ten; Earth has no one.
One, three, five—three heavens, thus *Qian* uses nine.
Two, four—two earths, thus *Kun* uses six.
One is the body of Heaven’s roundness.

Four is the body of Earth’s squareness.
Heaven’s roundness has a diameter of one and a circumference of three, accumulating to eight, corresponding to the eight directions and four corners.
Earth’s squareness arises from four and accumulates to twelve, each one employing three.
Thus, the four directions divide into twelve stations, and the four seasons divide into twelve months.
Heaven is one and becomes two, with yin and yang proceeding together.
Earth is two and becomes four, with yin and yang splitting into pairs.
Heaven’s roundness begins with one and accumulates to eight; Earth’s squareness begins with four and accumulates to twelve.
The number of the round is odd, so Heaven’s number is one and employs six.
The number of the square is even, so Earth’s number is two and employs twelve.
Heaven transforms the square into the round, ever preserving its oneness.
Earth divides two into four, ever holding to its squareness.
Yang governs advance, so Heaven combines one to make seven.
Yin governs retreat, so Earth removes four to make twelve.
The round begins with one and accumulates to six; the square divides one into four.
The round trims the square for its use, so one transforms into four.
Four minus one leaves three; three transforms into nine; nine minus three leaves six.
The operational number is completed in three and reaches its limit at six.
The square expands the round for its body: one transforms into three and combines with four; four transforms into twelve and combines with sixteen.
The structural number is completed in four and reaches its limit at sixteen.
Heaven governs use, so it conceals one within forty-nine.
That fifty displays the center yet conceals one means the number inherently contains it.
Earth governs structure, so it reveals four beyond sixty.
That sixty hexagrams exclude four externally means the number inherently lacks it.
The sun transforms once every thirty days; the moon transforms once every twelve.
In one year, the sun gains six days; in one year, the moon loses six days.
One solar year has three hundred sixty-six days; one lunar year has three hundred fifty-four days.
Heaven arises from one; Earth is completed in four.
*Qian*’s one line envelops *Kun*’s two lines to make three; *Qian*’s three lines envelop *Kun*’s six lines to make nine.
*Qian*’s one line contains thirty-six yang; *Kun*’s one line contains twenty-four yin, totaling sixty.
*Qian*’s one line: six sixes make thirty-six; six advances by one to seven, so six lines yield two hundred fifty-two.
*Kun*’s one line: four sixes make twenty-four; four retreats to three, yielding three sixes one hundred eight.
Of *Kun* relative to *Qian*, one hundred takes three, so three hundred sixty takes one hundred eight.
Earth’s use lies in Heaven, so it conceals one at the beginning.
Heaven’s body lies in Earth, so it hides four at the end.
Focusing on Heaven’s one, yang advances and yin retreats.
Focusing on Earth’s two, yin and yang alternately advance and retreat.
Heaven is three, Earth is four: Heaven’s one, three, five make nine; five fours make nine.
Earth’s one, two, three make six; two fours make six.
Heaven’s use employs three hundred sixty.
Earth’s structure comprises three hundred eighty-four.
Heaven’s roundness prioritizes use, its body relying on Earth.
Earth’s squareness prioritizes structure, its use following Heaven.
Heaven’s three threes make nine; Earth’s three twos make six.
Heaven doubles three to make six; Earth doubles four to make eight.
Heaven’s generative numbers are one, two, three, four; Earth’s completed numbers are six, seven, eight, nine.
Heaven’s six sixes make thirty-six; Earth’s four sixes make twenty-four.
Heaven divides its three, so the sun has thirty days, advancing to three hundred sixty days.
Earth begins with two, so the moon has twelve months, dividing into twenty-four qi.
Heaven’s operational number is three and reaches its limit at six; Earth’s structural number is four and reaches its limit at sixteen.
Heaven is three, Earth is two; Heaven is seven, Earth is three.
Heaven has three celestial bodies; Earth has four phases.
The precelestial four corners are *Zhen*, *Gen*, *Dui*, *Xun*.
The postcelestial four corners are *Qian*, *Kun*, *Gen*, *Xun*.
Heaven is three, Earth is two—these are the fundamental operational numbers of Heaven and Earth.
Three and two, two and three—these are the mutually employed numbers of *Qian* and *Kun*.
The North Star emerges thirty-six degrees above Earth; the rest remains hidden.
The South Star sinks thirty-six degrees below Earth; the rest remains visible.
Hexagrams employ six lines, totaling three hundred eighty-four lines.
The four positions of the Zhou encompass two hundred fifty-six.
The two sons, with twenty-eight yang, generate *Qian* Father’s thirty-six yang; the three daughters, with twenty-eight yin, generate *Kun* Mother’s thirty-six yin.
*Kun* Mother, with twelve yang, generates each of the three sons’ twenty yang; *Qian* Father, with twelve yin, generates each of the three daughters’ twenty yin.
Within *Kun* twelve yang are concealed; upon reaching *Qian*, they complete thirty-six yang.
Within *Qian* twelve yin are concealed; upon reaching *Kun*, they complete thirty-six yin.
*Qian* totals three hundred sixty-three, dividing its use by two to form the number of opening things.
*Kun*’s four sixes make twenty-four, removing one and retaining three to form the number of closing things.
Heaven’s bell has Earth’s three and two; Earth’s bell has Heaven’s two and three.
*Qian* envelops *Kun*’s even to make three; *Kun* divides the yang number to make six.
*Qian* governs sixteen hexagrams as odd; *Kun* governs twenty-four hexagrams as even.
Yin is two and incomplete; yang is whole at three.
Within *Qian* thirty-six yang are concealed; within *Kun* thirty-six yin are concealed.
*Qian*’s thirty-six yang govern advance, advancing to three hundred sixty.
*Kun*’s twelve yin govern diminution, diminishing twelve days in a year of twelve months.
Yang obtains three for use and advances: six sixes make thirty-six; three hundred sixty hexagrams divide into structural transformations—eight eights make sixty-four, yielding four thousand nine hundred twenty hexagrams.
Thirty-six are Heaven’s operational hexagrams; sixty-four correspond to Earth’s structure.
In the *Precelestial Diagram*, the left is Heaven, three hundred thirty-six parts in extension.
*Zhen*, *Li*, *Dui*, *Qian* extend; *Xun*, *Kan*, *Gen*, *Kun* diminish.
The right is Earth, three hundred thirty-two parts in diminution.
*Xun*, *Gen*, *Kan*, *Kun* extend; *Zhen*, *Li*, *Dui*, *Qian* diminish.
Heaven exceeds Earth, so the sage upholds yang as the odd number—thus Heaven is three, Earth is two.
Earth exceeds Heaven, so the Supreme Ultimate’s initial division begins with the primordial numbers—thus yang is one, yin is two; Heaven is five, Earth is six; Heaven is five, Earth is ten; Heaven is six, Earth is four; Heaven is four, Earth is six.

Six is Heaven’s operational number. Twelve is Earth’s operational number. Heaven’s transformations proceed through six qi in six changes. Earth’s transformations proceed through four bodies in four divisions. Heaven’s number is twenty-five, combined as fifty, corresponding to the tallying numbers. Earth’s number is thirty, combined as sixty, corresponding to the hexagram numbers. Heaven’s six operational numbers belong to yang; Earth’s eight structural numbers belong to yin.

Heaven’s six changes yield six times six, thirty-six periods. Earth’s four changes yield four times four, sixteen positions. Heaven’s number six, each line thirty-six: halved, three times six makes eighteen changes. Five times six makes thirty days; six times six makes three hundred sixty. Earth’s number two, each line twenty-four: halved, two times six makes four times forty-eight lines; six times seventy-two seasonal markers; sixty times six likewise yields three hundred sixty.

Heaven stores its operations in six; Earth manifests its power in eight. Heaven has six changes, with three yielding six. From six to thirty-six, these are Heaven’s six changes. Earth has four directions, with two yielding four. From four to two hundred fifty-six, these are Earth’s six changes. Heaven encompasses Earth, thus employing six changes: one change sixty, six changes three hundred sixty. Earth does not encompass Heaven, thus employing four changes: one change four, two changes sixteen, three changes sixty-four, four changes two hundred fifty-six.

Six as the operational number belongs to yang; eight as the structural number belongs to yin. Six times six constitutes the operational use of the thirty-six hexagrams. Eight times eight constitutes the structural body of the sixty-four hexagrams. Earth follows Heaven in employing five; Heaven follows Earth in employing six.

*Qian* and *Dui* correspond to spring, with fifty-six yang and forty yin. *Kan* and *Gen* correspond to autumn, thus the inverse. *Qian* and *Xun* correspond to summer, with sixty-four yang and thirty-two yin. *Kun* and *Gen* correspond to winter, thus the inverse. *Zhen*, *Kan*, and *Gen* hold six yang and twelve yin; *Xun*, *Li*, and *Dui* hold six yin and twelve yang.

*Qian* governs surplus, completing a year’s three hundred sixty days. *Kun* governs deficiency, contracting by six days to remain whole. Heaven’s number twenty-five, combined as fifty, advances to one hundred. Earth’s number thirty, combined as sixty, advances to one hundred twenty.

*Qian* totals two hundred fifty-two, the operational number of the thirty-six hexagrams. *Kun* totals one hundred eight, the intersecting number of the twenty-four hexagrams. From the fifty tallying numbers, remove one, dispersing it into forty-nine for use. Within use lies structure: beyond the sixty hexagrams, four remain—four as the body of the sixty hexagrams. Within structure lies use: Heaven seven, Earth eight. Heaven employs seven; Earth’s body is eight.

Hexagrams with six times six belong to use, attributed to yang lines, transforming into eight. Eight belongs to structure, attributed to yin hexagrams, opening things through *Qian*’s operation. In the eighth month, things close through *Kun*’s operation, dividing into three parts.

Heaven nine, Earth ten. Heaven’s five and four make nine; three times three makes nine; nine times nine makes eighty-one; four times nine makes thirty-six. From *zi* to *si*, six to nine. From *wu* to *hai*, nine to six.

*Qian*’s number is nine, yet Heaven employs six—nine grows from six. *Kun*’s number is six, yet Earth’s body is four—six arises from four. *Qian* employs nine: three times eight makes nine, yielding two hundred sixteen. Two times eight makes nine, likewise yielding *Kun*’s one hundred forty-four. *Kun* employs six: two times twelve makes six, yielding one hundred forty-four. Three times twelve makes six, likewise yielding *Qian*’s two hundred sixteen.

Heaven culminates in nine, Earth exhausts in ten. Ninety marks the beginning and end of Heaven and Earth. The numbers of the eight trigrams total thirty-six. One and eight, two and seven, three and six, four and five—all intersecting numbers are nine. To speak of ten is merely nine’s counterpart. Yang culminates in nine; yin ends in ten.

Heaven’s ten arises from one becoming two, two and five making ten. Earth’s ten arises from two becoming four, two and eight making ten. Fifty takes one as its root, forty-nine as its use. Sixty-four takes four as its root, sixty as its use.

Here concludes the sixteenth scroll of *Heaven’s Original Unfolding of Subtleties*.

Here begins the seventeenth scroll of *Heaven’s Original Unfolding of Subtleties*, compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai, collated by Fang Hui Wuli of Xugu.

---

The blade, though sharp, cannot sever itself; the finger, though nimble, cannot touch itself. Thus, the Dao of Heaven and Earth, vast and boundless, cannot be fully grasped by the mind alone. Only when one attains stillness and emptiness does the spirit naturally abide within. The *Taiji* generates the Two Modes; the Two Modes give rise to the Four Images; the Four Images produce the Eight Trigrams. The Eight Trigrams govern the myriad things, each in its proper place.

The numbers of Heaven and Earth unite to form one thousand two hundred. The numbers of the Three Powers combine to make one hundred eighty. The numbers of the Four Seasons total seventy-two. The numbers of the Five Phases multiply to thirty-six. The numbers of the Six Directions yield one hundred twenty. The numbers of the Seven Regulators amount to eighty-four. The numbers of the Eight Winds sum to sixty. The numbers of the Nine Palaces reach one hundred forty-four.

Three times three makes nine; nine times nine yields eighty-one. Three times four makes twelve; twelve times twelve yields one hundred forty-four. Three times five makes fifteen; fifteen times fifteen yields two hundred twenty-five. Three times six makes eighteen; eighteen times eighteen yields three hundred twenty-four. Three times seven makes twenty-one; twenty-one times twenty-one yields four hundred forty-one. Three times eight makes twenty-four; twenty-four times twenty-four yields five hundred seventy-six. Three times nine makes twenty-seven; twenty-seven times twenty-seven yields seven hundred twenty-nine.

The Great Yang ascends, the Great Yin descends. Yang advances, yin retreats. The *Taiji* divides into the Three Primordials. The Three Primordials generate the Three Heavens. The Three Heavens give rise to the Three Qi. The Three Qi produce the Three Essences. The Three Essences form the Three Origins. The Three Origins complete the Three Powers.

The numbers of the Three Powers total one hundred eighty. The numbers of the Three Heavens amount to nine thousand. The Three Heavens contain the Three Qi; the Three Qi embrace the Three Essences. The Three Essences unite to form the Three Origins. The Three Origins combine to yield the Three Powers. The Three Powers harmonize to generate the myriad things.

One times one makes one; one times two makes two; one times three makes three. Two times two makes four; two times three makes six. Three times three makes nine. Six times nine yields fifty-four. Nine times nine yields eighty-one. Eighty-one times eight yields six hundred forty-eight. Six hundred forty-eight times one hundred forty-four yields ninety-three thousand three hundred twelve.

The Spirits and Gods

The *Book of Changes* (*Yijing*) reveals the mysteries of Heaven, Earth, spirits, and gods. It begins with the phrase on the obscure and the manifest, life and death, then proceeds to discuss spirits and gods. After Confucius’ commentary on the *Changes*, from Yan [Hui], Zeng [zi], Zisi, and Mencius down to the several masters of the Zhou [Dunyi], Cheng [brothers], Zhang [Zai], and Zhu [Xi], the teachings diverged. The two traditions of Śākya and Laozi departed from the creative transformations of Heaven and Earth, presenting an entirely different view. Buddhism uses the fear of spirits to guide people, urging them to take refuge so as not to fall into the cycle of rebirth. Daoism employs transmission to entice people, promising that through cultivation one may attain longevity. Descending further into世俗俗 practices, people carve statues from wood and earth to enshrine them, or invoke spirits through the shamanic cries of mediums. Is this all there is? Who can truly fathom the traces of the creative transformations? Alas, how lamentable!

Master Cai Xie said: Spirits and gods are the wondrous workings within Heaven and Earth. One must take them as a subject for contemplation and enter into reflection to grasp them. The *Great Commentary* of the *Book of Changes* states: "Looking up, one observes the patterns of Heaven; looking down, one examines the principles of Earth. Thus one knows the causes of the obscure and the manifest. Tracing things to their origins and returning to their ends, one knows the explanations of life and death. Essence and qi become things; wandering souls undergo transformation. Thus one knows the conditions and forms of spirits and gods." This is to apply the principles of yin and yang from the *Changes* to observation.

The patterns of Heaven pertain to yang and thus are manifest; the principles of Earth pertain to yin and thus are obscure. The sun, moon, and stars are manifest, yet in the vast and distant reaches, there is also obscurity. Descending into the Yellow Springs, all is obscure, yet in the sprouting and flourishing of life, there is also manifestation. Coming forth from the origin pertains to yang, hence it is called life. Returning to the end pertains to yin, hence it is called death. Human life is measured by a span of a hundred years. If one preserves and nurtures properly, even in old age, the yang may remain robust. If not—even in one’s prime, one may decline. Thus, when yang is dominant, yang recedes and yin diminishes; the vitality of life overflows, the bent straightens, the withered flourishes, and in the radiance of wind and moon, all is harmonious and abundant—here one may perceive the conditions of the spirit. When yin is dominant, yin advances and yang recedes; the vitality of life contracts, the straight bends, the flourishing withers, and like a waning moon or a chilling wind, when the yang qi is exhausted, death ensues—here one may perceive the conditions of the ghost.

Between Heaven and Earth, yang is solely concerned with generation. If there were no yin to bring about death, there would be life without end, and the creative transformations would nearly cease. Therefore, by examining the obscure and the manifest, one may know life and death. By examining life and death, one may know spirits and gods. One qi assumes myriad forms; one breath spans past and present. Comprehending the Dao of day and night, one may understand the creative transformations of Heaven and Earth, whose inexhaustible source lies herein. Is this not the vital qi of the Great Ultimate? Alas! How subtle it is!

Someone asked: *"The* Book of Changes *speaks of Heaven and Earth, sun and moon, and the four seasons, yet concludes with ghosts and spirits—this refers to the contraction and expansion of the two qi. Master Zhou speaks of Heaven, Earth, and humanity, yet concludes with life and death—this refers to the gathering and dispersal of the single qi within humans. But can Heaven and Earth not be spoken of in terms of life and death?"*

I reply: *"Heaven and Earth are their forms; life and death are their qi. Humans receive the qi of Heaven and Earth to live. The yang soul belongs to Heaven; the yin corporeal soul belongs to Earth. In death, the soul’s qi returns to Heaven, while the body and corporeal soul descend to Earth, reverting to the Great Origin as before. Thus, by examining human life and death, one may encompass the day and night of Heaven and Earth. Within this are the waxing and waning of the sun and moon, the transformations of the four seasons, the contraction and expansion of ghosts and spirits—who can fathom their bounds?"*

Master Cheng said: *"The* Book of Changes *speaks of ghosts and spirits as the workings of creation. Tracing spring to its origin, winter must follow; taking winter as the end, spring must return. Life and death are of the same kind. To know the Way of life is to know the Way of death; to know the Way of serving humans is to know the Way of serving spirits. Life and death, humans and ghosts—one yet two, two yet one."*

Master Zhu said: *"Ghosts and spirits are inherently difficult to comprehend. One should focus on the essentials: how much principle governs human life! From the moment we receive the nature of the Five Constants, there are the bonds of father and child, the righteousness of ruler and minister. Each of these principles of life must be understood, and then the principles of death will also be known. In serving one’s ruler or parents, one fulfills what is proper. Having exhausted the Way of sincerity and reverence, one may then turn this mind to serving ghosts and spirits—thus, in sacrifice, the departed are present; in worship, the spirits are present. This requires grasping the principle without deficiency. At the time of death, when the principle of life is exhausted, one rests in death without regret."*

Thus, Master Zhang said: *"Preserve what is right in life; in death, find peace."*

The Confucians hold that principle is neither born nor destroyed, while the Buddhists claim that consciousness is neither born nor destroyed. The sages do not speak of what comes after death—what more need be said? They only teach that between birth and death, one must attend to life with utmost care. The Six Classics record the deeds of the sages and worthies in full, yet say nothing of the threshold of life and death, for they regard it as an ordinary matter. Only the *Records* and the *Analects of Lu* provide detailed accounts of Master Zeng’s final illness, merely to instruct scholars in preserving the body and upholding principle. How could they emulate the Buddhists, who, ignorant of principle, regard sitting death or standing transcendence as marvels?

Hu Mingshong said: *"Humans are creatures of life, yet the Buddha speaks only of death. Human affairs are visible, yet the Buddha speaks only of the hidden. Master Hengqu’s view that ‘when form dissolves, it returns to the Origin’ means that humans receive this qi to live, and in death, return to the Great Origin. Thus, when a person dies, their qi disperses. The qi within the Great Origin then emerges anew to give life to others."*

He also said: *"Heaven and Earth are the substance; ghosts and spirits are their function. Heaven and Earth are spoken of in their entirety, while ghosts and spirits refer to their dynamic transformations, permeating above and below. Rain, wind, dew, thunder, and plants—all are understood by analogy. Spring and summer are spirit; autumn and winter are ghost. Day is spirit; night is ghost. Before noon is spirit; after noon is ghost. Inhalation is spirit; exhalation is ghost. Life is spirit; death is ghost. Speaking is spirit; silence is ghost. Extension is spirit; contraction is ghost. Qi coming forth is spirit; its return is ghost. The sun is spirit; the moon is ghost. After the third day is spirit; after the sixteenth day is ghost. Heaven’s creation is spirit; Earth’s transformation is ghost. Plants sprouting is spirit; withering is ghost. Human youth and vigor are spirit; decline and old age are ghost. Wind and thunder stirring are spirit; their gathering is ghost. The onset of wind, rain, thunder, and lightning is spirit; their cessation is ghost."*

Master Zhang said: *"The Great Void cannot be without qi. Qi cannot but gather to form the myriad things, nor can the myriad things but disperse back into the Great Void. This cycle of exit and entry is inevitable. As qi, when dispersed, enters the formless, it perfectly attains our body; when gathered, it takes shape without losing its constancy. Gathered, it is our body; dispersed, it is still our body. Knowing that life and death do not perish—one may then speak of nature!"*

Master Zhu said: *"Nature is simply principle and cannot be spoken of in terms of gathering and dispersal. What gathers to give life and disperses in death is merely qi. The so-called spirit, soul, consciousness, and awareness are all the workings of qi. Thus, when gathered, they exist; when dispersed, they vanish. But principle is never absent due to gathering or dispersal. Where there is principle, there is qi. If qi gathers here, principle also resides here—it cannot be compared to ice and foam. Ghosts and spirits are the spirit, soul, consciousness, and corporeal soul—qi, not nature. Thus, the rites of sacrifice operate through resonance by kind. If nature were involved, how could there be such resonance? Yet the qi that has already dispersed is gone without a trace. That which is rooted in principle and daily renewed remains vast and inexhaustible. Therefore, the sages’ sacrifices—setting up ancestral tablets, employing impersonators, offering mugwort and millet, libations and purifications—whether seeking in the yin or the yang, spare no effort, stopping only at the utmost. They say, ‘Perhaps they will partake.’ The profound sincerity, compassion, subtlety, and elusive intent therein defy expression—such things cannot be sought with the shallow knowledge and rigid views of the common world."*

How can it be said that upon receiving one’s formed body, this nature thereby becomes one’s own possession, enduring even after death without annihilation, standing alone as a distinct entity hidden in stillness, awaiting the sacrifices and prayers of others to be summoned forth and partake of offerings? If this were so, then the boundaries of its scope and the place of its abode would indeed be definable. Yet from the beginning of creation until now, the accumulated dead would have piled up without end, leaving no space to contain them. How could such a principle exist?

Moreover, the creative transformations of Heaven and Earth resemble a great furnace. The ceaseless generation of beings knows no rest—this is the true principle of reality, unconcerned with annihilation. Now to fix one’s gaze upon a vast, empty stillness and instead regard the consciousness of the deceased as the true principle of reality—is this not a grave error?

What the sages and worthies mean by "returning to wholeness" and "resting in death" is simply that one has not lost one’s proper place. Having received the principle from Heaven, one may die without shame. It is not that there exists some tangible entity to be preserved and returned, thereby ensuring that one’s indestructible and imperishable self may repose in tranquil solitude. Whether one dies young or lives long, one remains unwavering, cultivating the self and awaiting one’s end—this is a state of being without contrivance. It cannot be equated with the heterodox view that makes much of life and death, warning of impermanence’s swift approach, and thus misleads scholars.

Master Cheng said: "Ghosts and spirits are but the workings of creation. Heaven is honored, Earth is lowly—thus the positions of Qian and Kun are established. They are stirred by thunder and nourished by wind and rain." This explains that in the manifest there are rites and music, and in the hidden there are ghosts and spirits. Master Zhu said this refers to the contrast between the hidden and the manifest. To claim that the hidden contains ghosts and spirits while the manifest does not is to misconstrue the subject, failing to understand what ghosts and spirits truly are and becoming mired in doctrines of rebirth and karma.

How could one not see that within rites and music there are ghosts and spirits, and within ghosts and spirits there are rites and music? The two are one and the same; one must only distinguish their boundaries and differentiate them. Heaven and Earth take their positions, distinguished by the act of treading—this is ritual. Yet within the interaction of the two qi, there is never an absence of music. The stirring of wind and thunder is music. Yet when the wind subsides and the rain ceases, when thunder stills and lightning fades, each follows its proper order—within this, there is never an absence of ritual.

Ritual restrains human emotions, for qi contracts. Harmony is prized, and what contracts will again expand. Music stirs human emotions, for qi is spirit-like. Yet its movements and rests are measured, and what expands will again contract. The hidden and the manifest communicate; contraction and expansion alternate, and there is nowhere they do not commune with ghosts and spirits.

The Buddhists, however, regard death and killing as settled matters, claiming that the manifest becomes human and the hidden becomes ghost. How could they know that the noble person’s vigilance in solitude makes even the hidden corners of a room feel as though the divine is present? Ritual binds the sinews and body, while music nurtures the nature and emotions. The rites and music within oneself are the very presence of ghosts and spirits above.

The *Commentary on the Book of Changes* states: "The essence of qi refers to the seventh and eighth hexagrams, speaking of the spirits of wood and fire that generate beings in the southeast. The wandering soul refers to the ninth and sixth hexagrams, speaking of the spirits of metal and water that end beings in the northwest." The old yin and old yang, when contracted, become ghosts. The young yin and young yang, when expanded, become spirits. The southeast is the manifest; the northwest is the hidden. This does not mean that ghosts and spirits exist only in the darkness of Heaven and Earth while the manifest is devoid of them.

In later ages, rites decayed and music was abandoned; human hearts became frivolous and deceitful. Having lost their order and harmony, their communion with ghosts and spirits no longer followed the proper way. If one does not revere them, they become distant—how could there be any principle of resonance and response?

Master Zhang said: "Ghosts and spirits are the innate capacities of the two qi." Master Zhu said: "Master Cheng’s explanation of ghosts and spirits as the pathways of creation is fine, but it remains too vague. Master Zhang’s account is clearer, revealing the presence of yin and yang, contraction and expansion, and their ceaseless alternation."

In my humble opinion, the terms "yin" and "yang" alone cannot fully express ghosts and spirits. The numinous aspect of yin is called ghost; the numinous aspect of yang is called spirit. Their innate capacity is precisely their numinosity, enabling them to contract, expand, and transform.

Master Zhang further said: "At the beginning of a thing’s life, qi arrives daily and flourishes. Once the thing reaches full growth, qi daily returns and disperses. The arrival is called spirit, for it expands. The return is called ghost, for it withdraws." He also said: "At the beginning of a person’s life, the qi of Heaven and Earth continually accumulates, gradually growing and expanding within the body. Upon reaching its peak, it gradually declines and dissipates. Yet all this occurs naturally, not by any external agency—hence it is called innate capacity."

Master Shao said: "Yin is the shadow of yang; ghosts are the shadow of humans; the moon is the shadow of the sun; emotions are the shadow of nature. Yang, humans, the sun, and nature are all the masters of yin, ghosts, the moon, and emotions. Once the master is established, the shadow naturally follows. At the winter solstice, the first yang is born, corresponding to the birth of the first yin at the summer solstice. From the winter solstice to the sixth month, the six yangs are complete; from the summer solstice to the twelfth month, the six yins are complete. One yang in the heavens corresponds to one yin on earth; one thing in the water corresponds to one thing on the shore—this is true of all things. Yet yang is always the master, and yin is always the shadow, like the male and female. A day has twelve hours, and a year has twelve months. At dawn, the moon begins to brighten; at the full moon, its light is complete. At the first and last quarters, it begins to wane, and by the end of the month, it is dark. From this, one may perceive the conditions of ghosts and spirits.

Within nature lies the principle of benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom, and externally, it casts the shadow of compassion, shame, deference, and a sense of right and wrong. Every form that emerges casts a shadow. The human soul becomes spirit, and within it, the shadow of the spirit becomes the ghost’s corporeal soul."

When the sun reaches its darkest point, the moon loses its light; when a person grows old, the spirit becomes weary and restless.

It is also said: Before thoughts and considerations arise, ghosts and spirits do not yet know them. If not by me, then by whom? The practice of the Dao should reach a point where even ghosts and spirits cannot perceive it. Good and evil take form in speech and manifest in action, at which point others begin to know them. Yet as soon as they sprout in the heart and emerge in thought, ghosts and spirits already know them. *The Doctrine of the Mean* states: "The noble person is cautious and reverent in what is unseen, fearful in what is unheard." This is indeed the noble person’s discipline of vigilance in solitude, ensuring no shame even in the hidden corners of the house. Yet when it speaks of thoughts not yet arisen, it refers to the time before joy, anger, sorrow, and pleasure are expressed—ghosts and spirits cannot glimpse that boundary. Thus it is said: If not by me, then by whom?

In the depths of obscurity and the subtlest of matters, though not yet manifest, the incipient movement has already stirred. Though known only to oneself and unseen by others, ghosts and spirits already know it. Therefore, the noble person is vigilant in solitude, not waiting for words or deeds to take shape. Ever mindful of ghosts and spirits hovering above, they dare not harbor even the slightest reckless thought within. Once, a man played the zither and saw a mantis hunting a cicada; those who heard it perceived a killing intent in the music. The killing intent was in the heart, yet others heard it in the zither—how much more so for ghosts and spirits? In this, the noble person cultivates only reverence to keep the inner self upright. They listen to the soundless, see the formless, and thus do not wait for signs in countenance or utterance before exerting effort.

Shao [Yong] also said: The spirit of the human heart is the spirit of Heaven and Earth. To deceive one’s own heart is to deceive Heaven itself. How can one not be vigilant? Heaven and Earth are empty and luminous, needing neither ears nor eyes to see and hear all.

It is further said: When qi and form flourish, the hun and po flourish; when qi and form decline, the hun and po also decline. The hun transforms with qi, while the po ascends with form. Thus, as long as form exists, the po remains; when form dissolves, the po scatters. When the qi of the ears, eyes, mouth, nose, heart, spleen, gallbladder, and kidneys is whole, it is called a person. The numinous spirit of the heart is called *shen*; the numinous spirit of the gallbladder is called *po*; the numinous spirit of the spleen is called *hun*; the numinous essence of the kidneys is called *jing*. The spirit of the heart manifests in the eyes as sight; the essence of the kidneys manifests in the ears as hearing; the hun of the spleen manifests in the nose as smell; the po of the gallbladder manifests in the mouth as speech. Only when these eight are complete is one called a person. This explains the inner workings of the human form, refining the hun and po to gain understanding.

The human body possesses the numinous principle of Heaven, Earth, ghosts, and spirits, yet fails to reflect inwardly. Instead, it spends its days chasing externals, reaching white-haired old age and death without self-awareness—how lamentable! Metal and wood mark the beginning and end of generation; in humans, they correspond to spirit, essence, hun, and po. Essence forms the body, and the yin po clings to it; qi forms the hun, and the yang shen depends on it. These four cannot be separated, which is why the hun and po wax and wane with qi and form. When form changes, the yang hun departs; when the po dissolves, the yin form decays. Thus, the ancient kings regarded dismemberment and incineration as the ultimate punishment, and for the dead, they divined burial sites to ensure peaceful rest.

The kidneys belong to the north, where Heaven’s One Water resides, hence they store essence. Essence first transforms into po, and the po is the source of essence—it is the servant of essence and qi, accompanying their ingress and egress. Water generates wood, and wood is its child, so the gallbladder stores po. The heart belongs to the south, where the Great Void’s fire governs clarity, storing shen. The yang hun is born of shen, and the hun is the attendant of shen and qi, following their ingress and egress. Fire generates earth, and earth is its child, so the spleen stores hun.

In the human body, spirit and essence are the masters, while hun and po are their agents. When essence flourishes, the po flourishes. Only utmost sincerity can generate essence, and utmost essence can generate spirit. Sincerity is what Heaven’s One produces without falsity. If one can focus on the One and not dissipate their essence, they attain sincerity like a spirit. The heart can govern qi but cannot master the One. If it dissipates essence, the heart becomes enslaved by form. How then is one different from beasts, not merely dying without a name?

Human life arises from spirit, essence, hun, and po—the functions of nature—and from blood, qi, water, and grain—the functions of form. Only through mutual nourishment of inner and outer can spirit and essence grow strong, and hun and po flourish. Nature is received from Heaven and must have a repository. The heart stores shen, the kidneys store essence, the spleen stores hun, and the gallbladder stores po. The heart governs these repositories. Thus, when it manifests in voice, scent, speech, and sight without violating principle, it is called numinous. Form is endowed by Earth and must have a storehouse. The lungs are the storehouse for transmitting qi; the liver is the storehouse for transmitting blood; the stomach is the storehouse for transforming water and grain, while the intestines and bladder separate the clear and turbid. Thus it is said: "Of the natures endowed by Heaven and Earth, humans are the most noble"—how could it be like the heterodox teachings on hun and po?

Once, a student of immortality told me: "Begin with the dragon, tiger, quail, and tortoise." He also said: "Begin with the heart and kidneys." And again: "Seek within the Five Agents of the Five Viscera," yet he never revealed the ultimate secret. Later, I obtained the essential method, and the starting point was quite simple. It is simply a matter of focused effort—yet today, people know but do not practice.

Master Zhu [Xi] and Cai Xishan explored this their whole lives and deeply understood it. They once said: Daoists favor shortcuts, using terms like lead, mercury, and the Mysterious Female, exchanging names to confound people. In truth, it is merely essence and qi. Qu Yuan’s *Chu Ci* contains the theory of "illuminating the po."

Speaking in terms of essence and spirit, "illuminating" (荧) means "to nourish" (营), the gathering of yin numinosity that manifests as luminosity. If the *po* does not receive the *hun*, then the *hun* cannot bear the *po*, and the person dies. Moreover, the *hun* is active while the *po* is tranquil; the *hun* is fire and two, while the *po* is water and one. To "bear and nourish the *po*" is to apply the *hun* to the *po*, to use movement to guard stillness, to use fire to press upon water, and to use two to guard one. It is like a person mounting a chariot and being carried upon it—then the *hun* is at peace and the *po* is clear and bright. Fire does not scorch, and water does not overflow; this is the essential method for long life and enduring vision. Yet this still does not explain the *how* of it.

Master Zhu [Xi] has a poem that says:
*"Thieves unlock the secret of life’s mystery,
Stealing past the gate of birth and death."*
And again:
*"Yet fear defying Heaven’s Way,
Can stolen life ever be at peace?"*
Thus, he too knew but did not practice.

Master Zhu said: *Essence* and *qi* are terms used in relation to things; *hun* and *po* are terms used in relation to humans; *ghosts* and *spirits* are terms used when speaking of them apart from humans. While alive, they are called *essence* and *qi*; after death, they are called *hun* and *po*; in things, they are called *ghosts* and *spirits*. *Qi* is substantial; *hun* and *po* are half-substantial, half-insubstantial; *ghosts* and *spirits* are mostly insubstantial with only a little substance.

He also said: When the two—essence and *qi*—unite, *hun* and *po* gather to form a person. When the wandering *hun* departs alone, the yang has already dispersed, and the yin has nowhere to return, thus becoming transformation. Zichan said: "At the beginning of human life, the first transformation is called *po*; once the *po* is born, the yang is called *hun*." The Tang scholar Kong said: "When a person is born, the first transformation is into form. The numinosity of form is called *po*. Within the *po* there is naturally yang *qi*. The spirit of *qi* is called *ling*—these are the names for *hun*, *po*, spirit, and numinosity. At birth, the movement of ears, eyes, heart, perception, hands, and feet is the numinosity of the *po*. As essence and spirit, nature and perception gradually develop awareness, this is the spirit of *qi*."

Moreover, the *hun* is yang and belongs to fire; the *po* is yin and belongs to water. "Heavenly One generates water"—yin and yang first interact. Once the *po* is born, warmth gives rise to the *hun*; the *po* comes first, then the *hun*. The *hun* is always the master, the trunk. *The Huainanzi* says: "Heavenly *qi* is the *hun*, the yang spirit; earthly *qi* is the *po*, the yin spirit." Yue Qi said: "The clarity and sharpness of the heart are called *hun* and *po*." The *po* belongs to the physical form; the *hun* belongs to the spirit. *Essence* is again the *po*; the *po* is the spirit of essence. *Spirit* is again the *hun*; the *hun* is the spirit of *qi*."

Master Zhu said: The *hun* is spirit-like and the *po* is numinous; the *hun* is yang and the *po* is yin; the *hun* is active and the *po* is tranquil. While alive, the *hun* is borne by the *po*, and the *po* restrains the *hun*. After death, the *hun* scatters and returns to Heaven, while the *po* sinks and returns to Earth. That which moves and acts is the *hun*; that which does not move or act is the *po*. When the *po* is strong, the ears and eyes are keen, and memory is sharp. When the elderly grow dim-sighted, hard of hearing, and forgetful, it is because the *po* has weakened.

If the *hun* is fiery and the *po* is cool, and one can use the *hun* to guard the *po*, then the *hun* has something to guard and becomes tranquil, while the *po* is warmed by the *hun* and gains vitality. The heat of the *hun* generates coolness; the coolness of the *po* generates warmth. Only when the two do not separate can yang not scorch and yin not stagnate, achieving harmony. Otherwise, the more the *hun* moves, the more the *po* becomes still; the hotter the *hun*, the cooler the *po*. When the two fail to harmonize, death follows.

He also said: While a person lives, *hun* and *po* interact; after death, they separate. The *po* has a form within, like a crystal’s ancestor, and thus manifests as the clarity of ears and eyes. The dark halo of the moon is the *po*; its light is the *hun*. It is like incense: the sap that exudes when burned is the *po*; the fragrant smoke that rises afterward is the *hun*. The *hun* is the flame of the *po*; the *po* is the root of the *hun*. Fire is the *hun*; the mirror is the *po*. A lamp has flame—when an object approaches, it burns. A mirror reflects, yet remains within. Fire and the sun are external manifestations; metal and water are internal manifestations. Fire and the sun are the *hun*; metal and water are the *po*. The light of a person’s eyes is the *po*; the ears are likewise. Why is it the *po*? Because the ability to hear is the *po*. The nose knows scents; the tongue knows tastes—all are the same. Yet one cannot take the ability to recognize words as the *po*, for recognition belongs to the heart. Whether sweet, salty, or sour, it must pass through the tongue.

Yin governs storage and reception, so the *po* can remember. Yang governs movement within, so the *hun* can manifest outwardly. These two are never truly separate. When essence gathers, the *po* gathers; when *qi* gathers, the *hun* gathers—this is the basis of human and other beings. When essence is exhausted and the *po* descends, *qi* scatters and the *hun* wanders, and there is no more awareness.

Speaking of the human body, though *qi* belongs to yang, the physical form of the *po* already belongs to yin—within life is already the principle of death. Though transformation belongs to yang, the *hun* ascends while the physical form of the *po* descends, thus possessing yin and yang. Even now, the living are half spirit and half ghost. Before death, spirit is master; after death, ghost is master.

In the *Liyun*, Zai Wo said: "I have heard the names *ghosts* and *spirits*, but do not know what they mean." The Master said: "*Qi* is the fullness of spirit; *po* is the fullness of ghost. The teaching on ghosts and spirits is the utmost." The *Jiaotesheng* says: "The *hun*-*qi* returns to Heaven because the *hun* originally attaches to *qi*; when a person dies, *qi* must rise." It also says: "The physical *po* descends to Earth because the *po* originally belongs to form; when a person dies, form returns to the soil."

The sages, in managing the affairs of the living and the dead, established sacrificial rites. Since existence and nonexistence differ, they created distinct names. They changed the name of the living spirit to *hun* and the name of the living ghost to *po*, combining *hun* and *po* to name them *ghosts and spirits*, honoring them in ritual. Thus it is said: "Illuminating the Mandate of ghosts and spirits to instruct the common people."

Then the myriad masses stand in awe, and the ten thousand people submit in reverence.

Yanling Jizi, mourning his son, said: "Flesh and bone return to the earth—such is fate. But as for the *hun*, there is nowhere it does not go." The *Erya*’s *Shiwen* states: "The word *ghost* means *return*, for flesh and bone are hidden away in the earth. Its *qi*, however, rises and soars above—this is what we call the unfathomable, the *spirit*." In truth, the root of ghosts and spirits lies in *hun* and *po*.

The fullness of *spirit* refers to the functions of breathing through mouth and nose, awareness, and movement. The fullness of *ghost* refers to the clarity of ears and eyes, the ability to see and hear, and the vigor of essence and blood.

Someone asked about the theory of life and death. Master Xie said: "It is the exhaustion of *qi*." The questioner asked: "Are there ghosts and spirits?" He replied: "I once asked Master Mingdao: 'If I tell you there are none, how will you believe it? But if I tell you there are, you must go and seek them out.' That is the answer."

Master Zhu said: "Master Shangcai’s explanation of ghosts and spirits is excellent. He said: 'What is fitting draws people to emulate it, not to die for it. What is fitting—such as offering sacrifices to ancestors and parents—requires utmost sincerity to move and reach them. One must not treat it as a matter of life and death. What is unfitting makes people distance themselves; one must not treat it as a living concern, nor ignore it entirely, lest it vanish.'"

When asked about the purpose of sacrificial offerings, he said: "It is their intention—fasting for three days, abstaining for five, seeking in the realms of yin and yang, the four directions, and above and below. The aim is to gather one’s own spirit, thereby reaching the ancestral temple and summoning the spirits." Yet though this is so, to insist on their existence is wrong, and to deny it is equally wrong. Within this lies a subtle principle, in the realm of "as if present, as if absent." Only by resolving this can one proceed correctly. He said: "It is not a matter of confusion—if you will it to be, it is; if you will it not to be, it is not. Only then do you grasp that ghosts and spirits, though unseen in the void, manifest everywhere, for they are the wondrous workings of Heaven and Earth."

He also said: "When yin and yang unite, they become *spirit*; when form and *qi* part, there are *ghosts*. To know this is wisdom; to serve them is benevolence. Fasting and abstinence are merely means to gather one’s own spirit. The ancients used a *shi* (representative) to invite the union of yin and yang, drawing their *qi* to that person—this was not done in vain. When sacrificial sincerity and reverence are complete, *qi* naturally gathers. Thus, in ancient sacrifices, the spirits were summoned and came."

When asked, "If ancestors are already dead, how do they come?" he replied: "As Master Shangcai said: 'The spirit of ancestors is the same as our own spirit.' The response in sacrifice may be sought in yin or in yang, each according to its kind. Seek, and all will come. It is not that some entity lingers in the void, awaiting the arrival of descendants. Rather, the presiding sacrificer, being of the same *qi* in continuous flow, already harbors that *qi*. By exhausting sincerity and reverence, one moves and reaches them."

Someone asked about distant and collateral relatives. He said: "They all flow from the same source, originally without interruption. Though a person dies and *hun* and *po* scatter—*po* being more settled—one must summon the *hun* to return, so that it may reunite with the *po*. The sages taught descendants to perform regular sacrifices to gather the *qi* of their ancestors. Though it may seem scattered in the moment, its root remains here. When sincerity and reverence are complete, that *qi* is drawn and gathered here. The descendant’s body is here, so the ancestor’s *qi* is here as well. There is a bloodline that runs through, which is why spirits do not respond to those outside their kind, and people do not sacrifice to those outside their clan—because their *qi* is not connected."

Master Zhu said: "The contraction and expansion, coming and going of ghosts and spirits are merely *qi*. Human *qi* constantly interacts with the *qi* of Heaven and Earth, though people do not perceive it. When the human heart stirs, it reaches the *qi*, which then resonates with this contraction and expansion. As the earlier scholars said: 'Where there is principle, there is the *qi* of yin and yang.' This *qi* permeates even the finest hair or thread. When this heart stirs, that *qi* responds. Where the numinous light of ghosts and spirits shines, it is radiant; where their *qi* rises, it is like steam; where it stirs one’s spirit, the wind is solemn and mournful. Thus, the *Sacrificial Rites* speak of seeking in yang to repay *qi*—*qi* is *spirit*. Hence, the establishment of ancestral temples, the burning of offerings at the grave, the lighting of torches, and the use of fragrant herbs to make the *qi* ascend—all belong to the yang category. There is also seeking in yin to repay the *po*—*po* is *ghost*. Thus, offerings of millet, sacrificial meats, liver, lungs, heads, and hearts, along with *yu* and *kuai* (sacrificial wines), are poured onto the earth to seek them. The smoke rising from split wood is the *qi* of moisture—this is *po*. Uniting *hun* and *po* as one is how one seeks the *qi* of ghosts and spirits in sacrifice."

In my humble opinion: Breathing is the *qi* of our body; clouds and rain are the *qi* of mountains and rivers. Rising above is the *qi* of numinous light and brightness. When it moves here, it responds there. From the first generation of humanity, the ancestors of *qi*-transformation, it has been passed down to this day. Descendants, ancestors, Heaven and Earth, mountains and rivers—all are connected by a single *qi*. The earlier scholars said: "Though the *hun*-*qi* of the dead scatters, setting up a spirit tablet to preside over it requires gathering some of that *qi* here." In ancient times, from the moment of death, they would summon the *hun*, restore the *po*, and establish multiple spirit tablets—always seeking to maintain a connection to that spirit. The ancients used the blood of sacrificial animals to anoint tortoise shells, for when the shell lost its numinosity, they would use this living *qi* to reconnect it.

Master Cheng said: "The great mountains and rivers that bring forth clouds and rain do so simply because *qi* steams upward."

Qi is spirit. People today do not understand this principle; at the first sign of flood or drought, they rush to temples to pray, unaware of where rain and dew originate. The great mountains and rivers can summon clouds and bring rain—why not inquire of them? How can one seek this from wooden or clay effigies?

Someone asked Master Zhu: "When people pray to Heaven and Earth, mountains and rivers, is it that my *qi* moves and their *qi* responds? When descendants sacrifice to ancestors, is it that my *qi* moves and their *qi* responds?" Without the numinous *qi* of spirits, it constantly expands and contracts without cease. The *qi* of humans and ghosts, however, dissipates without remainder—though the speed of dissipation varies. Yet if one’s own *qi* connects with theirs, then through utmost sincerity and reverence, as Master Shangcai said, "Where there is intent, there is presence."

Master Cheng said: "By its functional operation, it is called *gui*; by its wondrous operation, it is called *shen*." He also said: "Ghosts and spirits lie at the edge of creation and transformation." The heat of the sun and cold of the moon, the brightness of day and darkness of night, the growth of spring and the withering of autumn, the flourishing of summer and the storage of winter—all that generates and completes the myriad things are the functional operations of ghosts and spirits, manifest and visible. He further said: "The wondrous operation that cannot be seen—perceiving without form, hearing without sound, embodying things yet leaving no trace. The Dao is neither existent nor nonexistent; it is both present and absent. Suddenly, in an instant, it transforms and changes. In the emptiness of space, there is thunder and wind, rain and lightning. On the branches of trees, flowers and leaves suddenly bloom, wither, or open. These are the impartial and upright ghosts and spirits of Heaven and Earth, phenomena people commonly witness without finding strange."

Yet when ghostly fires appear at night, bricks fly in broad daylight, beams strike chests, or whispers cling to ears—with a thousand monstrous forms too numerous to record—these are what the *Great Change* calls "a cartload of ghosts." The *Spring and Autumn Annals* speaks of stones speaking in Jin, of bronze tripods casting omens, of a hundred anomalies subdued by Han swords, and spirits howling when tested. The ghostly fan of Minister Fan, the serpentine form of Kong Daofu—should these be believed or not?

It is said: These are all born of the chaotic intermingling of a single *qi*, tainted by the dark and perverse, and thus deemed strange. They are not the upright *qi* of Heaven and Earth. For ghosts and spirits arise from yin and yang just as humans arise in the world. As there are many kinds of people, so too are there many kinds of things, and many kinds of ghosts and spirits. Throughout Heaven and Earth, they densely intermingle, assuming forms according to their natures, each fully endowed—one cannot say they do not exist.

Those born of Heaven become the sun, moon, and stars, yet may transform into comets, eclipses, or hail. Those born of Earth become mountains, rivers, grasses, and trees, yet may take the forms of mountain spirits, sea deities, *chimei*, or *wangliang*. Those born of water, fire, earth, or stone may bring cataclysmic floods, raging wildfires, dust storms, or collapsing cliffs. As for humans, the upright become sages, worthies, and noble gentlemen; the aberrant become fools, the dull, the stupid, the violent, and the tyrannical. In greater extremes, if one does not observe the turbid Yellow River or the clear Wei, they become the chaotic monstrosities of the cosmos. In death, some become wise and upright spirits, receiving sacrifices for a hundred generations; others, harboring grievances and suffering, coalesce into ox ghosts and serpent deities. Their transformations are beyond enumeration—can one truly say they do not exist? It is merely a matter of whether they are upright or not.

Master Zhu said: "Winter’s cold and summer’s heat are the norm of principle. Yet if summer grows cold or winter hot, can one say this principle does not exist?" This is why the sages spoke of constancy and did not discuss the strange.

Master Cheng further said: "The case of Boyou becoming a *li* is a different principle." Master Zhu said: "It is not the common principle of life and death." He also said: "When one’s *qi* is not exhausted but death is forced upon them, they naturally become a *li*." Zichan established an heir for him, giving him a place to return. These words exhaust principle to its utmost precision—one may say he truly understood the conditions of ghosts and spirits.

In the *Zuozhuan*, when the people of Zheng were startled, saying, "Boyou has arrived!" they all fled, not knowing where to go. Some dreamed of Boyou in armor, saying, "I will kill Dai, and then kill Duan." The people grew even more fearful. Zichan established Gongsun Xie to appease him, and the disturbances ceased.

The Master said: "When ghosts and spirits have a place to return, they do not become *li*. I provided him a place to return."

Jin’s Zhao Jing asked: "Does Boyou still become a ghost?" The Master replied: "When one consumes abundant essence from things, their *hun* and *po* grow strong, and thus their spirit may attain the clarity of divine illumination. Even common men and women who die violently—if their *hun* and *po* still cling to others—they become malevolent *li*. How much more so for Liangxiao, a descendant of our former lord Duke Mu, whose use of things was vast, whose absorption of essence was great, and whose clan was large and well-supported? That he died violently and became a ghost is only fitting."

Tang’s Master Kong said: "He held high office, wielded power, and enjoyed lavish sustenance, so his use of things was abundant and his *hun* and *po* strong." Someone asked: "Former scholars spoke of ghosts and spirits as both existent and nonexistent. Yet what the *Zuozhuan* records cannot be called nonexistent." Master Zhu said: "Humans receive the *qi* of Heaven and Earth, which must eventually disperse—only the timing varies. Where their spirit reaches, it moves Heaven above. In ancient times, Jing Ke admired Yan Dan’s righteousness, and a white rainbow pierced the sun. Wei Xiansheng devised the Changping strategy for Qin, and Venus devoured the Pleiades. When Han executed the filial widow, there was a great drought for three years. When Jin killed an innocent commander, blood flowed backward up the pillars. Other examples, such as Duke Jing of Qi dreaming of the ghost of Wuqiu or the Wang clan of Han exonerating the injustice at Xueque Pavilion, are all recorded by historians and cannot be dismissed. I shall categorize these accounts so that later discussions of ghosts and spirits do not lose their bearings and drift without guidance. This is also the meaning of ‘revering and keeping them at a distance.’"

The explanation is as follows: The two *qi* of yin and yang disperse between the two realms. Everywhere the eye falls, ghosts and spirits are present. If we do not distinguish them by their domains, all becomes one indistinguishable mass, the realms of the hidden and the manifest grow confused, and the human Way cannot be established. Thus, in Heaven, they are the sun, moon, stars, wind, rain, frost, dew, the four seasons, and cold and heat—clearly, ghosts and spirits move within them. This is what the *Book of Documents* calls "serving the Six Ancestors," and should be regarded as one category. On Earth, they are the Five Peaks and Four Waterways, the mountain lords and river sovereigns who summon clouds and rain to aid the work of transformation—these cannot be called nonexistent. This is what the *Military Triumph* refers to as "passing through famous mountains and great rivers," and should be regarded as another category. Those whose merit and virtue remain among the people and are recorded in the sacrificial canon—such as Gou Long and Hou Ji for their achievements, or the Master for his virtue, who as sages and worthies endure through ten thousand generations without fading—should be regarded as yet another category. When a person dies, they are called a ghost, their *qi* already dispersed. Yet where the descendants’ spirit gathers, the ancestors come to partake. This is what the *Analects of Lu* means by: "Sacrifice as if they are present; sacrifice to the spirits as if the spirits are present." This is not only true for the scholar sacrificing to his ancestors. From the Son of Heaven to the common people, all have their proper ranks and measures, which must not be transgressed—this should be regarded as another category. Below this, there are mountain goblins, earth spirits, water monsters, and tree demons; where there is no ghost, there is still debate, and strange phenomena arise. The turnip was punished for falsehood, yet it appeared in person—these cannot be disbelieved. Some hang from the tip of a writing brush or cling to a winnowing basket; some generate thunder or summon cranes. Between Heaven and Earth, such wandering *hun* and stray ghosts exist, capable of deluding people. They cannot be called nonexistent, but they are not of the proper kind—this should be regarded as another category.

When categories are precisely distinguished, principles become perfectly clear. As Gu Yong said: "Understand the nature of Heaven and Earth, and you will not be deluded by the supernatural." The ancients said: "Govern the world with the Dao, and its ghosts will not be divine." This is the Way of cultivating the self. The human body is the meeting place of ghosts and spirits. Within this mere shell, inside and out, there is nothing that is not the *qi* of Heaven and Earth’s yin and yang. When this heart stirs, it responds. Thus it is said: "What fills Heaven and Earth constitutes my body; what leads Heaven and Earth constitutes my nature." When my heart is upright, those impartial and upright ghosts and spirits naturally respond. If there is any impropriety, those wandering *hun* and perverse *qi* will entangle and cannot be resolved.

Master Zhu said: "It is like a fish in water: the water outside is the same as the water inside its belly. The water in a mandarin fish’s belly is the same as the water in a carp’s belly." Though this saying is small, it can illustrate great truths. Since later ages, the theories of licentious and excessive sacrifices have arisen, and the virtue of the heart’s ghosts and spirits has been neglected. Alas, how lamentable!

*Tian Yuan Fa Wei*, Scroll Seventeen, concludes.

3. This sentence appears in the *Siku Quanshu* as: "This refers to the interior of the human form."
4. "玄" appears as "元" in the *Siku Quanshu*.
5. "谓" originally written as "为," amended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
6. "去" originally written as "云," amended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.

8. "味" originally written as "朱," amended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
9. "甘" originally written as "行," amended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
10. This sentence appears in the *Siku Quanshu* as: "The transformed *hun* of life is called *shen*."
11. This sentence appears in the *Siku Quanshu* as: "The transformed *po* of life is called *gui*."
12. "魂" appears as "魂气" in the *Siku Quanshu*.
13. "盖" originally written as "益," amended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
14. "尽" originally written as "书," amended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.

19. From "The famous mountains and great rivers can summon clouds and bring rain" to this sentence, these lines are absent in the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
20. "朱" originally written as "失," amended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
21. "朱" originally written as "未," amended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
