*Tian Yuan Fa Wei*, Scroll Eighteen.
Compiled by Bao Yunlong Jingxiang of Luzhai.
Collated by Fang Hui Wuli of Xugu.

The Transformations

Heaven and Earth transform, and yin and yang arise; yin and yang transform, and the myriad beings arise; the myriad beings transform, and sages and worthies arise. In ancient times, those who became scholars achieved success in three years and underwent one transformation in ten. They began as scholars and ended as sages. All people transform their qi-endowed nature to return to the Heaven-ordained nature. It is also said: "Reverence alone suffices." Reverence leads to unity, unity to sincerity, sincerity to manifestation, manifestation to clarity, clarity to movement, movement to change, and change to transformation. Only the utmost sincerity in the world can bring about transformation.

Someone asked: "Why is *Transformation* placed at the end of the twenty-fifth scroll?" The answer: Since the Supreme Ultimate first divided, Heaven and Earth have been like a great furnace, with the myriad beings born within it. From non-being to being, all emerge through refinement from within. From being to non-being, all gradually dissolve and return within. From the sixty-four thousand eight hundred years of the precelestial era’s inception to the postcelestial era from Yu’s *jiazi* to the *jiawu* year of the Yuan dynasty—over three thousand five hundred and ten years—how many transformations and changes have occurred among people? How many sages and worthies have appeared across the ages? Mountains and valleys shift, the cosmos renews, and not a single person or thing remains constant. Only through the preservation of classical texts can these be examined and verified.

Cold transforms into warmth, warmth into heat, heat into coolness, and coolness back into cold—within these transformations lies further change. Thus, spring transforms into summer, summer into autumn, and autumn into winter. The alternation of cold and heat continues without end. People and things are likewise bound within the transformations of Heaven and Earth and cannot escape them. Rotten pillars give rise to sesame and *fuling*; fish transform into dragons, the hidden taking flight. Sparrows transform into clams, the flying descending. Hawks transform into turtledoves, the vicious becoming virtuous. Turtledoves transform into hawks, the virtuous turning vicious. Wildcats transform into leopards, leopards into tigers—small things can indeed transform into great ones. Yet when a sage neglects reflection and acts recklessly, even greatness may diminish—how lamentable! Judging by yin and yang, nothing is immutable; how much more so for humans, who are the most spiritually endowed of all beings?

The blind man’s son can become a Yao; the man from the roadside can become a Yu; the seemingly foolish man from the humble alley can become a sage—these are those adept at transformation. Those who fail to transform well include Dan Zhu and Shang Jun, who were unworthy of their fathers; Xia Gui and Shang Xin, who bore no resemblance to their ancestors; Xia Hui, whose virtuous younger brother could not transform his elder brother; and Huan Chi and Sima Niu, whose elder brothers refused to emulate their younger siblings. Humans, as the most spiritually endowed of Heaven and Earth’s creations, yet fail to refine their qi-endowed nature to return to goodness, thereby betraying the transformative power of Heaven and Earth.

How, then, can one excel at transformation? Only by cultivating utmost reverence to attain the realm of sincerity can one stand without shame as a human and align with Heaven and Earth. Scholars aspire to worthiness, the worthy to sageliness, and sages to Heaven. Without reverence, how can this be achieved? This chapter on *Transformation* concludes the scrolls for this reason.

Master Cheng Shuzhi said: "*The Book of Changes* is about change, adapting to the times to follow the Dao." Master Zhu said: "Change is the gradual process of transformation; transformation is the completion of change." He also said: "Yin changes into yang, and yang transforms into yin." When change and transformation are discussed in contrast, change is growth, and transformation is decline. Yet when considered as a whole, all is transformation. At the turning point, it becomes change. Change is the shift from yin to yang, from non-being to being, from the subtle to the manifest, from night to day. The soft changes into the hard, and cold into warmth. From sprouts growing into branches and leaves, suddenly flourishing and taking on new forms—this is change. Transformation is the shift from yang to yin, from being to non-being, from flourishing to decline, from day to night. The hard transforms into the soft, and warmth into cold. Whatever has form and substance gradually diminishes until it vanishes—this is transformation.

He further said: "Yin changing into yang is progress, naturally growing vigorously. Yang transforming into yin is retreat, naturally fading away without a trace. Yang, having reached its peak, turns back and retreats. Yin, having retreated to its limit, rises and advances. Thus, transformation is the image of advance and retreat. Yang transforming into yin and yin changing into yang—this is transformation. What enables transformation is the Dao. The Dao is the inherent mystery; transformation is the mechanism it rides. Thus, yin changes and yang transforms, and the Dao is present in all. It is present in both, hence unfathomable. As it is said: 'One who knows the Dao of transformation knows the workings of the spirit.' Therefore, the spirit has no fixed direction, and *The Book of Changes* has no fixed form. The spirit has no fixed direction—it may reside in yin or yang. Just as it is spring, it may already be summer; just as it is autumn, it may already be winter. *The Book of Changes* has no fixed form—it may be yin or yang. Just as it is spring, it may also be summer; just as it is autumn, it may also be winter. They alternate and interchange, unbound by any fixed form."

Master Yichuan said: "Change is like a thing in the process of changing but not yet transformed; once transformed, there is no trace of the old." *The Essential Principles of Mount He* states: "Returning to the original form is called transformation. Gradual alteration is called change. Even if the old form suddenly changes, it is still called transformation." This is the ancient view. Master Zhu’s explanation is more precise. He said: "In transformation,裁 (cutting) is preserved in change." *Cai* means to cut or sever. He also said: "To extend and enact it is preserved in *tong* (permeation)."

"Permeation (*tong*) is the permeation of change. Having already determined through *cai* (cutting), one extends and enacts it. For example, a year is cut into four seasons, a season into three months, a day into twelve hours—this is change. Yin and yang alternate; without *cai* (cutting), how could there be fixed forms? Endless coming and going is called *tong* (permeation). To handle change appropriately is *tong*. When there is *tong*, there is no exhaustion.

Transformation (*hua*) arises naturally, *cai* is human intervention, and change (*bian*) alters things. Transformation is a gradual, day-by-day process. For instance, the weather gradually cools, and upon reaching the Beginning of Autumn, it is decisively cut off—what follows is autumn, which is change. The twelve hours from *zi* to *chou* to *yin* and *mao* all transform imperceptibly, but after *hai*, the *zi* hour is sharply demarcated as belonging to the next day—this is what is meant by change. Again, like the movement of stars, which never pause for even a moment: if one were to record their positions hour by hour or from dusk to dawn, the daily discrepancy of one degree would make it impossible to track. Thus, the Dipper is only demarcated month by month. Whether it is the *hun* (dusk) or *dan* (dawn) culmination of a given day, it is determined by this monthly *cai*. When transformation reaches the end of a month, that is change.

Another example: a year of three hundred and sixty days progresses day by day, and when it reaches completion, this is transformation. From spring to summer, summer to autumn, autumn to winter—when the sage cuts this progression into four seasons, this is change.

It is said: 'Permeate its changes, so the people do not grow weary.' One must find someone who can permeate change. If left to transform on its own, how could it be managed? As in 'The dragon soars too high and meets with regret'—this is a failure of *tong*. To handle it without regret is *tong*. To permeate change is simply to ensure constant, unobstructed flow.

For example, a person’s circumstances—wealth or poverty, peace or peril, barbarism or hardship—these are changes. To act rightly in wealth, rightly in poverty, rightly in barbarism or hardship, so that there is no situation in which one does not find contentment—this is *tong*. To cut at the point of transformation is change; to handle it well is *tong*.

The changes of all things in the world arise solely from the two forces of yin and yang. When yin reaches its limit, it turns to yang; when yang reaches its limit, it turns to yin—one can only follow them. When the sage reaches the utmost point of goodness, there is still another principle at work. Before the stage of 'treading on frost, solid ice will come,' as soon as yin begins to stir within yang, one employs a hundred methods to restrain it—this is indeed so. But if yang were to persist unchecked, how could the myriad things come to fruition? It is simply the natural course of things. The rise and fall of a state’s fortune also follows this urgency.

When Yao had reigned for seventy years, his vigor naturally declined, so he sought out Shun and entrusted the realm to him, thus renewing the cycle. If he had continued without pause until his death, even the legacies of Wen and Wu would have declined. The urgency of the Cheng and Kang eras gave way to complacency in maintaining prosperity, and when it reached its limit, King Zhao’s reign could not be revived. After Emperor Xuan of Han, the dynasty steadily declined until Guangwu arose—yet after one or two generations, it could not be sustained, and the imperial line repeatedly faltered. Emperor Guangwu of Liu was like what Kangjie called 'spring in autumn'—a matter of timing.

It is also said: When observing people within the workings of heaven’s mandate, there is not a moment of rest. Fortune and misfortune, regret and shame—there is no pause. It is like a great cartwheel, hurtling forward in decline. The sage simply follows its course, observing how the principle operates, applying this principle here and that principle there.

The six lines of *Qian* are all yang, each taking its image in sequence. They describe the sage’s dwelling in different positions and stages. Whether hidden, emerging, cautious, leaping, soaring, or at its peak, each is appropriate to its place. 'Hidden' means remaining below, not yet in use—like level ground where even if there is water, it is but a shallow pool, while the deep abyss cannot be fathomed. 'Leaping' means moving without yet taking flight—still grounded, but no longer walking. With a single bound, it rises, feet leaving the earth. Once it leaps, it ascends to the heavens, no longer bound to the abyss, yet still unfathomable. Below, it departs from walking; above, it nears flight. Neither in heaven, nor on earth, nor among men—unlike the second nine, which is secure. Here, one cannot advance or retreat; it is not up to oneself, but subject to the timing of heaven.

In the sage’s terms, this is like Shun’s trials, King Wen’s two-thirds dominion, or Tang and Wu’s victories at Mingtiao and Muye. At the top nine, it is the position of Yi and Zhou, yet it is already difficult. It seems that people within heaven’s mandate have not a moment’s respite. One may leap into action, possessing virtue but no position. If the effort is incomplete, one does not lose the status of a hidden dragon. Fortune and misfortune, regret and shame—there is not a moment’s pause. Like the wheel of a great cart, hurtling forward in urgency, the sage simply follows its course. As it is said: 'Preceding heaven, heaven does not oppose it; following heaven, it accords with the timing'—how could there be the slightest trace of self-interest?

It is also said: *Qian* is the utmost vigor under heaven. Nowadays, people only judge its vigor by whether it appears vigorous, not waiting for events to unfold before seeing it. Master Zhu said: I once noted that when Liu Yuancheng met others, he would sit for a long time without much conversation. Though urged to stay, he would not engage in discussion. When asked about this, Yuancheng said: 'A person who sits for a long time without shifting is surely noble.' Thus, observing how a person sits can reveal their nobility, longevity, or short lifespan. Later, when he met Master Caotang, he remarked that Yuancheng loved to talk."

In Nanjing, a crossroads of the four directions, scholars and officials from the southeast who passed through would invariably seek him out. His residence teemed with guests, and he would speak freely and extensively, without the slightest restraint. He passed away in the second year of the Xuanhe era, at the age of seventy-eight—only three or four years before the Jingkang disaster. Yuancheng died in the same period as Chen Liaoweng; one wonders, had the two masters lived through the Jingkang era, how would they have been employed?

I say: In the early Shaoxing era, Ma Yongqing of Weiyang composed a *yulu*, recording that he had personally seen Yuancheng at around sixty-three or sixty-four, his bearing majestic and his spirit robust, his speech grand and his manner forthright. Whether in cold or heat, dawn or dusk, he would always meet guests in full attire. Even while engaged in conversation, his body remained upright, shoulders squared and back straight, his posture unmoving—even his hands and feet never shifted. A truly formidable man. Ma noted that when he recorded these words, twenty-six years had already passed. Thus, in the fifth year of Shaoxing, only fifteen years had elapsed since Yuancheng’s death.

Yuancheng initially studied under Master Sima Wengong, never uttering a single careless word. Once he grasped this principle, he upheld it steadfastly throughout his life. His virtue was perfected, his conduct revered, and all within the realm looked up to him. After crossing the ridge, he abstained from wine and desire, enduring harsh climates and poor water and soil without falling ill. His vitality and longevity were beyond that of ordinary men.

Master Shao’s *Xiantian Fangtu* and *Yuantu* depict the four images intersecting to form sixteen configurations. The eight trigrams interact to produce sixty-four, and from this wondrous interplay of resonance, all beings and things arise.

When the qi of heaven and earth commingle, they give birth to beings and things. Observing their convergence reveals the extent of qi’s reach, from which one may discern the origin of their kind. When heavenly qi descends to earth, in humans it produces males, and in creatures, males. When earthly qi ascends to heaven, in humans it produces females, and in creatures, females. From the union of male and female, yang and yin, arise endless generations of transforming beings.

Once beings and things are born, their qi follows the ascending and descending, the interplay of heaven and earth’s qi. Humans receive the central qi of heaven and earth, and thus respond to the qi of all four directions. Creatures receive the partial qi of heaven and earth, each responding to what they encounter. Therefore, by observing the convergence of heaven and earth’s qi, one may understand the origins of beings and things. By observing the resonance of heaven and earth’s qi, one may understand their mutual generation.

Heaven and earth are forms; their convergence is through *Qian* and *Kun*. When *Qian* and *Kun* are not in use, their convergence is through *Kan* and *Li*. Who but one who understands the Dao could recognize this?

The qi of a single day spans twelve hours; the qi of a month, thirty days; the qi of a year, twelve months. Wherever qi reaches, all things receive it according to their endowment, and by this endowment, they receive the qi of heaven and earth, becoming the root of endless generation.

Humans receive the central qi of heaven and earth, thus moving in harmony with them, gathering the qi of all things within their bodies. This is why humans are nobler than heaven and earth and more spiritually endowed than all creatures. One who can grasp the Dao of heaven and earth and wield its mechanism is, in effect, heaven and earth themselves.

It is also said: Those who receive the central qi of heaven and earth become human, possessing the qi of all four directions. Thus, humans are the noblest of heaven and earth and the most spiritually endowed of all things. The human head is round, mirroring heaven; the feet are square, treading earth. Facing south and turning north, left to the east and right to the west, ears sharp and eyes bright, hands raised and feet planted—all align with the proper order. Dwelling at the center of heaven and earth, they occupy the position of *zi* and *wu*. The five human relationships—ruler and minister, father and son—and the five constant virtues—benevolence, righteousness, propriety, wisdom—are rooted in the heart and manifest externally.

Those who receive qi that is clear, upright, and unobstructed become sages and worthies. Those who receive qi that is turbid, biased, and blocked become fools and the unworthy. Even those near the east tend toward benevolence and softness, while those near the west tend toward righteousness and hardness—such differences in endowment are inevitable. The Yi and Di barbarians, though human, are fierce and lack propriety and righteousness because they inhabit particularly biased lands, and thus their endowment is also biased. This is the work of qi, not of inherent nature.

Humans stand upright, while birds and beasts lie horizontally, and plants grow sideways. Those that lie horizontally receive qi that is particularly biased. Thus, their heads face west, their backs to heaven, and their feet to earth—yet even within this bias, traces of benevolence and righteousness remain. Those that receive eastern qi embody benevolence; those that receive western qi, righteousness—thus occupying the positions of *mao* and *you*. Though birds lie horizontally, their heads are raised, slightly distinguishing them from beasts. Those that receive eastern qi embody benevolence; those that receive western qi, righteousness.

Creatures with scales, shells, fur, or wings represent qi gradually entering the earth. Those that grow sideways receive the qi of the earth. Those that receive northwestern qi are hard; those that receive southeastern qi are soft. All kinds under heaven are encompassed thus.

The dragon receives the true east; the tiger, the true west. Hence, the east has the Azure Dragon, and the west, the White Tiger. The *Shuowen* states: The *lin* is the benevolent beast of the east, and the *qingyu* is the benevolent beast of the west. Generally, those that receive eastern qi have horns; those that receive western qi have fangs. Horns signify constant benevolence. Those that fall short are also gentle and docile, like cattle and sheep. Those that exceed are harsh and predatory, like jackals, wolves, dogs, and pigs.

Western beasts all move at night, while eastern beasts are dazzled by night. Only the dragon defies this pattern, for it is divine. Birds lie horizontally but raise their heads, their qi rising from the earth and gradually nearing heaven—thus they raise their heads and can fly. Those of the east and west ascend from below: their heads are short, their tails long, their feathers patterned. When about to fly, they must first soar upward. Those of the southwest descend from above: their heads are long, their tails short, their feathers dark and plain. When about to fly, they first plummet downward.

Within these creatures, benevolence and righteousness reside as well. Among those that ascend, the smaller are like chicks, flying close to the ground. The greater are like *luan* and phoenixes, soaring straight to heaven. Among those that descend, the smaller are like wagtails and cranes, flying near the celestial pole. Ducks and teals fly low to the earth, even entering water. In the southeast, birds are dazzled by night; in the northwest, they fly by night. From the *xu* hour to the *you* hour, they gradually enter the earth; from the *yin* hour to the *mao* hour, they gradually emerge from it.

Thus, in the west, creatures are like beetles and butterflies—often trapped, for their qi gradually sinks. In the east, creatures are scaled and feathered—often flourishing, for their qi gradually rises. Hence, creatures like tortoises and toads excel at concealment and stillness, while fish and snakes excel at transformation. The interplay of yin and yang, the rise and fall of qi, bring this about.

Plants draw the qi of the earth, so they all grow downward, the opposite of humans. This reversal of qi—whether counterflow or compliant—admits no alternative. Those that grow in the east are supple: they wither in autumn and flourish in spring and summer. Those that grow in the west are hardy: they wither in spring and flourish in autumn and winter. Branches and leaves turn toward yin, their leaves never leaving the earth, so beasts lodge there and feed on their foliage. Trees reach toward heaven, so birds perch there and feed on their fruit. Such is the pattern.

The three directions of *hai*, *zi*, and *chou* produce no life; all other directions do. Precisely because they produce nothing, heaven and earth generate themselves. This is why heaven and earth endure and life never ceases. The winter of the year, the dark and new moons, the night of the day—these are the Dao of heaven and earth.

Zhang Guanwu said: A person’s lifespan of a hundred years—if reckoned by the long count of yin, one year adds one year. If reckoned by the division of yang, one year subtracts one year. Heaven begins with the odd numbers one, three, five, seven, and nine. From *zi* to *si*, from six to nine, from few to many, yang grows and yin wanes, its qi rising from below. Yang arises from below, as yang within yin. This is what is meant by yang first dividing to establish the great limit. When yang divides, it becomes empty—emptiness is yin. When yang reaches its peak, yin is born.

Earth continues with the even numbers two, four, six, eight, and ten. From *wu* to *hai*, from nine to six, from many to few, yin grows and yang wanes, its qi descending from above. Yin arises from above, as yin within yang. This is yin growing to fulfill its finer count. When yin grows, it becomes full—fullness is yang. When yin reaches its peak, yang is born.

That yang first establishes the great limit is like a person’s lifespan of a hundred years, which has a fixed number. Yin comes to diminish yang, eroding its already fixed count. In terms of form and number, yin seems to add a year each year. In terms of division, yang truly subtracts a year each year. Thus it is said: When yang comes, life begins; when yang departs, death follows. It is not that yang departs—it is that yang is consumed by yin, exhausting its allotted number.

Master Zhu said: Speech must have order. Without order in speech, nothing can be accomplished. In the *Yijing*, *Gen*’s six in the fifth place says, “Restraining the cheeks.” The cheeks are the jaws and the carriage of speech. To restrain the cheeks is to speak without recklessness, with order—then others will readily listen and act, and regret can be avoided.

Han-era Jia Yi had literary talent and a grand bearing, but his mind was narrow. When he undertook matters, he could not contain himself, bursting out with everything at once. He was restless and impatient, losing the sequence of his words. No wonder he met with failure. When Dou and Guan advised the emperor, they were modest and deferential, yet in the end, they accomplished nothing. Men like Han Xin, Deng Yu, and Zhuge Liang spoke with order, so their rulers readily listened, and they carried out their ways throughout their lives without altering their principles. How could this merely avoid regret? Their principles had a fixed pattern.

Someone asked: In *Xian*, the top six is the cheeks; in *Gen*, the six in the fifth place is the cheeks—why? The answer: In *Xian*, the top of *Dui* is the mouth; in *Gen*, the nine in the third place is the heart. Thus, the fifth yin line represents the cheeks.

Master Shao said: What makes humans more spiritually endowed than the myriad things is that their eyes can take in the colors of all things, their ears the sounds, their noses the scents, and their mouths the flavors. These—sounds, colors, scents, and flavors—are the substance of the myriad things. Eyes, ears, noses, and mouths are the functions of humans. When substance and function interact, the Dao of humans and things is complete.

All things under heaven possess principle, nature, and destiny. Only by exhaustively investigating them can they be used. If the eyes, ears, noses, and mouths of the world become one’s own, then the eyes see all, the ears hear all, the mouth speaks all, and the nose comprehends all. In this way, one takes the heart of the world as one’s own heart. The heart then plans all, and one can observe the ten thousand hearts with a single heart, the ten thousand bodies with a single body, the ten thousand things with a single thing, the ten thousand ages with a single age. One does not observe with the eyes but with the heart; not with the heart but with principle. What thing under heaven lies beyond this principle?

Thus it is said: In the tranquil observation of the myriad things, all are self-possessed. And again: Observing the myriad things from the perspective of heaven and earth, the myriad things remain the myriad things. Observing heaven and earth from the perspective of the Supreme Ultimate, heaven and earth are also things. If one can fully realize the Dao of the Supreme Ultimate, one can encompass heaven and earth, shape the myriad things, and hold creation in one’s grasp. How could the thousand thousand things be mere trifles, or the thousand thousand people mere common folk?

He also said: When the world is about to be well-ordered, people value conduct and righteousness. When the world is about to descend into chaos, people value words and profit. Valuing conduct fosters sincerity and solidity; valuing words fosters deceit and cunning. Valuing righteousness fosters modesty and deference; valuing profit fosters plunder and contention. Thus, the Three Kings valued conduct and entered into righteousness, while the Five Hegemons valued words and entered into profit. Even the likes of Boyi were surpassed—how could one bear to speak of it?

In the Three Dynasties’ eras of order, there was no governance that did not take the regulation of human relationships as its Way. In the Three Dynasties’ eras of chaos, there was no disorder that did not arise from the disruption of human relationships. To abandon human relationships is to become a barbarian.

Since ancient times, no dynasty has slaughtered as many people as the Qin. Yet the slaughter of many does not always require the blade. It is said that when the people of the world have no path to survival, how much worse is it to kill them in great numbers with the blade?

Master Zhu said: "The human mind and the mind of the Dao, refined into one and held to the center"—these sixteen characters alone determine the right and wrong of the self, the peace or peril of the world, and the order or chaos of the realm. Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, Wu, and their transmission to Confucius and Mencius embodied this. The long-lived rulers of the Three Dynasties established their hearts upon this and governed their states by it.

After the passing of Master Meng, this learning was no longer transmitted, and the pursuit of profit and advantage took its place. From the Han and Tang onward, for fifteen hundred years, governance has been but a patchwork of makeshift repairs. Though some eras have known brief stability, the learning transmitted by the Two Emperors, Three Kings, Zhou, and Confucius has never once been fully realized in the world.

Scholars of the age, if they possess even a modicum of talent, refuse to humble their hearts and devote themselves to the work of the Confucian school or the practice of sagely learning. They seek only the abundance of their catch, unashamed by the impropriety of their gains, and mock those Confucians who adhere to rules, standards, and measures. This is why true learning has faded, and the world’s decline has grown ever steeper.

He also said: The difference between the noble person and the petty person, between rising and descending, lies in the slightest divergence at the outset of their intentions. Those who follow the heavenly principle ascend; they advance ever upward, day by day. Those who follow human desire descend; they sink ever lower, day by day. The petty person, ever descending, falls into deeper ruin with each passing day, dragged down by human desire as one drowning in water.

In the beginning, the division between rising and falling may seem slight, but over time, the gap widens immeasurably. Though one may possess wisdom and strength, even these cannot save or sustain them.

The ancients, recounting Confucius’ words, said: "Those who know from birth are the highest; those who know through learning come next; those who learn through hardship come after. Those who suffer hardship yet do not learn—such people are the lowest."

He also said: "Those above the level of the average person may be spoken to of higher things." Only the supremely wise and the utterly foolish cannot be changed.

Ban Gu said: Since the creation of written records, we may know of the early people. The classics and their commentaries speak of the emperors before Tang and Yu, who bore titles and regalia, though their ministers cannot be named. The various masters, however, have much to say of them. Their purpose was to make virtue manifest, expose evil, and admonish later generations, so their words have been preserved and transmitted.

Explanations of the classics’ meanings are called *zhuan* (traditions). It is said: "If one associates with Yao, Shun, Yu, Hou Ji, or Yi for goodness, they will act accordingly; if one associates with the likes of Gonggong, Huan Dou, or others who seek evil, they will be punished. Those who can be led to goodness but not to evil are called the supremely wise. Jie, Zhou, Long Feng, and Bi Gan sought to lead them to goodness but were punished; the reckless warriors of Jie’s court and the flattering ministers of Zhou’s court led them to evil, and they acted accordingly. Those who can be led to evil but not to goodness are called the utterly foolish. Duke Huan of Qi, when aided by Guan Zhong, became a hegemon; when aided by Shu Diao, he fell into chaos. Those who can be led to either goodness or evil are called the average person."

Thus, the nine ranks are established, and the classics and their traditions are thoroughly examined. The succession of generations is arranged to encompass the essentials of antiquity and the present:

The highest of the highest, the sagely ones: Fuxi, the Taihao Emperor; Shennong, the Yan Emperor; the Yellow Emperor, Xuanyuan; Shaohao, the Jintian clan; Zhuanxu, the Gaoyang clan; Emperor Ku, the Gaoxin clan; Emperor Yao, the Taotang clan; Emperor Shun, the Youyu clan; Emperor Yu, the Xiahou clan; Emperor Tang, the Shang clan; King Wen of Zhou; King Wu; and the Duke of Zhou; and Confucius.

Fuxi, Shennong, and the Yellow Emperor are the Three Sovereigns. Shaohao, Zhuanxu, Gaoxin, Yao, and Shun are the Five Emperors—this is already stated in the *Preface to the Book of Documents*. Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu are the Three Kings.

The Duke of Zhou, son of King Wen, younger brother of King Wu, and uncle of King Cheng, established rituals and composed music, personally bringing about great peace—his sagely virtue cannot be surpassed. Confucius traced his lineage to Yao and Shun, taking King Wen and King Wu as his models. Above, he aligned with the celestial seasons; below, he followed the patterns of water and earth. He composed the Six Classics, leaving instructions for ten thousand generations. From Fuxi to Confucius, he synthesized the great accomplishments of all.

The historian Ban [Gu] classified the worthies of antiquity and the present into nine ranks. These fourteen sages he rightly placed in the highest of the highest. Yet why did he further divide the upper-middle and below into eight ranks? Names and realities do not match; confusion and disorder prevail, all losing their proper order. I have appended the critiques of Zhang and Yan to the end.

Zhang said: Laozi’s silence was what Confucius took as his teacher. Though not among the sages, he was surely a great worthy. Wenbo’s father was well-versed in ritual canons; his actions were praised by the sages, and his words became models for later generations—yet he was placed in the fourth rank. Tian Dan, with the isolated city of Jimo, restored the might of Qi; Lu Lian’s broad learning made him indifferent to glory and profit; Lanzi overawed the King of Qin and deferred to Lian Po—yet these were ranked fifth. The Grand Concubine Ji indulged in sorcery and was fond of sacrificing to ghosts and spirits; the people of Chen emulated her, and the state abounded in licentious sacrifices. The eunuch Mencius violated the great elegance, preserving himself only through castration; filled with resentment, he composed biting satires—yet these were ranked sixth. The depravity of Miao Du’s lewdness, confounding and disordering ritual norms, was too vile to recount—yet he was placed in the seventh. The remaining discrepancies are numerous and tangled.

Originally, this work was not yet complete when I encountered the disaster of the Dou family, which may explain such errors.

Yan said: The theories of the six schools differ in weight. The hundred practices vary in their inclinations and rejections, making uniformity difficult. Zhang expressed his views and selectively cited the *History of Ban*. Yet even in his compilation, there are still significant inconsistencies. He merely recognized the titles of the ancient emperors and kings. As for the various personages recorded in the histories and biographies, no explanation is needed. Only when matters are obscure or uncertain does he occasionally address them.

Shaozi said: Among people, there are differences of tens, hundreds, thousands, and tens of thousands, each further divided into sixteen grades. From the supreme emperors and kings above to the scholars, farmers, artisans, and merchants below, the worthy and the foolish stand far apart, utterly distinct.

Man is the highest of all creatures. The sage is the highest of men. If one being is born among a trillion creatures, is he not a man? If one man is born among a trillion men, is he not a sage?

Thus, among a thousand thousand commoners, one who divides himself from the multitude is a petty man. Among one by one scholars, one who encompasses a trillion people is a great man. Creatures vary in size, and men vary in worth.

Therefore, it is said: There are men who are worth one man, ten men, a hundred men, a thousand men, ten thousand men, a hundred million men, and a trillion men.

It is also said: Men are of three ranks, wherein good and evil reside. The highest rank is good without teaching; the middle rank becomes good after teaching; the lowest rank remains ungood even after teaching. Those who are good without teaching—what are they if not sages? Those who become good after teaching—what are they if not the worthy? Those who remain ungood even after teaching—what are they if not fools?

Now, when we speak of teaching, we mean teaching those who can be taught. Those who cannot be taught are beyond the reach of instruction. Though they cannot be taught, they may still be warned, perhaps to avoid falling into vicious power.

Thus, those who do good are called auspicious men, and those who do evil are called inauspicious men. The auspicious man’s eyes do not gaze upon colors contrary to ritual propriety, his ears do not hear sounds contrary to ritual propriety, his mouth does not speak words contrary to ritual propriety, and his feet do not tread upon ground contrary to ritual propriety. He does not associate with those who depart from the Dao, nor does he accept what is not righteous. He seeks the worthy as if starving or parched, and avoids evil as if fleeing a pit or net. His goodness is like gold and stone, his weight like hills and mountains, his bearing like the luan and phoenix, and his qi like orchids and irises.

Some say: If he is not called an auspicious man, the Five Constants are not to be believed.

The inauspicious man’s speech is perilous and rash; he pursues fame and chases wrongdoing. He indulges in wine and lust, hates the upright and reviles the just, delights in disaster and rejoices in misfortune. He resents the good and virtuous as if they were enemies, and violates laws and statutes as if they were his bedding. In minor cases, he harms himself and destroys his nature; in major cases, he overthrows his clan and severs his ancestral sacrifices.

Some say: If he is not called an inauspicious man, I do not believe it.

The *Book of Changes* says: Without the accumulation of good, one cannot establish a name. Without the accumulation of evil, one cannot destroy oneself. This is what is meant.

Master Zhang of Nanxuan said: Men are of three ranks. The highest does not change, the lowest does not change, and those who sway this world are always the middle rank. When gentlemen gather in the court, all are transformed to goodness—then there are two parts gentlemen to one part petty man. When petty men gain influence, the middle rank is all drawn in—then there are two parts petty men to one part gentleman.

I say: Kangjie’s words teach men to do good. Nanxuan’s words urge the court to employ the goodness of gentlemen. When the household is ordered, the state is governed. The words of these two masters may serve as a model for ten thousand generations.

Shao further said: In the age of Yao and Shun, it was difficult to be a petty man. Though the Four Fiends existed, they could not unleash their evil. In the age of Shang Zhou, it was difficult to be a gentleman. Though the Three Benevolent Ones existed, they could not fully realize their goodness. This, too, depends on how those above transform and change.

Master Cheng said: By nature, men are similar; by habit, they grow apart. This refers to the nature of qi and disposition, not the original nature.

Master Zhang said: After form comes the nature of qi and disposition. Returning to it, the nature of Heaven and Earth remains. Thus, in the nature of qi and disposition, the gentleman has that which is not nature.

Master Zhu said: Among men’s similar qi and dispositions, there are also fixed differences of beauty and ugliness that cannot be altered by habit—these are the utterly foolish.

Someone asked: What is the original nature?

He replied: Mencius’s teaching of the goodness of nature is correct. This is what Master Cheng called the nature of the utmost origin and the most profound source. This is what Master Zhang called the one source of the ten thousand things—the nature of Heaven and Earth. It is the flowing of heavenly principle before receiving life, what is called “tranquil at birth.” What lies beyond this cannot be spoken of; it may also be called fate.

Speaking of it in terms of principle, without admixture of qi and disposition, it is the original state that Heaven-and-Earth bestows upon the ten thousand things—pure and perfectly good. Descending into man, it resides within qi and disposition. Thus, it is said to be inherently good. Returning to it, the nature of Heaven and Earth remains. This means that the nature of Heaven and Earth has never departed from within qi and disposition. Thus, it is said: After form comes the nature of qi and disposition.

Again, he was asked: What is the nature of qi and disposition?

He replied: What gives birth to the ten thousand things is principle. What generates the ten thousand things is qi and disposition. Men and things receive this qi and disposition to form their shapes, and the principle within them is called nature. However, what is called qi and disposition has unevenness in its bias, correctness, purity, impurity, clarity, obscurity, thickness, and thinness. Thus, the nature within it also varies in its manifestations.

Gaozi’s saying that “life is what is called nature,” Master Cheng’s “nature of birth and disposition,” Xunzi’s “nature is evil,” Yang Xiong’s “good and evil are mixed,” and Han Yu’s “three ranks” all refer to the nature of qi and disposition, yet they do not know the original nature.

I say: Mencius taught the goodness of nature and understood the root, yet he did not speak of qi and disposition, which is why he gave rise to the disputes of Xun, Yang, and Han. Thus, Master Cheng said: To discuss nature without discussing qi is incomplete. Xun, Yang, and Han spoke of the nature of qi and disposition, yet they did not know what nature truly is. Only after more than a thousand years after Confucius and Mencius, with the advent of Lian and Han, did this become clear.

Thus it is also said: To discuss qi without discussing nature is unclear. Huang Mianzhai remarked: Master Zhang of Hengqu distinguished between the nature of Heaven and Earth and the nature of qi and disposition, after which Master Zhu’s doctrine was established. Truly, these words are correct.

It is further said: Qi may be biased or upright, and the principle it receives follows accordingly in bias or uprightness. Qi may be dull or bright, and the principle it receives follows accordingly in dullness or brightness. When wood-qi is abundant, metal-qi declines, hence benevolence is often plentiful while righteousness is often scarce. When metal-qi is abundant, wood-qi declines, hence righteousness is often plentiful while benevolence is often scarce. Such is the nature of qi and disposition, which possesses both good and evil. Since there exists a nature of good and evil in qi and disposition, it is not the original nature of Heaven and Earth.

If one seeks it in Zisi’s “centrality before arousal,” when this heart is unaroused, its center is serene, and myriad thoughts do not arise—this is the pure and perfectly good nature of Heaven and Earth. Thus, though qi may be biased, principle remains upright; though qi may be dull, principle remains bright. Though qi may be deficient or excessive, principle knows no victory or defeat. Yet when it is stirred by external things, qi moves and principle follows. Or principle moves and qi carries it along—thus the perfectly good principle submits to qi, and good and evil are thereby distinguished.

The late Master once said: Before this heart is aroused, qi does not act. This is why there is good without evil. It is further said: Why is nature near while habit is distant? It is replied: Speaking of what is constant, those whose nature is good become habituated to goodness and daily advance toward loftiness and clarity. Those whose nature is evil become habituated to evil and daily sink into filth and baseness. Speaking of what changes, those whose nature is good may become habituated to evil and lose their goodness. Those whose nature is evil may become habituated to goodness and lose their evil. In all four cases, they begin close but end far apart.

Citing Master Cheng’s words: Human nature is originally good. Speaking of talent, however, there are those of the lowest stupidity who cannot be changed. There are two kinds of lowest stupidity: self-abandonment and self-neglect. If one governs oneself with goodness, there is nothing that cannot be changed. Even the most dull and foolish can gradually be polished and improved. Only the self-abandoning reject it with disbelief, and the self-neglecting sever it with inaction. Though they dwell with a sage, they cannot be transformed and entered—this is what Confucius called the lowest stupidity. Yet their endowment is not necessarily dull and foolish. Often they are strong-willed and surpass others in talent and strength, as was the case with King Zhou of Shang. The sage, seeing that they have cut themselves off from goodness, calls them the lowest stupidity. Yet examining their end, they are indeed foolish.

It is also said: The moment one becomes lax is the moment of self-abandonment and self-neglect. Master Zhu said: The self-abandoning have a defiant intent. They do not despise ritual and righteousness but simply refuse to listen to good words. The self-neglecting have a weak intent. They acknowledge that ritual and righteousness are good and accept others’ advice, yet they themselves do not act.

Master Zhang said: When virtue does not overcome qi, one’s nature and destiny are commanded by qi. When virtue overcomes qi, one’s nature and destiny are commanded by virtue. To exhaust principle and fulfill nature is to align one’s nature, Heaven, virtue, destiny, and the heavenly principle. What cannot be changed in qi is only life and death, longevity and premature death. Thus, in discussing life and death, one says “it is destiny,” speaking of qi. In discussing wealth and honor, one says “it is in Heaven,” speaking of principle. This is why great virtue necessarily receives destiny.

Master Lü said: The reason the noble person studies is to transform qi and disposition. When virtue overcomes qi and disposition, the foolish can advance toward clarity and the weak toward strength. If one cannot overcome them, then even with the aspiration to study, the foolish cannot become clear and the weak cannot stand firm.

That which is uniformly good without evil is nature, common to all people. That which is endowed with varying degrees of dullness, brightness, strength, and weakness is talent, differing among people. Sincerity is the means to return to what is common and transform what differs. To seek beauty from an unlovely endowment requires a hundredfold effort to achieve it. Now, with a careless and reckless approach to study—sometimes practicing, sometimes stopping—one attempts to transform an unlovely endowment. When it fails to change, one says, “My endowment is not beautiful; it cannot be transformed by study.” This is truly self-abandonment, and its unbenevolence is extreme.

That great virtue necessarily receives destiny means that virtue is principle. Master Cheng said: To act is to carry out the heavenly mandate, to reach the heavenly principle. To necessarily receive destiny is to obtain its response. Heaven’s retribution is like shadow and echo. To receive its retribution is the constant principle; not to receive it is the exceptional principle. Yet people, in their shallowness and narrowness, seek it amiss, for the heavenly mandate cannot be altered. However, what can be altered is within the power of those with virtue. Through cultivation, one may extend years and generations. Praying to Heaven for eternal destiny, or for ordinary people to become sages and worthies—all follow this Way.

Master Hou said: Those who obtain the constant are like Shun. Those who do not obtain the constant are like Confucius. Shun rose from a commoner to possess the world—those who plant nurture it. Jie fell from Son of Heaven to a commoner—those who overturn topple it. Heaven does not establish or abolish principles for Shun or Jie; it is inherently so. Extending this principle, one can grasp the pattern in all things.

Master Zhu said: Transformation does not occur in a single day through sudden enlightenment. The advancement of human virtue is likewise. “At thirty, one stands.” Before reaching thirty, one must stand; one must begin at fifteen with the aspiration to learn and gradually transform. “Not deluded,” “knowing destiny,” “ear harmony,” and “following the heart” are all the same.

Master Hu said: “At fifteen, Confucius aspired to learn.”

What is this learning? It is the Great Learning—the Way of cultivating the self, ordering the household, governing the state, and bringing peace to the world.

"At thirty, one stands." What does it mean to stand? It means dwelling in the world’s broadest dwelling, establishing oneself in the world’s proper position, and walking the world’s great Way without retreat.

"At forty, one is not deluded." What does it mean to be not deluded? It means that neither wealth nor honor can corrupt, neither poverty nor lowliness can sway, and neither power nor force can bend—standing resolutely above the myriad things.

"At fifty, one knows Heaven’s mandate." What does it mean to know? It means the four virtues of Qian—origination, prosperity, advantage, and perseverance—are practiced with clarity, flowing harmoniously with the myriad things, each attaining its proper place without disorder.

"At sixty, one’s ear is attuned." What does this attunement mean? It means that what passes is transformed, what remains is spirit-like, nearly attaining the realm of Heaven.

"At seventy, one follows the heart’s desires without overstepping the bounds." What does this mean? It means that one’s movements are of Heaven, and thus it is so.

Zigong said: "When the Master governed a state or household, those he established stood firm, those he guided advanced, those he pacified came, and those he moved harmonized—could Heaven alone achieve such? I say: The Sage’s learning knows no bounds with Heaven, like laying the foundation for a thousand myriad years. Yet his aspirations were vast and enduring, so before thirty, he transformed every fifteen years. Entering the learning of the great person, he opened his resolve, and after another fifteen years, having long maintained his course, what he learned remained unchanged, and he stood firm with established virtue. Thereafter, he advanced steadily, transforming every ten years, progressing gradually. Having passed beyond doubt, he advanced to firmness, thus penetrating the principles of all things as they ought to be, without uncertainty. After another ten years, knowing Heaven’s mandate, doubt was no longer worth mentioning. This heart and Heaven’s mandate flowed together without obstruction. After another ten years, his understanding entered his heart, and nothing hindered him—he attained without deliberation. Through long accumulation of cultivation, he reached the state where his heart’s desires followed without transgressing the bounds. In these ten years, he acted with ease, hitting the mark without effort. For the Master’s learning was the learning of the great person of Qian. The first thirty years were divided into two stages to open the foundation of his vast and enduring resolve. The latter thirty years were divided into three stages to reach the state where his heart’s desires followed without transgressing the bounds. Unshakable was his resolve in learning, guarding against evil and preserving sincerity to stand firm. Knowing the end as well as the beginning was freedom from doubt. Harmony between sound and qi was attunement of the ear. Thus he saw Heaven’s pattern without transgressing the bounds. Therefore it is said: The great person harmonizes his virtue with Heaven and Earth, his brightness with the sun and moon, his order with the four seasons, and his auspices and omens with ghosts and spirits. How could this be achieved by a single day’s effort? Thus I say: The Master’s learning was the learning of the great person of Qian.

Huang Mianzhai said: Some explain that the Sage established standards with humble words to encourage others, but if the Sage’s words were all empty and vain, how could they reflect the Sage’s upright and magnanimous heart? Profound indeed is this saying. Master Zhu also said: The Sage did not feign humility while being truly sagely in heart. He further said: "Extending knowledge" and "making intentions sincere" are two thresholds for learners. Extending knowledge is the threshold between dream and awakening; making intentions sincere is the threshold between good and evil. Passing through the threshold of extending knowledge is awakening, otherwise one remains in dream. Passing through the threshold of making intentions sincere is goodness, otherwise one remains in evil.

Heaven is man, and man is Heaven. At the beginning of a person’s life, what he receives from Heaven is already within him. Once this person exists, Heaven is also within him. All speech, sight, hearing, and movement are of Heaven. Thus it is said: "Hold fast to Heaven’s bright mandate," always seeing this principle clearly before one’s eyes. Learning the basics is a matter of practice. Principle resides within practice. If one can fully master the basics, then learning the basics leads to reaching the heights, and Heavenly principle is present. This is what it means for a person to be born—principle and qi unite. All thoughts, considerations, words, and deeds are qi, yet principle abides within them. Thus the Five Constants and the myriad virtues all manifest principle.

Master Zhen of Xishan said: "Exhausting principle" pertains to knowledge, leaving no thing uninvestigated. "Fulfilling nature" pertains to action, leaving no principle unembodied. When knowledge is fully exhausted and action is fully realized, one reaches the mandate. The mandate is what Heaven bestows upon me. The round outside and the hollow within are the substance of the heart; empty spirituality and awareness are its function; benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom are its principles.

Master Hu of Wufeng said: Great indeed is nature! The myriad virtues are complete within it. Heaven and Earth are established thereby. A spring-fed stream, though cold, does not freeze. A person of virtue, though in dire straits, does not falter. When the sun sets, its light is entrusted to the moon. When Confucius passed, the Dao was entrusted to Meng. Master Zhou said: The sage emulates Heaven, the worthy emulates the sage, and the scholar emulates the worthy. Yi Yin and Yan Yuan were great worthies. Yi Yin felt shame if his lord was not like Yao or Shun, and if a single person was not in their proper place, it was as if he were flogged in the marketplace. Yan Yuan did not shift his anger, did not repeat his errors, and for three months did not stray from benevolence. Aspire to Yi Yin’s aspirations, learn what Yanzi learned. Surpass them and you become a sage; equal them and you become a worthy; fall short and you still do not lose a good name.

Master Zhu said: According to the depth of one’s effort, one reaches near or far without losing a good name, for one possesses the reality of doing good. Thus Master Hu said: If one dissuades people from making official rank, wealth, and family prosperity their aim to seek favor, then say: "Aspire to Yi Yin’s aspirations." If one dissuades people from taking knowledge and learning as attainment and complacently resting without seeking to advance, then say: "Learn what Yanzi learned." Only when one truly establishes Yi Yin’s aspirations and cultivates Yanzi’s learning does one realize that the words of the *Tongshu* encompass the utmost greatness, and the undertakings of the Sage’s gate are boundless.

I say: For scholars who wish to transform their qi and disposition, nothing is greater than emulating the sage and emulating Heaven. Yet one must begin by emulating the worthy. Yi Yin, while plowing at Xin and delighting in the Dao, never lacked Yan Yuan’s learning. Yan Yuan, though dwelling in a humble alley, asked about governing a state, never lacking Yi Yin’s aspirations. Had these two great worthies exchanged places, both would have been the same. If scholars can reflect on themselves and be sincere, not engaging in superficial adornment, emulating them will surely succeed. Establish resolve and study with sincerity, without shame before oneself. Serve parents and follow elder brothers with sincerity, without shame before one’s family. Be loyal, trustworthy, earnest, and respectful with sincerity, without shame before one’s village.

Advance in thought to exhaust loyalty with sincerity, without shame before one’s office. Possess the goodness of substance prevailing, without the disgrace of reputation prevailing. Proceeding thus and holding fast, the worthy may be emulated. Abiding in nature and resting in it, the sage may be emulated. Its subtleties are invisible when issuing forth, its fullness is inexhaustible when expanding, and Heaven may be emulated. Those who aspire to the resolve of Yi and the learning of Yan—can they not seek these within themselves?

Master Cheng said: When the expansive power is fully realized, then Heaven and Earth transform and the grasses and trees flourish abundantly. When the expansive power is not realized, then Heaven and Earth close and the worthy withdraw into obscurity.

Master Zhu said: What one does not desire, do not impose upon others. Simply persist in expanding and advancing, and the myriad things will each attain their proper place. Expand it within a single household, a single state, or the entire world, and none will fail to attain their proper place. How then could Heaven and Earth not transform, or grasses and trees not flourish? If the expansive power is not realized, then upon stepping outside, one immediately encounters obstruction.

The *Book of Mencius* is simply about expanding the expansive power, daily gathering in one’s straying heart. This is drawing inward from without, daily knowing and expanding it. One must also push outward from within, hence it is said: Cultivate the self and the world is at peace—all of this does not go beyond the single word *shu* (reciprocity).

Master Zhu said: Reverence to straighten the inner, rightness to square the outer. These eight characters are inexhaustible in a lifetime’s use. When reverence is established, the inner is straight; when rightness takes form, the outer is square—this can be relied upon for a lifetime. Rightness is the heart’s judgment in affairs. When the heart judges within, the outer becomes square and upright, and the myriad things each attain their due.

The *Cinnabar Scripture* says: When reverence overcomes indolence, there is good fortune; when indolence overcomes reverence, there is ruin. When rightness overcomes desire, there is compliance; when desire overcomes rightness, there is misfortune. Reverence stands upright; indolence collapses. Acting according to principle is rightness; not acting according to principle is desire.

Furthermore, the character *zhi* (straight) is explained by *jing* (reverence), and *fang* (square) by *yi* (rightness). Reverence without rightness leads to errors in conduct. Rightness without reverence lacks a foundation. Only when reverence and rightness are established together does one avoid the isolation of "straightening the inner with reverence." "Squaring the outer with rightness" is the work of learning.

"Straight" means direct and unobstructed, with no trace of crookedness in the breast—this is the meaning of decisive and square integrity. Reverence is like a mirror; rightness is the reverence that can reflect. Without rightness, reverence is dead and lifeless, not the living reverence.

It is also said: One must make this a fundamental concern. When nurturing it to the point of thorough penetration, "reverence to straighten the inner" naturally leads to "rightness to square the outer." Even in quiet sitting, one must examine reverence and dissipation. Reverence is the heavenly principle; dissipation is human desire. In one’s dwelling, one must examine whether one is respectful or not; in handling affairs, whether one is reverent or not.

There are those who focus solely on understanding the state of quiescent non-movement. Yet when they encounter affairs, they become utterly disordered, disturbing even their quiescent state. Others focus solely on handling affairs but neglect the foundational work. One must be thorough from top to bottom, penetrating both inner and outer, to be truly adequate.

It is also said: Reverence is the virtue of Heaven. Reverence thoroughly penetrates above and below, without distinction from Heaven.

Master Cheng said: "Do not be irreverent"—this can be used to respond to the Lord on High.

Master Fan said: The three hundred canons of ritual and three thousand rules of decorum—one phrase summarizes them all: "Do not be irreverent."

Master Zhu said: Yao was the first sage to emerge and govern; the *Canon of Yao* in the *Book of Documents* is the first canonical text. In describing Yao’s virtue, no other word is used before "reverent." Now, when examining the sages and worthies, though their words number in the thousands, and their affairs range from great to small, none do not originate in reverence. When one gathers one’s spirit here, one can fully perceive the principle, and only then can one cultivate the self, order the household, and extend it to the world.

What form or shadow does reverence have? It is simply the gathering of body and mind. Not allowing a single thing to intrude is to be single-minded. Mingdao taught people to sit in quietude, as did Master Li. One must sit quietly to gather oneself; only then, when facing affairs, will one have strength.

It is also said: The character *jing* (reverence) pervades both movement and stillness.

Master Zhen said: If the winter qi fails to close and store properly, and warmth prevails without frost or snow, then the following year’s yang qi will lack strength, and the five grains will not ripen. The human heart is the same. If in stillness one is scattered and anxious, how can one be measured in activity? Thus Master Zhou took "abiding in stillness" as fundamental, and Master Cheng took "abiding in reverence" as fundamental—both express this principle.

Master Hu of Wuyi said: The single character *jing* (reverence)—after ten years of devoted practice, one must distinguish it.

Master Zhu said: It seems that even Heaven and Earth have a governing principle; only then can they undergo their transformations—this is the reverence of Heaven and Earth. That by which King Tang could match the Lord on High began with his daily ascent in sagely reverence.

I note: Master Lü of Donglai said: Reverence is the first word transmitted by the hundred sages. Where the sagely learning applies effort, even in the *Book of the Charge*, it is said: The subtle transmission of the Duke of Zhou was received by King Cheng. As he neared his end, he revealed it to his ministers. The subtle transmission of Confucius was received by Zengzi. As he neared his end, he revealed it to Meng Jingzi. Both centered on reverence alone.

The complete embodiment of the Supreme Polarity in the sage requires no cultivation yet remains in reverence—how much more so for scholars! Those who know this and cultivate it are the good fortune of the noble person; those who do not know this and act contrary to it are the misfortune of the petty person. Whether one cultivates or acts contrary depends entirely on the distinction between reverence and dissipation.

When reverent, desires diminish and principle becomes clear. Diminishing them further and further to the point of nonexistence, the sagely learning may be emulated. Thus Master Zhu said: What Master Cheng contributed most powerfully to later scholars was the single character *jing* (reverence).

Scholars, can you not strive for this?

*Tian Yuan Fa Wei*, Scroll Eighteen, concludes.

From "from the reign of Duke Xiang to that of Kings Cheng and Kang" through "the timing of the seasons"—all these passages were originally omitted and have been restored according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.

From "It is further said: Observing people within the celestial cycles, there is not a moment of leisure" to these passages—all were originally omitted and have been restored according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.

"In vain" (*tu*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "to bring about" (*zhi*).
"Of" (*zhi*)—originally "not" (*bu*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Barbarians" (*yi*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "bandits" (*dao*).
"To act" (*zuo*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "world" (*shi*).
"Barbarians" (*yi*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "bandits" (*dao*).
"In" (*yu*)—originally "son" (*zi*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Qi" (*Qi*)—originally "spring" (*chun*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
The character "next" (*ci*) was originally omitted and has been restored according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Grand" (*tai*)—originally "great" (*da*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition; "hidden" (*fu*)—originally "mysterious" (*xuan*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Blazing" (*yan*)—originally "fire" (*huo*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition. The three characters "Shennong Clan" were originally omitted and have been restored according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
The two characters "Yellow Emperor" were originally omitted and have been restored according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"In" (*yu*)—originally "to offend" (*gan*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Father" (*fu*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "mother" (*mu*).
"Six" (*liu*)—originally "one" (*yi*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Obscured" (*mei*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "dark" (*an*).
"Craft" (*gong*)—originally "king" (*wang*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Three" (*san*)—originally "two" (*er*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Bestowed" (*fu*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "endowed" (*fu*).
This passage originally read "discussing nature without discussing qi is not by means of," emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Of" (*zhi*)—originally "person" (*ren*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Zhu" (*Zhu*)—originally "various" (*zhu*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Grasp" (*xie*)—originally "narrow" (*xia*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Talent" (*cai*)—originally "inch" (*cun*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Where" (*suo*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "able" (*neng*).
"Its" (*qi*)—originally "possess" (*ju*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
This passage in the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads: "Knowing Heaven’s Mandate is attaining the heavenly principle."
"Waves" (*bo*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "flow" (*liu*).
"Violate" (*wei*)—originally "establish" (*jian*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Foundation" (*ji*)—originally "its" (*qi*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Constant" (*chang*)—originally "ought" (*dang*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Endowment" (*fu*)—originally "bestow" (*fu*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Manifestation" (*jian*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "light" (*guang*).
"Perish" (*wang*)—originally "scholar" (*shi*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Heaven" (*tian*)—originally "great" (*da*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Disloyalty" (*er*)—originally "or" (*huo*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Waves" (*bo*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "burst" (*jue*).
"Not" (*bu*)—originally "offer" (*feng*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
"Others" (*ta*)—the *Siku Quanshu* edition reads "earth" (*di*).
"Those who know" (*fu zhi*)—originally "not yet arrived" (*wei zhi*), emended according to the *Siku Quanshu* edition.
The *Siku Quanshu* edition omits the character "those" (*zhe*).
